THE French Chirurgerye, or all the manualle operations of Chirurgerye, with
divers, sundry Figures, and amongst the rest, certain nuefovvnde
Instruments, very necessary to all the operationes of Chirurgerye.

Through Iaqves Gvillemeau, of Orleans ordinary Chirurgiane to the King, and
sworn in the City of Paris.

And now truly translated out of Dutch into English by A.M.

IMPRINTED At Dort by Isaac Canin. M.D.xcvij.





TO THE MOST HIGH, MIGHTY, ROYalle, and Victoriouse Princess, the most
Christiane, and Virtuose defendresse, of the sincere, true Christiane Religione
LADY ELISABETH By the grace of God, Queen of Englande, France, and Irelande.
c.
MOst graciouse Queen, your Royalle Mtye. is not ignorant (I trust) of the
great services, which through, the hands, of expert Chyrurgians,
operatinge on Mans body, are offerede as sweet smelling sacrifices, up into
the nostrells, of the omnipotet, Longevalle Emperioure of the Caelestialle
influences. Whereof also all renoumnede, providente Caesaristes, have
dilligently providede, and furnishede themselves, when as they are wounded,
by their means, and aid (next unto God) to be curede, and have of there
health restauratione. I could not therefore (being also incitatede, and
councelede thereunto by divers and sundry of my friends, vvellvvillers) omit
this boocke, composede by the most expert, and very learnede Chyrurgiane Mr.
Iaques Guillemeau, addictede Chyrurgian to the King of France, of the which
(without doubt) the French natione, do reap great service, and commodity
thereof, and therefore I have for the service and utility of your Renoumnede
Majesties subjects, poeple, and also to the commodity of our contrye (being
vnitede in the bond of amity together) with all expeditione possible for me
causede it to be translatede both into English, and Lovvdutch because that all
younger, and junior Chyrurgians, might heerin behold, and view, (as in a
resplendente Lookinge-glasse) the most part, and principalle Instruments of
Chyrurgerye also with a very excellent methode, and doctrine, for all the
especialle operations, which a Chyrurgiane (or any other man being in
necessity) other by land, or by water, might have any use for. And seeing
that there is no King, nor Prince on earth, which with such fervent desire,
and fidelity endeavours by all means possible, to maytayne, and protecte,
the health and wealth, both of body and soul of their subjects, and
poeple, as your (most Illustriouse Mtye.) doth for your peculiar inhabitants,
being of God thereunto constitutede, and ordaynede. The excellence of this
boocke, hath therefore, incitatede, as it vveare copellede me to use this
audacitye (in sign of gratitude, for so many, innumerable benefits, which
next after God, of your Royal Mtye. through your continualle aid,
opitulatione we reap) humblelye to present, and dedicate this very
necessary boocke, unto your most Magnificent Mtye. firmelye trusting,
assuredly hoping, that your renoumnede Mtyes. subjects, not only within,
but also far without your Mtyes. Realms, dominiones, shall heerebye be
greatly aydede, and thereof also reap great commodity. I have effected this
vvorcke, partly to demonstrate the sincere love, affection wherewith
(through the good acqvayntance of your Mtyes. Gentlemen, Capitaynes, and
valiant Sovldiours, which your most Royal Mtye. hath vouchsafede so many
years to send us) I affect your Mtye. as also to cause all men together with
me gratefully to acknowledge, the ineffable mercy of God, which hath
imbuede, and adorned your renoumnede Mtye. with so many noble, vvorthye, and
excellent virtues, and very miraculously hath defendede, and protected, your
Grace, against all the sly, and vvylye practises of your Majesties enemies, by
this means to continue, and by your hands to protecte, not only your
dominion, and other nations, your neightvours and vnitede subjects (amongst
the which we account our selves, most bounden to your Mtye.). But also heerby
to cause the florishinge of all spiritual, corporalle arts in the midst of
your Realm, and Kingdom. Amongst which, the Art, and Science of Surgery
is esteemede, accoumptede (as the first, and most vvorthieste, and necessary
part of Physic) whereof I here present your Mtye. one of the most
exquisite, and perpolite boockes touching, and concerning the same. Most
humbly in treating your Mtye. that it would vouchsafe your Highness not to
miscontrue this my temeritye, but take, and construe my audacitye, which I
have in this my dedicatione used, to no other end, then that with all my
heart, and mind I endevoure to gratify your most renoumnede Mtye. be at the
command according to my bounden duty) of your most Royal Mtyes. subjects,
as much as is possible for me. Thus fearing least I might be some
interruptione, hinderance unto your Highness, I heervvith, commit your Royal
Mtye. into the divine protectione, and hands of the omnipotent King, and
Father of Heaven, whom incessantly I pray, to give ominouse, fortunate event
to your divine attempts, to the furtherance of his holy vvorde, and reformede
church, being congregatede together under the shaddovve of your Highness
vvinges.

Your Mtyes most humble, vvellvvillinge subject, who in sign of gratitude
addicteth himself vvholye to the service of your Highness and your subjects.
Maximiliane Bouman Surgeon at Dort.

TO THE KING
MAn being allianced, both with Heaven, and earth, is through his corporal
necessities constrained, to conterate, and spend the best time of his Life, in
Sleep, repose: notwithstanding being resuscitated, erected out of a
profound, deep somniation, through the best part of himself, which is his
Soul, he pursues, only and favours very soberly, the most vile and
abiecte things, the concupiscence, and appetite whereof, he communicates, and
participate the, with brute, and irrationalle beasts. For man is not only
this mask or vizard, and this figurated and articulated Massa in certain
members, which with our eyes we discover and behoulde, with our fingers we
may touche and sensibly feel: but is rather, the virtue, and internalle for
me, which effectuateth life in him, vigour, agilitye, and temperature, the same
being as it vveare, a radiant beam a little sparckle, and parcel, of the
Divinitye, transmitted, and descended out of the most gloriouse Empire of
Heaven, to effectuate, and bring to pass in this wretched world, in our
body, a short, and brief ligatione: For who is there which doubts, that
the Soul, remembringe, and calling to mind her first originalle, would not
address, and endevoure her self to learn, yea and if it vveare possible to
relerne, all those things, which be honourable unto her Creator, might also be
profitable to all other creatures? for this cause it is, vvhye we have not our
countenances declined towards the earth as the brute beasts, but have them
elevated towards Heaven, because through such contemplacione, and by reading
of the true book of life, we might draw therout, and practice on the ordre,
and imitate, the constance which the celestial corps, do observe, and one
their perpetualle, and profitable agitatione thereof. Let us therefore consider
on the Sun, which is as it vveare a Prince, and moderatoure of all other
celestial constellations: what doth she othervvyse effect through her
environing round of the earth, then light us, calefye, and warm us, vivisye,
administre life unto us? but what commodity, and profit is it unto us to
search the sun on high have we not the earth it self which with our
feet we inculcate, and tread one, and accuse the same to be brute,
insensible, doth she refuse, to receave, and conceave, and again
continually, and without cessation superfluouslye procreate, and liberally
impart unto us, all that which is necessary, unto our vesture aliment, and
nurture? It vveare also indeed great shame, perfidiousnes, and execrable
disloyaltye, and treason for us, whom God hath adorned with so livelye,
vigorouse, and fruitful a spirit, that as all things which are inanimate,
doc travayle, and labour for our proffite, we should show our selves
stupidouse, and involuntary to help the one the other in all things possible
and advaunce them: for as the wise, and sage men heretofore have said, that
even as all the mundayne, and terrestrialle things, are created for man, and
his use, in like sort also are men created for God, and the one man for the
other. This consideratio most illustriouse, mighty King, hath urged, and
impelled me, after that I next unto your majesty, have discharged my duty, and
function, which I was ovvinge unto your Highness, and that especially in
curing of my Lord, the Earl, of Montpensier and my Lords of Buhy, of
Montigny, to peruse, revolve certain rescriptiones, and memorialles, which I
had compiled, and collected together, when I served the deceased King, as
well touching the manual operations, as also the comparisone, and conference
of the ancient instruments of Chyrurgerye, with the nue inventede, because
through the discourse rehearsal of the same, and by the discriptione and
defiguratione of the foresayed instruments, to demonstrate, what commodity
we might hope, and reap out of the one more then out of the other: All
which, having according to my possibillirye, as much as this pitiful time
would permit allovve of finished, I have embouldened myself, to consecrate,
and dedicate the same unto your most renoumned Majesty: whom supliantlye
with all humillity I desire, request, and entreat, benignly to receave it,
as a gift, or present, which is not disagreeing unto the present time, nether
will be discommodiouse to your highness. For because this Kingdom of France,
is blinded with rage, error, through the fatalle censure of the most fearful
God, doth ruinate it self, into his own spoil, of what art I pray (saying
that all other arts do decrease, under this fear of wars) might it rather
implore comfort secourse, the of Physic or Chyrurgerye? And what is more
Royalle, more magnificall heer on earth, yea and more divine, then to recreate
the afflicted, to relevate, consort the depressed, humbled, and to restore
health to all men, without which all dulcor, pleasantnes of our life is
nothing else then amaritude, and Gaul? Wherefore most magnificent, and
renoumned King, permit^ pray and humbly entreat your highness, that under
your Heroical name, this Pamphlet may be published, because through the same,
as alsoe through your most illustriouse acts, of the which the posterity,
perpetually shall not be silent, may be revealed, and known, how fain your
Highness saw this poor Kingedome of France flourish again: which now
seems to respire, and in short time exsperateth to have an ominouse and
happy end of her miseries, and calamityes, through the prudence wherewith
your Majestic valour, and vigilant valiance is accompaniede, and
associated. From Paris, the 15. of September, 1594.

Your most humble, and most obedient subject, and servitoure GVILLEMEAV.

The Epistle to the benevolent Reader.
THere is a common saying, is now taken for a common proverb: that all
things have their time, their place, and their sayson: by which occasion
divers, sundry interprises, which indeed of themselves are good, do leese
their grace, and decorum, only because in their condecent time they vveare
not practised, put in ur. But it is not so with the vtilitye, which the
common wealth expects, through our industry, principally, especially,
through the studies of good arts: such fruits are never importune, nether at
any time out of season, nether abortive: but are at all times very vvellcome,
profitable, on what time soever, on what manner, or of whom they might be
brought: In which fact, the maxim, or proverb may very truly be spoken of
Maximo Fabio, vvhoe said, that all intentions, which to the publiqve profit
of the commonevvealthe vveare assayede, intended is allvvayes well hopede of,
to have a good evente, is esteemede to be a happy intent, or praemeditatione:
And contrarily those are esteemede infeliciouse, unfortunate, are of a
funestalle, and sorrowful event, which are intendede, and attendede, unto
the damage of the commone vvealthe.

Wherefore I need not doubt, of that which I praetende to maintain, and
publish into light, shall of any one vvhatsoever, be taken in bad part,
because I have conceavede, and publishede the same in time of jars, and
wars: For what time might I have elected more convenient, propre, more
profitable to meditate one this Chyrurgerye, which compraehendeth her
operatione, propre Instruments, then at this time, France being in all
qvarters, in al parts, armed, in vveapons? animated against her own
entralles, we having our own minds exvlcerated as it vveare, and hardened
the one against the other, by which occasion we to our great grief, and
sorrow, must behoulde, at all hours, so many poor wounded, mutilatede
persons, their corps being vvouded through the rage, and malevolence of their
foes, on the which we are constrained to employ our hands, instruments to
reduce again the same to their former, and accustomed health again? Or what
might they othervvyse hope, and expect of me, then that which is according,
and agreinge unto my vocatione, calling* For as the Poet says* The
Neptunist of winds, of storms, and of tempest
Discoursinnge on the shore, to his mates can talk best;
The Cerialist, of Oxen, Horse, and Plowes
The cruel Martialist, of wounds, and of his blows.


Therefore having seen, and also with my own hands practised the space of
tvventye years the operation of Chyrurgerye, in most of the famous, renoumned
Cities, of France, the Lovve Countries, as well one great, as small
persons, and that with such diligence, and methode, that I couplinge, and
comparinge, the use of the ancient Chyrurgians, with the practice of the
modern, and latter Chyrurgians, I then endeavoured my self, as much as the
disease, the Patient also would permit me, to temperate, the rigoure, and
severitye of the first Operatours, or Chyrurgians, through the supple, and
gentle or sweet dexteritye of those which followed, to assimilate the the
one with the other, supplyinge in the counterchang, of that which was
vvantinge in the one, through the dexteritye, and industriouse curiosity of
the other, reducinge the same unto the best form of being, alsoe the
vvritinges, or scriptions, of the most expertest Chyrurgias, of this preset
time, with the vvritinges judgement of the whole, through the controulinge of
the vvritinges of the one of the other: So that I have intended to make a
collection: in form of a Manual for my self, to opitulate, address my
memory, in time of need, and promptly to effectuate, any manualle, and not
vulgar operatione: but the liberalitye through the which I am so voluntary,
to communicate the same unto certain of my good friends, vvhoe are studiouse,
desiruose of Chyrurgerye, hath precipitated me into this present prodigality,
of the which I suddaynlye retired drevve my self backvvarde, blushing as it
vveare ashamed, considering the means thereof, as being vnvvorthy to come into
light (although notvvithstandinge everye one hath a vain imaginatione,
affection, unto the same which is proceeded from him) so that I omit the
hardiness which I might have had, to publish the same unto the print, I
could very difficultly contain my desirous, covetuouse hands, of that
which I with great labour, diligence, and through lunge experience had
collected together, to have in one momet abolished the same, if I had not
purposed to reserve the same for my own particular, and peculiar commodity.
But my foresayed friends debellating against my timorous fearful defensions,
through certain experience of time passed: to vvitt, that my book of the
diseases of the eyes, after certain days he was comited to the press, was
unto all Ch^rurgians very necessary, profitable: and that my first, second
Tables of Anatomye, had administered unto all men such great service, and
promptitude of the Anatomy, and cognisance of the parts of mans body: They
have in the end resolved me of my doubtful fear of prodigality, adding also
therbye that this fear of prodigality, could be dammageable unto no man
then unto me, and that it could make no breach into my credit at all.

Through these foresayed remonstrations being convicted, persvaded, under the
pretext of great furtherance, which younger Chyrurgians might heerby reap,
and not being possible for me any longer to resist, the importunitye of
certain sproutes, beginners of Chyrurgerye, who vveare of opinione, that this
present piece of vvorcke, with all the deformityes thereof, might be profitable
for them, and being certifiede, that if by any means they could attain them
into their hands, they would commit the same to the print even such as it
vveare. So that I am constrained to employ my self certain hovvres of the
day, in correcting of the same and a little more imbellishe, and adorn, the
same, with 4 portrayctures of mans body because the same entringe into the
world, might be effected with more favour, credit, whereof the two first,
do demonstrate all external parts, as well anteriorlye, as posteriorlye, in
the other two, all the superficial Veins, are evidently laid open unto us,
which as little rivers do run through the face through all other
external parts of the body extend themselves as well behind as before,
because the same being of the young Chyrurgians noted, they with less danger
might open any of them.

Farther I have enriched this vvorcke, with divers defigurations of
Instruments, not of all Instruments, but of those only, which I esteemed to
be most necessary: For even as the sage, prudent, and vvyse Nature, hath
instituted such an ordre, to mans body, that she with few parts, can
accomplish, many great and excellent actions, In like sort must the
Chyrurgiane, an imitatoure, and administre of Nature, endevoure, and
constraygne himself, with few Instruments to execute, and effect, many,
great Operations. And to speak the plain truth all this great company and
treasure house of Engines, Molitiones, and of other Chyrurgicall Instruments,
are more for curiousity, oftentatione, then for any necessity, and use. I am
also very certain of that which our Hippocrates says, to wit, that we
ought not to constraygne, or restraygne, the liberty of our corps, and the
Operations, unto the penury, and deficiency of Instruments: but much more
amplify, and enrich the same, unto the commodity of the body, and ease of
the Operations: but I would rather exoptate, and desire, that this vanity,
superstitiouse bravadinge of Instruments, vveare ruled with more religiouse
mediocritye.

Also I have made, a great ample Table, or Index, in the end of the Boock,
wherein through Alphabetical ordre I have illustrated, the most notablest,
difficultest things, which in the inqvisitione might be an impediment, unto
the vnexperte in the art of Chyrurgerye, in seeking of their farther
commodity. And if so be any man object unto me, that this my discourse, is
only compilede together of certain rapsodyes of the antiqve Chyrurgians: I
vvillingely here confess, acknowledge, that in this Treatise, there is very
little, or nothing at all of mine own Invention: For I am not the man which
lives by an other mans mutuated supellectilles and I rather publiqvely confess,
that I have collected all this out of ancient Chyrurgians, being great shame
for me to be repraehended, and surprised, of a secrete theft. For what
noveltyes are under the Sun? as the vvyse man says: And as the Comicus
says, what should we now adays say, which hath not before bin spoken?
But I may at all times with veritye say, that I am not entered into this matter
to sow, and feminate the same in an infertille grovvnde: For I have added
thereunto great amendmente, and have eradicated, vveeded out all spinosities,
thistles, whereof they vveare replenished with great difficulties,
seqvestringe on the one side malevolet herbs, which choacked the good
fruits of the truth, composinge all things in good ordre, to add thereunto
clearness, and facility: through the which excellent, modern, ancient
Authors may be understood of those, which can attain to the klovvledge of
no strange langvages. Nether have such Personages, out of which I have taken
any of their operationes, observed in all their treatises one ordre,
perfectly, and one manner of operatione, but one divers places, and in divers
Books they have described them: wherefore they are not to be reprehended, who
with a conseqvence, have redacted them together, because they might the easier
be compraehended, and understood. And although those which heretofore have
written of Instrumetes have cotentede themselves only vvithe the
representation, of the simple Figures: In have heer endeavoured to present
dimensions, and proportiones, their longitude, and crassitude, and so
naturally, that nether the Chyrurgiane, nor the Smithe which we cause to
compose the same might have any occasion of hesitatione, or doubt, nether
fall into any error, of their commensuratione appropriatinge unto everye
one of them his name in Greecke, Latin, French, report also his own
opportunity, and usage thereof.

name in Greecke, Latin, and French, and report also his own opportunity,
usage thereof*

I know right well, that in this vvorcke there vvilbe some operations of
Chyrurgerye vvantinge, and that it is not vvholye perfect: But I assure my
self, that everye man of a good spirit, judgment, will acknowledge, that I
of purpose have omitted the same, and that especially because certain
operations, thereof are not now a days in use, practise: as is the manner of
inscisione of the skin of the Head, which the Greeckes call Pericuphismos:
Also the extirpatione of vvoemens breasts which hang toe long: of the
Cauterizinge of the Liver, and of the Mil^e: of the extractio of a stone out of
the blather, because I my self am not therein instructed: Al which operations,
I permit to practise the right vvorshipfull Monsr. Collo Chyrurgiane at Paris,
a personage so rare therein that all France hath not his like, with all
dexterity to effectuate such an operatione: In like sort also Mr. Pineau, also
Chyrurgiane at Paris, which is the resplendente star of Light unto all the
Anatomistes of these times. Nether do I doubt, that he which dilligently
searches this vvorcke, that he shall find any diffaulte therein: for I doubt
whether that any curious poeple should accomplish, the same, saying the
Author himself could not sufficiently accomplish the same: But I hope, to
receave of the benevolent Reader, some excuse, he noting, and considering,
that such operatios which are very titillouse can not at one time be begun,
and also at the same time finished, and that it is sufficient, with great
difficulty rightly to imprint the same. Wherefore if this enterprise seem
alienate, and strange unto any body, and they mocked thervvith, I certify
unto them, that I envye no man, which can meliorize the same: And as
touching those which seem not to be in the world for any other use, then
to censure, repraehende others without vvillinge yea and being able to
effectuate ought themselves, let them iovise, joy themselves, of their
privileadge, as long as pleases them, wherewith I do not much molest my
self. Othervvyse, I have written only for the junior, younger Chyrurgians, and
not for those, which are vvholye and completlye edoctrinized, and search for
nothing else then for a perfection of all things: I have not exalted my heart
so hight, as those severe censurers would require, and I suffice my self
with my accustomed manner, with the behouldinge of the commodity of the
French iunioritye, of that which my small wit, could set forth: I only
desire this one thing of them, that they would vouchsafe to meliorize the
same, as I certaynlye know they well can, and do not retain their own
commodity thereof. And it vvilbe a great joy, and pleasure unto me that therein
they surpass me: I shall very well know to apply the same to my profit,
and commodity, which they through their great labour, and industry, have
brought forth, nether will I conceal that which of them I shall have
learned.

And although it be the opinion of the Sages that we ought not to lightly,
thereveale secretes, of Physic, unto the common, vulgar poeple treating of
such matters, in their maternal, motherlye langvage, because it might be
esteemed of little worth, be of all men little accounted of: And although I
could easily, have uttered, and expressed my intent, in latin, yet I rather
published the same in our French language: And that first of all, because all
annciente Learned men, whether they be Greeckes, Arabers, or Latinistes, have
written al their doctrine, and experience in the maternal language. The
primates, and princes of Physic, Hippopocrates, and his faithful, and fidele
interpreter, commitatoure Galen, Paulus Aegineta Oribasius do vvitnes of the
same. Avicenna, and Averroes, have also done the same, vvritinge in the
Arabiane Langvage. Celsus and Plinius, the first and they which have only
written in Latin, have alsoe done their endevoure, and have also disired to
imbellishe, and eternize, their Latin langvage, contrye, through the terms,
and theoremes of Physic. Farthermore also, by how much the more a thing is
more commone so much the more is it better, and by so much the more may this
science of all men be known, lauded, and extolde. The Physic of
Hippocrates, and of Galen, the Phylosophye, of Plato, and of Aristotle, are
they therefore obscured or less esteemed of, because they vveare vvrittene of
the Auncientes in Latin, and Arabic langvage? And those which of our time,
have written, in French Dutch Italiane, as many renoumnede me have done,
vvhoe in all sorts have indevoured, to do some service to the common
vvealthe, hade they here in committede any thing vvorthye of repraehension?
they which write in unknown langvages, besides that they are profitable
unto strangers, and to their onre native soil discommodiouse, are
assimilated and resembled unto farmers, which would rather till, an other
mans field, then their own propre grovvnd, to the great dishonour, shame,
detrimente, of their country, and inhabitants.

VVherfor I most humblely entreat, and obsecrate all men, to receave
gratefully, thanck fully this my labour, and divine desire, which I have to
see the junior Chyrurgians, with all their minds industriously labourig to
follow imitate me: if so be it pleased that any more learned ma the I am
vouchsafede to perlegate with pleasure, employ a small of their time in the
reading, of my scriptsons, and vvritinges, I most courtiouslye desire the,
that they would more with good will, then with a spirit, which to
narrovvlye espies, to repraehende the faults, vue circumsplectlye note the
defaults which might be therein: Least that through to great curiosity, and
to seriouse subtilty, that same happen to me, as in times passed, happened to
the Philosopher Theodosio, who turning over the precepts of Philosophy with
his right hand fro his Anditors, they who with their left hand could
also reach him, through malice, and impuritye of spirit, turned, misconstrued
the same in bad sort. Which if so by any means I can attain unto, and
know that this my vvorcke, (published through the importunitye of the
iunioure Chyrurgians) shall be aggreeable, and acceptable, unto many, it will
then administre courage, unto me, to pursue, and address the remanent of my
studies, wherein I am entered: that is, to medidate, and to publish for the
younger Chyrurgians, all those things, whereby Gods honour might be increased,
and be profitable, for the common wealth.

Candidus imperti meliora, vel utter nostris:
Carper vel noli nostra, vel ede tua.
Some men do read, to reap some good thereof,
Others to mock, and hovvlde thereof a soffe,
It is more ease heerin to reprehend,
Then any thing therein, for to amend.
To the gentle, curtiouse Reader.
SOmetimes with my self cosideringe, in the balance of my cogitations
vveighinge, how perrillous, dangerous an attempt it is (most benevolence,
benign, curtiouse Reader) in these times, and ages, I omit ether to write, or
divulgate any vvorcke, but to translate it out of one language into an other.
Yet I would not, nor indeed could not partly to satisfy the great
importunitye of certain vvellvvillers of the English natione, and poeple, but
especially of my reverente master, vvhoe although no English man, yet I
assure thee one who through certain acqvayntance of English Captains in
these Lovvcountryes, doth affect all English men, would heerby show
himself grateful not only unto them, but to the whole society of our
Majesties subjects, by incitatinge, as it vveare compellinge me his poor
servant here unto, obiectinge unto me my inconstant levitye, vvhoe having
receavede so many, so great benefits of the poeple, students in Englande
would not impart this excellent, exquisite, and perpolite piece of vvorcke
unto them, saying that also the Printer, was so vvillinge to incitate me here
unto, here by alsoe expressinge his affectionated good will both to our most
renoumnede Majesty (whom God lunge praeserve) and her subjects. I standing
in a dump, being with these objections mute, Nesciens quid agerem, aut quo
me verterem, not knowing where to hide my self, at the last as one being
erectede, and suscitatede out of a svvound, I feeblely made this ansvver, that
it fitted not nether was agreinge to my harsh, rude, and illiterate stile to
begin such a piece, of vvorcke, and I being no English man borne might
chance (as without doubt I have) to take Sissiphus his labour on me, in not
vvritinge good English. And which is more, least I should attribute any
occasion of offence to my most reverent brother who being a Doctor of
Physic, I but an illiterate, ignorant youth applying my mind unto
Chyrurgerye, might suppose me to be so pratchante, highminded that I sought to
aequall my self with him who through his affection he bears to Englande
hath also translatede an excellent book of Physic. But at the last I being
fully resolved of my doubts, of the persuaded, that it would of all men be
taken, construede to a good end, I could not any longer resist their
importunate assaults on the imbicille vvalles of my ansvvers, but they having
therein made a breach I was constrained to yield my self a captive,
acknowledge them victors in consenting to their requests, by taking this
labour of Atlas on my shoulders. Therefore I would not vvillingelye, have
that this light inconstancy should be objected against me, for not
impartinge unto thee (most gentle Reader) these my obliterate lines of this
expert, excellent, and perpolite Chyrurgiane Iaqves Gvillaemeau, whom for
his excellentie of his manuale operations I assure thee such a one hath never
in English bin traslated: Behold now therefore to show my self
greatefulle for those benefits which vvhilome I have receavede in this
florishinge, blessed country of Englande, both in the Vniversitye, and in the
other Cities thereof, I here humblelye praesent unto thee this my first
attempt, who although indeed no Englisheman borne, yet bearing a true
English mans heart unto our Majesty endevouringe continually to show my
self grateful unto her, her subjects. And although it be more gross,
impolite, then decent, convenient, to ansvver the fine, scoffing heads,
wits of these times vvhoe almost can do nothing else then scoff, mock,
flout, at other men's industryes, and labours, yet I pray the (gentle Reader)
not to eesteeme them all vain, indecent, and invtile, for Non cuivis la
contingit adire Corinthum, everye man can not have a nose like a shooing horne,
that is everye one hath not such a fertile wit as they have for I
acknowledge my self to have but a barrane, vnfrictefulle grovvnde that there
doth nothing then Filix innascere agris meis, and how is it possible then to
reap good Fruits thereof? And know (curtiouse Reader that I take it to be
a godly thing to publish such a piece of vvorcke for the aid, succour of
all yonge Chyrurgians to operate, vvorcke on mas body, for what more
praeciouse thing is there on earth then man: and if then we endeavour to
praeserve, the health of our bodies it is then necessary to have such boockes
out of which we may learn the means whereby we might do it, I have the
more vvillinglye translatede the same into the vulgar English tunge because
everye one hath not the gift of langvages, although it be not so exqvisitelye
done as it vveare convenient it should, tamen est laudanda voluntas, I have
done my best indevoure thereunto, for there where ability lacks, my goodvvill
notvvithstandinge is to be receavede, and not to be repugnede, rejected. Nether
shalt thou here (gentle Reader) have any exquisite methode, nor the flovving
stile of Demostlenes, and eloquence of Cicero, but a plain common, and vulgar
stile for it was not of the Author composed in the first for such me as are
expert in these operations, but for the commonaltye, and younger Chyrurgians,
that they heerbye might learn the true operations of Chyrurgerye. If my bold
audacitye, or audaciouse bouldnes shall administer occasion of offence to any
man, I recurre to that common refuge, which promises remission to him which
gives his first assault. Wherefore if the learned will vouchsafe to read
this my first beginning without envye, or malice against me, I shall think
my industry very vvellimployede not to have bestowed all my labour in vain:
but if through the harshnes of my illeterate rude stile they are caused to lay
it out of their hands, sight, yet I humblely request them to take him up
again not for my sake, but for his own, vvhoe although defilede, polluted
with my impolitede stile, yet they may gather in the garden of his exqvisite
vvorckes, as fragrant gillovvfers of Chyrugicall operations (I dare avouch) as
any ever vveare in English. And if there be any of Momus his partakers, which
do revile, scoff, flout me, who are so scrupulouse, praeiudicialle, that
with no Physic they can be cured of their critical dissease, tell them I
pray thee (gentle Reader) from me, that it is far easier for them to
repraehende, then to correct or meliorize, and that they dare not for the best
cap they have take such a thing in hand, least they peradventure should
fall into the pitt which for other they had made, for there is an old, and
inveterate proverb as I remember in Latin Quod tibi fieri non vis, alterine
feceris, let them (I say) read this proverb I trust it vvilbe a purgatio for
them. What shall I say more? for indeed I can say nothing as the Comicus
says quod non fit dictum prius, If therbe any which with their Argus eyes,
will vue, so narrovvlye princke, and look, for faults in this my labour
without doubt they shall find faults sufficient, but I would request them
to leave their eyes at home with Lamia, when they loock for any faults heer
in, spit in a box, the spittle of quips, quiddityes, cast them behind some
chest of oblivione that they never may any more be spoken of, and let them not
despise this my poor farthing, for it may be heerafter shall find my self
doing with some other piece of vvorcke more excellent. If therefore (benevolent
Reader) we find this vvorcke to be acceptable unto thee, my master shall
think his motion to have bin blessede and happy, the Printer his irritatione
commodiouse, and I my labours, and industryes well bestovvede, and it shall be
as it vveare a Calcar unto me thereafter to employ my self with the
translatione of some other excellent piece of vvorcke. If not I would friendly
desire such as scoff heerat, to read, dilligently consider these two verses
which the Author recites, and are these.

Candidus imperti meliora, vel utter nostris:
Carper vel noli nostra, vel ede tua.
Thus being loath to interrupt thee any longer with vain, illiterate
speeches I commit thee to the tuition of God thy Creator, his son Ihesu
Christ who with his effused blooredeemed thee, and of the holy ghost, who
inspires thee with his spirit, continually vvishinge increace of all
virtues, and lunge life unto thee.

Thyn as his own, or else not worthy of vital breath. A. M.

THE THESAVRARYE, OR STOREHOVSE of Chyrurgerye: wherbye are also added Four
figures, or portrayctures of mans body: Two of the which do demonstrate unto
us, all the external parts of the same: and the other two, the most
visiblest veins fit for phlebotomye, which lye dispearced under the skin
Through Iaqves Gvillemean of Orleans, Ordinatede Chyrurgiane to the King, and
svvorne in his Chastelet at Paris.
The Praeface.
WE say in France for an ancient, verified proverb: That it is not
sufficiete enough that we do any thing, but that we do the same
exquisitlye, profitablelye. Which can not with any convenience be excuted or
accomplished, without knowledge of the causes, which do lead, and conduct
us, to the perfectione of the effect: So that according to the sayings of the
Philosophers, Science is nothing else, then the knowledge of that, What Sciece
i^
which we have in hands, through his proper causes. Unto the which all
Artificers must endevoure to attain: through the which Chyrurgerye, although
notwithstanding, we can not attain to the knowledge, cognisance of the
present subject, the accidents thereof, through such occasions as we have
discribede, the same being as it vveare an inferior science, and dependinge,
on the Naturalle, and Physical sciences: we ought notvvithstandinge to know
at the least this poyncte, and to observe, and follovve the same, and that
through the most sensible, and apparent causes, which are Materialle, and
Instrumentalle.

Which hath movede me to represent, Wherefore the Author hath collocated the
Instrumetes in the forefrontutes of his book.
and as it vveare evidentlye unto all men's eyes demonstrate, not only the
materialle, and formalle principles, whereof our subject, of mans body, is
created, and compilede together, but the Instrumentalles, through the which
we as by such means maintain the same in his natural health, as lunge as
there is any health before hands, and also again restore the same, being
imbecillede, debilitated, through the combat which we have against the
dissease, or sickness.

Nether to speak truth is it a thing conveniente, and seemlye, that any
great, or vvorthye Captain, should assail his enemy, before he
apparantlye know him, and have for his own commodity, and advantage made
electione, and choice of the place, field of combat, hath ordrede and alsoe
armed his soldiers: no less also, must he besiege any strong, The office,
duty of a good Captain.
and fortified City, without first having advertisemente, knowledge, of the
most feebleste place of the same, where in storminge his soldiers may least
of all be endamagede, he having sufficient admunitione, to make a breach.

In like sort also vveare it great temeritye, and rashenes, for any
Chyrurgiane, to take in hand any operatione, of Chyrurgerye, without first
of all considering one the subject, The office of a good Chirurgiane.
one the which he intends, and purposes to operate, or vvorcke,
convenientlye to situate, and collocate the same, and being fournishede of all
his Instruments conveniente for such an operatione, therbye to attain unto
his pretended intent. I humbly reqveste, the most benevolent, and gentle
Reader, to have me excusede, although I have not collocatede, them in such
number, as I vvillingelye could wish and desire I had, having
notwithstanding causede the same to be representede: but through the injuries
of this most disturbede, molestede time, there are some of the Plates (unto my
great molestatione, and sorrow,) lost, and could not by any means
possible so suddaynlye be again pourtrayctede, engraved: the which
notvvithstandinge in the next editione I hope to collocate them.

many other fashons, to prevent, and evite all confusions vvhatsoever.

Sundry Instruments to draw out Bulletes



Declaration of the Characters contaynede in the Table, of the Instruments of
the Head.


















































I have here endevourede, to present, the most convenienteste sorts of
Elevatoryes, which now a days are most in use, in such magnitude, greatness
of form, as might be most conveniente for the Chyrurgiane, considering all
other small instruments or ferramentes of the Head, as are the Raspers,
which of set purpose I omittede, least I should cloy the Chyrurgiane with
to many Instruments, because by those in place of others he might content and
suffice himself, because that such an infinite, number of instrumetes do
serve more for ostentatione, and pride then for any necessary use.

Instruments fit for the extirpatione of any Ioynete



Declaratione of the Characters contaynede in the Instruments, which are apt
to opene any Apostemations.


The use of those said plates, is only to hallucionate, deceave, those,
which will not permit an apertione to be made in their Apostemationes, and
are to timorouse, fearfulle of the hand, or lancet of the Chyrurgiane, as
commonly are vvoemen, little children: to open the Apostemation beside their
expectation, and intelligence: you must have in preparation your Caraplasma,
and before the application thereof you must have in a readiness a plaster of
Diapalma, or some such like, which must be somewhat larger then your plate, on
the which plaster you must apply the plate, which in like sort also, with
the acuity thereof must be coverede with the salve Basilicon, which being
finish de, you must apply this plaster being in this sort preparede, on the
place, where you conjecture the foresayede Apostematione must be openede,
therone the Cataplasma, on the which you must the stiffly crushe, and by that
means make and apertione at one crushinge in the Apostematione, through the
poyncte of the Lancette, which is one the plate fastenede, into the concavitye
whereof the matter as then will issue out: I my self have oftentimes vsede
this same manner of apertione, which although it seem to be but a simple
shift, it is notvvithstandinge an hallucionatione which oftentimes is very
necessary.



























































The use of this foresayed Instrument is this: to wit, that in those fistles
in the which are two apertios, as in those in the which is made a counter
apertione: and where it is necessary, requisite, to make of these, two, holes
or orifices one apertion it is first of all reqvitede, that we sound the
depth of the fistle, with this sounding iron which is here defigurede unto
you: which at the one end hath a little buttone, at the other end is pearcede,
eyede like a Needle, where with we make our Seton: which being thrust
from the one apertione to the

Diverse Instruments to open Apostemationes



Declaration of the Charcters, which are contaynede in the La oris,
Matricis, other more Instruments for the Mouth.














































There are some which can not very well suffice themselves with this plate,
by reason that the gould smithe can not so conveniently make the same, that it
aequallye of all sides do touche the Palate of the Mouth, so that in stead
thereof they use, a tent made of linte, or of Sponge, to the which intent
there are divers which are providede of them, because that if the one fell
out, they incontinently have an other in a readiness, preparatione to put
therein, because othervvyse they shoude speak through the Nose.















These artificialle teethe are sometimes made of Ivorye but because Ivorye by
reason of the spittle vvaxeth immediately yellovve, and fulvide, they are more
fitter to make of any other bone, the same being very harde, and thick and
dence, or solyde, as is the bone of a fish which in French we call Rovart.
Farthermore any body by att may make teeth of white vvaxe which being
meltede, liquefactede with as much gum Elemni, or a little Masticke, white
Coralle, and preparede pearls there of a paste being made, of the which we
may form as many teethe as we please. This paste is also very commodiouse
to replenish thervvith a hollovve tooth, because there might no viands
remain therein, through which the teethe do more corrupt, and more
intolerable payne is heer bye suscitatede.

(...)

The Speculum of the Mouthte, and Womb



Declaration of the Characters which are contaynede in the figures of the
Hoockes, and Knives which are necessary, and conveniente, to draw forth a
dead Child out of the Mothers belly: also of the Pessaryes, Plates and of
other things necessary to the Ligatione of the fistles of the fundament.








I have divers and sundry times bin sent for, to divers Woeme, which
vveare in difficult labour Childebearth, notvvithstandinge I never vsede any
hoockes, or other ferrealle Instruments thereto, for which occasion I also
council all Chyrurgianes, to use none of them, then in extreme necessity,
for if so be there happen any other accident there unto, as excoriatione, or
any effluxione of blood, we must then attribute the same unto the
Instrument, which the Chyrurgiane vsede thereunto: Nevertheless I have here
causede them to be set down unto you, to use them in the extreameste
necessity: and here is to be notede, that the Insculptor, or Ingravere to
adorn, imbellishe his labours, hath participatede some propre, and perpolite
fashone to the handle, which indeed is thereon invtile, and needeles, because
it is better, that they be plain, smooth because they fast themselves on
nothing.

The first which I have seen use such manner of Pessaryes, was the right
vvorshipfulle Mr. Rousset, on of the Kings Chyrurgians, and of the Lady of
Nemours, which hath learnedlye, and excellently written, of divers sorts of
the Woomens parturio, where this form of Pissaryes are defigurede, and the
manner also of his use.







This plate, or this invention hath oftentimes bin vsede, of Mr. Girart Raber
Chyrurgia at Paris, which was a very inventive, perquiringe man, in
searchinge out of Chyrurgicalle Instruments, and also the most experteste
practitionere of his time.

Diverse Instruments to draw and cut of Teeth

Haeckes to drane forth a Child



The Declaration of the Characters which are contaynede in the table of the
actualle Cauteryes.
















































































It is impossible for the Chyrurgiane to present in this place all the
figures, portraytures of the Cauteryes, wherewith he must cotente himself:
for he must sometimes cause them to be forgede according to the reqviringe of
the Operatione, the part, where on he intends to use the same, wherefore I
in this place have done my endeavour to place collocate together such Cauteryes
as are most necessary, novvadayes in use, wherein I only have cosiderede,
observede their magnitude, crassitud: And as cocerninge their branches, the
handles of the same, have only bin observed in two, which are notede with
A, h, the Exsculptor having ingravene the same, somewhat shorter, smaller then
he ought: I most comonlye use such Cauteryes which be reasonable short,
because those which are of to great a longitude, bigness, do affrighte the
Patient, as allso those which are too lunge can not so easily be conductede,
rulede, because they do most commonly vacillate, turn this way, that way in
the hand.

(...)

The figures of Diverse Actualle Cauteryes.



The Declaratione of the Characters which are contaynede in the Table of those
Instruments which are propre, and conveniente, to the executione of all
manualle operations which in the Eyes may be by any means vsede.
Although that I in this Chyrurgerye have not discribede the manualle
operatio, which may be vsede in the infirmities of the Eyes: Yet
notvvithstandinge I would not, nether indeed could omit in this
storehouse and treasurye of all Chyrurgicalle Instruments, the portrayctures,
and figures of those Instruments, which are necessary, commodiouse unto such
an effect of deliberate, set purpose omittinge the operations of the Eyes,
because at large I have discribede, amplely set forth, everye severalle
operation, in my Treatize of the Infirmities, and diseases of the Eyes, which
I committede to the Press in the year M.D. Lxxxv. to the which I commit
the gentle Reader, there to behold the practice, severalle use of all
operations of the Eyes.

































































We must here note, that this pipe, or canulle, must have a little ringle,
where by we might hold fast the same, for if so be with a buttone we
houlde it, we might then chance to burn our fingers, because the cauterye
internallye passing through the same, it should not so completlye be
effectede, as it ought to be.

Canalle which is open and shut



The summary of the principalleste, and most especialle poynctes, which are
cotaynede, and spoken of in the operationes of Chyrurgerye.
The Preface of the Chyrurgerye. Containing four Chapters. Definitione, and
originalle of Chyrurgerye, with the operatione of the same. Chapter. 1. The
prognosticatio, judgement of vvoundes. Ch. 2 Signs to know vvhate parts of
the body are hurt. Chap 3 Adverticemente to give assurede report before the
Iusticiaryes. Chap 4
The first Treatise, of the Operatios of Chirurgerye, wherein is discoursede,
the means to draw out all strange, and alienate things out of the body.
Containing five Chapters. The vitilitye, necessity of drawing out all
strange, and alienate things. Chap 1 How that the Chyrurgian with all
dexteritye to draw forth a bullet, must considere on the differeces thereof,
and on the nature of the part, wherein the bullete is contaynede. Chap 2 The
bullets, all other alienate things, must in the first dressinge be dravven
out of the body, if it be possible, and the means which we must therein
use. Chap 3 How we ought to draw out the bulletes if they stick fast in
the bone. Chap 4 The Chyrurgiane ought not to be to curious in extractinge the
bulletes. Chap 5
The second Treatise of the operatione of Chyrurgerye, wherein is discoursede
the means, to Trepane the pan, or Craniu of the Head. Contaynige six
Chapiters. The signs by which we may discern the fractures of the sculle.
Chap 1 The Coutrefissure of the bones of the Head. Chap 2 What fractures we
ought to trepane. Chap 3 How loge we ought to defer the trepaninge. Ch. 4
What quatity of bones we ought to take away. Ch. 5 The methode, and manner of
trepaninge. Chap 6
The third Treatise, of the Operations of Chyrurgerye, of the sutures, and
sovvinge of vvoudes. Containing six Chapiters. What suture, or stitching is,
the vtilitye there of. Ch. 1 What we ought in stitchinge of a vvounde, to
considere. Chap 2 What is necessary unto suture, the manner of doing of the
same. Chap 3 The Species, differences of suture, how long we must suffer them
to continue without resectione thereof. Chap 4 The means to thrust in again
the guts, the Epiploon hanging out of the belly. Chap 5 The Gastroraphia,
or suture of the inferior part of the Belly. Chap 6
The fourth Treatise of the Operations of Chyrurgerye, wherein is discoursede
of the apertione of Apostemations. Containing five Chapters. Of the generalle
apertio of all Apostemations. Chap 1 Of the vlcerations, Ateromata, Steatomata,
and Melicerides. Chap 2 The means howe to cauterize, inscide the Empiemata.
Chap 3 The manner of doing the Paracentese, to draw out the water of the
Dropsye. Chap 4 The means to cure the Hernia aquosa. Chap 5
The fifte Treatise of the Operations of Chyrurgerye, of the disseases of the
Nose, and Mouth containing seven Chapters. Of the Polypus, Chap 1 The
Haremouthe, or clovene Lips. Chap 2 The tumefaction, superfluous flesh of
the gumes, called Paroulis, or Epoulis. Chap 3 The retractio of the tongue,
called of the Greec Kes Anchyloglosson, of the tumefactio Batrachos. Ch. 4.
Means to tie the palate of the mouth, and to cut of the same. Chap 5 The
tumor of the Almondes, and the Apostemations thereof. Chap 6 How to draw,
break, cut of teethe. Chap 7
The sixte Treatise of the operations of Chyrurgerye, containing nine
Chapters. What we ought to considere, before phlebotomye. Chap 1 How we
shall convenientlye open a vain. Chap 2 What we ought to considere the vain
being opened: and the blood issuinge out. Chap 3 The number of the veins, and
Arteryes, which are commonly phlebotomizede. Chap 4 Of the apertio of the
Arteryes, or Arteryonomye. Ch. 5 The Aneurisma, the manner how to ligate, and
inscide the same. Chap 6 The Cirsotomye, which is the manner how to inscide
the Varices. Chap 7 Horseleeches, their use, and manner of applicatione. Chap
8 Of boxes, and applicatione thereof. Chap 9
The seventh Treatise of the Operations of Chyrurgerye, containing six
Chapiters. Of Caries, and corruption of the bones. Chap 1 The fistle of the

Deo sit Laus.
THE FRENCH CHIRVRGERYE OVT OF ALL the ancient professors of Physic, and

figures of instruments, very necessary to the operatione or vvorcke of
Chyrurgerye.
Through Iaques Guillemeau of Orleans, public Chyrurgiane to the king, and
sworene in his Chastelet at Paris.
The praeface. To the Chyrurgerye, containing four Chapters.
The discriptione, and originalle of Chyrurgerie, and what the operatione of

The discriptione, and originalle of Chyrurgerye, and what the operatione of
Chyrurgerye is. Cap. 1.
Having with my self resolved, The invetion of sciences.
and purposed to describe the operation of Chyrurgerye, first of all it seemed
expediet unto me to observe these three necessary things. Namelye and
especially, what Chyrurgerye is. Secondly, how, and from whence, she hath
participatede and taken her originalle, and beginning. Thirdly what the
operatione of Chyrurgerye is. Concerning therefore her beginning originalle,
unto all me is it apparent and we rightvvell know, that the invention, and
finding out of all arts, and scieces vvhatsoever, hath bin by all men in such
great estee, and admiratione (which Hippocrates in his boocke of aunciete
Physic vvitnesseth) that the inventors or authors of the same, have bin
extolled even unto the clouds, by the which is vvitnessed, and testified,
this invention te have bin so perpolite, that it was adiudgede to be a thing
impossible to be discovered and brought into light, by any other, the through
some God, or at the least, by such a person which hath bin infused, with
divine inspiratione: In so much therefore, Chyrurgerye is auncieter then
Physic.
if this may be testyfyede of any arts with aeqvitye and right, it may
truly and boldly be said of this art of Chyrurgerye, the inventors, and
authors of the which, have bin canonisede, esteemede and extellod as Gods, as
for example there hath bin Apollo, Aesculapius, and Hippocrates, vvhoe have had
divine honour and reverence of all me. The two first of these authors, have
allonelye leerned, that part of Physic, which through externalle
medicametes, and by inscisions cured the infirmities, and diseasses of men's
bodies. By the which playnlye is to be understood and judged, that Physic
at those times was not as then inveted created, or fovvnde out, when that
Chyrurgerye, was sufficietlye practised and known in all places: the which
Homer testifies and vvitnesseth unto us in the second book of his Iliades,
where he affirms that the sons of Aesculapius, Podalyrus, and Machaon, in
the besieginge of Troye have with there hands handelede and cured many and
diverse vvouded persos without molestinge or troubling of themselves with
any internalle diseasses or sicknesses, as with agues, or with the plague,
the which notvvithstandinge praedominatede and vexede sore the whole host or
camp of the Greeckes: and consequetlye Physic and the use thereof being
known, and in practise, Chirurgerye was very confusedlye hadeled amongst
Physic. But for as much that it is very difficult a harde thing to excel
in divers, and sundry arts, and because it is impossible, the vvorcke of
Physic, Chyrurgerye, and Apothecary for one man to do, therefore the art of
Chyrurgerye is secluded and separatede fro the other two, having alsoe her
own professors and practitioners. The which because that it may berightlye
understood, it is to be noted and observed, that in the old and ancient
times, by Chyrurgerye, the third part Therapeuticae was not understood,
even as vvee in these times take it, as consequently shall be showed: Divers
misconstrvinges of this vvorde Chyrurgerie.
But they understood by this vvorde Chyrurgerye nothing else, then that only,
which through the operatione of the hades is finishede and brought to pass
wherefore it is yet manifestly written by the aunciet professors, that by
Chyrurgians was nothing else understood then certain servantes, which in
those times vveare callede Chyrurgians, not because they had the perfect
science, or knowledge of Chyrurgerye, but because they earned there sustenance
and livinge through the vvorckinge of there hands. Now to conceave aright
what Chyrurgerye is, it is expediet and necessary, that we ascede somewhat
higher, and industriouslye seerch try what Physic is saying manifestly
that Chyrurgerye is thereof separated and secluded.

Descriptione of Physic.
Physic therefore is nothing else (as Hippocrates in his book De art
vvitnessethe) then a knowledge, or science, which teaches, how to cure any
sicknesses to frustraete, and annihilaete there impregnable forces, because of
themselves they are incurable.

Parts of Physic.
This Physic again hath sundry parts, of the which the first is
Physiologia, which handelethe and treatethe, of the structure and situatione
of mans body, wherein is to be regarded and noted on the seven vnnaturalle
things. The second is Hygiena, that is a Nource, and fosterer, or praeserver,
of our health, which instructs, how we shovvlde continually preserve our
present health, keep the sovvnde body liber, and free from all diseasses,
sicknesses: and this consistethe in six vnnaturalle things. The third is
Pathologia, which treatethe of the cause, and occasion of the sicknesses of
the accidents unto the same, the which three things are against nature. The
fourth is called, Simiotica, and treats of the demostratinge signs or tokes,
not only of that which is all ready passed, but alsoe of that which is as
yet to come, in respect as well of sickness, as of health. The fifth is
Therapeutica, that is the curing or sanable part of Physic, which
instructs, learns, howe to repelle and cure sicknesses, and diseases, The
parts of Therapeutica
restore again to former health: This praenominated Therapeutica is redivided
and again dealed in three parts, the first whereof is called, Dietetica,
which teaches, how to observe a sober good diet: The second part,
Pharmacia, which instructed us, how to compovvnde our medicaments. And the
third part is callede Chirurgia, which cures or helpethe the affected
patients through manualle or handlye operation for Chirurgia hath his
derivatione of a Greecke vvorde, and is compovvnded of Cheir which signifyeth
a hade, and Ergo which betokens operatione: nether may we by this vvorde
Chirurgia not on lye understand, all operations which are only effected by
the hands, but most of all and especially, all that is done, or may be done
one mans body toward the curing of all external diseases.

And as cocerninge this vvorde operatione, What this vvorde operatione
signifiethe.
it is nothing else, then an artificialle and normaticke applicatione, which
is brought to pass, and wrought by the hands, one mans body, wherewith the
decayed health is reiterated, and again restored. And if we vveare desirous
to know what the office, duty of a true chyrurgiane is, or what his
operations are, how and in what manner he must work, effect them, and
what conditions are expected, at a chyrurgians hades, I have described them
fully, at large in my general table, and discriptione of Chirurgerye,
wherefore I will make in this place no more metione of them.

Of the prognostication or judgment of wounds Chap. 2.
IT is certain that the praedictione, or judgement of vvoudes, The use of
judgment.
yea alsoe the generalle knowledge of all disseases, is a thing most
necessary and expedient for a good Chyrurgiane: therefore Hippocrates alsoe
esteemed it very conveniente, profitable, that a Chyrurgiane must excelle in
the fore knowledge of things, and be apt ready to give judgment and his
opinione of all disseases, because by that he may attain, and at chiue unto
great credit, and fidelity by all men, and having on this manner set forth,
and published his knowledge shall be irrepraehendable, and of all men admired,
so much the safer,, with more providence finish his vvorcke, the boulder
illustraete the operatione of his art, and give judgment there of without
any scoff, or check, and be able to give a true report, when that by the
aucthoritye of some learnede chyrurgiane, or by the aucthorytye of some
Potentate, he shall be therein imployede, ordained to report his opinione, ether
of the wounded persons life, ordeath, haultnes or criplenes. Even as alsoe the
foresayed Hippocrates says in his Porrhet that the Chirurgiane must end
voure him self to have a wise grave gesture, Hippocratesin his Porrhet.
because that if it so chance as he adjudged it should, he might reap
honour, goodvvil, not only of the patient, but alsoo of all the circumstants,
and behoulders: Contrarilye if that othervvyse it happened or chanced then he
said, and judged it should, and that his judgement fail him, he shall not
only of all me be hated but alsoe be esteemed as an ignorant, sottish
fellovve. Beyond all this Galenus says, and testifies unto us, that by our
upright, and sincere judgment, by the friends Kindrede of the patiete,
although he come to dye, we shall be liberated, freed from all bad reports
sclaunders, because that through the death which consequently followed,
nothing shall be alleagede against us.

What a Chyrurgiane ought to know in giving judgement
And for the givinge of such a perfect, and complete judgment, it is necessary
for the chyrurgiane not to be ignorant, which parts of the body being
wounded, easily, or difficultlye may be cured, which of the are praesent
death, and in fine, the tokens how to know what parts are wounded or
hurt, because that out of there natures, quallityes, dispositios, we may
certaynlye hope, or mistrust of their health, and safety.

Such judgement therefore ought to be taken, out of the very being, and
substance, or essence of the vvouded part, alsoe of the use, the action, and
situatione of the same, there must alsoe be noted, the proportione, and figure
of the same vvounde, and the accidents or other chances, which are incident
unto her, considering alsoe the complexione and temperature of the body, the
sex, the age, on his handelinge, and trafiqve, manner of livinge, the contrye,
the constitutione of the time then praesent, and one the time and season of the
year.

All great vyoundes are dangerous,
To conclude, all men that have received a great vvoude, are ether in danger
of death or in danger of being mutilate. We eesteme and account the vvounde
to be large, for three occasions: A great vvounde is taken three manner of
ways.
The first whereof is, because that through her latitude, or vvidnesse,
circumiacente places, she hath a threefoulde survayinge and measuringe to
wit, in length, bredthe, and debthe, as some there are which be deep
broadlye carvede: or right overthvvarte or cross vvyse vvholye percige
through the principalle muscles of any part, by the which alsoe sometimes
the bones are hevved cut quite through, broken, wherefore some times they
must be stitchte, bound and ligated together: or because that the veins, the
arteryes, or the sinneus themselves, come to be squised and plettered. The
second, cocerninge the vvorthynes of the wounded part, for although the
vvounde be but little, in her meatinge yet notvvithstandinge we esteem her to
be great, because that the strength and action, of the wounded part or
member, is very necessary for the whole body 'and for the whole life of
man, which part having left his natural use and action, it consequently
follows that the personne, must immediately decease dye, as we may by
experience see that daily chances in the vvoundes of the Brains, of the
Heart, and in the vvoundes of the Liver.

Thirdelye, because that some vvoundes are of a vvorser nature, dispositione,
as being venoumede, rebellious, and entermingled with some bad and dolorous
accidents, which sometimes far surpass excel the vvounde it self, as it
is evidentlye planlye to be seen in the vvoundes of the Ioynctes, which
immediately, may fall into a very exceeding bad estate, because that those
parts and places are circumligatede or clothed with very small store of
flesh, being only decked co verede with the Tendones, with sinnues, with
Ligaments, that very tender, and sensible pellicle Perioflium, by the which
these parts or members are in more danger of falling into a convulsione of
sinnues, into Phrensye, payne, and inquietude, as much as a far more greater
vvounde in any other part of the body.

Those vvoundes are accounted incurable which do happen in the Brains,
Wounds which are esteemed incurable.
or in the ventricle of the same, in the Heart, entrace of the stomach, in the
Vena Cava, the entrace of the Liver, the back bone, quite through the Lunges,
in the small guts, or entralles, the stomach, the Kidneys, or in any great
veins, or arteryes about the throat. Wounds that be difficult to cure,
But these vvoudes which will difficultlye be curede are they by the which
the Lunges, the Liver, the mebrane of the Brains the Milte, the Matrix, or
vvombe, the blather, any of the great guts, or the Middelriefe, are vvouded
with very small vvounde. Alsoe are those vvoudes dangerous, whereas the
great veins, Wounds that are dangerous.
Arteryes, under the Emunctoria or arm pits, in the Hockes or bedinge of the
knees are wounded: and indeed all vvoundes are suspicious, in all places
vvheraboute any great Veins, or Arteryes are situated placed, because that
through their effusione of bloede, the wounded person is bereft af all his
ability forces: the vvoundes of the Secrete parts or privityes, and the
vvoundes of the Testicles are alsoe by this reason very perilous dangerous,
as alsoe are these which happen and chance to light between the fingers.

The figure or form of the vvoundes
There is alsoe great heed to be taken one the manner form of the vvoude: for
those vvoudes which chance, or come by crushinge, or pletteringe, are far
vvorse, then those, which are only hevvede, so that it is much better to be
vvouded with a sharpe edgede or cuttinge sword, the with any other blunt
vveapone which is not sharpe.

The round or circle vvoundes are vvorste difficultest to be curede, but the
certayneste easyeste to be cured, are those which pass by the length of the
fibres right like a linye or line.

The vvoundes of the Hipp, which are received in the membranous muscle, What
the vvoundes of the hipp are,
are very exceeding dangerous, especially if it be a thrust or stab,or
else when the apertione of the same is very small, hath no issue, but it is
so much the vvorse if it so chance there be any fracture or separatione of
bones, it be where it will in any place of the hipp: so far forth as if
the vvounde be in any internalle part of the Hipp, about the great veins,
then the vvounde is passing exceeding dangerous, for there ensuethe out of
hade some great inflammatione there unto, consequetlye thereafter a Gangraena,
or mortificatione: he that happens to receave a vvounde in the foremoste
part of his arm, is alsoe subject unto the same danger of inflammatione,
and mortificatione, and causes great payne trouble, because that those
vvoundes most commonly are present death.

What we ought to judge out of the accidents of vvoudes.
Now as much as belongs unto the judgement opinione, which is to be
considered noted out of the accidents of all vvoudes, Hippocrates teaceth us:
in so much as in the great bad vvoundes, as are the vvoundes of the Sinues,
of the Tedones, of the Ioynctes, of the bones, little or no svvellinge or
inflammatione at all apperareth, illustratethe it self it is a very evell and
bad sign, for vvhye, it signifyethe unto us that those humours which
considering the payne should have assembled, packed themselves that vvaye have
circumcinglede themselves rovvnde about the vvouded part, have retracted
themselves into some principalle especial part of the body.

Accidetes of a de parted svvellinge.
Those vvoundes in the which there appearethe any svvellinge, or tumefactione
very selden or never fall into any Phrensye, or into covulsione of Sinnues,
because that the venomous humours, which might, ascend draw toward the
Brains or into the sinuishe parts of the body, have congregate and
gatherede themselves together about the vvounde: but if that one the sudden
the svvellinge without any evidente reason, as in example, in the applying
of any remedies, through purgations, or through phlebotomisinge or blood
lettinge, came to depart with draw it self into some other place and that
in such, as are wounded behind in there back bone, that person is troubled
with convulsione of synnues, through the with dravvinge of the matter, into
the sinnuishe parts of the backebone:and the vvounde being in the foremost
parts of the body, and the matter of the svvellinge, ascedinge vpvvardes,
through the great veins, toward the Brains, the patient strayghtvvay
fallethe into a madness Phrenesye: or if so be that the matter draw
toward the breast, then there ensue great intolerable stitches, or
Apostemations, because that those bad humours can not by any means possible
be consumed or vvastede, but descend sink down into the cocavitye of the
breast. And if that the svvellinge now departing be of a very high red
colloured, that thervvas store of blood therein, which is descended sunk
down toward the guts, then it necessarylye cosequentlye fellovveth that
the patient falls into the Blodye flyxe, or Dysenterye.

As far forth therefore as there procedethe any convulsione of synnues out
of a vvounde, The convulsione in vvoundes is very dangerous.
especially from any great inflammatione, that is a sign of death, for
therbye we may playnlye, note, mark that the synnuishe parts of the body,
must needs be hurt, and that the Brains are in some, or other passione.

If that into any vvoudes, which have fiercelye bled any Spasmus happen,
that is a bad sign.

All thrusts of the sinnues, and Tendones, Thrusts in the synnues are very
daugerous.
are very dangerous, and especially, when as the skin and the flesh
chance to shut together, for therein engenderethe a sharpe corrodinge matter,
which resuscitatethe and causes great intolerable payne, because she can
not by any meannes get issue, out of the which procedethe follovvethe
convulsione of synnues, inflammatione, and a Gangraene, or mortificatione. The
Organicke parts, which are vvholye cut of can no more be restored again.
In so much as any especial and principalle, or Organicke part, or any
instrumentalle part, totallye vvholye is hevved of, and sequestered and
separatede from the body, it can not by any means be restored and cured,
because that the blode, and the vitalle Spirits are vvholye exhalated,
through the great veins, which are clean a sunder, and that by the which
the cure must be done, and the foregoinge health again restorede.

The temperature of the body, the time of the year, the age the handling
and traffic, and the manner of the patients livinge, the knowledge of the
cotrye are a great help aid for the prognosticatione of vvoundes: a child,
or a yongema, which as yet is in-his grovvth will better and with more ease
be healed or curede, then an aged personne: and a strong ma, a great deal
sooner then a feeble, and debile person. A lean and sclender person, vvilbe
curedevvith more facility, then a thick, and gross lived man. And a sound
body far sooner, then a sick and corrupt body. That man which labours,
easier, then a stillsittinge and idle person. A soberman, far sooner, then a
bancketter, and one that haunt the hoores. The most conveniente time of the
year, to cure heal a vvoude, is in the fore summere or Vernal time of the
year, or at the least in such a time, in the which it is nether to hot nor
to could, because that extreme heate, or extreme could,are the enymyes of
all vvoundes, and especially the changinge of heate, of could, wherefore
alsoe the fore winter or Autunus, is very unprofitable for the same. In some
regions and countries, Other cosideratios to be his in prognosticatinge.
the vvoundes are ether easier, or difficulter to becurede: for at Rome, the
vvoundes of the head are difficulter to be curede, then the vvoundes of the
Legs, the which notvvithstandinge at Avignon we find clean contarye.

As far as the wounded person keep and continue his perfect Sense, and
memory nether gettethe any agues, we may then assure our selves, that the
vvounde will quickly and easily be curede. We ought not thetfore to
discourage the patient, although, he have an ague by the great vvoude, as
lunge as the inflammatione is yet before hand, and as lunge as there
engenderethe any matter. But an ague is very dangerous, Signs to the
contrary.
when she issuethe from a small vvounde and especially, when she hath lunge
continuance, after the inflammatione, and supuratione, or when she incitateth
the patient unto Phrenesye. When the patient parbrakethe against his will,
green gall, or immediately when he hath received the blovve, or at the time
while the inflammatione is as yet during, that is alsoe a bad sign,
especially in these which are wounded in a synnue, or in annye sinnuishe
place: But the parbrakinge, or vomitinge, which comes by the frevvil of the
patient. is nothing suspicious, especially in those, which are used to
vomitinge, if that a man praesentlye after meals, or after that the
inflammatione is come, or the vvounde being in the head, Consideration for the
vvonds of the head.
do not come to vomit. The Chyrurgiane must use great foresight, in givinge
judgement of the vvoundes of the Head, for the ancient Chyrurgians, have
always esteemed the doubtfulle suspect, because of other bad accidents
until the Fifteenth day, and the modern and, ionge professors, until the
hundred the day.

The Iurists, or lavvyers, have constituted and ordained fifteen days, for
the judgment of the vvoundes of the Head, to know of a certayntye, whether
the patient by occasion of that vvoude came to dye or not or by reason of his
own fault, or by any other occasion. Because therefore, that the younger
Chyrurgiane, shall not be over rash, in givinge of his judgment, least he
come te glyde and fall into any bad reports, or in any repraehensione. As
much as appartayneth unto me, I have known some, which have continued in a
very good estate unto the thirteenth, fourteenth, or until the fifteenth day,
and then have fallen into agues, and many other accidents, by the which they
in the end chased to dye. VVee must alsoe note, that all naughty and bad,
accidents most of all publish themselves, at the full Moon, more then at
other times, because as then all manner of moisture grovveth and increases
more, then in the decreasinge of the Moon, alsoe more in the summer, then in
the Winter.

The signs and tokens of the deadly fractures of the sculle, are agues in the
Winter, The signs of the deadly fractures of the sculle.
before the fourteenthe day, and in the summer before the seaventhe day: Item,
a naughty and bad colloure of the vvounde: little matter of the same:
mortificatione of that which is inflamed: slimy or viscouse consistence of
that which is corrupted: dries ariditye in the skin of the head, as it is
in a piece of pouldrede flesh, with a brouvvne, leadishe, and black
colloure, which signifyeth the beginning of the corruptinge and putrifyinge
of the sculle, which the vvaxeth ravve, as we may see, when it is rotten,
and grovvne black: whereas before it was even and smooth. Finallye we see
therein, a pale, and yellovvishe colloure, to vvitt when the foresayede bone is
vvholye corrupted rotten, through the purulent matter, which was sunk to
the grovvnde of the vvounde, and was there gathered together.

The patient begins to rage, he hath little pimples one his tongue, he
gets alsoe convulsione of synnues, one the contrary side of the vvounde:
some fall inte an Apoplexia vvheron follevvethe death. The Practitioners of our
times, have observed in all vvoundes, whether allreadye therein vveare a
Paralisis, or else thereafter happened thereunto: and onethe contrary side a
Convulsione, or sometimes also in the wounded part a Convulsione, and in the
other side a Paralisis, sometimes also in both the parts, ether a Covulsione,
or Paralisis, and sometimes one each side ether a Convulsione, or a Paralisis,
without the contrary side being in any sort thervvith infected. The good
signs of healinge, in the fractures of the Head, after that it is trepanede,
or else after that the broke parcels of bones, are taken out of the Head, are
these namelye when that the Membrane of the Brains hath her naturalle
colloure, and her naturalle motione stirring: when the engendringe and
grovvinge Flesh is red. When that the patient may easily turn about his
Neck, and alsoe easily vvagge his lower chavve bone. But in so much as the
Membrana hath no stirring, Bad signs.
and is black, leadishcolourede, lividouse or with any other bad colour
tainted defylede: Then the patient ragethe, vomits exceedinglye, falls into
a Paralysis, or in Spasmo, if that the Flesh of the vvounde be leadishe
colourede the neck the chavvebone both of the stad stiff all these are very
bad signs. And when the vvoode, is at a good estate, the begins the flesh
of the Membrane, or of the sculle to grow, and to vvax double, and fills
all the void places with flesh, that have bin open between the bones,
yea sometimes covers even as it vveare with pomegranate Kernells the scull it
self.

The tokens howe to know which parts of the body are wounded Cap, 3.
TTe vvoundes most commonly are knovven by there first aspect, alsoe sometimes
the place of the vvoude certifyeth unto us, what parts are internallye
wounded: But saying that it often times chauncethe, that these vvoundes',
which to our estimatione be not profound or deep, penetrate unto the
internal parts, we will here therefore recite the signs, by the which we
may know, what internal parts, within the body are vvouded, because there
by we may know, whether the patiete may be cured of them or not.

Signs vven the brains are hurt.
If so be that the Brains or else there Membrane is vvouded, the there issues
blood out of there Noses, with some alsoe out at there Ears commonly there
follows a vomitinge of choler: some lye almost out of the memory beside
themselves that although you call cry unto them, they give no answer:
others see in there faces as if they vveare affrighted feared: some turn and
wind there eyes, this way and that vvaye, as if they vveare touched stricken
by Gods hade, the third or the fifth day most commolye they fall into madness
Phrenesie: other get the Spasmu, before they dye: some there are which
pluck the medicametes from there Heads, so that the vvounde comes to lye
bare, vvaxeth could.

When as the back bone is hurt, Signs when the back bone is hurt.
or vvouded, the beginnethe the patient to be feeble lame in his sins, or else
he gets covulsione of synues, which is called Spasmus: leases the sense of
feeling, sometimes the patient can nether retain his urine, Sperma, or Stole,
but of it self departs from him.

If the Heart be vvouded, Signs when the heart is vvouded.
there issues out of the vvounde, great store of black, dence, or thick
blood, especially when the right side of the Heart is hurt: But when the
left side of the Heart is hurt, then issuethe out of the vvounde fine subtle
