red blood the pulse of the patient is very debile feeble, variable, is very
pale coloured in his face: The could svveate in all his body bursts out,
and hath a very vnsaverye smelle, eve as we may note in other sicknesses of
lunge cotinuance. His hands and feet begin to be could, out of the which
present death ensues.

When the Lunges are hurt, the breaths the patient with great difficvltye,
The wounds of the Lungs.
divers times reiterateth, dravveth anevve his breath as if he there by sought
indevoured to do him self some solace coforte: he voydeth of ten times through
his mouth frothy blood, through the vvoude fair, red, vitalle blood,
with peepinge hissinge breathe: he endeavours for the most part to lye one
his vvouded side: others often times erecte them in there bed with out any
occasion: Some lying one the wounded side, can speeke, but turning
themselves one there sovvnde side are quite deprived of there speech: sometimis
they are bloesinge in there faces, sometime clean pale, and at the last issues
out of the mouth of the vvounde great quantitye of filthy matter,

The signs of a wounded Liver are these; The Liver being hurt.
nalye that out of there right side they avoid a great quatitye of blood:
Both sides of the body, are as it vveare plucked toward the back bone: The
patient is very pale in his face as if he vveare half dead: His eyes are
fallen invvardes, and hath intolerable payne, being ignorant through his
impatientie what he shall do: he is't best at ease when he lye the one his
belly: he hath a very pricking stinging payne, which dravveth toward his
breast, and also toevvarde the sides of the same. Heavinge and contractinge his
shoulders together must he breathe, and sometimes through, parbrakinge he
avoydethe choler. Hath a very violent, fervent pulse, he is easily incesed
to ire, sorrow: sometimes he hath an ash coloured face, his urine alsoe
sotymes very bloody: his stoels like matter, and dye most comonlye, with the
Hickcoughe.

When the kidnyes are hurt, then descedeth as it vveare by degrees comethe the
payne into the flanckes, Of the wounds of the kidneys.
and so forvvarde unto the testicles: the patient can very difficultlye be
released of his urine, he pisseth blood, or at the least his water is
bloody. Sometimes his urine is quite retaynede, by the which occasion, the
patiete being extreamlye svvollene dies.

The milte being hurt.
If the Milte be hurt or wounded, then the blood issues out of the left side
of the paciete, is blak of colour. The same side, alsoe the stomach begin
to be indurated, harde: the patient vvaxeth thirsty the payne retracteth it
self towards the breast, as in the vvoundes of the Liver.

The vvobe being hurt.
Now the VVombe being hurt the vvoma has great payne in her flanckes, in
her Haunches, in her hippes: she avoydeth blood partly through the vvounde,
and partly through her privityes, after the which sometimes follows a
parbrakinge of Cholera Others ca not speak: some lye out of memory others
which do not rage, say that they are troubled with great payne in there
sinnues, in there Eyes: when they draw toward death, they are troubled
with the same accidetes, which we have recited of the Heart.

The Middle reefe,
When the middle reefe or Diaphragma is vvouded, the are the sides of the
patient drawn, shruncke vpvvardes: they have exceeding great violent payne,
internallye in the back bone: they have very retardate breathe, and there
issues out of the vvounde frothy blood.

The entrance of the stomach,
The entrance of the stomach being hurt the patient beginnethe to have the
hick up, avoydeth choler: when as he eats or drinks, he casts it
strayghte way fro him again: he hath a small, feeble, obscure pulse: he
gets a little could svveate, with a cooling of all externalle parts.

The stomach the guts,
The stomach, that gut Ieiunum, have hoth of them one manner of token,
because that there meat drink issues out of the mouth of the vvounde
sometimes being half digested, and altered in Chylum: they feel a payne, as
if a man would rent tear there Heart out of there bodies: they get
hardness in there sides, sotimes alsoe parbraketh the patient Cholera, vpvvards
through the mouth, his spittle is bloody: betveen these two is no other
difference, then that the gut Ieiunu hath his place situatione somewhat lower
then the stomach.

The bladder beige hurt, we feel payne, Hurts of the bladder,
in the flanckes: that part of his belly a little above his privityes
extendethe stretchete it self: in stead of urine the patient pisseth blood,
or else the urine issues forth of the vvoude: the entrance of the stomach is
perturbed out of ordre, wherefore the wounded vomit Cholera, or at the least
are afflicted with the hick cough: they begin to vvax could in hands
feet, and consequently ensues death.

Instructione, how to give a certain report of all wounds whatsoever before
the magistrates. Cap. 4.
ALl such which before any magistrates, Codsideratione to be had before the
giving of report,
of any vvouded or sick personne, will are disposed to give report
instructione, shallby no means intrude themselfves, before that of the
magistraete they shall be requested thereto, sent for, saying that most commonly
proferede witness is repraehedable: he that takes such a thing in hand
ought first of all to visit and see the patient, because he might ripelye and
dilligetlye consider of al things, namelye especially one the greatness of the
dissease, one the situatione, place of the same, not only, as the may give
good instru^one, but alsoe, one all occasios, on the praedictione fore saying
of the vvoundes, without having vnadvisedlye therein hasted him self: for it
is a harde and difficult matter, to give a perfect coplet judgment of the
end of all vvoudes, or other sick persons, because of the accidents which
might chance thereunto, for those vvoudes, which we do not esteem of
sotimes are occasion of death, cotrarylye those of the which we expected
nothing but death, are yet notvvithstandinge cured. We know, that some there
are cured healed, notvvithstandinge although they vveare wounded in the
Membrane or pellicle of the Brains yea some which vveare hevvede in the
substance of there Brains: as I alsoe remember some to have bin cured, which
vveare hurt in the luges, in the Middlereefe, in the Liver, in the small
guts or etralles, the Bladder, the Kidnyes, or in the Matrix or vvombe.
Although we according to the judgement reason of the ancient professors,
esteem such vvoundes to be deadly incurable. Contrarylye we se some me men
dye of small and vndiepe vvoundes: we must therefore in such things make a
distinction among the wounded for some there are which are of so good a
temperature, and state of body, that of a great and large vvounde which in
any other mans body wear praesent dea the, they are cured: contralye there
are others which being wounded in any externalle parts, which are
nothing nocete or daugerous to the life of the patient, without any
penetratione, of the which, notvvithstandinge they chance to dye, ether of
the bad costellatione, or constitutione of the time, or by the refluxione of
any bad humours unto the wounded part.

Some me dye of a small wound.
Although therefore, that some vvoundes are curable, and alsoe of a good
constitutio dispositione, without any bad or cotrarye sign therein to be
marked, but for all that we may not give any absolute judgment or report of
the same, but must only say that the vvounde is curable, so far forth as
there be nothing praetermittede, which consists and belongs as well in
the patient as unto the Chyrurgiane which has him inhandes, it belongs
alsoe unto the circustantes, and unto other external things.

The judgment must be provident lye given.
We must thefore suspede our judgement, and keep it in secrete for a season,
without givinge any absolute report the first day, because that the good or
bad signs ca not so soon reveal and disclose themselves: we must alsoe
consider that all vvoundes must have there originalle, there increasinge, and
there estate, and during this time, seek by all means possible to repress
the vntemperatenes, which is fallen into the wounded part, through the
blovve, through the external air, which striveth with nature, against the
which nature strengthenethe and fortyfyeth her self, to expel and drive
away the foresayed vntemperatenesse, during the which time, we can not
certaynlye know, which of the shall obtain the victory, the only some
time thereafter, vvhenas the foresayed vntemperatenesse makes her self
known, through any signs which she reveals in the concurringhe humours
because nature, through certain signs, doth demonstrate that she hath
obtained victory, and is become the mistress of that vnteperatenesse. These
signs and tokes are especially known, by the matter before had: and because
that the purulence or matter, is not engendered the first day, it necessarilye
follows that on the first day, we give not any absolute report of a
vvounde, but must first consider, whether it is bent, because that nature one
the days of Crisis demonstrates her self, by the which we may judge of the
end of the sickness. The first, and certanest day of the Crisis is, the
seventhe, for the fourth, is not indeed the day of the Crisis, but well the
demonstratinge day of the seventh, which is the upright and trevve day of the
Crisis. After the seventh, follovvethe the eleventh, of the which the seventh
is the demonstratinge day, then the fourteenth day follows, after that the
tvventithe, and then the last is the fortithe: For if that before the fortith
day, we perceave no bad signs, it is then evidet and plain enough, that
the vvounde vvilbe easily cured.

It is best therefore that we do not report or give any judgment before the
seventh day be passed by, in which time, When that the report must be done.
the accidetes begin to reveal these (...) ^her on as the we must have a
specia^ (...) ^de and care, whether there be more good, then bad accidetes
before hades, or the contrary, we must consider one these signs, three manner
of ways: For they reveal themselves ether in the quality of the body, of
the wounded part, or in the livinge, and vitalle in the animaele, or
naturalle actions: Or in the excremetes, which are common, or in the whole
body, alsoe in the particular excrementes, which the vvoundes reject and
repelle from them. The signs which reveal themselves in the qvalitye of the
body, are considered, when we regard on the figure, and one her colour:
the animaele, and spirituale actions, consiste in the stirring, in feeling,
and in reason. The vital, or the livinge actions, consist in the pulse: and
the naturalle actions, cosiste, in the appetite, in the digestinge, and in the
expellinge and drivinge forth of the excrementes. The common excrements of the
whole body are the filthynes of the Nose, the tears out of the Eyes, spittle
out of the mouth, or the substance and filthy matter of the guts, the urine,
or that which we opvvards parbrake: The particular excrementes, are filth,
matter, ad the blood: in which excrementes, we must mark and note the
quantitye, the consistence, colour, smelle, and sometimes alsoe one the taste:
saying that if there be more good, that is so much the better: we must alsoe
note, that through the actions we may best judge of the forces. Having thus
therefore togethere considerede and noted all these things, the Chyrurgiane
shall be able to geve his sentence and report certaynlye, ether of life, or of
death, of lamnes or criplenes.

THE FIRST TREATISE OF THE OPERATIon of Chyrurgerye, wherein is discoursede and
handelede of the means and way how te extract and draw forth any
alienate thing out of the body containing five Chapters;
Of the profit, and necessity that comes to draw all alienate things out
of the body. Chap. 1. How the Chyrurgiane, convenientlye and fitlye
extractinge and dravvinge forth the bullet, ought to consider marcke their
diversityes, and in what parts or places they lodgeor are collocated. Chap.
2. We ought to extract and draw forth the pellet, or any other things,
if it by any means, be possible, at the first dressinge. And praemeditate,
one the means, how we should best and convenientest effect the same. Chap.
3. After what manner we shall draw forth those bulletes, which stick
fast in the bones. Chap. 4, That the Chyrurgiane ought not to be too curious,
in extractinge or dravvinge forth of the bullet. Chap. 4.
Of the necessity, and vtilitye, of the extraction or drawing forth of all
strange things. Cap. 1.
The excellentie of Chyrurgerye.
Amongst all operatios of Chirurgerye, the ancient Chyrurgians of old
times, have especially considered, one the hadlinge, where with all we most
convenientlye, and aptly, drevve forth out of mas body all manner of strange
alienate things, as are Bullets, Arrovves, and all other sorts of vveapons,
or all pieces of brokene bones, and many other such like things as being most
necessary, for this foresayed operatione.

It is a Chyrurgiane full of craft,
That out of the body can pluck shaft.
An excellent similitude.
And even as in the vvarrelike affairs, those Capitaynes are esteemed to be
most courageous and valiant, which one the sudden can best discern and
know there enemies. and allure and as it vveare draw them, forth to their
own furtherance, to battle: oras in chasinge, those hunters are esteemed
fittest and expertest, which suddaynelye can discover, or disclose, the
harbour of the persecuted dear, and knows how he may with al foresight
chase the same: Even so have the Physicians alsoe, had those Chyrurgians in
great estimation, which first of all, have found out that part of mans
body, in the which the bulletes, or Arrovves, or any other alienate things
have bin hidden and lodged, and which have had knowledge science fitlye
convenientlye to draw them therout: considering that through there
continuance in the same, above al things they are clean contrary unto us
unsufferable, as that which is dead, livinge: so it is a hinderance unto us,
and especialye to that part, wherein the foresayede weapon, stickethe, and
that as well of it self because that it vvholye, hinderethe the curing of the
vvounde, or else because it bringethe to gether many heavye accidents, the
which is cause of lamnesse or criplenesse in the wounded part: or else
causes the health which we suppose to have gotten not to be perfect, and
sure, but is subject unto a far vvorse return renueinge of the same. Yet
for all that I know some which are cured, Some me are curede containing the
bullet in the body.
in the which the bullets as yet tarryae in some parts of there body wit
hout being necessary to have searched for the same to curiously, or to pluck
it out although that we might easily have attained there unto, touched it,
because of the danger that thereof might ensue: as for example, a violent
bleedinge through laceratione of some vain, or arterye: any great violet
payne, through the stretchinge or dravvinge for the of some synnue, or
Membranes: An especially it is not necessary, curiously to search after such
things, or to draw them forth, which ca not rot, or corrupt, as that
which easily, or in time can come to rot. But yet it is allvvayes better,
that all which is strange and alienate unto mans body, and is contained in
the same, be dravvene therout, then to let it tarry therein, because that the
health (as we have al ready said) is far surer, concerning the danger,
that is then present, to cause the cured vvounde again to burst open, when as
the bullet, or any such like thing, comes to repraeset himself: or that we
should not be constrained to make an apertione any where, whether the fore
said bullet through his ponderousnes might chance to be descended.

Which are the strange things,
The Strange things of the which in this place we have spoken, are of two
manner of sorts, for they are, ether external, as Iro, vvoode, bullet, stone,
or vvolle: or they are any substance of our bodies, as any splinters or
parcels of bones, the cogealed blood, which we notvvithstandinge esteem it
strange, because they are separated from our bodies, and are no more
partakers of our life, or vital spirits, as they vveare before when they as
yet vveare all one with them, wherefore they are now become strange.

The intete of the Author,
My intent purpose is nothing else, then to describe unto the yonge
Chyrurgiane, the practice, of all bullets, and of al other strange things,
which might thervvith enter mingle themselves, or thervvith might be crushed
to pieces, how to draw the out of the body: Because in th^se our days
here in France there is no other shot used: saying that the knowledge of
the extractinge of the same, may easily lead us unto the knowledge, howe to
draw forth shaftes, Arrovves out of mans body, of the which the ancient
professors have very copiouslie written.

Howe that a Chyrurgiane aptly fitlye to draw a bullet out of the body, ought
to consider their difference, the nature of the part, in the which it is shot.
Chap. 2.
THe Chyrurgiane being requested and desired to draw forth the bullet,
Wherein consists the danger of dravvinge forth of bullets.
having pierced penetrated some part of the body with least trouble,
hinderance to the patient fitlye, finelye to accomplish the same, because that
sometimes they are drawn forth with insufferable payne, shall first of all
consider mark, that the danger consists, ether in that part, in the which
such bullets are shot, or in the species forms, and difference, of the bullets:
it is therefore first of all necessary, that he observe, and know, the
nature and qvallityes of those parts, for some parts will more gently and
softly be handled, then others: wherefore he must consider, on the substance,
qvantitye, figure and form, compositione, vnitinge, originalle, temperature,
and one the necessity use of the same part. To the which end purpose, he
must note what vvoudes, in the which the bullets have bin shot, are
incurable, or difficult, or easy to be cured, because that we may admonish,
the friends, and kindrede of the patient, of those accidents which might
happen unto the vvoude, the bullet being drawn therout, alsoe of the
certayntye or fear, which through the danger, we are to expect, of the
healinge of the same. Advise of Celsus.
For first of all Celsus says, that a prudent, and vvyse Chyrurgiane, shall
not at any time take in hade, such a patient, for whom it is impossible to
be curede, escape death, because he administer no occasion unto others to
suspect, think, that he hath bereft the patient of life, which othervvyse
through the bad fortune of his wounds is deceased. And saying that the
danger is great, but yet notvvithstadinge entermingled with some good hope,
the he shall admonish the friends of the patient, that the matter is heavye,
and suspect, because if so be the art science, be suppressed, and overvvonne
of the dissease, that they should not as then think, ether that he hath not
known thereof, or else that he hath sought to abuse the friends. But that such
is the office, and the duty, of a vvyse and prudent Chyrurgiane, also is it
the fact of a Runagate Qvacksalver and deceaver, to make any thing, seem
to be very dangerous, which of it self is easy, and light, because we
should think, more art to be in him, then indeed theris. It is alsoe
seemlye, that the Chyrurgiane cofessinge, the cure to be easy light, do pane
his credit, reputation, because as the he might the more vvillinge, think one
that, which might be most commodious and profitable, to the curing and
healinge of the patient, and that the dissease which of it self is small,
through the negligence of those, of the which the patient is kept, be made
no greater.

To this end Paulus Aegeneta counceleth us, Opinione of Paulus Aegineta.
that if so be, the bullet continue, or tarry, in any of the vvorthyest
parts of the body, as in the Brains, Heart, Liver, or throat, in the
kidneys, bladder, or Matrice, and the signs, tokens of death revealinge
themselves we cannot by any means possible convenientlye without great
pains, dilaniatione of the wounded part draw forth the foresayed bullet,
that as the we may not offer to touche it, because that without doing any
good or furtherance, we do not administer unto the ignorant common poeple,
any occasion, of evell speaking or blaminge us.

But allthoughe the issue of the vvounde, be as yet uncertain, having
admonished and fortoulde the danger, Many men preserve there lives above the
opiin of the Chyrurgian.
we may with Gods help take the cure in hand: because that some having an
Apostematione, in any of the vvorthyest parts, against the opinione
certayntye of the Chyrurgiane, have as yet beheld praeserved there life: yea
we have alsoe seen beheld some, in the which a parcel of there Liver, one
part of the Epiploon, the whole VVombe hath fro them bin separated, or
hevved cut of, the patient for all this hath bin cured, and praeserved to
life. We have alsoe at some times in a vehemete great squinantie, pierced made
an apertione in the throat. But if so be that we suffer the bullet to
continue in the body, or in any part of the same, without extractinge of
the same, when that easily he may be drawn forth, it necessarylye
follows, that the patient must dye therofe, it might also bring the
Physician, or Chyrurgiane into the name of beige cruel, mercylesse, where
other vvyse the bullet beige drawn forth, it might be that the patient might
as yet be curede, wherefore the Chyrurgiane must always endevoure do the
best he can, when he hath forttoulde, admonished the friends of the patient,
of the danger, because some times great fearful vvoudes beyode the
expectatios of all me are cured, wherefore we ought not at any, time to
permit or suffer the patiete without help or succour, where there is any
small hope of being cured. We must not at any time leave the patient.
And all though that all things of this our operatione fall not out
according unto our mind, yet notvvithstandinge, must not we leave to
effect that which this art science reqvireth, that which our conscience
vvitnesseth unto us.

Consideration of bulletes,
Above all that the Chyrurgiane must considere, on the wounded part, he must
alsoe farther consider one the difference, varietye of bulletes, which
consists, in the matter, form, qvantitye, number, and facultye of the same.
As concerning touching the matter, although that the foresayed bulletes,
most commonly are of lead yet for all that sometimes the besieged, they that
skermishe in the feelds are constrained to use tin bullets, Copper, pieces of
iron, steel, peasen, yea alsoe little stones or pebbles. As concerning the
figure or form thereof they are most commonly rovvnde, although there are some
shot with three corners, some four corners, some of other fashons.
Touching the qvantitye, there is great difference concerning the greatenes of
the pieces, by the which the bullets are shot. As for the number,
constitutione of the foresayed bullets, there are some which shoot, with
more. the with one bullet, with some which are fastened the one, with the
other, with little chained Bullets, with hayle shot, which spread
themselves, in the body of a man. Speaking of the forces or faculties, of
bullets I have not as yet geven any credit unto them that said the bullets
might be poysened, because as yet there hath bin never a famous Chyrurgiane
which ever had a venomous shot vvounde in hand in these our times which
hath made any metione therofe, as is playnlye showed, in the discourse of the
deceased the right vvorshipfull M Ambrosius Pare, chief Chyrurgiane to the
king his majesty which hath very learnedlye discussed as it vveare
grovvnded this questione in his boockes of Chyrurgerye.

The bullets, all other strange things ought in the first dressinge to be
drawn forth, so far as it is possible to be done: of the means how to
effect the same. Chap. 3.
WHen that the Chyrurgiane shall have considered, one the part, also a little
observed, one the varietye, difference of bullets, he shall choose, a certain
number of instruments which cotinuallye he ought to have by him, especially
those, vvich he supposes to be most fittest to draw forth the fore said
bullet with most facility ease. We must endevoure to draw forth the bullete
at the first dressinge.
The which he shall at the first dressinge, without any longer delay, unto
the next day, put in ur and practise: for the bullet being drawn out,
shall the patient, Chyrurgiane alsoe be freed from great danger, the which
both of them with all right ought to desire and lunge for. The bullet may
alsoe praesentlye, while the vvounde is fresh green, be felt with the
finger, or most fitlye with the privet or searchinge iro without tarrying
until the next day, because that the wounded part, through any
concurringe humours, svvelleth out of hade, through the payne of the vvounde,
by the which the entrance of the vvounde comes to svvelle, as alsoe through
the force of the bullet, any of the Mebranes or Tedones, being bruised the
foresayed entrace of the vvounde, stops: because most comonlye shotten
vvoundes do not enter right, or liniallye into the body, but turning, alsoe
with any Membranes, or tendones rovvnde about vvounde, even ^ngled, as alsoe
the bullet through his ponderousnes, running this way or that way, chances
to fall into some hollovve place, which sometimes frustrateth, annihilateth
the knowledge of the Chyrurgiane, so that by any means possible, he can
nether touch nor find him: above all this, the foresayed vvounde, is the
second day more sensible, tender, then when she is fresh, as the patient
allsoe while that the vvounde is fresh warm, is better able to abide the
sovvnding or searchinge iro, or privett: that which is more, the wounded
having obtained no time to think, The vvouded feels not his vvoude at the
first.
consider of his vvoude, his heart being as yet puffed up with vayn glory,
is not as yet at the first dressinge, posessed with any timorousnes or fear,
as he is in the second or third dressinge: wherefore in his frist dressige,
with more courage, patience, is able to bear the payne, and dravvinge forth
of the bullet.

First of all therefore, if so be that by any means we ca nether find nor
feel the bullet, to acomplisshe effect this work, fitlye convenientlye,
with the least trouble payne of the patient, we are constrained to cause the
patient to sit or stand, in such a form, posture, or, collocatione as he sate
or stood, when as the bullet was shot into his body, yea alsoe with the
same gesture, behavioure, One what manner we must set the patient
which he used: for the chainginge of the form as when we fight, or when
we are laid aloge, or when we stade upright, or being set down, causes
a great alteratione, in the being, and situatione, of these parts of the
patient, because the veins, arteryes, synnues, bones, muscles, may one so
many ways fashons be turnede vvoude, as they have divers uses, actions unto
the which they at fit, it be ether in the erectige, or in the stoupinge, or
in the turnige: which often times is the occasion, that altough we search,
feel for the bullet, any muscle lye any other way turned, then it did, when
the foresayed muscle was pearcede with the bullet, the vvoude in that place
most commolye being stopped, so that for the most part the searchinge iron can
not enter that place where the bullet lies: But when we shall have set the
patient on such a manner, as he stood, or sat, when he was shot, the come all
the parts into the same situatione, as they vveare when he was shot,
wherefore, as the the proof or searchge iron, may pass very easily,
without any let, eve into that place whereas the bullet lies. And so far
forth as the patient, cosideringe his great imbicillitye vveakenes, hath not
the strength to stade in that ordre, we must as the at the least, ether
sitting or lying, turn, wind him on such a manner, whether he sittige or
lying, in as much as is possible, as he was when he received the shot. Why
the wound must bedilated.
Now being in such a situatione, we are first of all counceled, if so be
the vvoude be narrovve, as alsoe all aunciete Chyrurgias teach 'vve must as
the dilate the vvoude, because the bullet, or that which is therein, may have a
free passage: for there is nothing, that causes more in flammatione, then
dilaceratione, or tearing of the flesh, when as with violence we seek to
draw out any thing out of the vvoude, wherefore it is better to make the,
dilatatione with a little rasore then with violence to tear op the vvoude:
further we must note, that in the dilatige of the vvoude, we chance not to
hurt any synnues, veins, or arteryes: the which if you perceave, you shall
eschevve avoid the, or with a little blunt hoocke pluk them one the one
side, proceed betrvvixt them, as Celsus in his 5 cap. of his seaveth book
teaches us.

After that we sufficietlye dilate the vvoude we must them search for the
bullet or that which is shot in the vvounde, The second instructione.
feel first of all with your finger, if it be possible, as with out doubt it
is, the best searchinge iron in feeling we must consider what way the
bullet hath taken, if it be deeper the the length of your finger, as then we
must use there unto the common searchinge iron, which must be reasonable thick,
and at the lower end very rovvnde, for if so be, it be to sharpe pierces,
and goes between two muscles, with out follovvinge that way which the
bullet vvet.

When as therefore we have certaynlye fovvnde out the bullet, The third
instruction.
the third instructione is that we draw him forth to the effectinge of the
which, there is nothige surer, the to draw him through the same place, by
which he entered, especially when he hath not penetrated to deep, or passed
through any great vain, arterye, or synnue because the way is allreadye
made, wherefore we need not make any other inscisione.

Amongst all instruments which ever I have used, I have contented my self best,
In scisione one the contrary side.
with that instrumete, which we call, the spoonevvyse, or spoonefashoned
bullet dravver, because this instrument, may both be used fore a searge iron, a
bullet extractor alsoe, so that as soon as we heervvith feel the bullet we
may alsoe at that time, pluck him out: but pearcinge very deep, one the
cotrarye side is little substance, without great veins, we with our fingers
may feel the bullet, it is the better, rather couceled, there to make an
apertione, namelye, on the contrary side, of the entrace of the bullet, then to
draw the same by the way which he entered, considering the great distace,
and space, through which he being drawn backvvardes, must of necessity
pass bye, as alsoe the payne is far greaater, when with the bullet, with
the instrument, the vvoude internallye comes to tear, bruise the Flesh, the
when by inscisione we dilate the vvoude, by which she is alsoe sooner cured,
because that which is cut, more re easier sooner is healed, then that which
through dilaniatione is wounded: and if it be possible, the foresayed
inscisione must be made right under the bullet, but sovvhat greater then the
bullet, because the we need not draw him forth with any violence, do not
chance to lacerate the vvoude: This being in this sort finished, we shall
find the bullet a great deal nearer unto us, more easier to be drawn
therout, without passing by to great tedious a vvaye, in the dravvinge forth,
of that way by the which he entered, Vtilitye of inscisione.
yea which is more, the vvounde will a great deal sooner be cured, and with
less danger, the vvounde having two issues, to wit the one under, the
other above, partly because she may receave the remedies the better, as alsoe
cosideringe the matter, which one both sides hath her issue, which othervvyse
might be retained, collect or gather it self together in the bottom of the
vvounde.

As soon as the bullet is draw forth, we must show it the patient,
because he may be joy full glad, to be released of that which was such a
molestatione trouble unto him, and was cause of so great payne, and by the
which he might have gone so lange a time with payne, without being thereof
cured: we must endevoure by all means, ether by dravvinge him forth, through
his entrace, or else through the cotrarye side of the vvounde, well knovvinge
howe to effect this dravvinge, with all fitnes, conveniece, because we
should not forsake the vvouded, nether discourage him, through our lunge
operatione, or vvorkinge: effect that alsoe with the least payne possible,
because that the payne of the wounded patient therbye, which is great might
not be increased, Instructione howe to work vvyselye.
for it is to great a shame, to bring a wounded person which bath enough
to suffer, into greater sufferace payne: we must alsoe dilligently note, that
we do not bye any means hurt any particular part, and especially the
great veins, arteryes, or synnues, or any other vital parts, one the
which we must have an especial eye, and care: for it would be to great a
disgrace, shame, thinckinge to draw forth the bullet to do more harm, the
if the bullet had stayed therein.

What sotimes deceaveth the Chyrurgyane.
We are oft times deceaved, nether ca we alsoe find the bullet, because he
is clothed, with cotton, with vvolle, or with any other parcels of
apparel, which the f^re said bullet, carries into the body with him: or
else because the flesh, or the Membranes which are bruised, or with violence
of the bullet have bin rete, cover the foresayed bullet. It is alsoe sometimes
evident, that the foresayed bullet, erreth fro his right way, chances to
come somvvhether a side, into some other part, yet notvvithstadinge, having
in the passage met with any bone, is for all that removed into some other
place, as between any muscles, between any Mebranes, or betvvee any
ligametes, wherefore the Chirurgiane not finding of the same, searchinge for
him accordige to the rectitude of the vvoude, must turn, wind the instrument
one all sides, feel with his hades one the out side not only the vvouded
part, but alsoe the circumiacent places, because therbye he might know
whether that the bullet might be suck: which he shall discern, and mark,
ether cosideringe any payne, spaninge of the skin, hardness, or through any
blevvnes or cerulitye, which comonlye, is noted seen about that vvouded
part, where, the bullet is covayed, sunk. We must also dilligetlye note,
that when as we think to have found the bullet, we do not rashelye make
an inscision, before vvea are certified of the place of the bullet, and so
draw him forth of the same: for that Chyrurgiane vveare vvorthye to be
laughed to scorn, which ca not accoplishe his praetended purpose after that
he hath so intolleratlye tormented the patient.

Albove all this, we must note, and mark, The bullet sometimes carries
sothinge with him in to the body.
whether the bullet have trailed nothing with him into the vvounde, as
paper, vvoole, cotte, linne, or any thing else of the patients clothinge, or
apparel, or alsoe any parcel of the patietes armour, or harnas, or any
pieces of vvoode, stones, or iron, which the bullet mightby any means have
carried with him into the wound: as such a thing might well chance, when the
bullet any where against a vval, or against ons harnas, on the which the
fore said bullet chances to rebound, breaking, receavinge some small
parcel of that on the which he plettered, carries it with him into the
body: If so be there be any such like thing, carried, into the vvoude with
the bullet, we ought to have no less care of that, yea rather more, the one
the dravvinge forth of the bullet, for such things are vvholye cotrarye unto
nature, because they corrupt and rot in the vvounde, out of the which
needs, must follow some great Inflamatios, or Apostematios, so conseqvetlye,
the vvounde in a loge time ca not be cured, which othervvyse might easily be
cured, much sooner, although that the bullet as yet tarried therine,
especially if that the bullet be of lead, because that lead, vnitethe makes
it self all one with our nature. Means how to know whether the bullet have
take any thing with him.
And perfectly to know, whether the bullet have carried any such thiges
with him, we must consider, one the harnas, if that be any vvheare torn, or
broke, if it be a mayled doublete, how many mayles there are vvantinge, the
which one that makes mayle doublets easily ca tell you: we must alsoe note,
the patietes apparel, his shirt, whether there be any great or small pieces
thereof torn rete: or whether there be one, olye hole fovvnde there.

How we ought to draw forth those bullets which stick fast in the bones, or
in the iunctures or ioynetes of the same: alsoe what we ought to note whenas
the foresayed bones are crushed, beaten to pieces. Chap. 4.
Those bullets which stick fast in any bones, are very difficult to be
drawn out.
THe greatest davnger that consists in dravvinge forth of a bullet is, when
the fore said bullet sticks fast, in any bone, or in any ioyncte
between the bones. If so be the bullet, stick fast in any bone, it is
most convenient that as the we draw him forth, with that instrument which
we call Extractor, or Grovvnde dravver whereof we must set the point one the
bullet, wide it very fast in the same, when it is therein fast, we must
softly easily try, if he will not follovve, turnige the hands, this way
and that way, the better to make him loose, and stir. But if so be that we
can not so soon get out the bullet we must then suffer him, to continue
therein some certain days, with in the which time, the external flesh
will begin to rot, through the which the vvoude vvilbe somewhat dilated,
vvyder, and the bone will somewhat disclose open, during the time, we must
everye day stir the bullet, with the instrument lift him up because that thus
doing, the foresayed bullet might by little little be removed Loosed: and if
that we perceave that the bullet sticks to fast, can not by any of the
fofsayed means bedravvne out: then is this the last remedy, that we quite,
clean pearce and boar through the bone, with the Extractor or elsi with
the point orpiramide of a Trepane, pearce boar, against, rovvnde about the
bullet in divers places, Elevatorium or vpheaver.
because there may be made some place for an Elevatorium, on that sort to lift
up the bullet drevve him therout, if that with all gentleness it may by any
means possible be brought to pass for we are in no vvyse counceled, with
violence or vehemence to effect it. And if it be a little and small bullet,
that he stick in any place fast, as in the middle of the greatest focile of
the shin bone, or in the middle of any rib, or in the sternon, or in any
bones of the head, we are as then counceled, to trepane that bone, setting
the Trepane on the bullet, so that the fore said bullet, be rovvnde about
compassed vvithinthe circkle of the Trepane, and that then the trepane might
abolish and take away all that, which retained and held fast the bullet.

On what sort we must draw forth a bullet out of a ioyncte.
But when the bullet is any where, in any ioyncte pierced penetratede
betvvene two bones, as for example, in the knees, we must then as easyely as
it is possible, endevoure with the instrument to stir the bullet hither,
thither, hut allvvayes straight forevvarde, now toward the hipp, and then
toward the leg because that therbye we might the better, vnshutt make
loose, with the least payne that may be, the ligametes, tedones, which
cotayne houlde the ioyncte so close together: by the which apertione, and
dravvinge forth the space, distance between the bones, vvilbe a little
dilated, so that at length, with less payne trouble, with the little
spoonevvyse bullet dravver, we shall be able to draw forth the bullets. But if
we feared, least we should put the patient to some intolerable payne,
through the distendinge of the ligamentes, tedones, my council is as the,
that the yonge Chyrurgiane, endevoure, to follovve and use the same, which I
did or have used on my lord of Floyon: which in the skirmish of Maestricht,
A notable History.
got a shot in his knee, the bullet lying fast, occulte in the iovncte
betvvee the bones, the which nether I, nor any chyrurgianes, of Do Ihon of
the Eeaste, could find, at the last it seemed good unto me, against the
opinios of all the rest, we should shut fouled together the knees of the
patient: which he doing with some payne, the bullet, through the fouldige of
the Knees, the bones which placed pressed themselves together, was drive
therout, revealed it self externallye, under the skin, and one the sides of
the foresayed ioyncte, where I having made a little inscisione, my self have
take the same forth of that place.

In like sort, if so be the bullet, We must dilate the vvounde if the bone be
dilacerated
have rebovvnded against any bone, and the bone through the violence thereof be
broke, crushed to divers pieces, the bullet as yet remaining therein, or else
having pierced quite through, it is as then the surest way to dilate the
vvounde, as far forth as the wounded part is able to suffer, it, that ether
in the entrance, or issue of the vvounde, at the least if it have an issue: the
which being finished, we strayghtvvayes with the finger, or with any
other instrumete, must search for the spliter, and finelye draw forth, those
parcels which vvholye are separated fro the bone, alsoe in like sort, the
bullet, if so be as yet he sticks therine: if that there vveare any great
pieces, or parcels of the bone, which as yet are not vvholye separated fro
the bone, but are yet fastened vvithe the Periostium, or the ligamentes, we
must not with any violence draw the forth, because through such violence
we might urge great payne convulsione of synnues, wherefore it is far better
to place situate them by the bone, on the which as yet they are half
fastened: For nature, expellethe them most comonlye vvithe the matter forth of
the vvounde, without any payne, they separate alsoe fro the bone, through
the increasinghe of the new flesh, the which repells the same from him,
or else in time they conjoin themselves together, and are so again cured, as
I have of te times known the same to chance, and as yet in fresh memory,
Monsr. de la Tour, ordinated and chosen gentleman of the kings chamber of
presence, which in the Baricades, or Treches of Parris, got a shot in his left
leg, the entrance whereof was in the oppermoste part of his shin bone,
breaking the least focile, in divers pieces, of the which also one piece of
the same focile came forth, through the apertione, which the foresayed piece,
through the violence, blovve of the bullet, had made himself, which cleanlye
pierced througe the Muscles called Gemini Solores, wherefore m'Habricot, Barber
Chyrurgia at Parris, made an inscisione in the foresayede muscles partly to
extract draw forth those splinters forth of the same, partly, to restore
again those broke pieces of bones, into the vvounde, which as yet vveare not
separated fro the Periostium: and is in the space of two months again cured,
the foresayed pieces of bones being therein very sovvndelye healed, and I
alsoe have treated him until such time as he was vvholye fully cured.

The bullet sometimes pierces through the grissles.
Sotimes the bullet pierces clean through, some cartilage or grisselye part,
or through some Tendones, which being softer the the bone, doth not break,
but only splitteth asunder or bends it self, as soon as the bullet is
passed bye, erects it self again, occludeth, the passage, where under the
bullet hide the himself, the which, al though we endevoure to search for
with our fingers or searchinge iron, it for al that is impossible we should
find him, which comonlye chances in the vvoudes of the breast, when as the
breast bone is pierced. As I remember such to have chanced, in my lord of
Malicorne being wounded, hurt before Maillezes in Poittou.

How that the Chyrurgiane ought not be to curious in searchinge out the bullet.
Cap. 5.
Good doctrine for a Chyrurgiane.
ALlthoughe, the bullet be of any strange, alienate, substance, yea alsoe as
intolerable for nature, as is the livinge with the dead, the principalle
intention, to cure all vvoundes is, that we must first separate that, from
the vvounde which oppugneth nature, yet notvvithstandinge ought not the
Chyrurgiane be too curious, in seeking all contrary things in the wound or
to too bold audacious, in dravvinge the out, except he lightly or easily,
can find them, vvithe small payne grief to the patient ca draw them out.
Because that often times, in the first, we can find nothing, but the vvoude
being come to good suppuratione, and matter, the bruised flesh rovvnde about
the bullet corruptinge rottinge, at the last makes him an apertione, through
the which nature in the end, expellethe the bullet without any pane, as is
the nature qvallytye of the vital livinge parts, to expel fro them the
mortified and dead parts: the which we ought to understand of all other
contrary things, which are retained in a vvounde: as sometimes being the
surest best way, that we recommend such things unto Nature, The Chyrurgiane
is the servant of nature.
follovve her instructions, then that we in vain torment, and molest the
same, saying that it is she only, which curethe the disseased, when as she
through the Chyrurgiane, as through her servat is opitulated helped: For I
have known many, and divers, which being shot, the bullet hath continued in
the body, are yet for all that perfectly cured, and that in short time alsoe,
without at any time thereafter to perceave any impediment thereof: which
especially chances the bullet being of lead, the which in time by little
little comes to unite it self and with our nature agrees: wherefore for a
renoumned example I will allege, the vvounde of Mosieur of Chardon, the
first gentleman, of my lord the Cardinalle of Bourbo, who being the
Chapelayne, of my lord of Angvie, hath gotten a shot in the skirmish of St.
Laurent, in the midst of his leg, of the which shot the bullet, remained
fast sticking therein, the vvounde being vvholye, compleatlye cured, without
perceaving in 28 year any hiderance or molestatione of the same, wherefore he
reqvested mr Pare, me, Example.
that we should remove take a way the fore said bullet from his leg,
fearing least that the same, in time, might be any hinderance or molestatione
unto him, the which we alsoe effected, finding the bullet united and
coalited together, as well with the bone, as with any Mebranes, and so fast
grovvne, and joined together, as if the bone, the membrane, the bullet, had all
of them bin but one substance. It happens alsoe sometimes that the bullet
through his ponderousenes, heavyenes by degrees, dessendethand sinks
dovvnvvardes, towards the exterior skin, The bullet through his
ponderousnes sinks dovvnvvardes.
that part notvvithstadinge being cured, through the vyhich he descedeth or
sinks, without the perceavige of the patient, where, without any great
trouble or molestatione of the patient, he may be cut out, which one this
manner is far surer, the if in the first we had tormented him heervvith,
brought him into danger of his life, considering the great dilaniatione or
tearing of veins, arteryes, and synnues, which the hardnecked and obstinate
Chyrstrgiane, in searchinge and in dravvinge forth of the bullet, might be the
cause of: wherefore I, in all sorts do council, that when the bullet, can not
by any means possible fitlye or convenientlye, without any great torment of
the patient be drawn forth, we as then quietly suffer him to tarry therein:
In thus doing we shall follovve the practise of the ancient professors, yea
alsoe of Hippocrates, An example of Hippocrates.
which recites to have cured a certain personage, which had received a shot
with an arrovve, in his flanckes, where of the head, or iron, tarried therein,
and yet against the opinione of all men, is thereof cured helped: which
foresayed arrovve head or piece of iron, six years thereafter he took from
him. Paulus Aegineta alleges, Paulus Aegineta.
that he hath of ten times seen, that the arrovves have bin lost in men's
bodies, that the same, in a lunge time thereafter, the vvounde being cured, are
through apostematione of that part come again to light revealed themselves.
Albucasis says, that he alsoe hath seen one, An example of Albucasis
which was shot with an arrovve into his shoulder, of the which shot, the
foresayed arrovve, therein stuck fast, yet for all that is cured, but that the
arrovve, seven year thereafter at the length through the bone Coccix is come
forth and taken out. Wherefore we must not be to curious, in dravvige forth
the bullet, with great danger, torment to the patient, to our utter shame
disgrace.

THE SECOND TREATISE OF THE OPEratione of Chyrurgerye, wherein is discoursed,
of the means, howe to Trepane the Cranium, of the Head, Containing six
Chapiters.
Of the signs tokens by the which we may know when there is a Fracture in
the Head. Chap. 1. Of the counterfracture, of the sculle. Chap, 2. Which
Fractures we ought to trepane. Chap. 3. How lunge, we ought to tarry,
before vvee proceed with the trepane. Chap. 4. How great a quantitye of
bone, we ought to take therout. Chap. 5. The manner, and methode of
trepaninge. Chap. 6.
Of the tokens, through the which, we shall know the fractures, of the sculle.
Cap. 1.
Chyrurgerye must not rashelye be effected.
THe operations of Chyrurgerye, which with great prudence, consideratione,
vvilbe effected, we must not all to rashly or overbouldlye begin them
saying, therefore that it is dangerous, to open the Cranium of the head,
before we may attain unto the operatione, we must dilligently and truly
consider, whether it be very necessary, or not, which is revealed unto us,
by those signs and tokens which the ancient Chyrurgianes have beqveathed
unto us: For the sign or token, is the demonstratione unto us, which
discovers, that which before lay occulte, hidden: The which fore said
tokens, of the wounded part, must be taken, and observed, ether of the
accidents which chance unto the vvounde, after the blovve, or fall, or allsoe
of the instrument, wherewith the vvounde was made.

We must therefore first of all note, Discriptione of the signs
which part of the head, hath received the struck namelye, whether the
Cranium, one this place be thin, or tender, thick or strong, because the
bones of the head, are not in all places of aequalle crassitude, or of aequall
tenuitye: (...) there we must mark, whether the vveapos, wherewith the
blovve was given, have allsoe cut of the hair, that it shevveth it self
right in the vvounde for if it be so, it is to befeared, that the bone is
bared or vncovered, out of the which we may easily judge, the Cranium to be
hurt, because that it is all most impossible, the hair to be cut through,
the which for the most part slips aside, with out hurtinge of the Cranium
or breaking there of. Having thus considered and noted all this we must
interrogate the patient, whether he have parbraked vomited any Cholera,
whether he lost not his sight, and his eyes darckened, whether he lost not
his speech, or hath voided any blood through his nose, through his ears,
or through his mouth, whether after the blovve he fell not down to the
earth, and there lay still, without any reason, as if he had bine one sleep:
For it is impossible that all these fore said signs chance unless the scull
were dilaniate, or broke. In like sort if the patient, lye without his
memory or reason, if he rage, if he be vexed with Paralisis, Divers accidetes
of the vvoundes of the head.
or with Spasmo, or with them both together, it is then credulous, that the
Membrana called Dura Matter, is hurt, and suffers great payne. And although
that none of these accidents have happened unto the patient, yet
notvvithstandinge all ways theris something doubtful, whether the Cranium
be hurt broken, or not: and to be thereof certified we are to note, with
what instrument the wound was made, as ether with a stone, with a stick,
with an iron, or with any other vveapons: also whether it wear great, or
reasonable great, light or heavye, smooth or rugged, whether the blovve was
smitten violently, or easily, with greath ire, fury, vpvvardes or
dovvnevvardes: of all the which we may be certified of by the patiete, when
as we ask him on what manner he was stricken, or from whence he is fallen,
vvherone, to wit on the earth, or on the pavement, whether he have great
payne, and in what place, for how easier the blovve hath bin stricken the
less fear there is of a broken scull: yet notvvithstandinge, there is nothing
surer, Celsus.
as Celsus says, the that we search the vvounde, give judgment through a
more surer sign, wherefore, if so be, the vvounde be great enough, we must
as then search it with the finger, scrapige the bone with your nail, one
that manner to perceave the rete, and dilaceratione, and if so be we can not
effect that with the finger, we must as the try it with the searchinge
iron, which must not be to gross and thick, nether to sharpe and thin,
because, if that per adventure we perceaved any natural hollovvnes of the
sculle, What form the searchinge iron must be of.
we should not think esteem it to be a dilaceratione, or fracture: nether
must the privet or searchinge iron be to thick, or gross, because it should
not praetermit overrune a little or small dilaceratione, without perceavinge
it, and staying there at. When as therefore we lead and perfricate the privet
or searchinge iron over the sculle, perceave no vnsmothnes, then all even, and
smothe, then we say esteem the sculle to be with out danger, not
dilacerated or torn. But if so be we perceave any thing, which is rugged
and uneven, and that the searchinge iron stadeth thereat fast, if so be, it be
not on the sutures or commissures, and ioyninge of the sculle, or any natural
cocavitye hollovvnes of the Cranium, it is then a sign, that the head is
broken, or lacerated, and rent wherefore the Chyrurgiane must dilligently
mark, that he do not in steed of the fracture, take the suture, because that
those comissures, The Chyrurgiane may easily be deceaved.
often times do deceave the sight of the Chyrurgiane in his judgement, and
that as touching the similitude, which the commissures, are participatige
with the fractures: And we are to note, that in some persons, the futures
have no natural situatione, or place, A suture, or commissure, is the ioyninge
together of the bones of the head.
but to the contrary we must also note, whether that there be any
dilaceratione one the fracture, or not: for it might chance, that the natural
similitude of a suture, might well be a rent or dilaceratione, which is not
so easily to be perceaved, known, because that a suture considering her
nature is vnsmothe and rugged, even as a dilaceratione is vvonte to be.
Wherefore Hippocrates, Hippocrates hath bin deceaved.
acknovvledgeth to have bin deceaved, in the person of Antonomus, of Omilos,
vvhoe being thrown with a stone in the midst of his forehead, and a
bout the sutures, died within the space of fifteen days thereafter, because
that he was not trepayned, and because alsoe the foresayed Hippocrates,
thought and judged the skull not to be dilaniated or torn. Wherefore, it is
the surest, and certaynest way, principally, when by, or in the vvounde there
are these foresayed accidents, with agues, that as then we denudate, and
uncover the sculle, because the sutures sometimes have no certain place, for
sometimes we espy the, in the midst of any bone, which a man would
think to be a fracture or dilaceratione: it might alsoe chance that the
foresayed sutures, or the next parts, unto the same, The futures of the head
are variable.
might be dilacerated and torn, the which we can not certaynlye espy,
without making denudatione of the Cranium, and to our sight uncover it: when
as therefore we doubt, whether there be a fracture or fissure, and can not
certaynlye espy it, because of the smallenes of the vvoude, as then there is
less danger, and the cure thereof happens so much the sooner, the surer,
when as therein we make a reasonable vvyde apertione, rather then being thereof
uncertain, we as yet proceed with such a small apertione, in curing of the
same vvoude, because that it is vvholye impossible, othervvyse to know any
certayntye, of that which thervnder lies hidden, as we easily see in the
great apertions, which thereafter, may very easily be cured. It sometimes
alsoe chances, that the Cranium being sufficietlye denudated rovvnde about
the fissure, yet for all that with the tacture, or with the searchinge iron,
or with the eyes, we can not by any means possible espy the fissure or
rent, because it is as subtle and fine, How we shall espy the hayrye
fissure.
as a hair, wherefore it is allsoe called the hayrye fissure, or ret: to the
which purpose we must above on the sculle or bone, vvype a little ink, or
any other black medicamente, because that the nigreditye or blacknes, may
imprint in the fissure some sign of his blackenes, if so be that there be
any, which bare bone, we must there after scrape with an exfoliative
trepane: For that which is burst or rent, notvvithstandinge retains his
blackenes, by the which means, we shallbe certified of the largeness there of
and profunditye of the same.

Of the counter fissure, in the bones of the head. Chap. 2.
THe ancient Doctors, and all so certain professors of our time, make
mentione, that we sometimes find a counter tear or fissure in the sculle, as
if we had received a blovve in the occipitialle part of the head and had the
tear or fissure in the antereore part of the same and there revealed it self,
or in any other part of the head also, whereas the blovve did not alight.
Signs of a counter fissure.
The conjecture which we ought to have of a counter fissure, is, that we
surely know whether the patient vveare roughly stricken, and whether after
the blovve, any bad accidetes have followed, as if through the blovve he fell
to the grovvnde, hath there lien impotente, hath vomited Cholera, is full of
agues, although it so be that we can find no fissure in the Cranium in the
place of the vvounde, and there where the scull is denudated. We must alsoe
farther note, and observe, vvether the patient often times lay his hand, on
any other place of his head, then where the vvounde is, complayninge of any
payne or heavines of his head: we must one that place which we suspect
apply some Cephalicke plaster, which when we remove, the same, we must
then note, whether any one certain place of the applicatiane, be moyster
then in another place, or else any where under the payster, the skin be
softened and a little more svvollene, then the other, which finding it to be
so, it is the a sign, that in the sculle there must needs be a fissure, that
it is necessary, there and in that place to denudate the Cranium: for sometimes
we find the bone there to be broke: The ancient Surgeons have beyond all
this written, Opinione of the aunciete professors touching inscisione.
that although we have inscided the skin, without any reason or occasion,
it easily and lightly vvilbe again curedand if so be that the sculle be
dilaniated torn, and we having forgot to detect denudate the same there
immediately follows a great imflammatione, which very difficuttlye can be
cured. But I as yet could never attain to the knowledge of such a fracture,
nether can I perfectly adhibite any credit thereunto that there can by any
means chance to be such fissures shut up, and closed or joined on with the
other and so grovvne together, or being missing of the broken bone unto
that, which finds it self in the fracture of the other side, right over
against it. I have seen, that with the shot of a gun, that the first table
was vnhurte, and yet not vvithstandinge the second was crushed and broken,
when I perceaved that the patient, had gotten many bad accidents, namelye,
that through the blovve he fell to the grovvnde, had vomited, forth at his
nose voided blood, was vvholye abashed feeling payne about the vvounde,
having alsoe an ague: and fearing least the patient might have dyed, I
trepanned him, whereas when I had pierced the first table I found, that the
second was burst, yea and that reasonable vvyde: wherefore thereafter I was
urged to believe that the counter tear or fissure, in the other side of the
sculle, which by the ancient professors was described, must be understood
in the same bone, for the first table, is right against the second situated.
It might alsoe chance that nether the first table nor the second should be
hurt, or broken, yet notwithstanding the Diploe, that is the porositye which
is between them both, An observatione of the auctor.
was so plettered crushed, that the little veins, wherewith it was,
replenished, vveare broke burst and avoided blood from them which in time
corrupts and rots and alsoe corrupts the bone internallye, the which in
some time thereafter we perceave, for the bone vvaxeth leadishe coloured, on
the which the Chirurgiane must well note.

It may allsoe chance, that the sculle, be safe and sound, yet through the
violence of the blovve, any veins which contain that membrane which is
called Dura matter, Veins which burst within the brains.
as well of those which pass through the sutures as througe any of the
other small holes internallye are situated with in the brains, might come to
break, out of the which there will issue blood, which thereafter cogealeth
and with great payne changes in to matter, with many other fearful
accidents. In this dissease theris all ways payne about that vain which is
burst, and if so be we chance to open in this place the skin, the bone
there under hath a pale and dead Colour: but it is a difficult matter to
judge, and know it, wherefore considering often times of the impossibility,
to do any remedy or cure therine by want of knowledge, death suddaynlye
ensues thereon. The which Hippocrates recites of the daughter of Nerius, A
history must of the daughter of Nerius, described through Hippocrates.
which was but 20. years old who in playing being smitten on the bone
Bregma, with an outstreched arm, of one of her playfellovves, strayghtvvayes
without any respiringe is, fallen into a Vertiginem who as soon as she was
brought into the house is fallen into a violent ague, with payne in the head,
and rednes of face: one the seventh day she voided a goblet fulle of reddishe
matter filthylye stinckinge out of her right ear, by the which she seemed to
be somewhat lighted and easyed but when the ague again returned unto her,
she was then vvholye abashed, and almost lay without any reason or
understanding, with Spasmo, in her right cheek, or side of the face,
without being able to speak vvherone the foresayed Spasmus hath possessed
the whole body, with shaking, qviveringe, with the tongue tied, and with
irremoveable eyes, and dyed on the ninth day.

What Fractures of the head we ought to trepane and wherefore we must trepane
them. Chap. 3.
We ought not at all times to trepane.
Although that in all fractures and fissures of the sculle, the yoghe
Chyrurgians take there light or refuge straight unto trepaninge: it is
notvvithstadinge better, that in the first we try, with players, and
with many other remedies, and inventions thereto constituted, and ordained:
considering one the consequence and importace, of the foresayed Fracture,
whether, it be needful or necessary, to take away any parcel or piece of
bone, considering the excellentie, and vvorthines of the brains, which are
such vvorthye parts: wherefore we must vviselye and dilligently consider and
mark, whether the vvounde give a good digestione forth of her, and purifieth
her self, and whether there begin in the vvounde to grow, any red
pomgronate Kernel wise flesh, or whether the ague which considering the
suppuratione in the vvounde, be abated or asvvaged, or hath left him which so
in tollerablye vexed and tormented him, whether his appetite be again
restorede, and whether the patient reasonably takes rest or sleep, without
having any troublesome accident chance unto him, vvherbye we might
suspect, that the membrane called Dura matter, or the brains, are molested,
affected, or suffer any payne, ether through a splinter, or through any
blood, or through any matter, which might be come and situated it self
therone: And the matter luckylye ominouslye procedinge, The fissures recollect
then selves sotimes.
we may continue the fore said remedies, and the use therofe, because that
sometimes by this means the fissures, are replenished filled up with some
obdurate substance and callositye, which agrees and may be likened unto the
callositye or cicatrice of other bones.

Sometimes only the first table of the sculle breaks and rents without
pearcinge or penetratige the Diploe: Diploe is the porositye between the
botables.
Some times allsoe is the fore said diploe crushed plettered, and broken yet
the second table safe and illaese or vnburte, wherefore as then it is not
necessary to detect and make bare or to trepane the membrane Dura Matter, yea
and as then we may perceave the manner as it vveare of a splinter, of the
sculle therein lying erected. The fracture at sometimes alsoe is so great,
with such a huge quantitye of massacred, and crushed bones, which lye half,
and half separated, or else for the most part clean beaten theroute, that we
may playnlye with our eyes behoulde the membrane Dura matter, so that the
blood which drives therone, may very easily flovve out of the vvounde, in
like forte allsoe may the remedies therein easily be applied: To the which
fractures, the trepane is nothige behoovinge or needful: And if so be in any
sort you perceave any skilfers or splinters, by the which that membrane
might be pricked, or crushed, you must take them very easily therout.

It happens alsoe some times, not only in the little children but in aged
poeple, The bones of the yonge Childrene are bet invvardlye.
alsoe in who me the bones are so thin and tender, that with any rude
blovve they are invvardlye bent, (as we see a tin or a copper pot to be
bruised) without the external skin being any where externally hurt: To
the which disease of the bones, we must only take our refuge flight unto our
extractive, dravvinge plasters, vvherbye that the depressede bone, might by
any means be elevated, and heaved again into his right place.

Hippocrates, comprehendethe in very Short words in his blooke De Locis in
homene, The opinione of Hippocrates tou-
the whole cure of the fractures of the sculle demonstratinge those unto us
which we ought to trepane, The opinione of Hippocrates touching the curing
of fractures.
or not. As far forth therefore (says he) as if the bone be broken, or
massacred, there is as the no perrille, or danger at all, with moysteninge
medicamentes must it be cured and helped, which quiet, and take a way the
inflammatione, soften and mollyfye the bone, because that without payne we
might take the broke pieces of bone therout: but insomuch as the bone only
chaucede to be burst, it is then very dangerous, must of necessity be
trepanned, because that the matter which distills through the rent, or
fissure one the membrane, do not in any sort corrupt and rot, the
foresayed subiacent, or subiectede membrane: for as without having any issue
again, she come to sink through this angustnes, and narrovvnes, as then
she causes an ague, and sometimes allsoe distemperatenes of mind: wherefore
we must needs trepane, and make a large apertione because that the sanious
matter may not only have an entrance, but an issue alsoe: when as we do not
in any sort suspect that the membrane Dura matter, sufferethe any thinghe, or
is in any sort crushed, or pricked with any pieces of the broke bones, that
there is no matter run therone, by the which she might be troubled, it is
not the necessary that we trepane, or open the sculle. For what occasion
we ought to trepane.
The Chyrurgiane is constrained to use the trepane for divers occasions, and
take therout the broken bones: First of all to give an issue and passage to the
congealed or not congealed blood, which was sunk on the membrane Dura
matter, through the effluxione of those veins, which are as well situated in
the flesh of the head as betvvixt both the tables, under the sculle, which
as there do restrain that membrane fast unto the sculle. Secundaryly, because
the matter, which continually soacketh through the fissure on the membrane
do not chance to spoil, or inflame the same, because that through such an
anguste passage she can have no issue, the which in the end might be the
cause of the patient his death. Thirdly, to draw forth the broke bones
splinters, through the which the foresayed membrane might in any sort be
pricked or lye crushed:

Fourthlye, according as the work reqvireth, to apply convenient and
necessary remedies into the vvounde: Fifthlye, because it might serve in
steed of a repercussive, defensive ligature of inflammations, which
othervvyse may be used in all other broke parts and ioynctes, The head may
not to strictlye be bovvnde, the reason why.
exceptinge only the head, because that this, considering the rotunditye
rovvndnes there of can not by any means possible therone be effected: for a
ligature, which must be stifflye fast vvoude, rovvnde about the fracture, to
praeserve and keep the broken bones one by the other, might in the head be
the cause of paye, and inflammatione, it would alsoe hinder the agilitye of
the arteryes, and the ascendinghe of the fuliginous excrementes, which
through the futures of the sculle, do evaporate: it would alsoe repelle the
external blood of the vvoude, send it toward the brains, membranes
thereof, out of the which might ensue very bad accidents.

How lunge we ought to tarry, before we begin to trepane, and of the places
which in trepaninge we must choose, or eschew. Chap. 4.
HIppocrates charge the Chyrurgiane, Opinione of Hippocrates.
in his book concerning the vvoundes of the head, when as in the first he
hath bin theruto fetched or sent-for, having together noted, the fracture of
the bone, with the molestinge and troublesome accidents thereof, that without
any longer delay, he shall within the space of three days begin to
trepane, and especially the vvether being hot, thereby to prevent the
inflammatione: but not to the membrane, least she lye bare, and so the
external air chance to distemper her, and cause therine some corruption and
rottinge, least also that in so doing we might chance to tear the membrane,
in boaringe through the sculle which cleaves unto the same, or any of the
other fasteninges of the membrane: or in placinge of the instrument thereon, she
might thereby be hurt or vvouded. Wherefore, (as he says) it is far better,
when as there is but a little more bone to pearce or boar through, the barede
bone begins to stir it self, that as then we desist, until such time
as it of him self falls out. The Chyrurgiane is sometimes to late sent for.
But if so be that the Chyrurgian hath not in the first bin thereat present,
the patient hath of some idiot or ignorant fellow bin dressed, which hath
not know the fracture, and because there was no apertione made in the bone,
to give air or light, unto the matter, there are chanced thereunto (as is a
fore recited) great inflammations, and many other fearful accidents: we
must then (if it be in the summer, and the putrifactione one the sudden
reveal it self) within the seventh day treparte the patient, before the
brains are vvholye infected, and chance to mortify: But in the winter when
as the corruption proceeds and goes somewhat tarder ofsloer forvvarde, we
must then effect it with in 14 days: because these days being passed, as
well considering the imbicillitye of his forces, as the greatness of the
dissease is incurable, although as then we trepane the scull, it is then to
late, wherefore in no vvyse must we effect or do it.

Opinione of Avicenna.
Avicenna will have that we straight ways begin to trepane, and if so be
we must needs defer it, that we should defer it no longer then two or
three days at the most, and that especially when as the Dura matter is
pricked or lies crushed, of any broken bones.

Celsus says, that he which tarrieth any longer from trepaninge then three
days, are not to be excused, because that such delay causes a concursione of
humours, on the Dura matter which finding no apertione, or issue, disorders
the foresayed membrane, and spoils it, out of the which necessarylye must
follow great inflammationes.

But our use and practise in these days, is that as soon as we are sent for,
whether it be in time or out of time, early or late, and we espy, ether
through searching with our, fingers, through the searchinge iron, or by our
sight, that the scull is broken, rent, or torn, and that the Dura matter
suffers any thing, we out of hand proceed with the operatione, and that
so much the sooner, when as we note, that there accidents demonstrate
themselves, without any longer delay and especially, in debile
aegritudinous, or corpulent bodies, because in them there are revealed far
vvorse accidents then in others, wherefore to tarry or defer the trepaninge
any longer it would be small commodity unto us, but better that altogether
we deferred it.

And although the Chyrurgiane vveare not in the first sent for, and that the
seventh day of the summer, and the 14 of the winter vveare praetermitted, and
let pass, he shall not therefore refuse to do his utter most endevoure, with
trepaninge, for it is yet better somewhat to late them never, considering the
good hope which we must have of doing the patient any good, help, and
comfort, which othervvyse without trepaninge of the broken bone, or the
elevatione of the same, can by no means be done, which we only understand
of those, which are not clean without hope.

What places may bear the trepaninge.
Saying therefore that we have the time day limited unto us, in the which we
ougt to trepane, we must consider what places, or parts of the sculle are
able to abide the trepaninge, and which not. We must first of all there fore
consider, that the bones which vvholye are in pieces, or being crushed, or at
the least a great part of the same being separated, can not easily be
trepanned, because he might chance to crushe the trepane one the membranes,
howe easily soever he lean thereon. We must alsoe note, that we do not
chance to set the trepane one any suture, because in so doing, we should
with great payne, and with great blood shedding, cut of the veins,
arteryes, and synnuish filamentes, which have a vnitye and fasteninge, with
the Pericranium, and the membrane Dura matter, We may right well trepane one
the sides of the future.
which have the free passage through the foresayed sutures, to retain the
Dura Matter, and administer life, and nurture unto her. But if it so chanced
that the fracture chanced to be one the suture, we must then apply the
trepane, on both the sides of the suture, with out in the least touching of
her: for if so be we trepane but on the one side only and not at the other
side of the suture it vveare then impossible that the blood or matter, should
have at that hole any issue or passage, the membrane being betvvixt them
both: nether may we trepane on the fontanelle, or opening of the head in
yonge Children, because that there tender imbicillitye as yet is notable to
suffer and abide the trepane. The inferior, or descending parts of the sculle,
are not convenient or fit to be trepanned, because the brains through there
ponderousnes, might chance to sink therout, or the membranes through the
apertione might chance to be extruded: But if so be we vveare urged there
unto, we must make but a very small apertione.

We ought in no vvyse to trepane the temples of the head, We may not trepane
the temples of the head.
because we should not hurt the temporalle muscle, considering divers
synnues, arteryes and veins, which are therein divided and entermingled
through the which there might be caused to great payne, fluxione of blood,
agues, Spasmus, and the patient might chance to dye: Because that there under
the bone called Os petrosum is situated, and that considering, the movinge and
stirring in the temporal muscle, which happens in speaking or eating,
the vvoude might be far more dangerous, and Hippocrates alsoe says, that
the inscisione of the same muscle, might be cause of a great, and villanouse
distortione out of the which one the same side, a Paralisis on the other a
covulsione of synnues might chance to ensue. Nether ought we to trepane that
part of the sculle, a little above the eye brouvves, because in this place
there is a great concavitye, fylle of air, and white slimy, my, humiditye,
of nature, in that place constituted ordained, to paepare the air, which
ascedeth up to the brains, the which indeed is vvorthye to be known
observed, because that the Chyrurgiane, might not in any sort be deceaved,
taking the foresayed concavitye to be, a depressione of the bone, which
needs must be trepanned: if it so chanced, that any of these foresayed parts
vveare broke, as are the temples of the head, we ought then to apply the
trepane, a little above the teporall muscle: We may not trepane that bone
above the eyebrovves.
if so be that the part or portione of the sculle, be broke a little above the
eyebrovves, we must as then make choice of that part of bone, which bounds
on the fracture, as above in the fooreheade: it is right true, that if so be
the foresayed bones, vveare depressed, and crushed, that as the we ought to
elevate them, and if they be clean separated we as then must pluck them out
in like sort as we must do in the sutures.

The sutures and temples of the head may sometimes be trepanned.
Yet considering all this, we are oftetimes compelled constrained, to trepane
in all places of the scull: The which a renoumned, expert Chyrurgiane called
Andreas a cruce, confesses often times to have done, without any danger.
And I dare my self boldly affirm, that I in the years, of 1591 1592 have
my self trepanned, and have seen others trepane, in the foresayed prohibited
places, as one the sutures, and one the temples of the head. Notvvitstandinge
I would council the yonge Chyrurgiane, that in as much as is possible he
avoid eschevve, the trepaninge of these places, but rather make choice of any
other part, which part, may be a little descendinge, because having made
the apertione, the blood, the matter, all impuritye might therout have there
free passage. Considering in the days which go before and which
conseqventlye follovve after the trepaninge, on the singularitye, and
vvorthines of tvose parts, we must command the afflicted and wounded
person, that in all things he vvilbe sober, and observe a good diet, both in
etinge and drinckinge, abstayninge especially from wine, and phlebotomye, as
much as is needful, because that the humours, should not concur unto the
vvouded part, and that alsoe we keep his head warm, with light
coveringes of the head, because could is a great enymye unto the brains all
synuis he parts.

What qvantitye, or what bigness of the bone in trepaninge we must take out
Chap. 5.
WE must in the first, according unto the quantitye, How great the
apertione must be for to trepane.
and bigness of the bone we purpose to take out make an apertioin the skin
denudate the foresayed sculle: Therefore if so be there be no vvounde nor any
apertione, and the skin externallye as yet vnhurte, this shall as the be the
convenientest apertione, which with hands may be made, which we shall make
with two crossevvyse overthvvarte inscisiones, in such a form as this in the
