margin, demonstrates unto you, or else in form of a borghondiane cross,
which in his middle praesenteth four corners. In so much therefore, as if the
hurt, have made a vvounde, and inscisione, in the skin, we must suffice our
selves thervvith, with such as it is, if so be she be thereunto fit and apt,
making an other transversall inscisione, namelye overthvvarte the vvounde,
because these two as then may present one. But if so be, the vvounde be very
ample, large, we must as then only cut the skin one the one side,
begininge the same in the middle of the vvounde, because so the vvounde may
present this letter T, in the which vvilbe but two corners.

These foresayed inscisions, are cenvenientest done for the inflammations. The
inscisione must be done before the inflammatione.
But if so be we perceave the vvounde to be large enough to give place unto
the trepane, or any other instrument, vvhatsoever, wherewith we might
endevoure and seek to elevate, and restore again the broken, or depressed
bones, in so much as if there be any, we must thervvith content our selves,
exstendinge the foresayed vvounde at the first with linte, with little
plumaceoles, thereof being made and therein crushed, on allsydes, corners of the
vvounde.

But in what sort or fashone soever, we make our inscisione in the skin,
we must allvvayes note that we do not suffer any portione of the
Pericranium to remain one the sculle: which under the skin, decks and
covers the whole sculle: because if so be the foresayed membrane
Pericranium, vveare per haps, torn with the teeth of the trepane, might be
the cause of great inflammatione, payne, and agues, where fore it is better
that we clean, and vvholye separate it from the sculle, which being done,
we must then damme stop up the vvounde with white linte, by the which the
next day ensuing we shall find the vvounde wide open, if as yet there vveare
any parcel of the skin, or lip of the wound, which might be a
hinderance unto the trepane, the which in the turning about might chance to
touche, we shall with the scissors clip it of, without deferringe it
until the next day.

When as we having considered, one the place where to set the trepane, we
must then note, how much, howe broad, we ought to boar the sculle. What
qvatitye of bone we ought to trepane.
First of all therefore, we must vvholye take avvaye all the broken and crushed
bones, which vvholye are separated from the sovvnde part, and alsoe from the
Pericranium, considering that they can never thervvith be united joined again.
But when as the broken bone is depressed, any part thereof as yet thereon
vveare fastened, with the sovvnde parts thereof, which crushed the membranes
of the brains, or any acuitye of the same, as yet stick theri, we must not
therefore for all that cut it of, and vvholye take it avvaye. But must by all
means endevoure, easily to lift elevate the same, situate it next unto the
borderinge bones, exemptinge only out of the same the small pieces, which
might hurt the membrane, prick it, because by this curing remedy, the bones
again renevve, unite, and join themselves together with the circumiacent
bones. In so much as if there be any more rentes or fissures before hades,
which fro the one side, disperse themselves this way or that way, we shall
not need to pursue the unto their end, but we shall only take some part
thereof away, because most commonly they join, saulder as it vveare together
again, the which is a far more better opercle for the brains, the that
new incarnated flesh, which after the trepaninge grovveth therein, where
we have taken the whole broken bone therout, wherefore we must take no
bones therout, then with great discretione, then as few as is possible, The
scull is the natural opercle, or cover to the brains.
so that they do not prick, trouble the membrane, with there acuitye edge,
that there remain distance enough, to give passage unto the blood, and the
matter which is therone gatherede, to depart, for as well the membrane as the
brains, shall be better defended, through the bone which as yet they Keep,
which is there natural defece, as if we qvite clean toke it away, through
which discoveringe, the foresayed brains might be hurt or hindered.

The Diploe is the distance betvvene the two tables.
It chances often times that the first table comes to be broken, rent unto
the Diploe, yet for all that the second table remaining untouched, wherefore
it is not the needful to apply the vvhol trepane therone, to boar the bone
qvite out, but in this case we must olye use the exfoliative trepane, When we
ought only to use the exfoliative trepane.
thereby to administer any passage or apertione unto the blood, which, being
sunk betvve the foresayed Diploe, through continuance of time beginning to
corrupt, might in the same time change alter the second table alsoe, and
cause any accidents thereof to ensue. If so be in the vvounde we perceave any
splinter, which exalts it self with some high eminence, we must not be
so curious, to take the same immediately a vvaye, or cut it out seige that it
is any where fastened, but rather commit it unto the work of nature, which
will deminishe separate no more therofe, then shall be necessary need full,
because she is wise and prudent in all her works. It might alsoe chance
that the bone, not being broken or rent hath only bin above contunded,
hurt, or externallye denudatede, which beige so, we must only above scrape
it even, and grate it.

Of the manner methode howe to trepane well, and artificiallye. Chap. 6.
So many and divers species forms of fractures, as there are even so are
there divers means where by we may succour the wounded patient. When as
therefore the fracture, is nothing else, then a right running fissure, we must
then consider whether it pearce or penetrate through both the tables: the
which me may know through the Raspatoriu, or through the exfoliative
trepane, where with we must grate the first table, unto the Diploe, if that
the foresayed fissure, Diploe is the space between the two tables.
as there doth not depart or vanish out of sight, nether the accidents
desist, finding allsoe the fore said Diploe plettered crushed, or broke, any
matter which through the second table comes to distille into this place,
it is as then a sign that the foresayed fracture pierces, unto the second
table, stretches it self one the Dura matter alsoe: Methode howe to trepane.
we are as then counceled to apply the whole trepane therone: ordinarylye to
effect this, as it is needful to be done, we must cause the patient to sit,
one such a manner as the part which is broken reqvireth: we must stop his
ears with cotton, decline and lay his head one a company of pillovvebeares,
which must be indifferent harde, cause his head of one or two men to be held
fast, because he stir it nether this way nor that way: then we must cover
the lips of the vvounde, with any plasters spread vppen fine linen cloth,
least that of the air, of the turning round of the trepane they might be
hurt or vvouded, Al-this being in such sort fineshed, we must situate and
settle, the perforative trepane very fast or steadfastly, on the broke bone, on
such a place where as we desire to have the acuitye or point of the trepane
situated, there to make a perforatione, which being effected, we must therone
situate the whole trepane whereof the poyncte, must be set in the hole or
perforatione, the which before was made with the perforative trepane, and
so easily turning it, the bone first of all shall raceave the poyncte, by
and by the crovvne, or the teeth of the trepane, without ether glidinge this
way or that way, or remove out of there circkle, because of the acuitye or
poyncte of the trepane which must restraygne the trepane, with out slippinge
out of his place. There is a certain industry in the depressione of the
trepane, so that it both turns round, pierces or cutteth alsoe: for if so
be we lean to lightly therone, it then pierces and cutteth little, or
nothige at all: if it so chased that as then we depressed is somewhat to
harde, it will not then turn round, wherefore heerine we must use a medium
betvven them both, or mediocritye, lift it some times out, to purifye, brush
it, and then annoynte it with oil of roses, because that it might the betre
pearce and enter in. The trepane having now made a reasonablve deeped circle,
as then we must take away the point out of the middle thereof, for if it
pierced deeper the the crovvne or teeth of the trepane, he should be sooner
passed by or through the bone then the trepane with his teeth, by the which
the membrane called Dura Matter might chance to be hurt, the foresayed poyncte
therefore being taken therout, we must again set the trepane in his hole, or
circkle, and markinge that the trepane hath pierced the Diploe, Signs to
know when the trepane is entered into the Diploe.
and passed therbye, the which me may perceave by the blood which will issue
therout, through those little veins which there are openede, we must then
finnishe the trepaninge with more discretione and heed taking there unto,
unto the concavitye of the sculle, turning the foresayed trepane very easily
vvyselye, laying the left hand very lightly therone, that therbye we may
the better espy, when that the sculle shall be pierced qvite through, that we
do not in any sort hurt the membrane: because thereof might ensue,
inflammatione, and the danger of death. Note the form or figure of your
trepane in the sculle.
Now to mark this, we must often times lift up the trepane, to try the
thickness of the bone, through the which the trepane hath passed, which we
must with a little privette or searcher try, or with a propre instrument
which here to fore is only for this intent described. By which means, we
must alsoe note, whether it be in no place clean pierced, for although we
rightly aeqvallye turn the trepane, it may never the less happen, that the
foresayed bone be one the one side clean througe, unto the Dura Matter, and one
other side not so deep: when we therefore perceave this, we must proceed
with turning, depress the trepane one the other side where the bone as yet
is not through somewhat more, because the bone may aeqvallye be pierced, or
if it will not so be, we may then with the same turn of the trepane, pearce
the bone one the one side, denudate the Dura Matter one the other side, which
sometimes I have known to be done. Such an in aeqvallitye, comes partly as
well considering the head, which is round, as concerning any concavityes
or furrovves which are situated in the second table, which touches the Dura
matter, wherefore the bone is in one place thicker then in an other.

In like sort we must alsoe plant the little groundedravver in the
perforatione, the which in the first was made with the poyncte of the
trepane: Or we must, stick that Elevatorium which is at the poyncte of the
little foresayed grovvndedravver in that circkle, which by the trepane is
made, thervvith to lift out the little piece of bone, or make it loose, by the
which we shall easily espy, whether it yet hold very fast, whether it
need any more to be board, if we see that it hath pierced unto the Dura
matter, we shall then with the foresayed Elevatorium, After what sort we
ought to lift out the perforated bone.
or with the groundedravvere, vvholye lift it out, with out breaking of it
or doing any violence therone, because there through we do not chance to
hurt the membranes but give it rather one turn or two, because that so much
the easier we might lift it out, at one ti-time. This being done and the
round bone being taken therout, we must then smooth and make plain, the
edges of the perforatione, and grate them, and take away all his acuitye and
ruggednes, from him, for if so be there remain any small splinter therone or
any other vnevennes, the which not being clenlye taken thereof, it might be
agreat hinderance unto the membrane: and if so be that through trepaninge there
chanced any of the poulder of the bone to fall one the membrane, we must
endeavour by all means to get it out: If so be it is sufficient to remove the
first table, without touching of the second, we shall not then plain and
scrape the edges only of the pierced hole, but allsoe the whole bone, because
that thereafter without any trouble to the patient the skin may grow
therover: for if so be that it come to grow over the rugged and vnsmooth
bone it will be a great hinderance to the patient, and cause a nue payne,
because that the flesh will not be so good, vvhervvhithe the rugged bone
shall be covered.

And this is our practise, Manner we to trepane unto the membrane.
and manner of trepaninge unto the membrane Dura matter, and the manner allsoe
how immediately to lift the bone out of the perforatione, although that
Hippocrates, in his book of the vvoundes of the head, strictlye prohibiteth
to boar the hole unto the membrane, and presently to take it therout, because
the external air, suddaynlye alightinge one the same, might chance to hurt
the same, by the which occasion she might thereafter chance to rot: and more
alsoe if that we take the bone out of the same the which as yet might be
fastened with the foresayed membrane, we might chance to tear the same, or
any small veins therone fastened: or if that we stuck the trepane unto the
membrane, we might hurt the same: it is therefore the surest vvaye, says he,
that when as the bone is all most clean through, and begins to stir,
that we then desist, and tarry until such time as it of it self falls
out: But our trepane with the crovvne, or teeth is such, that unless the
Chyrurgiane be very ignorant, might in any sort thervvith, hurt or crushe
down the membrane.

Nue inveted trepanes.
We have inveted other forms of trepanes, which we have in this book also
set down there form which we cal Terrebellum alatu, the vvinged trepane,
which takes no pieces of bone with it but diminishes, and consumes them,
wherewith by no means we may hurt the membrane: there are some which
having used them, find them far surer, and expediter in there operationes,
then those with the hood. But if so be there be any great massacringe of
bones, or depressinge of the same, the the membrane is there through depressed
and crushed, and is alsoe sometimes pricked with the splinters, of the broken
bone. In these two dangerous matters, we must help, and succour the
patient or wounded person one some other sort, that as soon as it may be
possible, in the extractinge of the same, if so be they be vvholye separated:
What we ought to do when as the Dura matter is crushed through the broke
bones or splinters.
To which purpose, it is oftentimes necessary to trepane, cut away some
part of the sovvnde bone, which borders and is situated next unto the
plettered bones, because that our Elevatorium, might in the elevating of that
which is depressed dovvnvvardes have sufficient place, rest it self one the
sovvnde bone, with out in the elevatione to depress the brokene bones any
more dovvnevvardes: for as Hippocrates says, the bones which are broken, and
depressede, can not without great danger be board or perfotated, because
that the depressinge of the trepane, or Elevatorij, by any means of them can
not be suffered. It often times happens that the second table is more
depressed the the first, wherefore to that intent we must let the Elevatorium
pass through the perforatione of the trepane, between the second table,
Elevatorium is an instrument wherewith we use to lift up any thing out of a
wound.
and the membrane Dura matter, because in that sort we might lift up the broken
bones and splinters again, and take them out if they lay loose or separated.
And if so be there vveare no hole, above all this, the place of the fracture,
could by no means suffer, to have therein made a hole, it is as then my manner
to take my grovvndedravvere with three feet or poynctes and set therone,
Practise invention of the author.
then make choice, of the greatest, which is most fittest for me, considering
the fracture which is in the bone, to intrude the same therein, and easily
wind the same therein, houldinge your hand always hanging, and not
depress the same to harde, because he will easily enough enter therein, when
he hath a little pierced we must as then with a certayntye, the easyest way
possible endevoure, to lift up the broken and crushed pieces of bones. But if
so be, there be any piece of bone so far shoved under the sculle, that it
lay above the membrane, and the same considering his greatness, and the
angustnes of the apertione, could not by any means be taken therout, ether
by Elevatories, or Pincets, we must the take our refuge, (if so be we will
not through the trepane make the apertione any bigger) to the cuttinge
pincers, to the Parrates bill, with the which we may cut of as much bone
as we please, with out any pains, or danger, making one this sort the
apertione somewhat vvijder, to take out of the same the foresayed piece of
bone, which drives one the Dura matter. Touching the inflectione or bending
invvardes, without fracture, which commonly chances in yonge children, or
in these which have a weak tender sculle, when this is crushed, or bendt
invvardes, as is a copper or time pot, those are better to be cured with
extractige plasters, then with the trepane or with the grovvndedravvere.

The French Chirurgerye THE THIRD TREATISE OF THE OPERATIon of Chyrurgerye,
wherein is discoursede and handelede of the sovvinge or suture of vvoundes.
Containing six Chapters.
What the suture, or sovvinge together of avvounde is and the use thereof.
Chap. 1. VVherone we must note in the sovvinge of a vvounde. Chap. 2. What is
needfulle, to the sovvinge, and of the means, how to do the same. Chap. 3.
Of the species, or differences of sovvinge, and of the time to remove the same.
Chap. 4. How we ought to repose again the guts, with the net, vvhenas
they hang out of the body. Chap. 5. Of the Gastroraphia, or sovvinge of the
bellye- Chap. 6.
What sowing is, and the use thereof, and in what impediments she is
necessary, and in what parts. Chap. 1.
Six things which in sovvinge must be considered
THe Chyrurgiane ought to consider six especialle things, which concern the
sovvinge of vvoundes: First the use thereof, that is, in what impedimets it is
necessary, in what parts: what we must therein consider: what there is
vvantinge to effect the foresayed sovvinge: after what manner we ought to
do it, howe many fashions, differences there are of the same. Descriptione of
sovvinge.
Therefore sovvinge of a vvounde is nothing else, but a vnitinge, and couplinge
together of the dissevered parts: which vveare contrary to nature, separated
and parted one fro the other, which fore said vnitinge must be effected
vvithe a threded needle.

Invention use of sovvinge.
The occasion, why we in any vvounde, or separated part, use this sovvinge,
is to unite again, join them together, whereof the convenientest means is,
this sovvinge, the use of this combinatione, and that especially in all such
parts, which we can not conveniently oraptlye bind together, even as we
may playnlye see, in the great vvoundes of the arms, legs, which are
overthvvartlye wounded, in like manner alsoe in the body vvvich happen in the
length thereof, for the lips or edges of the same, are so much separated one
from the other and causes the vvoude so to gape, that she could not
without great danger be cured, unless that in any place we sovved it, to
bring them together and vnition: because that all incarnate, or fleshye parts
of the body, are drawn through with certain sinnuishe fibers or
filametes, the which being overthvvartlye, or contradictorylye separated the
one, from the other, the one lip of the vvoude is dravven opvvarde, the other
dovvnevvard or the one on the right side, the other one the left, all
according as the vvoude or the separatione is more or less, overthvvarte,
cotradictorye, loge, deep, or vndeepe. In like sort is the sovvinge, very
necessary in a vvoude, wherein a piece or parcel of flesh hangs one the
one side, and one the other end as yet connexed, even as it commonly chances
most commonly, in a great hevve or slash, through the which the ear,
hangs by the head, or in any other part, as in the nose, the which held
fast but at one end only.

It happens alsoe some times, Sovvinge in a separated place, is unprofitable
and needeles.
that the whole part is clean cut of, wherein the sovvinge is nothing
profitable, wherefore therein we must not use any sovvinge at all, to cure the
again, for then in the separated part is no more life, therefore can no more
be nourished of the body, by the which the curing ought to come.

And although that all those parts, which against the course of nature are
separated fro the other, might behove to be healed again, ca not bear or
suffer to be united or coalited, even as are the synnues, Tendones, Cartilages
or grissells, because after the opinione of ancient professors, and as alsoe
the use and practise teaches us, there must consequently follovve of one
prick, or thrust with a nedle, in the synnues, or tendones, great payne of
all sorts, inflamatione, convulsione of synnues, and some times alsoe death it
self, through the sympathy, or compassione, which they have with there
first originalle, the which Galenus, hath showed unto us, in a vvoude,
belovve the Hockes in the which, The tedones are dangerous to sow and why.
considering the greatness thereof, it was very needful that there should
be a profound suture, that not only to bring the supersituated places,
together, but alsoe the profoude lying parts of the vvounde: the which he
endevourige to effect, hath separated the tedones fro the muscles: for because
there is great danger consists in the hurtinge of the synnues, even in like
sort is there danger in hurtinge of the tendones, that considering the
synnuishe fibers wherewith the muscles of the belly are connectede, which in
time change themselves into a tendone, which we commonly call the great
synnue.

What we ought to consider in the suture, and so winge of a wound. Chap. 2.
Why we may not in the resovvinge of a vvoude draw the lips or edges close
together.
WE must not at any time sow up a vvounde, least in the first she be well
purifyed, cleansed, as well externallye, as internallye, if at the least with
out any great danger or peril it may be effected: extractinge and taking
therout all that is contrary unto the said vvounde, as is congealed blood,
which coming to corruption, might cause great inflamatione, oft times
alsoe a convulsione in that part, through which the sovvinge breaks,
tears, so conseqventlye hinderethe the vnitinge healinge of the vvoude:
wherefore in the resovvinge of a vvoude, we must deligentlye note, that we
do not contract draw together on all sides) the lips or edges of the
forsayed vvounde (except it vveare in the haremouth, in the cleaved or severed
lips of the mouth) but must leave some distance between the one the other
lip or in any place some or other apertione, because the matter which
gathers it self together, in the bottom of the vvounde, may issue therout,
and by that means the medicamentes may be therone applied. In like sort the
Chirurgiane must in sovvinge consider, use medicoritye, and not take to great a
qvantitye, ether in the length, or in the depth of the flesh, and of the
skine, through the which there might ensue and follovve great payne, which
we ought not to esteem for small, thereof allsoe would remain an
illfavored vnseemlye cicatrice or scarre: As to the contrary the not deep
sovvinge of the vvounde, might easily break and burst again, then in vain
it vveare sovvede. For if so be, that the needle, be to near thrust through
the edges of the vvoude, the the thread because of his tenuitye tears
through the skin or flesh, when as we thrust the needle to deep and
far, from the lips or edges of the vvounde, then there rests and
remains a great part of the skin, Flesh with Flesh comes far
sonner to unite, the skin with skin.
which will not be united: wherefore indifferentlye we ought to pearce
together as much of the skin as of the flesh, because the flesh far sooner
and easier comes to heal and cure, with the flesh then the skin with
the skin, and that is considering the natural temperatenes of the foresayed
flesh, which is warm, and moyste. And as touching the distance of the
stitches, and soovvinge, we ought not to lay them to close one by the other,
nether to far separate sequestered one fro the other, as the they can not
retain the vvounde: And when all to ny the other they are laid, cause
payne, through all the stitches, and dravvinge together of the same, through
the which payne is caused a concursione of Humours unto that place: we must
therefore use a mean therine well considering, the largeness, and length of
the vvounde: but above all we must mark, that we thrust not, through or in
any synnues or tendones, considering the payne, the Spasmus and many other
fearful accidents, which thereof might ensue, as allreadye we have said.
Sovving of a vvounde may not be effected with any violence, in those parts
which we endevoure to unite and bring together, but the is sovvinge
laudable, when as the skin stretches it almost together, of it own
accorde, whereas in this sort it will not be effected or brought to pass
easily, that the lips of the wound with violence must be joined, it is as
then most convenient, that we relay the stitches some distace one fro the
other, lettinge them be somewhat looslye sovved: for if it chanced we drevve
them somewhat stiffly together, the skin might then easily rent or tear,
so burst a sunder through the tumefactione which thereof ensues: If that
therefore, Sovvinge may not by any violence be done in the lips of the
vvounde.
we disire perfectly to cure a vvounde, we ought as the so to dispose of
the sovvinge, because that in thus doing, the humours, blood, matter, and the
external air might be repelled, from the lips, or edges of the vvoude,
because such things, may hinder, or at the least defer, the curing of the
same: and in so much, as the lips of the vvounde are not well united the
one with the other, and kept in that stature, vnitinge, the wound can verve
badlye hardly be cured.

And alsoe if the lips of the vvounde, We may in no wise sow the lips
of an inflamed vvounde.
do greatly chance to svvell, inflame, they shrinck in themselves, or else
if they be to much bruised or plettered, we must not the in any sort resovve
it again: for the suture would straight vvaye break, and so consequently
the inflammatione increased, but must delay it so lunge until the foresayed
inflammatione be seaced the vvounde be come to good suppuratione and matter,
and is prepared and ready, to be united brought together.

What is necessary unto the sowing of a wound, and howe we ought
convenientlye to do it Chap. 3.
WHen as the Chyrurgiane desires aptly convenientlye to sow a vvounde, he
must first of all have in his hand a needle, thereto a thread, and a canulle:
Cocerninge the needle, she must be of a reasonable length, sometimes right,
sometimes croocked, according as the parts reqvire, nether must she be made
of to harde a steel or mettle, where of they easily might chance to break,
but of gentle steel, because they might rather bend then break: although
she ought to be stiff, smooth, infrangible, havige a sharpe triangled
poyncte, because that in her entringe she might both cut prick, so the easier
pierce or go there through, because that in her govvinge through, she might
make a lunge little wound or hole, not round, because those little rovvndes,
are more difficult to be cured, What form the needle must have.
then the lunge holes. In the head she must be one both sides hollovve, in
form of a gutture, because the thread might therinne be hidden buried, not
hinder the percinge or entrance of the needle, in the dravvige through thereof,
because she then touches the body in that sort more easier and softer: It
will not alsoe hinder according to the sayige of Avicenna, that we somewhat
anoint the needle with oil, vvherbye the payne of the prick night be
somewhat easyed, the foresayed needle enter the easier.

What form the thread must have.
The thread must be smooth, soft rovvnde, and without any knott, all
eqvivalent with the thickness, greatness of the needle, but not in any sort to
thin, least he chase to break or cut through the lips of the vvoude. We
may rather take a thread, the silk, because the knott of the silk easily
vnlooseth, through his vveaknes: it is right true that the foresayed thread,
must not be to harde, because there through he might hurt, the vvoude, not to
soft least he break nor chance to corrupt, before the time the vvounde be
fully healed, vvherthrough the lips of the vvoud, might again dissolve
vvaxe loose yet notvvithstadinge now a days we had rather use a thread of
carmosyne silk, the a hempen thread, or of flax, which Galenus disprayseth
mislikes, in his third book of methode, where he best likes of a thread of
vvollen which may be likened unto, a brovvne thread, or a vvhyte silk thread
ravve, without being dyed, or coloured: Why the thread may not be dyed or
coloured.
because that in the dying sometimes is entermingled poysen, as in the scarlet
dye there comethe Arsenicu. The fore said Galenus uses strings made of small
guts, as are lutestringes, but cosideringe there moisture, they quickly
begin to svvell, rot, wherefore we had rather use a strong brovvne
thread, which we must draw through vvaxe vvaxe it because so much the
less he might corrupt rot, houlde the faster. And to effect this surely to
sovvea vvoude we must have a Canulle which one the one end must be roude,
split, with a hole, partly because the edge or lipp of the vvoude, which
with the needle we would pearce, because that the fore said lip may
stay, rest it self, on the pipe, without stirring this way or that way,
through the whole to espy, when the needle is half pierced through as then
to draw her through with her thread, without ether the nedle, or the thread
to draw the lips of the vvounde toward, them, because through the split
of the pipe, it might the the easier fro thence be removed, to rest thero the
other edge thereafter, when she allsoe must be thrust through. And
convenientlye to effect this sovvinge of a vvounde, How to sow a vvounde
convenietlye.
we must first thrust through the upper lip of the same, situatinge at the
first the holed end of the pipe with the left hand, internallye within the
lip of the foresayed vvounde, because she may rest therone, and not stir
this way or that way, then with the right hand we must thrust the needle
through the externalle part of the lip, invvardes, then set the Canulle on
the external part, of the nether lip of the vvounde, thrust the needle
internallye outvvardes, of the foresayed lip, gently draw through, the
thread, because as then we may couple join again the lip together: if it
be needful to lay more stitches, we must lay them as is a fore said: some me
hold the lips with there fingers in steed of a Canulle.

The kinds and differences of sowing, or sutures, the the time, and the
methode, or rule to take the away. Chap. 4.
THe aunciet Chyrurgianes have invented fovvnde out divers sundry ways of
sovvinge of a vvounde, Divers forms of sovvinge, accordige unto the diversitye
of the vvounde.
cosideringe, markinge one the vvouded part, nature or qvallytye of the
disseases: for the vvoundes of the arms, or legs, as in those places we may
better with more convenience use the dry suture: the vvoudes of the belly, are
alsoe on an other fashone sevved, then the vvoundes of the guts. The
profound deep vvoundes are sovved, on an other fashion, the the not deep
vvoudes: Alsoe are the debile imbicille persos sovved one an other fasho, as
are vvoeme, the those which are robuste strong, by great pains taking and
labour have a harde skine, there in to eschevve fly the deformitye which after
the sovvinge vvilbe seen.

All ancient professors have used, Three sorts of sovvinge by the ancient
professors.
have had in estee, three sorts of sovvinge of vvoundes, as naemelye, the
incarnative, the blood stoppinge, the conservative sovvinge. The incarnative
suture is so called because that through her vnitinge, she joins together
the separated parts, Incarnative suture, her use five manner of ways.
if so be without violence covenietlye they may be brought together: we use
this manner of suture in all fresh vvoudes, or in these, which are renewed. And
this sovvige is effected after five manner of ways. Where of the first is
called, Incarnative or knotted sovvinge.
the knotted suture or sovvinge, because that in evetye stitch, which pierces
through both the lips of the vvoud, is every time cut of, with both the
ends of the thread above the vvounde is knit together: betvvixt the which
stitching, we lay as yet other. This suture is done with a vvaxed thread, as
is all ready said, noting that both the lips must aeqvallye be joined the
one with the other, with out that ether the one or the other, yield it self
more this way the that way, placinge the first stich in the middle of the
vvoude, with a perforation of both the lips of the vvoud, by the which the
thread being passed, shall one the one side of the lip be dravven together,
above dobblelye knit to gether, cuttinge both ends of the thread short of,
because they should be no hinderace, unto the remydyes which we shall
apply: The which in the renuinge of the dressinge might be the occasion of
payne, or brekinge of the stitches, when as with the plasters we chanced to
pluck them. If the vvoude he great, we must as yet lay more stitches, in the
middle of the Spaciu, one both ends of the vvounde, so proceeding forvvardes
until the whole vvounde be laid with stitches or sutures, everye stich a
fingers breadth the one fro the other, because at the least the lips of the
vvoude might be brought close together, considering, duelye perpendinge the
general observatione above metioned, especially that the stitches be not
laid the one to near to the other, or the one to vvyde fro the other because
as heer tofore we have said, through the great multitude, and through so
many pricks, they bring are cavse of vehement payne: cotraryly they lying to
fare the one fro the other, the vvoude as the being not sufficiently
occluded, or shut up, do little profit, comoditye, therefore they may not be
laid to close by on another, nether to far separate the on fro the other,
they alsoe which are laid to looselye, suffer the vvoude to gape to vvyde,
they which to fast, strictlye draw together the vvounde, cause
inflammations, copell the vvoude burst open, wherefore heerine we ought te
observe a mediocritye. The second incarnative suture.


The second incarnative, or flesh making suture, is done with one, or with
more needles, as if so be the vvounde be great, deep, as the we begin to
stick thrust the needle in the lip or edge of the vvoude, without
dravvinge of the foresayed needle qvite through, although she be threded, but
must suffer her to continue therine, and wind the thread about her, in form
of an S as the Tailors do, when as they are minded safely to keep there
threeded needle, any where one there apparel, as you may see in the figure or
form of the haremouth: so put as many needles therein as shall be needful,
according to the greatness of the vvounde, wind the thread there about, and
let them continue therein until such time as the vvoude shall be healed joined
again. This sovvinge or suture is used, in such vvoundes as stand vvyde open,
in those in the which the lips are separated the one sovvhat vvydlye fro the
other, they which with the common stitchinge might not be contained, or Kept
together.

The third incarnative sovvinge, is called, The third incarnative suture.
the penned stitchinge, because most commolye it is done with little quills,
or shaftes of a pen, not that the foresaved qvilles, do effect the
sovvinge, but because they contain it, alsoe are a let hinderace that the
thread doth not cut the lips of the vvoude clean through, which if with
al expeditione with the least payne we desire to make the we must do as
heerafter follovvethe. We shall first of all take a strong double thread
which is separated in two, at the end having a knot, which with the
needle we must thrust through both the lips or edges of the vvounde,
redoinge the same so often, in divers places, as the greatness of the vvoude
shall reqvire, allvvayes remebringe that everye stitch must be a fingers
bredthe the one fro the other situated laid, or therabout.

This being in this sort finished we must as then one that side of the wound
where the knott lies of the thread, between everye dubblethrede stick a
little shaft or qville, or else a little stick of vvoode, which is vvounde in
linen clothe, must be as lunge in the vvounde it self: which aftervvardes
with the thread, we must draw close unto the lip of the vvounde: and one
the other lip of the vvoude, we must through that same dubblethrede, in like
sort alsoe put a qville, or a little stick, wound about with linne which
in the first must be dravvn with one of the foresayed thredes, and with a
double knott be knitted together, bringing by this means the lips of the
vvounde as close together as is possible, in this vvyse proceeding with
dravvinge together and knittinge, all the thredes which there are, in such
sort as the first was dravven together knit. Such sovvinge, is used, in
great, deep vvoudes in the which we fear least the common sovvinge or
stitchinge might chance to break, cut through the edges of the vvounde.

The fourth is called the dry stich, or sovvinge, The fourth incarnative
suture.
because this may be done without pearcinge of the skin or flesh: which
we are vvonte to do with two strong pieces of linen cloth, which are cut
of the side or edge of the clothe, and which in the ends are cut sharpe or
picked, as here before in the figure we may see, on the which other end we
must spread a very dravvige fast houldinge plaster, which qvicklye may be
dried, which must thus be praepared, we shall apply this same one both sides
of the wound, a little higher then the foresayed vvounde, in such sort that
both the sharpe ends of the pieces of cloth, may be situated close unto the
edges of the vvounde. This being cleaved very fast to the skin, we must as
then sow both there ends close together, without touching of the skin,
and draw them together with the thread, through the which means, we may
compact the lips of the vvounde as close together, os we please. Such a
manner of stitchinge is very vvillingelye used in the vvoundes of the face
vvheas we desire to have no great cicatrice left therein.

The fifth kind of sovvinge is no more in use.
The fifth incarnative sovvinge or stitchinge, is effected with iron hoockes,
which one both there ends vveare crooked, with the which we must hold
both the lips of the vvounde, bringing the foresayed lips close together:
but because they cotinuallye prick are never without causing of payne,
moreover might chance to stick in any membrane, which might suscitate cause
payne, concurringe of humours, inflammatione, in the vvouded part, it is now
a days no more in use. The second general suture
The second suture is, the bloode-stoppinge suture, so called, because she
stencheth stops blood, and hinders that the air can not enter into the
vvoude, when as in manner of revolutione we turn the needle, (as the
furriers are vvote to do, when as they sow together the skins, lay the
stiches, reasonable close, harde, on by the other. Some use this suture, or
stitchinge, when as the great veins or arteryes, are hurt or cut of,
cosideringe the great shedding of blood, through the which they are
compelled to do so, so the more easier to join bring together and shut
the lips of the vvounde.

But this stitchinge, is nothing to certain, because the one stitchbrekinghe,
all the other break go loose, moreover the blood which is therein contained
causes that part to svvell, and leases as it vveare himself between the
muscles, which alsoe thereafter by cotinuance of time corrupt are mortified.
VVherfor I would council the yonge Chirurgiane, that he should rather tie or
bind the mouth of the vain or arterye, or with a Cauterium cauterize it
together, the to use such a stitchinge, which is far covenienter, in the
vvoundes of the small guts, or entralles, of the blather, or in the vvoudes
of the stomach to be effected, if at the least in the two last it may any
way possible be brought to pass.

The third sovvinge, The third Stitchinge.
or stitchinge we call the conservative, or the praeservinge suture, because
she praeserveth, Keeps, the lips op the vvounde which are very vvyde
separated the one frome the other, or else because they are plettered, crushede,
or else because there is any part of them lost, through the which they ca not
by any means be brought joined again, the one with the other, reqviringe
only to be defended, maintained in that estate, till such time as the vvounde
be come to a good suppuratione incarnatione: where through the vvoude, is so
much the the sooner cured cicatrised, without any great, or deformed
cicatrice, remaining in the place. This stitchinge is alsoe used when we
suspect, that there is any thing cotrarye to nature in the vvoude, which we
vvillinglye would have therout, wherefore we do not bring the lips of the
vvounde so close together, as in other sutures, contentinge our selves, that in
such sort we may continue it.

Touching the time, when we ought to cut a sunder the stitchinges, there is
no certayntye thereof, because in some bodies, the vvoudes are sooner cured,
then in others, alsoe the one part heals sooner then the other, wherefore
we ought not to cut loose the stitches, before the vvounde be so far come,
that she hath no more need of the stitches, which cuttinge, must alsoe be
done one divers manners, as the stitchinges are divers: for the knotted
stitchinge, must be done, by cuttinge everye stitch apart, right at the
knott, liftinge up the thread with the privette or searchinge iron, having in
this sort cut of the knott of everye stitch, we shall as then with the
pincet or pinsinge iron draw them out by there knot laying the one finger
one the stitch, to hold that fast that the one lip of the vvoude, be not
draw out vvardes. Sovving of haremouthe.
That stitchinge which happens to be done with one or more needles, as in
the haremouthes, in these we cut a sunder the whole thread, which is vvoude
roude about the needle, the gentlelye takige the same avvaye, thereafter the
needle alsoe. The pened suture, The penned suture.
is take away through the discidinge or cuttinge avvaye, as is above said,
that we ought to cut everye stitch above the knot, and there gently draw
forth the thredes therout. The dry stitch is taken away, when as ether with
water or with any oil we moysten the same. The furriers fashoned suture.
And the suture of the furriers fashone, is very difficult to be taken
avvaye: in this stitchinge, we must first of all begin to cut, the first
stiche, and so proceeding forevvardes, with the scissors, or shears, unto
the end, if at the least it be to be done, and then easily and gentlelye take
every stitch out apart, allvvayes houldinge fast the lips of the vvoundes
with pour finger, least that they in any sort lift up them selves because if
it so chased we could not easily resovve them again.

How we ought to thrust in again the guts, and the net, which is sunk out
of the belly. Chap. 5.
Gastroraphia or belly suture.
IN the great vvoundes of the belly, which enter into the concavitye of the
same, therein is used an other manner of sovvinge, which the ancient professors
call Gastroraphia, that is as much to say, as the belly suture or sovvinge.
But because in such vvoudes, most commonly the guts the net come to sink
out, it is therefore first of all necessary, that we hadle of the means,
through the which we might again restore in to there former place the
guts, or the net, for othervvyse can not the sovvinge be effected, or
brought to pass.

The small guts being hurt, clean cut a sunder, can not by any means
becured.
When as therefore it chances, that the guts are sunk out of the belly,
we must then first of all dillygentlye consider, whether in no place they
are wounded, or hurt, secondly, whether as yet they retain the natural
colour, for if so be the small guts are clean cut of, especially that
empty one, which we call Ieiunum, it is then impossible that he may be
healed again, because of a great company, and divers great veins, which
are therein, allsoe considering his subtyle and synuishe tunicle or koate, and
because he continually receaveth the Cholera, and is situated more nearer the
liver, then any other. But the great guts being hurt, we may rightvvell
sow them, yet not without a steadfast hope that we are able to cure them,
although in respect of the small guts, by the great ones, which are
hurt, it is allvvayes better to have a doubtful confidence then a certain
despair, as much as belongs unto there curing. If so be any of the be
black, or pale, (which is a certain sign they have lost there feeling)
there is nothing to be done unto them, or at the least very little. But if
so be as yet they retain the vitalle colour, we must by all means
endevoure to bring them in again, and that with all possible expeditione,
because the external air, in the which they are not vvote to be, doth
straight ways change and spoil the. And first of all if they be wounded,
we must stitch the with the furriers suture, which above we have described,
when they are sovved, bring them into there natural place or situatio,
dilligently noting that we do not let the end of the thread hang in the
vvoude, because when as they are cured, we may draw the thread there out,
not let it sink into the belly, for the which occasion he ought be
reasonable lunge, without cuttige of him from the sovvige.

But when as we will begin or take such a thing in hand, After what
manner we must set the patient
we must as then convenietlye set, or lay the patient: even as if the vvounde
vveare in the bottom of his belly, we then lay the patient one his back,
with his thighs his buttocks aloft: if so be the vvounde vveare in the
upper part of his belly, the patient must lye one his back highly situated
all most sittinghe, because that the vvouded parts may suspend hang in the
belly: if so be the vvoude be in the right side, we must the situat the
patient one his left side, and if in the left side he be wounded, he must then
lye one his right side: when as the vvoude is so narrovve, so small, that
the svvolle gut, which hageth out, cannot again be put therein, it is then
necessary, ether that by art we drive away, consume the vvyndes which
cause the tumefactione and svvellinge, or that we dilate the vvounde: but it
is allvvayes best that we try first of all to repell the svvellinghe of the
guts, which must be effected with some resolvinge and strengtheninge
medicamentes, What we ought te do when the descended and svvollen gut can not
be put in again or restored into his place.
amongst the which must be entermingled some mollifyinge and softeninge
medicamentes: as when the guts are to dry, we must then bath the, in
warm water, in the which must be done a little quantitye of oil of roses,
or else with some gross black vvyne, because it strengthens and vvarmeth
the guts more then water: if so be the guts with these remedies, will
not as yet relaye there svvellinge, tumefactione, it was then the use of Mr
Pare, chief Chyrurgiane to the king, in divers places to prick them
through, with a needle, through the which the vvyndes as then depart.
Having thus finished all this, if so be there be such a quantitye of guts
sunk out of the belly, that they can not in any sort be put in again at
the same entrace, or vvoude, because she is to little, we must as then dilate
it with a crooked Lacet, which cutteth but one the on side, dilligetlye
notige that in any sort we do not hurt the entralls, which being finished,
a coveniet person thereunto shall with his fingers, hold a sunder the edges
of the vvoude, the Chirurgiane impose foresayed guts again into there places,
first of all intrudinge of those which vveare last, in the coming or
sinckinge out, exactlye noting, The entralls must everye one be brought into
his natural statione.
that everye circumvolutione of the guts, or everye revolutione of the same,
may obtain there own place, intrudinge the same therein with the fingers, and
one such a manner that the one may follovve the other: othervvyse that which
is allreadye thrust in, the finger being taken avvaye, might chance retire
and come forth again, unless that with any other finger we retained the
same, vvhylest that the other finger is finishing his operatione or work,
with bringing an other portione or part, into his place, and using this
manner,they may easily be reduced into there former places. Which being done,
(imposing or laying the hand one the vvounde) we must as then a little
stir and jog the body of the patient, where through the entralles, will
situate, settle, or place them selves everye one in his natural propre place,
even as they vveare, before they vveare removed out of the same. After what
sort we ought to put in the net.
If the net, issues out of the vvounde, and the same be not hurt, or without
any vvounde, and as yet sovvnde, we must genttlelye cause it to sink one
the guts, but if so be any part there of or portione is become black or
leadishe coloured, we must as the a little above the blackenes bind it, to
praevent the bleedinge, and cut it of under the foresayed succincture, and
immediately reduce that into the belly again which is not as yet black, is
yet soud, laying the end of the thread hanging out of the vvounde, because
thereafter we might easily extract and draw it forth, when as it shall be
thereof loosened, and the vvounde be come te good suppuratione and voydinge of
matter.

Of the stitchinge of the belly which we call, Gastroraphia Chapter. 6.
The sovvinge of the belly must not be done as the other sutures.
THe guts or entralles, and the net, being brought into there several
places, we must as the sow, stitch up the vvounde: but because such suture,
according unto the opinions sayings of divers, may not be done in that sort,
as other stitchinges, or sovvinges, to wit the ioyninge together of that,
which is of one nature and dispositione, the one with the other, as the
Peritoneum, with the Peritoneo, ( so forth of the skin and the muscles) but
we are vvilled and councelled that we join and stitch the Peritoneum, which
is a membrane, with the muscles, and with the skin Epigastri, because the
same being very synuishe, will very difficultlye be cured or healed, with
the other part Peritonei, which oppositelye is situated against it which is
allsoe of the same synnuishe substance. In fine we are councelled, to sow up
the edges of the vvoude which is in the right side Peritonei, with the
muscles, which are in the left lip of the vvounde, so that one this manner
the musculous flesh, of the right lip Peritonei, which heals with the
left lip of the foresayed Peritoneum, with the musculous flesh one the one
side, and the muscvlous flesh with the Peritoneo on the other side. Because
that Peritoneum, with the Peritoneo, can not be healed, vvherthroughe the
vvounde only above in the fleshye part might chance to heal, out of the
which might ensue a tumefactione, as if it vveare a roushing, or issuinge
forth of the navel, considering the foresayed Peritoneum, which through
erectione heavinge up of the guts, could not in any sort conjoin together:
and such a meaning must be done one this manner. The vvounde being great,
we must have a convenient minister or servat, which must with his hand one
the exterior part of the vvoude depress it down, because as then she may
be shut closed, nether the guts, nor the net, roush therout suffering only
a small portione of the vvoude vncovered for the Chyrurgian, who with his
threded needle, must begine his first stitch in the end of the one lip of the
vvounde, thrustinge through the external skin, and the musculous flesh of
the vvounde, without touching the Peritoneum, which is there under situated,
dravvinge the needle fro outvvardes, invvardes with her thread, thrust the
second stitch, clean through the whole lip of the vvounde, which
oppositelye is situated against the first stiche, to wit, the Peritoneum,
the muscles and the skin, first bringing the stitch clean through the
Peritoneum, and dravvinge the foresayed needle, from invvarded, outvvardes: And
the same being draw out, with her thread, he must then lay his third
stitch, as he did the first, beginning the stitch in the skin, in the
musculous flesh, of the first lip, without touching the musculous flesh,
of the first lip, without touching the Peritoneum, dravvinge the needle
from out vvardes, invvardes, the he must bring his fourth stitch, as he hath
done the second, thrustinge clean through the Peritoneum, all the musculous
flesh, the skin, dravvinge out the needle from invvardes, outvvardes, in
this sort he must proceed, and go forvvardes, until the whole vvounde from
end to end be clean sovved up, thrustinge through on the one side, only the
skin the musculous flesh, and one the other side the Peritoneum, the
musculous flesh, the skin: because the sovvinge of the Peritoneum only,
with the other part Peritonei, is not sufficient, nether the suture of the
flesh only, but this suture must as well be done in the one as in the other,
endevouringe allvvayes to unite the Peritoneum with the musculous flesh.

An other fashion of sovvinge of the belly, according to some mes sayings.
Some there are which effect this suture on this manner, follovvige, being of
opinione, that those parts, which are of one nature property, very easily
unite them selves the one, with the other, as the skin with the skin, the
flesh with the flesh, and the membranes with the membranes, wherefore they
bring there first stitch, quite, and clean through the skin and the
musculous flesh, from the first lipp, without touching the Peritoneum,
which is thervnder situated, dravvinge there needle from outvvardes,
invvardes, with her thread, laying there second stitch, in the lipp right
opposite against that, thrustinge then only through the Peritoneum, through
a little flesh, dravvinge the needle, from invvardes, outvvardes, the they
bring there third stitch, in the first lip of the vvounde, thrustinge
through the Peritoneum, a little flesh, without ether thrustinge through the
muscles, or the skin, dravvinge there needle from invvardes, outvvardes, then
they bring there fourth stitch in the opposite lying lip, thrust through
the skin, and through the musculous flesh, without touching the
Peritoneum, and in that sort proceed forevvardes, until the whole vvounde be
sovved, so that through one edge of the lip, but yet at divers times they
thrust through the skin and the musous flesh, and allsoe the Peritoneum.

The manner of sovvinge of the belly out of Celsus-
Among all other sorts of sovvinge the vvoundes of the belly, or stitchinge
of them this which we have taken out of Celsus is the least dangerous and
the best alsoe, easyest to be done. That we must have two threded needles, By
the left lip we must not onderstade the left side of the patient for it is
the right side of the vvounde, but rather the left side of the Chyrurgiane.
with one thread, the one needle at the one end, and the other needle at the
other end of the thread, where of we must take the one needle in the right,
the other in the left hand, we must begin in the end, of the superior part
of the vvounde one the left side, first of all pearcinge through the
Peritoneum, then the musculouse flesh, and the skin, dravvinge the needle
with the thread out, unto the one half, from invvardes, outvvardes, then we
must with the other needle of the left hand, make the second stitch, right
opposite against the first, in the right side of the vvounde, beginning with
Peritoneo, as we have said of the first stitch, on this sort the acuitye or
poyncte of the needle is far enough fro the entralles or guts, yet
notvvithstandinge the head close unto the forasayed guts: When as therefore
the needles have pierced passed through from the one side to the other, we
must as the change hands, in receavinge again of the needles aforesayed, take
the needle, of the right hand, in the left hand, the needle of the left hand in
the right this permutatione or changinge being in this sort effected, we
must then again thrust through the lips of the vvoude, as al ready hath
bin done, to vvitt, from the internal part, towards the external, so
proceeding forevvardes, as much as is sufficient, and allvvayes considering
that the one stiche be allvvayes laid right opposyte against the other, in
this sort proceeding, until the vvoud be vvholye sevved up, allvvayes
remembringe to leave a little apertione, in the bottom of the vvoude, through
the which the congealed blood, and the thread may be avoided, which
apertione, we must always keep aperte or open with a little leaden
hollovve pipe, which in the end must be tyede bovvnde with a thread, because
that by chance it do not come to slip into the belly of the patient. And
we must well and dilligently note, that this suture or sovving allvvayes
bedone with a good strong thread, What manner of thread needle we must have.
with a needle, somewhat crooked at the poynct, laying the stitches,
somewhat closer the one by other, then in other vvoundes of the body, because
the stirrige of the belly, far sooner causes the stitches to burst and
break a sunder, then in any other parts of the body, because alsoe the
vvoundes of the belly, are not so subject unto inflammation or incensions, as
other although that often times the belly is thrust through.

Chirurgerye THE FOVRTH TREATISE OF THE OPERATIon of Chyrurgerye, wherein is
discoursede and handelede of the openinges, and apertions of Apostemations,
Containing five Chapters.
Of the general way to open any Apostemations vvhatsoever. Chap. 1. Of the
tumefactions, which we call, Ateromata, Steatomata, Melicerides. Chap. 2.
Howe we ought to cauterize, make any inscisione in the Apostemations of the
breast. Ch. 3. Howe we ought to make the Paracentese, and take away the
water from them which have the Dropsye. Chap. 4. Howe we ought to cure the
Hernias aquosas, or water burstinges. Chap. 5.
Of the general way te open Apostemations. Chap. 1.
What we ought to consider before we make the appertione.
WHen as we endevoure to open any apostematios, to let the matter rune
therout, we must first of all before we com to the inscisione, or apertione,
consider, whether the matter might not in any ways be resolved or consumed,
through the forces of the natural caliditye, or heate, or else whether needs
and of necessity be changed into matter. When as an Apostematione, must
needs be brought to suppuratione of matter can by no means possible be
consumed or resolved, the signs thereof are these, namelye a burning heate,
erectige of the tumefactione, or svvellinge, more rednes, Signs of
suppuratione.
which is also harder the before, pricking, knocking, or beating payne,
ponderousnes, as if there did hang any heavye thing at that joint: And if
so be this apostematione be situated in any principal part, then there is a
chillnes and shiveringe thervvith associated, the ague allsoe is more violent
by day, then by night: sometimes alsoe the next kernells which are in the
body situated thereunto come or chance to svvel, be distempered. Signs when
the matter is fully ripened*
But the suppuratione, or resolutione to matter beige finished, or
accomplished, the tumefactione as the diminished, we the sensibly feel
pricking, with a little itching therbye, alsoe a little deafnes or numnes,
vvherbye alsoe some times is felt a great pricking, especially the matter
lying very deep: For the matter lying close under the skin, there the
demostrateth it self a little head which in feeling is soft, and when as
we depress with the finger it glides a side, where of the skin, in the
superior part of the same head opens it self, separeteth it self from the
inferior parts of the same.

When with the lancet we ought to open an Apostematione.
When as therefore all these signs reveal themselves, we may the boldly
freely, open the Apostematione, with the lacet, with out tatryinge any
longer, that it might of it self break through, because the matter may the
issve forth thereat, because that through to loge tarrying, with the opening
thereof, she do not come to diminish into the circumiacent parts, make other
concavityes. Although Celsus says, Opinione of Celsus.
that very seldom we ought to make any inscisione, under the armepittes, or
in the flanckes when the matter is ripened, come to a full and good
suppuratione, in like sort alsoe, when the Apostematione is not to great is
placed under the skin, but that as the we make the apertione with
Cataplasmes, let them of themselves break through, unless through the
intollerablenes, and great dolor or payne of the patient, the Chyrurgiane
vveare compelled, to open the same, because says he, that if with out
inscisione or cauterisatione we make the apertione, no deformed cicatrice, or

In like sort must the apostemations of the privityes be opened, before they

The fourth condition is, The fourth coditione.
that we must remember dilligently to make as small an apertione as we may,
survayinge the apertione, with the magnitude of the Apostematione: For if so
be it be little, we may as then make therine a mean and reasonable

we are the sometimes urged, to make two inscisions, or more then two, being

fore said Apostematione, because the humour do not chance here or there to
make any more cocavityes, eve as the humours, allvvayes internallye, corrupt
eat through the circumiacent parts, and there in diminish.

An admonitione considering all great Apostematios
And if so be that any apostematione, with great concavityes cam into our
hands, the skin there over might as yet becured again with the flesh, we
must as then make but one apertio or inscisione, through the which the matter
may issve: but if so be the flesh internally is eaten, corrupted away, we
must as then cut through with a right cut, the whole concavitye,
according unto the length thereof, which being done, the edge of the vvounde
in all places being very skin, bereft of all there flesh we must clip or
cut them clean away: because such skin restrayneth and keeps back the
matter, which causes the circumiacent parts to corrupt, hinders the
applicatione of the medicamentes: but when as we cut the skin, we must
do it triagle vvyse, or in form of a myrtle lease, because the apertione so
much the sooner, might chance to heal, because all rotuditye in vlceratios is
difficult to be cured.

Fro vvhece we must take our indication, of the greatness of the vvounde.
And touching all great apertions, which may be made in the apostemations,
must be made, according to the abundance, or the qvatitye of the matter,
accordige as the subject parts thereof require. As alsoe we must consider, on
the depth, mark that we thrust not the lancet to deep therein, but
reasonable deep: for when as we thrust to deep, it is the great dolor,
payne to the patient, causes alsoe much bleedinge: thrustinge not deep
enough, we effect brige nothige to pass.

The fifth coditione, must be observede, according to the apertio of all
great apostematios, that at one time we suffer not all the matter to rune
out, but rather by little, little, especially, the patient being debile,
old, yonge, or any vvoma with child, because all suddayn evacuatios, do
cause great imbicillity fayntnes because there through the vital spirits,
evaporate fly away, which in the cocavitye of the Apostemation with the
matter vveare shut therein, although they are against nature.

An other condition vvherone we must consider after the apertione of the
svvelling.
The apertione being done, we must hadle the wound, according unto the
diversitye of those parts, wherein the Apostematione was situated, alsoe
according unto the time season of the year. The Chyrurgiane ought especially
to eschevve the great tetes, which Celsus prohibiteth to use in the
vlcerations of the arm pits, flanckes, that because of the great veins,
arteryes, synnues, which in those places are situated, laying only there one
a sponge which is madefied, vvetted in vvyne. We comonlye lay thereon a flat
plumaciolle or tent made of linte, and therone a plaster, levinge so the
solutione apert, because that the remaining matter might issue come forth
thereat which othervvyse through a great loge thick tent, might therein be
retained, kept back. This being done, we must then dress that part, wind
it beginning to wind from the sovvnde part, unto the bottom of the
cocavitye if there be any, ending at the apertione because the matter may be
pressed crushed therout, without being therein retained, any longer, because
by to lunge tarrying therine, it do not cause a great hollovve fistulous
concavitye to come therein: then we must lay that part in such a sort that the
apertione may depend or hang, because so the matter may the better issue out
thereat.

Of the tumefactions, or swellinges called, Ateromata, Steatomata, and
Melicerides. Chap. 2.
THe ancient professors, The difference of these three Vlcerationes.
have allvvayes noted, only the species forms of vlcerations, that they only
differed the one from the other, in the matter, which is found therine. For
in the vlceration Ateroma, Signs to know them.
is a kind of matter like unto thin pap: the vlceration Steatoma, a kind
of fat matter, like suet. In Melicerides, is a matter like unto honey: So
that by the form of conjecture we may say, what matter therein lies
enclosed, but for a certayntye we can not say it, before such time as we have
fetched some of the matter therout: and one this manner they are allvvayes
known the one fro the other. when as we chance to touch the vlceratio
Ateroma, it spreads abroad, in that place vvhero we chance to depress it,
doth not so suddaynly, join it self together. The vlceration Meliceres,
differs fro the Ateroma, in his form, substance of his humours for his figure
is much rouder, and the substance of the humour which is therein, far
subtiler. so that it spreads more abroad, then the Ateroma, when with the
finger we depress it, it separateth it self far soener, alsoe joins much
sooner, when we take the fingers thereof. The vlceratione Steatoma, is much
harder, then both the other, in the touching of it, it stirs not, but
resists the depressinge of the fingers, commolye in the bottom is it
somewhat broad, and very seldom or never may we se any which are under
narrovve broad above.

We oftentimes find harde matter in these vlcerations.
These foresayed vlcerations, are in there beginning allvvay little, but in
continuance of time, they grovv to be greater, some are very harde, we find
sometimes therein wondrous matter, as if it vveare little stones, bones,
rovvled hair, mixed with any matter which is thick.

The matter lies enclosed in a little blather.
Philexomus says, he sometimes hath fovvnde, in the matter which therein lay
enclosed, little creatures, as if it had bine gnats, we must note that al
those foresayed humours, al these wonderful things, are enclosed as it vveare
in little blathers.

But as much as concernethe the apertione of the same, it hinders nothing
whether the matter be like pap, or like honey, or like suet, or what
matter soever it might be: for we have but one only intention, to vvitt that
we extract draw out the blather, or little bag, wherein the matter is
enclosed, for the manner howe to open it is this.

The operatione to cure the vlceratios is nothing different.
We must first of all lift up the skin fro the bottom, then cut it of,
making inscisione according to the greatenes of the vlceratione, dilligetlye
markinge least we cut the blather or purse wherein the the humour is enclosed,
because the matter do not chance to issue out: For the matter being issued,
the whole remanet operatio is perturbed, annihilated, because alsoe there by
the operatio falls flat down: so that as then we may or can very
difficultlye flea, peel or separate the foresayed membrane of the matter, from
the flesh, vvherthroughe the whole curatione, consists without reciduitye.

When as in this sort, we shall have cut the skin, the the little bag
demonstrates it self vvhyte, and outstretched, which with great
diligence, we must endevoure to separate thereof, with a Spatula, to vvitt,
from the skin, out of the flesh, or with any other instrument, which is
thereto serviceable, where with we must take it out, with all that which is
therein, without suffering any iott thereof to remain in the bottom, because
it chance not to increase again: And is it so happened that there chanced
to tarry any thing thereof in the vvounde, we must not as the heal together
the apertione very soon, but with puryfyinge medicamentes, purifye that
therout which as yet remains therein.

We must in the operatione dilligently consider, that we do not chance to
break any Veins, synnues, arteryes, or tendones.
We must alsoe heer note, that some of these vlcerations, are rotede between
any veins, arteryes, synnues, tedones, which eradication, reqvireth a great
lunge continued convenient manual, operation, because, thinckinge qvite and
clean to take away the little bag or purse from thence, we might chance
to burst any vain, arterye, synnue or Tedone, therefore if so be therebe any
part therone fastened it is then better to desist from our operation. When
this operatione is thus finished, in any small svvellinges, there is as the
no more danger, wherefore there rests no thing for us to do, then with
any curing medicamentes, to heal op the inscisione, laying therone a
Compresse, madefied in some defensive or other, because that in the dressinge,
all inflammations may be praevented, the separated parts the on again
conjoined with the other. But if so be the vvounde vveare great, could not
be cured, one this manner, it will not then seem discommodious, that we
stitch it up, laying the stitches somewhat vvyde the one from the other, to
let any medicamemt or other drop between them, to give passage to the
matter which engenders therein: then we must vvholye cure these vlceratios as
all other vlcerations.

Touching the curing of Quees evil which is alsoe done through manual
operatione, is alsoe one this manner effected, taking the foresayed Queens
evil vvholye therout, if so be it be possible dilligently markinge, that we
chance not to hurt any veins situated there about, especially of those
which are divided amongst the muscles, which are serviceable to the speech,
or voice, as are the recurrentes, or retrogradinge muscles, those which are
situated, abute the Laringa as well the principalls as common: For it hath oft
times happened that some have had this dissease of the Qveenes evil cut out
about there throt, which all there life time thereafter have remained hoarce
without speech.

The signs, if any matter be retained in the breast, are these, to wit,

the matter which is retained in the breast.

Dropsy of the breast and her tokes
When any of these disseases have continued any time, no remedies have
helped, no hope left, that the water, or the matter might by any means be
expelled out by the mouth, or else be purged through the urine, 40. days
being passed, we must as then come to the manualle operatione, endevoure to
open the breast, make a passage for the matter to issue forth, because the lunges
do not in any sort chance to corrupt, or rot. Which may bedone through

potential Cauterium, because the apertio, which we made therine, through the
Cauteriu, may the longer be kept open and apert, without troubling of the

the inscisione. And if so be we perceave no tumefactione, or svvellinge,
externally in the breast, we must then between the third and the fourth true
rib, beginning to account from under, about the midest of the one side, The
place of the apertione.

Howe that we must make the Paracentese, to draw away the water out of the

Descriptione of the Dropsye.
THe Dropsye, is a tumefactione, or inflatione, against nature, or Phlegma is
engendered, or also of some certain winds and ventosityes. This svellinge
sometimes alsoe, spreads herself through out the whole body: sometimes
allsoe only in the inferior part of the belly. The first kind is engedred,
of a phlegmaticke humour, the second kind of Dropsye, Three sorts of Dropsye.
is engedred of water wind tohether: wherefore the auciet professors have
thought that there have bin three sorts of Dropsye. Whereof the first is
called, The first kind.
Anasarca, Hiposarcidios, Leucophlegmatias, when as the universal body is
through soaked with water, through the which, everye part of the body
seems to be tender, or weak soft, pale, vnseemlye to the sight, so that
crushinge therone, the printes of your fingers for a little continuance tarry
therein: The second.
The second, is called Tympanitas, and of Hippocrates the dry Dropsye. when
as the inferior part of the belly is full of inflation, or winds, and
stiffly stretched out and spanned, so that smiting therone, it gives from
him a sovvnde like unto a Drum, it is right true that sometimes there are
alsoe a few humours mixed theramongst, because by continuance of time the
winds come to condence them selves, and change into some vvaterye substance.
The third kind.
The third kind of Dropsye is called, Asites, which is an inflation, and
strectchinge out of the belly, because of the aquositye wherewith it is
replete, having vnparted her name of Ascos, which is to say, a leather
vessel, wherein in former and ancient times, they used to keep any moisture
in, as we do in the hydes, in the bucks skins: when this dissease
increases, grovveth, Accidetes of the Dropsye.
sometimes the hippes, and the Scrotum begin to sveelle, which is a sign of
Leucophlegmatica, as differinge, from the self same being or essence of
Ascites: as in the contrary it is not so, when as any aqvosytye ascends up
to the breast. Ascites is subject to Paracetese.


Amongst all these three species, and kinds, there is but one of them subject
unto our manual operatione, to draw forth thereof all that water which
lies enclosed in the concavitye of the belly: In what persons the dravvinge
of of water must be used and in what persons it must be left.
but before we come to the apertione hereof, we must first know, which of

we take it not in hand, in those which through age are grow debile, and
feeble, or in those, which are corrupt not sovvnde of body, the intestines or
entralls, being very ill disposed and ill at ease, without any hope of
amendment, in those, which vvholye are grovven impotent, or in those which
are there vvithe borne, we must not in any vvyse through this operatione lay

constitutione, nether have any agues, having all there entralles sovvnde, and
tempered, in these, in the which the water as yet hath made no cocavityes,
whereby the liver, the milte, and the guts, might chance to be altered, in
those remains as yet some hope, ominouslye to effect and bring to pass this
operatione: in this manner we must with the same proceed. Namelye, How we
ought to effect this opening
and especially, if the Dropsye, resume his original from the liver, the

golden, The use of the pipe.
or silver, pipe, as thick as a qville or shaft, which must have a broad

mitigatinge this evacuatione, governinge of the forces accordige to the

Because there are some, which having made to great an evacuatione at one

The navel which heaves up it self may be pricked thorough
And considering that oftentimes, in those which are troubled with the
dropsy, there navel heaves up it self, yea and sometimes as big as an egg,
which stands as clear, as if it vveare a blather with water, there
through I have seen to be drawn, a silk or vvotle thread, by the which
the patient perceaved great ease, considering the great qvantitye of water,
which through this apertione, is dropping vvyse run out.

Allsoe beyond all this are the legs, the hippes, and the Scrotum often
times, svvollen: for the which there is no better remedy, the that we
scarifye them through the skin, once as broad, as the apertione is in
phlebotomye: Some there be which do this by the innermost ankle or four
fingers above it. In what places the scarifications may be made.
I have sometimes my self very luckylye ominouslye done the same, above the
knees, and in the insyde of the hippes, in the bag of the testicles, out of
the which first of all issues a little vvaterye blood, but immediately
thereafter the water superfluovslye issues therout without any inflammatione,
so that the foresayed scarifications, can not shut, or heal, before all the
water thereof be issued, run out, the patient all most grovven smaller, the
belly evidentlye vvaxed thinner, which in short time vvilbe effected, with
out any troublesome accidents, happeninge thereunto, without any
inflammatione in the foresayed parts. More over we are not to expect any
danger hereof as we might well do, when as we open the belly of the
Dropsy Ascites, because one this manner, the water with great qvatitye, at
one time is not dravven of: and if so be the patient, after that there is a
great qvantitye of water dravven of, began to be weak, we may as then
restrain the dropping of the water, when as we cover the scarifications,
with scraped linte, or with a few burned pieces of linen, and so stop
it, bind it. Out of the which scarificatios, if so be we are disposed to
draw more water therout, we as then untie the foresayed place, take away
the linte from the same cause the patient somewhat to vvalke, or ride in a
vvaggen or koache, if it be possible, with the leg hanging out, To cause
the water, Council of Hippocrates, to draw of the water.
very superfluouslye to run or issue out of the legs, Hippocrates

in such scarifications, the ancient professors, as Aetius, Asclepiades,

But before we must come to these scarifications, we must consider one the

which are very old, macilete, because that as well the one as the other, of

being able in any sort to retain keep back the same, vvhero immediately
follows death, by the which oft times, the Physicians, or the Chyrurgias,
are iniuriouslye without right blamed, and of all me hated had in little
vvorthe, as I my self have known it happen unto some, for the which I was
not a little greeved.

How that we shall cure the water burst, called Hernia aquosa. Chap. 5.
IN the Scrotum, which we call the bag where in the testicles are contained,
is cogregate gathered together a certain, vvaterishe moisture, through the
which the fore said bag comes to svvel, which tume factione, or
inflatione, of the Greeckes is called Hydrocele, Hydrocele.
which seems to be a particularre kide of Dropsye: must here note that this
svvellinge, sometimes comes but in one side only: if so be that this
dissease proceeds out of any antecedent or foregoinge occasions, it
necessarilye follows, that the blood, which into all parts of the body
is sent as nurriture, is permutated changed. into some vvaterye substance: if
so be of any blovve, or fall, it produces his originalle, then is contained in
the svvellinge a blooddye kind of moisture. This aqvositye, remains not, The
signs of a water burst.
in one certain place only because sometimes she is congreated situated

There is alsoe oftentimes, a ventosytye gathered together, in the bag, or
