Hernia, Whereby we may know the vvindye Hernia.
or burst, is partly harde and light, and is at one time engendered, on the

both sides.
if so be we find all these signs, and tokens, in both sides of the

Having shaved away the hair, Howe we ought to make the apertione.

Some there are which apply alsoe in the superior part of the Scrotum, a

enough to issue out thereat. Apertion by the thrusting through of a silk

Others, draw through the svvellinge, with a strong needle, a silk thread,

But the apertione which is least daugerous of these three foresayed, is, the

THE FIFTH TREATISE OF THE OPERATIon of Chyrurgerye, wherein is discoursede and
handelede of the disseases of the nose, of the mouth, containing seven
Chapiters.

the excrescece of the flesh of the gummes, called Paroulis, end Epoulis,

Of the Polipus. Chap. 1.
Root, original of the Polipus.
SOme men there are, which have a certain excrescece, of flesh in the
nostrells, which resumeth her originalle, out of the bone, Etmoidis, or
Chribleus, out of the bones of the nose. This excrescence happens or comes
to a man, by little little at once, and at the last, she vvaxeth so big,
huge in qvatitye, that she stops the whole nostrells, sometimes alsoe
hangs down to the lips, by the which the patient, is mervelouslye
molested, as well in speaking, as breathing. Sometimes also she grovveth in
the entrace, or conductione, through the which the air, the breath, are
drawn into the throat, the which we may easily see, when as the patient
opens his mouth, behind the pallaet of the mouth, in such a greatness
sometimes, that she stops the throat, vvherthroughe the patient comes in
the end to be choked, if that in time we do not prevent it, And because such
an excrescece of flesh, most commonly is soft, and pale, having divers
small sproutes, not mislike unto the feet, of the fish Purpura, it is therefore
of the Greeckes called Polipus, allso in our langvage Polipus.

There are no sorts of Polipus which being venomous, No sorts of Polipus,
can through Surgery be cured.
have need of the operatione of Chyrurgerye, because we may not touch the, in
like sort we may not touch those which are infected with the Cancer,
because there nature is thervvith polluted, they there through spoiled. The young
Chyrurgiane, may know them by there sensiblenes, Signs of a venomous
Polipus.
because they are very dolorous, full, of payne, extreme hard, being almost
purple coloured, with a very stinckig odor, or smelle, on the which the
Chyrurgiane shall do nothing, but must be handled as if it vveare the
Cancker, to vvitt, through koolinge, dryinge, through mollifyinge
medicamentes, through the which her excrescence may be praevented.

Signs of a tractable Polipus.
That Polipus, which is insensible, or where there is little, or no payne,
which is weak, loose, tractable, without beige irritated, pale, or which
is of a reddishe colour, those we may through the operation of Chyrurgerye
take in hands.

Howe we ought to cure the Polipum.
To the effectuatinge of which, the aunciete Chyrurgias, those which have
followed there doctrine, have effected this operatione cruelly enough: for
some there are which with an instrument cut rovvnde, or circkle vvyse this
Polipum, which to that end purpose, thereto they have caused to be made, and is
called in greek Polyticon Pathion, without touching of the cartilage, or
grisle, which being effected, they draw forth the foresayed descided
Polipus, with an instrument much like unto an ear picker, or else with some
kind of hoocke, then they cure heal the vlceratio, as it reqvireth to be don.
And as commonly there remains some little piece of root, in the bones
Etmoides, of the nose, the which they scrape of, alsoe from the foresayed
bones turning, thrustige therein, with an instrument, to fetch all therout,
that by any means possible might tarry, and remain therein.

An other manner.
Others endeavour to consume, annihilate the foresayed Polypum, with certain
causticke, or vstive medicametes, as with Aqva fortis, with oil of vitrioll,
or with the liqvefacted, moulten corrosive, in the which they dip, madefye
little small tents, which they lay therone, with or through a little pipe,
because they should not hurt the nose one the internalle or inner side.
Others there are, which with an actual cauterye, cauterize it, which
through a pipe they thrust therein. Others, after the example the four famous
Chyrurgians, An other manner to cure the Polipum.
which not being of opinion, that in no vvyse, or in any sort we vveare
able to cauterize the Polipus, unto his root, because he is so deep
ingraffed, they the make an inscisione, one the one side, of the cartilage of
the nose, unto the bone of the same, having in this sort discovered,
denudatede the Polipu unto his root or first ofspringe, originalle, then they
there cut, cauterise him, stitch together again the said inscisione, as we
are commonly used to do in the hare mouths. The wise, Learned man Guido de
Cauliaco, likes commends this, or such like operatione, but in no wise,
counceleth, to stitch up the inscision, before the foresayed Polipus, be vvholy
eradicated, rooted out, because if so be, that any parcel thereof remain, it
again increases, which happening he, to be so, all the trouble, pains of
the patient, hath bin done for nothing, to no purpose. All which
operations, being as sufficiently and completlye effected as they possible may
be, yet for the patient they are tedious trouble some, as often times, we have
with our eyes beheld, which to speak the very truth indeed, they have
never attained unto there perfect, and former health again, but rather far
vvorse, then better: whereby we vveare moved, to try. make experiment of a
far more ominous, and more convenient operatio, to the curing of this
dissease, which consistethe in the whole eradicatione of the same. The manner
howe to drovve out the Polipus, more certayner, convenienter, through
extirpatione.
To the which end and purpose it seems unto us convenient, that we situate,
dispose the patient, on the most convenient manner we may, in a seat,
towards the light, the Chyrurgian, with his left hand opening the
nostrell, wherein the Polipus is situated, shall with his right hand, thrust
therein the flat toges, as deep as possibly he may, (which tongues must be
sovvhat broad, like a Ducks) bil wherewith he must nipe the foresayed
Polipu, gentlelye with both his hands turn wind the same, thervvith alsoe
draw towards him a little, not at one pluck, because the root which is
fast settled in the foresayed bones, might alsoe follovve, the foresayed Polipus
at one time be dravven forth, and extirpated: which I my self may with the
truth allege to have done, with very little payne. Mr. Sourlin, a very
expert Chyrurgian, hath assured me, that he alsoe divers times hath done it
one the same manner, without any inconvenience. Admonitione for the
Chyrurgiane.
But the Chyrurgyane must consider, that he do not adveture on such an
operatione, then in that Polipus which is tractable, and not canckerouse,
which is great enough, to be griped, with out breaking to be extirpated,
wherefore, if it be not great enough, we as then let him grow, increase.
And so far forth as the foresayed Polipus, drevve himself vpvvardes,
avoydinge the nipinge, of the little toges, we must the draw it
dovvnevvardes, with a little hooke, because as then we may the better, take
houlde therone: the operatio being finished, there ensues an effluxio of
blood presetlye, thereafter, which we shall permit, suffer to bleed,
because that part may be thereof released, vnburthened: we must then cause the
patient, through his nose to snufle up red vvyne, that as oftentimes a day,
until such time as it be internally cured, without applying any pingveditye,
or oils thereunto.

Of the haremouthe, or splitted and cloven lips. Chap. 2.
Coloboma, Curtum.
THe cleavinge, or splittinge, which daily we see, in the lips, in the
ears, and in the nose, is of Galen called Coloboma, in Latin Curtum, when as
these parts through any natural dissease, are splitted, or clovene, as if
there vveare a piece cut out. To regenerate any substance is a work of
nature and not of the Chyrurgiane.
It is impossible, that we should ingendre, any other matter, restore, the
substance, which therein is vvantinge, because it is the work of nature not of
the Chyrurgiane: but he may bring the separated parts the one, by the other,
and cause such a substance, which in that part is deficiet, vvantinge shall
seem as it vveare there in engendered, so that very hardly we shall espy,
mark such an vnseemlines, deformitye, especially if the slit have bin but
small: for these disseases might per adventure be so great, that they should
be incurable, and vveare not conveniente for us to lay hands one them, if so
be we endeavoured to cure them, we might chance to make them far vvorse,
more vnseemlye, then before they vveare. Wherefore the Chyrurgiane ought
dilligently to consider, that one such kinds of fissures or splittinges, he
impose not his hands, and especially in those which are to vvyde separated
the one from the other, where there is to much substance deficient or
vvantinge, between them both, for when a Chyrurgian endeavours to cure
such a dissease, then must necessarylye the seiunged and separated parts,
ether join, heal together again, or else not: if so be they grow, join
together, the lip the is merveylouslye stretched, lies close to the teethe,
one the gumes: And if so be they ca not souder, join the on with the other,
the fissure, or splitting, vvilbe much greater, then before it was, because
cosideringe that, which one both sides was taken cut avvaye, through which
occasion, the patient, as then can far and much more difficulter eat, and
also speaks vvorse, because that a coplete, whole lip, is very coveniete,
necessary, as well to the commestione, as to the loquution, speech.

In what persons this operatione is dangerous.
This manuale operatione, is very dangerous in aged persons, in those which
are vnhealthesame, of body, or in those, which (as we commonly say) have
noughtye healinge flesh, because, that in mas body there is no part or
place, where in the Cacker ca sooner come, and being therein, wherein he may
be vvorse cured, with more difficulty healed. The means to cure such a
dissease, is on this manner: When must first of all situate the patient
towards the light, with our left hade, lift up the right side of the cloven
or splitted lip, as then cut with the crooked lancett, in the middle or
upper end of the foresayed side of the lip, through the skin, alsoe
between them both beginning as high, as near the nose, as possible might
be done, dravvige the foresayed lacett, unto or towards beneath or end of the

Lips which are cleft in two places.
The lips are some times split, or cleft in two sundry, divers places, but

As cocerninge, touching the fissures, of the ears, or nostrils, we must

Of the vlceratione, and excrescence of the gummes, called Paroulis, and
Epoulis. Chap. 3.
AFter the inflammation, or inscensione of the gummes, commolye succeed the
follovve the an excrescence of flesh, which of the aunciete professors was
called Epoulis, that especially about the check, or hindmost teethe. Such a
humour, excrescece encreseth by little, little, till at last it increases as
big as a hens egg, so that sometimes the teeth there through fall out,
and the patient not able to open his mouth. We may not provoacke the
Canckerouse.
If so be these excrescences be black, and Canckerouse, we must not apply
there one any sharpe, or corrodent medicamentes, nether with byndinge
separate, root them out: but must entreat, handle them, in such sort, as
before we have spoken of the Cancker, and like all palliative cures.

If so be this excrescence be tender, soft, The manner to cure Epoulis
tractable, we must whilst it is small, descyde cut it away, if so be it
be great, we must the bind it of, with a strong thread, which we must
alligate, and tie under at the root thereof, and by degrees knitt it closer,
till that in thus doing, the root by the thread be cut through, and the
excrescence fall out of the mouth. Such a ligature is more certain the
inscisione
Such a ligature or byndinge is far surer, the the discidinge or cuttinge of,
the same, because that therthroughe, we need fear no superfluous bleedinge,
the root better one this manner be taken out, allsoe the patient have less
payne, and fear to expect, then of the inscisione, or cuttinge, and
cauterisatione, which after the inscisione coseqvetlye follovvethe.

When as therefore througe byndinge, Howe we ought to handle the Epoulis that

Of the retentione of the tuge, which of the Greeckes was called,

The dissease of a hortened tunge.
THe tunge in some persons, or children, is shorter then she ought to be,
wherewith they are borne, and by the which occasio, they must with
difficulty utter the words which they would speak, the tunge being
intercepted, and hindered, by a certain ligament, which we call the spaned
vayn, vvherthroughe the toge, is restrained, as it vveare, seems to be
brydeled, or through any membrane thervnder situated, vvheron the tunge
rests, because she can not so move her self, and turn, as it vveare
convenient, she should. The children are very lunge time before they begin
to speak, when as they begin, they as the speak very festivouslye
hastily, without any hinderance, except in uttering of those words,
which are difficult to pronounce, as are those, in the which many R. R, R.
and L. L. L. come.

This dissease, is alsoe sometimes accidetalle unto a man, The dissease of the
tunge after a callouse vlceratione.
when as after any vlceratione under the tunge, there remains a harde
callousnes, anguste cicatrice, whereof the tuge sometimes, inspeaking must
double redubble her self, they which are troubled with this imperfection,
do speak with great pains difficulty, wherefore the ancient professors of
foretimes, have called the Mogilaous. The fore said dissease must be cured
only through the manualle operatio. To which end purpose, we must situate
place the patient as the matter is reqviringe, How we ought to cure the
tunge tyinge.
the servat of the Chyrurgiane having both his fingers vvounde with fine
linne (to vvitt, the thumb, the first finger) wherewith he must take the
foresaid tuge by the end, lift her up, towards the roof of the mouth:
which being done, if so be the membranes vvherone the tunge is fastenede, be
cause, of the difficulty of speech, the must the Chyrurgian vvitth a small
hooke, as is before describede, houlde the same thervvith fast, with his left
hand, when indiffierentlye he shall have stretched out the same, he must as
then ether with the poyncte of his scissors, or else with his crooked lancet,
cut, and discide it of. If so be the shortness be caused because the ligament
of the tunge, is to short, stretches it self more then is needful
toward, the middle of the tunge, or else because of any cicatrice: we must
then alsoe in like sort take houlde on all that with the hooke, pluck or
draw it, cut it of, which is obdurated harde, alsoe all that which with
the natural flesh of the tunge is not moved, or stirred. This operatione then
being effected, the patient must was he his mouth, with a little water
vinegar, dilligently stir the tunge, often times thrust her forth of his
mouth, rub or vvipe his finger thervnder, oft times with his teethe crushe
her up towards the roof of the mouth, because that the foresayed ligament,
or mebrane, do not chance to join grow fast again thero: Howe we may
hinder the healinge.
for as the stirring, removinge up down, are a hinderace, unto the healinge
of the same, so is all so qvietnes, stillnes, an occassion of the vnitinge,
cobinatione of the same: yea he must alsoe in the night seaso lay a compresse
under his tunge, there by to hinder, delay the curing of the vvounde, which
easily sleeping might chance come to pass, that because the tunge, nether by
eating, nor by speaking is in any sort stirred or moved.

Some of our aunciet professors, Membranous ligament or tunge tyinge.
to prevent the danger, of bleedinge in such an operation, thrust through the
membranous ligamet, or through the cicatrice, whereby the tuge is kept boude
tied, a needle with a thread, which by degrees they draw pluck together
until such time the membrane heervvith be cut a sunder.

When as we would cut the tunge tyednes in yonge children, which are nuely

The vlceratione Ranula, and the original thereof.
It may alsoe chance that the speech is impedited hindered, by reason of some
vlceratione, which comes under the tuge, called Batrachos, or Ranula, in
which vlceratione, there lies a vvaterishe humiditye therein enclosed, which
often times is not disliking unto the vvhyte of an egg, as well considering
the consistence thereof, as concerning the colour, allvvayes commonly being
included in a little membrane, like unto the vlceratione Ateroma, many other
could vlcerations or svvellinges. In have seen such manner of svvellinge,
tumefactions so great and so often times (after they have bin thrust
through to let out the matter) return again, that the patient, by reason of the
greatness could not speak, yea allsoe in davvnger there through of styfelinge,
or smotheringe, so that I have known them to have bin opened four times
in one day, and at everye season a hadefull of matter to issue therout, like
unto slime, and the vvhyte of an egg. The which hath binn so difficult to be
cured, that in the end we have bin compelled, to apply there one an actualle
Cauterium, in which large and vvyde apertione, we have imposed laid in
linte, being madefyed and made vvett in oil of vitriolle, by the which at the
last the patient is sovvndlye, and completlye cured.

Now to cure this dissease, we must first of all make an apertione, with the
crooked lancet, according to the length of that part and not thvvart or
crosseover, to give issue passage to the humiditye matter to run out, which
is the occasion of this tumefactione, or vlceratione: and if so be this
svvelling return, the surest remedy, as the is the actual cauterye.

Howe we ought to cure this vlceratione his return again.
To which intent, we must well, and convenietly situate the patiete, cause his
head to be stayed, held by his servant, with the one hand one his head,
and the other under his chin, there about where the foresayed vlceration is
situated, because partly there through it may be elevated and lifted up, for
the Chyrurgiane with more ease facility to attain to his purpose, when as
with the lacet or with the cauterie we will make there in an apertione: For
those parts being tender, soft, how little soever they be crushed yet
notvvitstadinge yield themselves invvardes where through the apertion might be
hindered failed, and all our labour be lost, bestowed in vain. The patient
therefore in this sort being settled and disposed, and being by some servat
held fast, must open his mouth, then shall the Chyrurgiane with his left
hand lay a piece of lattinne or a silver plate under the tunge of the patient,
which hath a hole in the midst thereof right on the place, where we desire
to make the apertione, then must the Chyrurgiane take in his right hand a
glovvinge or red hot Cauterium, which he must sittuate right one the hole
of the foresayed piece of Lattinne thrust the same into the concavitye of the
foresayed vlceration: in so doing are the circumiacent parts defended from
all combustione or burning, the vlceratione very convenientlye opened. This
apertion being on such a manner effected, brought to pass, we must cause the
patient to wash his mouth, with a mouth-vvater, or gargrise, which must be
made of barley-vvater, of Plantine, and of honie-vvater.

That which is black, Wen we ought not to cut away the palate.
we must not in any vvyse meddle with, or take in hands, in like sort

we must as then cut it of: as we must alsoe do when it is above thin,

This dissease may be cured, by taking away that, which in the palate is

and last remedy, to preserve and keep him from greater danger ensvinge.

And aptly to bring this to pass, we must cause the patient to sit in a

we as the easily take houlde thereon when as we would pluck him,

The second manner, of effectinge this same, is done with an actual, The

And when we desire to take away the foresayed pipe, we must the first of

A sort there are which do not use the foresayed pipe, but they dip,
moysten, a little cotton, or linen, which is tied to the privet, or

certain kind of spoon so called
(which of our praedecessors was called Staphilocausto) full implete with

palate which before was to lunge: it is right true, that some of this
corrosive in the spoon might chance to fall one the tunge, wherefore I would
rather council to use that corrosive which is thick and of some crassitude,
as that is which may be pulverisated beaken to poulder, because without
being pulverizated it can not show his forces one the palate. When as
therefore, through the applicatione of any of the fore said corrosives, or

yet changed, we must then yet once again apply the corrosive thereunto, then
cause the patient to gargrise his mouth with Oxicrate or with warm red
vvyne.

A tried remedy.
I have known the suncken palate to be lifted up again, with a little
povvnded pepper saulte, by a spoon, being therone fastened: others use there
to some astringet poulders. The third means, to cure this dissease, is
effected by ligature, because of the timorousnes which the patient conceaveth
of the cuttinge of, or cauterizinge the same, or effluxion of blood. The
tyinge, with the third means howe to cure the palate being sunk.
Which is effected and completlye brought to pass, when as with a strong
thread we tie the fore said palate vnderneathe, in that place where it
surpasses his natural length: through which ligature, the inferior part
can obtain no nurture, because the veins by the thread are suffulced
stopped up, so that in a short time the thread cuttethe of, and causes to
separate that which hath bin bovvnde, the superior part being almost
cicatrized, with out any fear of any superfluous bleedige. The means howe
very neatlye, and convenientlye to effect this operatione, and allsoe the
instrument thereunto servinge, is described unto you before amoge the figures of
the instruments.

The use of the foresayed palate of the mouth, Use of the palate.
shevveth unto us, that we ought to observe a mean, in cuttinge of the same,
when as it surmountethe the naturalle longitude, or else the use thereof is
brought to nought, and lost, because therthroughe, the voice of ma is formed,
and retained, as the mouth or apertione of a Citerne, when as we touch the
strings thereof, she as then geveth a sovvnde, even so the breath of man,
which is the substance of the voice, issuinge forth of the lunges being
divided, and spread over, and throughout the whole roof of the mouth, there
and in that place to receave the articulatione, and by the tunge, teethe,
lips, and nostrils, in the utterance thereof to be formed. Wherefore those
which have the same surpassinge the naturalle length, or in any other sort,
troubles them, have such a feeble, imbicille voice, that very difficultlye
we may hear them, or at the least speak through there noses: if so be it
be clean cut of, they leese there speech, and vvaxe mute and dumme. Above all
this it hath as yet a tvvofoulde use, viz the one to prohibit, the dust, and
any other substance, to enter into the throat, by our breathe, which
constinuallye we must draw invvardes, and hangs as it vveare dilated and
spread abroad like unto a tapistrye, over the throat. The other, and second
use of this pallatis, that it vvarmeth, and temperethe, the could air which
we draw in through the nose and through the mouth to the lunges, because
through the extreme could thereof, it might not chance to indammage the
foresayed tunge, wherefore they which have no palate, find themselves
allvvayes very much altered, changed, and a great could allvvayes one the
breast.

Of the swelling of the Almondes, and of the vlcerations of the same. Chap. 6.
ONe both sides of the palate, and in the hider parts thereof, in the
angustnes of the throat, which of the Greeckes is called Isthmos, hath nature
created and formed, two Almondes, the one right opposite against the other,
which are therefore called Paristmia: which be cause of the form, figure, and
qvantitye thereof, are vvholye likened to an Almonde, are for that occasion
called Almodes: there functione, and office is, that they retain in them, a
certain sputaminous humiditye, which sinks descends out of the head,
therbye not only to refresh moyste the mouth, the circumiacent parts of the
same but alsoe the throat, and the entrance of the stomach, because the tunge
in speaking might not vvholye be exsiccated and dried, and that the continual
speech might not thereby be hindered, and interrupted.

These foresayed Almondes, are situated, The use, situatione compositione, of
the Almondes.
in a moyste, and warm place, and considering there spongious, nature, are

vvherthroughe the passage of eating, drinckinge, breathing is stopped,

Remedies against the tumefaction Antiades.
The assuredest and most convenient remedy is, that we cause the praesentlye
to be phlebotomised, after that they shall at the first have used a glister:
and the in like sort cause him to be let blood under the tuge: that we box
the behind in the neck, that they use gargrises, which are indifferentlye
frigifyinge, and astringent.

Signs howe to know when the tumefacted Almodes, exvlcerates and there
remedies
This tumefactione oftentimes is of such a magnitude, that it exvlcerateth:
which easily we may espy, when as we perceave it to be rumpeled, and
vvhytishe, and when as before there hath bin any prickige therein. Which

vlceratione, diminishes intercepts the payne, and inflammatione thereof,
adminestreth better passage unto the meat, and drink, and finallye,
prologeth the breathe. It is right true, that we allvvayes in the first, ought
by all means endevoure to mitigate the payne, and cool the caliditye of the
vlceratione as much as is possible.

If so be these said Almodes, are groovve so lunge harde, with out any

the history of Albucasis shall at this time suffice, to instruct the younger
Chyrurgian, to imitate the same, which affirmethe to have had a woman in
hands, with such an vlceratione vvherbye her throat was almost occluded
and stopped, that with great difficultlye she respired, breathed, nether
could she any more eat, or drink, being in great peril, danger of

whom this vlceratione was so extreamlye svvollen, that it had yet spread
it self abroad into two branches more, into the nostrils. In which great
necessity, and perplexitye, with al expeditione he took, a little hooke,
wherewith he laid houlde one the one branch thereof, which was come into

immediately opened her mouth began to eat, drink water. At other seasons
alsoe thereafter he hath cut away there fro other pieces, but yet
notvvithstadinge, as oft as much as he descided cut thereof, is increased
again grovvne thereunto: which when he had marked, he cauterised the same, by
this means in the end hath praevented the excrescence of this tumefactione:
but being constrained to take his journey to some other place, could never
hear, or by any man understand, how it happened thereafter with her: it may
right well be thought, that the cauterisinge of this dissease unto his root,
could not in any sort increase again, as before it had done.

It happens allsoe sometimes, that the Almondes so chance to svvelle, that
there is no hope left, that we should perforate, or thrust them through, or
to bind tie the, because sometimes it is impossible for the patient to open his

smother choke. When as therefore we would effect this, we must cause the

Howe we ought to draw, thrust out, and cut, of the teeth. Chap. 7.
Divers Chyrurgicall operations.
THere are divers disseases inscidente unto the teeth, which through the

vvhyte tough matter, sotimes like vnslissed lime, or as it vveare some

any dissease in the stomach, which in times passed have bin infected vvithe

the other with a little alume water whereby the increasinge of this matter is
sovvhat praevented, retarded, Practise invention of the aucthore.
it fastens allsoe the gummes unto the teethe, when as they hage looselye
thero. Some there are which esteem this a great secrete which heretofore I

If there be any tooth corrupted, foraminous, concavous stinking, that cavseth

How we ought to draw a tooth.
When as therefore we would draw forth a tooth we must cause the patient to
sit in such a sort as is most coveniet, the make choice of the corrupted
rotten tooth, because we do not mistake the one for the other, which we
must make loose fro the gumes, fill up the cocavity or hollovvnes of the tooth

of the same: having houlde thereon, we must gently easily crushe the tooth

we vvaggle the tooth up or down that part of the cheek bone wherein the

disioyne, and pluck out of ioyncte the nethermost cheekebon, cause great
perturbation in the uppermost chavve eve to the eyes. The tooth being dravven,

The assuredest remedy which I ever have known to be used, is this, that

The French Chirurgerye THE SIXTE TREATISE OF THE OPERATIone of Chyrurgerye
Containing nine Chapiters.
Of that, vvherone we must consider, before we make an apertion in avayne.
Chap. 1. How we ought to phlebotomive, or open avayne. Chap, 2. VVhero we
ought to consider, after we have opened the vain, when she bleeds. Chap: 3.
Of the number of veins, arteries, which are used most comonlye to be opened.
Chap. 4. Of the apertione, of the Arteries. Chap. 5. Of Anevrisma, or broken
arterye, howe we ought to make an inscisione therein, after what manner we
ought to bind them. Chap. 6. Of the Cirsotomia, which is, how we ought to
make our inscision in the Varices, or burst veins. Chap. 7. Of horseleeches,
and of there use, and how we ought to apply them. Chap. 8. Of boxes, and
howe we must apply, and use them. Chap. 9.
Whereon we must consider, when as we desire to open a vain, or phlebotomize.
Chap. 1.
Intention, porpose of the author.
My purpose, and intent, is not in this place to discrib unto you, what
sicknesses reqvire phlebotomye, in what age, in what time or season of the
year, in what country, in what costitutio of the body, it must be done, of
what occasion, namelye especially, whether it be for any simple
evacuation, or for any deprivatione, or derivatione, or for any revulsione. In
like sort alsoe in what vayn we ought to make the apertione, what qvantitye
of blood we ought to detract out of the same, in what sort, and how much
at one time, howe often after the other or in what time of the sickness we
must make the apertione in the vain.

But my purpose, and meaning is, only to show, how we ought to open a
vain: vvheron we must think, dilligently consider, before we make the
apertione therein, after we have opened her: vvherone we ought to not, when
the blood issveth out of the same. This doing, shall the Chyrurgiane, be no
less vvorthye of praises, because he ca very excellently phlebotomize, then
the physitione is praise vvorthye, by his perfect knowledge, whether the
patient have need of phlebotomy, or not: Why phlebotomye is difficult,
daugerous.
For although, that it seems to be a small sciece, to phlebotomize aright,
very well, yet notvvithstandinge is it oftentimes, a difficult matter very
daugerouse, because the veins, are situated sometimes close to the Arteryes, yea
alsoe thervppen, as alsoe are the synnues, Accidents which are caused, take
there original of there hurtinge of any vain, synnue, arterye, or tendone.
the tedones. If so be therefore we chance, with the lancet to hurt a
Tendone, thereafter most commonly issues a Spasmus, or a Gangrene and
mortificatione, vvherthroughe the patient pitifullye ruefullye, and with great
torment ends his life. If there be then any arterye touched, opened, she
very difficultlye will be cured, whereby the patient bleeds to death: when as
we descide a vain qvite asunder, both the ends thereof are immediately
retracted, and drawn invvardes, the one end this way, the other end that
vvaye, so that under the flesh both of the ends are lost, no blood ca
possibly issve therout: if she be then with to great timorousnes fear
pricked, the skin only wherewith she is covered is as the inscided, the
vayn not opened: or else if she be only pricked with the poyncte of the
lancet, the blood as the issveth therout droppingevvyse and the subtilest
blood only comes out, where coseqvently thereafter she exvlcerateth.

Sometimes alsoe lies the vain occulted so deep in the flesh, that we can
not with the lacet find the, without great pains, yea also although we
espy them, yet through there perpusillitye, rotuditye, they avoid, eschevve
the poyncte of the lacet, how acute subtle soever the same be. So that there
are oftetimes divers occasions, wherefore phlebotomye is difficult, which to
an other which never hath done it seems to be very easy.

Before the Chyrurgian makes his apertio in the vain, if so be the patient be
restringed bovvnde in his belly, hath in a loge time not bine at stool, we
must the first of all cause him to use a Clistere, because through the
phlebotomye, the veins beige evacuated, emptied, do not attracte, draw unto
the out of the guts or entralles, any corrupted, rot humours, vvherbye any
of the vvorthyest parts might be offend, hindered.

Nether is phlebotomye expediet, Whereon we ought to consider before
phlebotomye.
when as the stomach, is burdened, ether with any cruditye, of vndigested
meat, or drink, or with any other viscositye vvhatsoever, as also it is
vvholye dissvvaded to do after any great evacuatione, or any other
occasion, whereby the patient might be debilitated, as is superfluous
parbrakinge, a great laske, great abstinence, continualle vigilatione, and
great conversation with vvoemen. When as therefore the Chyrurgiane in the
absence of the Physician, hath on all these foresayed things dilligently
considered, he must as then defer the phlebotomye. And if it so chanced,
that any person to praevent any dissease desired to be phlebotomized, he must
then cause it to be done, when as he is best of courage, as being freed from
all care, of sorrow, of ire, and beside all this, we must not phlebotomize
any person, which is to timorouse, fearful of phlebotomye, because the
afrightednes, the fear, causes the blood to vvithdravve it self towards
the internal parts of the body.

Howe we should convenientlye phlebotomize.
And the patient as yet being lusty, strong, we must then cause him to sit in a
stool, but if so he be feeble debile, is subjected unto fayntnes sovvndige,
as those are, which are of a hot nature, we must in the first let him sup
in a soft dressed egg, or a morsel of bread sopped in vvyne, then cause him
to lye one the bed, half sitting upright, stuff him under with cushes as
if he sate. And above all we must dilligently note, that the light of the
air, or shining of the candle, do rightly shine one the vain, because that
through the shaddovv therofe, the knovvledg of the vain, of the place where
she must be pierced, be not vanished out of your sight: then must the
Chyrurgiane, with his right hand, take the right hand of the patient, or
with his left hand, the left hade of the patient, out of the which he means
to draw the blood out depressinge the arm sovvat down vvardes, and then
with his left hand, or with a vvarmed table naptkinne, rub the insyde of
the arm where he intends to make the apertione: Double band in Phlebotomye.
And then bind the arm double a little above the elbovve, with a little
narrovve list of vvollen clothe, not to stiff nor to loose, because to
looselye bovvnde, or to stifflye tied do both of them hinder the bleedinge:
the must the patient, shut his hand close, whereby as well through the
binding, as through the closinge of the hade, the veins lye fast steadfastly
in the flesh, and not move this way or that way when we should pearce them,
vvherthroughe also they raise themselves, and svvell the more, through the
blood, that by this means is draw thither vvardes, by the which alsoe we
may the easier see, and feel them.

How we ought convenientlye, to make an apertione in the vain. Chap. 2.
Having dilligetlye considered all these things if the Chyrurgiane,
Continvance of the manner rightly to phlebotove.
determine to phlebotomise in the right arm, he must then take houlde one the
foresayed arm with his left hand, by the bending of the arm, or about that
place, wherein we purpose tO make the apertione, lay his thumb one the vain,
a little belovve the place, where we will prick, because that so he may
hovvlde the vayn stedefaste, without glidinge this way or that way because
sometimes the vain being, spanned like a little cord, might chance to
rovvle, glide under the lancet. And because the foresayed vain is oftentimes
implete with wind, vetosityes, she therefore yields, slideth away under
the lancet, vvherthroughe we can not fully pearce her, by this means the
apertione falls to small: all this being in this sort noted, we must with
the first finger of the right hand feel where with most convenience we
might make the apertione, setting an impression on the skin above the vain
where we purpose to prick, with the nail of your finger, then immediately,
take the lancet in the right hand, which between our lips we must have
in a readiness, with the which we finelye must make our apertione, in the
vain, causinge the poyncte, and acuitye of the same, gently to enter into the
concavitye of the vain, not abruptly, and rudely, with a hasty thrust. And
to make this apertione with more certayntye, without tremblinge of the
hands, the Chyrurgiane must take the lancet in the middle, with his thumb
his first finger, suffering his hand with three of his fingers to rest on
the arm, so lay his thumb and his first finger which hold the lacet one the
thumb which houldeth fast the vain, on this sort to hold fast his hand,
with out any tremefactione to use the same. Some there are which annoynt the
place wherein they intende to phlebotomise, with a little oil, or with
fresh butter, thervvith somewhat to mollifye, soften the skin, so with
more ease and with less payne, to pearce there through. To mollifye the
skin.
Others annoynte the acuitye of there lancet with oil because she might the
easier perforate the same, cause less payne, the perforatione, or vvounde
might better contain it self apert the blood if so be it wear gross, might
the subtiler, with more ease have his passage.

If so be then that the apertio be made with one thrust, it is the good, but
if not, we must immediately give an other prick, ether a little belovve the
same, or else a little above the sam, if the vain sufficiently demostrateth her
self. If therefore the apertio be to small, the blood issue out of the same to
subtilelye, so that we fear that we shall not draw sufficient blood enough
therout, then immediately we must thrust into the same hole, or apertione
again, a little dilate the same: because that oftentimes through to small an
apertion, the gross blood congeals before the apertione, and there
conseqventlye exvlceratethe.

Why we must give a staff to them in there hade which we Phlebotomize
Having convenientlye brought to pass this apertione, we must then give the
patient, in the hand of that arm wherein he is phlebotomized, a roud staff,
partly thero to rest his arm, as alsoe to turn rovvnde the foresayed staff
in his hand, because by this means the blood might the better shoot out:
the which if it do not rightly issue out, we must the mark, if the
ligature be not the cause thereof, as if it vveare to harde tyede, which as
then we must a little districte and make it loose, without vvholye looseninge
of the same.

Broad, narrow lancets.
The Chyrurgiane must alsoe have divers lancets, whereof the one must be a
little broader then the other: The broad lancets are very necessary when as
the veins lye high exalted, when we desire to make an ample apertion. The
small narrovve lancets are very necessary, when as the veins lye profoudlye
occulted in the flesh, and also when as in the high exalted vain, we
desire to make a small apertion: because sometimes the sick personne,
reqvireth a great, festivous phlebotomy, to the which end, we must make an
ample apertione, as we must alsoe do, if we conjecture the blood to be
gross, dece, when the patient is lusty, and strong.

Contrarilye, if so be we retract, draw back the blood, which
supernaturallye hath issued out of any part of our body, as in those which,
spit blood, or those which bleed much out of the nose, as then we must
make our apertion small, because that great evacuation of blood is not
necessary for them, because through there noses they have avoided sufficient,
then only in such accidetes, where in is only reqvired a regressione of
blood. Besides, it is necessary expediet, that in the foresayed evacuatione,
and sickness, we suffer the blood to issve lunge enough out of the apertione
of the vain, which the patient should not be able to suffer, if the
foresayed apertione vveare ample, because there would issue to much blood
otherwise therout.

In the Phrenesy we ought to make but a small apertione.
We are also in phlebotomye urged to make a small inscisio, in those, which
are troubled with Phrenesye, in those which are grovvne mad, because that
little vvounde might with all expedition be cured again, because that such
raging* frantic persons will all ways make loose the ligature or dressing
before wear thereof avvare, whereof they might com in danger of bleedinge to
death, but the apertione being small, although they loosen the arm, yet
notvvithstandinge they can not violently bleed because in such a small
apertione, the blood congeals, occludeth, stops the vvounde. We ought to
make in small veins, a small apertione, in great veins an ample apertione:
for if so be we make in a great vavne a small apertion, the should
necessarily follovve, that the blood, which coagulateth in the small veins,
be a hinderace unto the issuinge or running out of the blood.

Touching the form, figure of the inscisione, it is effected in three sundry
sorts, and fashons: whereof the first is made overthvvarte, the second
according unto the length of the vain, vvherthroughe the vain is cleft or
splitted, not pricked: the third is the mean between them both, which we
may well call the contradictorye apertione, because cotradictorilye she is
made. Three forms of inscisions in phlebotomye.
We make the apertione overthvvarte, when as we are not minded to reiterate
the phlebotomy: for when we bend the ellbovve, the both the ends of the
vain join together again, This figure or form is necessary, when as we
desire to make a large apertione. We must make the apertione sydelonge or
contradictorye, when we purpose to iterate the phlebotomye: and we making
the apertione in this manner, very seldom or never miss the vain: and
which is more, it is better for the circumstantes, and standers by, when the
blood in this manner issues therout. That inscisione, which is made
according to the length of the vain, is very expedient, when we intende to
renew the blood lettinge, that not only the same day, but one the next day
alsoe, because that when we bend the elbovve, both the labia, or lips of
the vayn, do separate divide themselves.

But in what form soever we make the inscisione, The vain must be inscided
in her middle.
yet notvvithstadinge the vain must be pierced in the middle, without vvholye
cuttinge a sunder of the same. Because her lips, might chance to be inverted
invvardes, and the blood could not resulte out of the apertione, but run
down a lunge by the arm, or both the ends of the vain, are draw
invvardes, without deliverace of any blood from her, or at the least, in the
first very little.

Whereon we ought to mark, after the thrust, apertione of the vain, and when
the blood issues therout. Chap. 3.
Although the vain be well opened, yet she drops through the afrightednes
of the patient
WE may know, that the vain most commolye is well opened, as easily we may
perceave by the blood, when as in the first, it rousheth and as it vveare
leaps out in great haste, but immediately alters unto dropping, which
through the afrightinge of the patient is caused, for which reason the blood
is dravven invvardes. Which when it chances unto us, we must with patiece
abide, and unbind it somewhat, and encourage the patient, causinge him to move
his fingers, turn, Gross blood doth nothing but drop.
and crush the same in his hand. The apertione in the vain is sometimes ample
enough, but because the blood is gross, it can not issue out thereat, to the
which end we must put a little oil therine, which to that purpose is very
profitable.

If so be therefore the Chyrurgyane, in absence of the physitione, finds the
patient very feeble, and yet notvvithstandinge, follovvinge the rule, ordinance
of the physitione, is very necessary to be well phlebotomized, we must them
do it providentlye, that although, at the first we have not drawn so much
blood as the sickness or dissease reqvireth, we must then, some certain
hovvres thereafter again renew the phlebotomye for the second time, and if it
be needful yet once again, for the third time, without over charging of
the patient with to great phlebotomye at one time.

It might alsoe chance, that before we could detract such a qvantitye of the
patient as was ordained, and reqvired, the patient might chance to be
debilitated, and in danger to fall in great fayntnes, sovvndinge, vvheron the
Chyrurgiane must dilligently consider: as he may easily perceave it, when the
patient vvaxeth pale, and oppressed at his heart, his pulls diminishinge, when
the blood runs down by his arm. What we ought to do when we perceave
the fayntinge to approach the patient.
Which when it chances, the Chyrurgian must then immediately seas the
bleedinge, laying his thumb, one the apertione of the vain, lay the patient
on his back, with his head one a cushen, sprincklinge could water in his
face, and give him a little vvyne in his mouth, and cause him to smell at some
vinegar, and then have a little patience, until that again he revives, comme
to himself: for as soon as he shall be come to his former strength again, we
may as the finish the phlebotomye.

The patient lying fiat on his back ca very well suffer phlebotomye.
Some there are which in any sort can not endure phlebotomye, how lovve
soever they sit, although it vveare on the bed, or although we continually
held vinegar before there nose, or gave them vvyne to drink, yet
notvvithstandinge they lying prostrate on the bed, with there head
reasonable high, they can as the very well suffer and abide the foresayed
phlebotomye, although we draw a good qvantitye of blood from them: As of
late I my self have known to happen, in an honourable, and coragious
gentleman.

After that we have detractede a reasonable qvantitye of blood out, we must
then dissolve and make loose the ligature, and crush out the blood of the
vain, least that the blood chance to congeal therine, and coagulate, and
so exulcerate: and if so be there appeerede any little parcel of pingvedity,
or fat, we must the gently thrust it in again with the head of a pin,
and not cut it of, and then vvipe of the blood which cleaves is dried or
exciccated to the arm, lay a little compress, one the apertion, which is
madefyed in could water, and wind the ligature, tvvice or thrice about the
elbovve, like a borgondian cross, without tyinge both the ends fast, before
you have caused the patient to bend his arm, to lay the ligature thereafter.
The Ligatione of the elbovve after phlebotomye.
This ligatione may not be to stiffly bovvnde, because the cicatrice by this
means, may qvicklye be sitvatede on the apertione of the vain. The arm
being thus fouldede together if so be the patient be able to vvalke he must
vveare his arm before on his breast in a scarf if so be he lye on his bed,
he must gently lay the same beside him, with out much movinge of the foresayed
arm, nether must he lye therone: for there have bin some, which in there
sleep have had there arms violently to bleed, with great danger of there
lives. How we may stop restraygne blood
The blood issues sometimes with such violence out of some parts of our
body, that with no copresses or ligatures we can restraygne the same:
which happeninge, we are the constrained to lay above one the top of the
apertione, the one half of a great bean, and the compresse therone, and in
this sort bind it together,

There remains sometimes a nigreditye or blacknes, Danger that happens to
phlebotomye.
viriditye or greenes about the apertione, but thereof ensues no evell, unless
any other accident chanced thereunto.

When as therefore we desire to renew the phlebotomy, To renew the
phlebotomye.
we must then lay one the inscisione saulted oil, because that hinders the
curing of the vvounde, and the fault keeps the blood from coagulation,
vvherthroughe the apertione of the vvounde is stopped, And if so be the
vvounde vveare so stopped, that the blood would difficultlye issue therout,
we must not then rigerously stretch out the arm, which the patient hath
carried on his breast dubbed, nether depress the vayn with to great violence,
to get the blood therout: Because such violence might cause great pain, and
inflammatione, To renew the phlebotomye.
but we must rather with a small privett or searching iron, remove that blood
which therein is exciccated dried, or rather once again make an inscisione,
after that we have bovvnde the arm somewhat higher, then the elbovve, as
before we have said.

How we ought to open the veins or arteryes of the teples of the head.
When as we desire to make an apertione in the veins or arteryes of the
temples of the head, of the forehead or under the tunge, we must then cause
the patient gently to vvring about his neck a table naptkine, or a tovvell,
thereby to cause the blood to ascede, on high, the veins to svvel, which we
intend to open. How we ought to open the veins of the hands feet.
And when as we desire to open the veins of the hands or feet we must
bath them in warm water, because through caliditye vvarmethe of the water,
the foresayed veins might erect themselves, the blood, the vain being
opened, might the better issue out thereof.

Of the number of veins, arteryes, which commonly, use to be opened in mans
body. Chap. 4.
THe aunciete professors of Chyrurgerye, have observed, certain veins, which
in mans body must be opened, according as everye divers dissease shall
reqvir: whereof the Chyrurgiane must not only know there situatione, there
divisione or separatione, but alsoe the names of the sam, because that he do
not mistake the one from the other. Vain of the forehead.
They which most commonly are opened, are 41. whereof there are in the head
17 even: the first whereof is called the vain of the forehead, which is
situated in the midst of the forehead, and is opened when as we have any
inveterated pain in the occipitialle parts of the head to trouble, molest us.
The second is called Vena Pupis, Vena Pupis.
which is situated right in the midst of the occipitialle, or hinder parts
of the head: this vain is opened, against the soporiferousnes, payne of the
head, which is situated in the forehead. The temporal vain.
The third is called the temporalle, or vain of the teples, whereof in each
side there is on, which in divers branches ascedeth in the temples of the head:
we open those veins against the superfluous lachrimation of the eyes, against
vehement payne in the ears, against the Hemicrania, which only comes in on
side of the head. Eeare vain.
The fourth is called the ear vain, one each side one, have there place,
situatione behind the ears: this vain is opened against surditye, Eye vayn.
payne, vlceration of the ears. The fifth is the eye vain, the which in the
great corner of the eye, close by the nose, we may perfectly see: she is
opened, against all disseases of the eyes, eyeliddes. The sixth is, Nosevayn
the nosevayne which hath her place in the midst of the end of the nose,
between the two gristles or cartilages: she is opened, against the heavines
of the head, against all reumes, of the eyes, eyeliddes. The seventh,
Lippevayne.
is the lip vain, whereof one each side are, two, in the internal parts

the palate, more other disseases of the throat. The ninth is very public,
lying in the neck, and is called the Iugularis, Iugularis.
vain, of the Arabians Gvides, on each side of the neck one. She may very

In the arms are six, in every arm three which in the fouldinge of the

Basilica,

We open this vayn, against the stoppinge of the Liver, against all

as well concerning her situatione, as original, because she takes her

In the hands, we have six, in each hand three. Eye vayn in the hand,

situated in the hockes, or fovldinge of the knees, is opened against all

which we open one the insyde of the leg under the ankle, in all disseases

Amongst all the other which are most comonlye opened, are those three which
in the folding of the arm are phlebotomized: to wit the head vayn Basilica
the media. Basilica, or liver vain is dangerous to be phlebotomised.
We must dilligetlye consider that when we make an inscisio theri that under
the Basilica or Liver vain lies an artery or great heart vain: under the
Median a synnue or tendone of the muscle Biceps or both of these together, but
under the Cephalica is nether synnue artery or tendone sitvated, Cephalica or
head vain is opened without danger.
wherefore amongst all other veins, there is none which with less perril
danger may be opened.

If so be that through mischance in opening of the vain Basilica we chased
to hurt the artery which there under is sitvated as I have knoovvne to have
chased, we must praesetlye for the stoppinge and restrayninge of the blood,
and to the curing of the arterye, without leavinge any Aneurisma, Remedy for
a vvouded arterye.
cleave a bean in two pieces, lay the one half of the bean, one the
apertione of the vain, with a compress therone, gently tied, without
visiting of the same in three or four days, or once offer to touch it. If so
be in the apertion of the mediane, we chanced to prick the subter situated

same, thereby to keep the apertione aperte, then lay a plaster of
Diacalcitheos, rovvnde about the wounded part of the whole arm, which
hath bine liquefacted, with oil of roses, and vinegar.

Of the Arteriotomia, or apertion of the Arterys, or hartvaynes. Chap, 5.
COncerning the Arteriotomia, or apertione of the Arteryes, the praedicessors
ancient professors vveare vvont to effect it especialle behind the ears, in
the temples of the head, against all continual, rebellious fluxions, Rheumes
of the eyes, in like sort alsoe, against all diseases of the head, which have
take there original, fro hot, dampishe, or subtle rheumes: as yet now a
days we do, but not who lye as they vveare vvonte to do, in opening of
the same: for so far forth as if the Arterye be small they then cut her
clean a sunder, they also cut a piece thereof away, both the ends are dravven
invvardes, where by she as then bleeds no more.

And if the Arterye, be great, violently beats, it is the surest way, that
we tie her under above and then between both those ligations cut her of, but
the thredes wherewith we bind her, must be strong closly tied because that
through the continvall beating of the arterye, the thredes loose, the arterye
opens, if so be it be not stiffly bovvnde: because that she should not
chance to corrupt, before the inscision be replete grow full of flesh: where
through the mouth of the foresayed artery is stopped. How we use now adays
to to open an artery
But now a days we only make a simple inscision in the artery in such a
manner as we make an apertion in the veins, without cutting of the same
clean of having dravven as much blood therout as we desire, we the lay a
little plaster of masticke on the apertion, a little compresse tied thereon
rovvnd abovt the whole head as close as is possible. I know right well that
there are some which houlde this apertione of the arteryes very suspect,
because it ca hardly be stopped again, and in doing this, there remains a
cicatrice, in those parts which are situated rovvnde about the foresayed
arterye, before the same is fully cured, and there through often times an
Aneurisma caused, which is very troublesome, Aneurisma is a dangerous
dissese.
and dangerous for the patient. But I may with verity affirm it to be true,
that oftentimes I have seen opened the arteryes of the temples of the head,
without any of the foresayed accidents being happened thereunto, the which I
coucel the yonge Chyrurgiane to do it only in this place, because such an
inscisione, is more fitter, less dangerous, the the whole cuttings of, and
ligature of the same.

Of the swelling Aneurisma, and of the means howe to bind, and cut of the
same. Chap. 6.
Description of the tumefaction Aneurisma.
THis tumefactione Aneurisma, is caused most commonly, through the dilatation
of an arterye, which only we must understand of the small Aneurismata,
being impossible, that the arterye, should so dilate, as it vveare vnshutt in
the great Aneurismata which oftentimes we see: vvherfor we will rather
say, and houlde with the opinione, of the aunciet professors, that Aneurisma
is then caused, when as the blood, and the vital spirits, are repulsed out
of the arteryes, through the apertion, or orificia of the same, which we
call Anastomosin, or else when as the tunicle of the arterye is burst, it be
ether through a vvounde, or by any other occasion: as we may se, when as
the Chyrurgiane, purposinge to opene the vain in the elbovve by chance
prickethe the arterye which is theruder sitvated the skine which is thero
lying, cicatrizeth it self and the perforatio of the artery, through her
cotinvalle reverberatioe, tarrieth uncured, open is not stopped, or with any
carnal substance replete, as being unprofitable for any use in no sort can
be bound so close as the arterye of the temples of the head, but through the
blood vitalle spirits, which by degrees issue therout, are congregatede under
the skin, so conseqventlye cause the svvellig supposing they very well
did know, therein to be matter, or any other slimy substance, or viscositye,
for which reason, they have made an apertione therein, vvhervppon a little time
thereafter death hath followed, because of the blood, and of the vital
spirits, which in great hast have rushed therout, without any remedy howe
to restraygne them. Now perfectly to know such a svvellinge, and to
discern it from other tumefactios, Signs of this tumefactio.
we must observe, that in this tumefactione there is, a continual
reverberatio, the foresayed tumor, being of one colour with the skin,
howe great or small, the inflatione be, it is alsoe tether, and soft in the
touching of the same, glidinge away under the finger when as we depress
it, yea and almost through the foresayed depressione vvholye vanishes out of
our sight, considering the blood of the vital spirits, which through the
foresayed depressio, are crushed in the arterye, wherefore they alsoe, as
passing through a little apertio which violence, do make a noise, or
sovvnde, which having taken away, and removed the finger immediately shoot
again in there foresayed concavitye, as alsoe again in there forth coming, of
the foresayed angust apertione, we may hear a certain sovvnde: which
comonlye chances when as this Aneurisma, is caused through an Anastomosis,
and not of any vvounde, because, the Orificium being apert, the vital
spirits, as being most subtle, are before the blood driven out, so that the
whole tumefaction, is almost replete with vital spirits. But if so be the
Arterye be burst, there as then issues much blood therout, which brings
in the tumefaction more blood, the vital spirits, wherefore alsoe it is more
obdurate, and harder, and the said blood coagulateth, and therein rots, and
corrupts.

As much as concerns the curatione of this tumefactione, it consists only,
Curation of Aneurisma.
in the bindinge of the foresayed arterye, and especially the same beige
somewhat thick, for they which are great, and especially the arteryes of
the neck, the arm pits, or of the flanckes, may or can in no sort be
tied, because it is impossible to find them, make them bare, and if we allsoe
make an inscisione therein there then follows such a quantitye of blood, and
of the vital spirits, that most commolye the patient, dies under the hands
of the Chyrurgiane.

That artery, which is situated in the bending of the arm may easily be
cured, Of an arterye which of the Author was tied above the Aneurisma
as out af this subsequent history we may note. My lord of Maintenon,
desired me to visit the son, of my lord, of Belleville, which through
phlebotomy in the bending of his arm, had gotten a little Aneurisma, which
through continuance of time, is grovven as great as a fist, wherein, in the
end the blood which therein was included, coagulated: so that in the
foresayed tumefaction Aneurisma, there came a corrupting and rottennes, which
he one the out side of the foresayed skin, of the svvellinge he espyede,
which from the vital and livinge colour was changed into a black, and
purple colour, vvherebye alsoe there was an apertione caused in the skin: To
the remedyinge, and curing of the which, especially the superfluous
effluxione of bloede, which might thereof ensue, the loss alsoe of the vital
spirits, if so be the apertio as yet vveare dilated: I as then councelled,
the Physicians, and Chyrurgians, to praevent farther evells, that we ought to
tie, bind the Arterye, (which was situated, and his place in the bending of
the arm) somewhat higher then the Aneurisma, to the which opinion,
propositione, in the end they all consented, agreed: The which alsoe with
happy successione of all causes was brought to pass, in the praesence of the
vvorpshipfull master Drovet, Doctor of Physic, at Beauvais, and of the
Chyrurgiane dvvellinge at Avet, which vveare come thither to cure him.

On what sort the Author tied this Arterye.
First of all, I considered, on the arterye, in the uppermost, and inner part of
the fore arm, as she from above descedeth under the armpittes, unto the
bedinge of the arm, three fingers breadth therabove, on the which place when
I had seen, and fixede mine eyes therone, I made an inscisione, in the skin
according to the length therofe, which lay opened right above the Arterye
where by tacture we might feel her, which when I had found, and discovered
her, I thrust a crooked needle thervnder, wherein was a strong thread, where
with I tied the foresaeyde Arterye, with a double knott: this being done, I
took away, and purifyed, all the congealed, or coagulated blood, all other
impuritye out of the vvounde or svvellinge, and have washed the same with
Aqvavitae, Remedy against the putrefactione.
in the which I mixed a little Aegyptiacum, by this means to cleanse it the
better from all putrefactions: and the patient, is within a month thereafter
ensuing, vvholye cured, and grovvne sovvnde, with out retayninge any lamnes
in his arm: wherein I my self have vvondered. If so be that in any other
external part, there chanced to come any Aneurisma into the Chyrurgians
hands, he must then know, that allvvayes for certayntye, he shall find the
arterye in her uppermost part, or discover, and bare her, so bind, and tie
her with out any more ceremonies.

Of the Cirsotomia, that is, of the manner, howe we ought to cut of the
varices. Chap. 7.
Discriptione of a Varice.
VArices, or burstene veins, are troublesome, because of there greatness, their
extracting, thickness, and their gibbositye above there nature, property.
VVherfor, ether because they cause payne, and hinder the action of that part,
or else because they soacke in some certain vlceratione, with some humiditye,
(whereby she can not be cured) we are urged to open them, and cut themof,
Curation of a Varice.
or else with some actual cauterye, to cauterize them, so consume, and bring
them to nought. To which purpose, the ancient professors, Council of the
ancient professors for the curing of the Varices.
have ordained, that we should cut out, and clean take away, those which
lye croockedlye and dubbelye folded, in divers rovvnde revolutions, or else lye
entangled the one with the other. But before we proceed to such an
operatione, it is necessary, that first of all, we bath that part with hot
water, to cause the gross blood somewhat to separate, and vvexe or grow
subtle, and the veins to svvel and exalt themselves.

But the most gentlelest remedy, is that which daily we use which is the

when as we have phlebotomised, prohibitinge the patient, not to stir, or
go, which said Varices, if so be they chanced to svvell again we must as
before a little distance there after make therein an other apertione.

But if so be we intende vvholy to discide, and cut them out, we must first
of all, How we ought to cut away a burst vain.

The aunciete Chyrurgians have cauterized the Varices,
That burst vain, which runs right, although she lye overthvvarte, if

This operatione being after this sort finished they applied therone, some

Of the Horseleeches of there use, howe we may apply them. Chap. 8.
THe Horseleeches are little, Descriptione of Horseleeches,
and perpucill creatures, like vvormes, of the longitude of a finger, or
theraboute, nether are they of any great crassitude, or thickness, unless, it be
when they with sucking of blood are grouvven thick. The end of there head
hath a rovvnde hole, much like unto a little lamprell, wherein are to be
beheld, and seen three stings, or acuityes, proceeding out of three corners,
which so violently, strongly prick, that thervvith they can pearce the
hides skins of all creatures vvhatsoever, vvherone they depede fast
hanging, until such time as they have sucked themselves rovvnde, and fall of
with there one accorde. They live, are ingedred in the water, especially in
all stagnes, standing vvaters, because they delight, and are solaced, in
filthy, and muddye places. I esteem, suppose, that there is no Chyrurgiane,
but very well knows them because of all men they are knovven, but everye
one doth not know which are the venomous, or not venomous, which
notwithstanding, is very necessarve to be known, considering the accidents,
mischances, which might thereof ensue, and follovve, as in example, great
tumors, Inflammations, Messalinus dyed of the bytinge of a venomous
Herseleech,
venomous vlcerations, through there venomous momorsiones bitinges, yea also

in his knee, dyed thereof, is vvitnessinge unto us.

The vonoumous, are as well known, discerned by there greatness, as there
colour, Signs of a venomous Horseleech,
and alsoe by the place where they are caught. For those which are thick, and
have a thicker head then the rest of there whole body, shining, like unto

black strokes, or lines, and vveare caught in standing pooles, where all
manner of foetide, stinckinge Carrions, with more other filthines is thrown
in, they are all venomous, wherefore in any sort we must not use them.

But those, which are small, rovvnd, Signs of good Horseleeches.
have a little head, are of colour much like unto a liver a rovvnde belly,

running vvaters, they are not venomous, we may safely, and with all

of scarifyinge: we apply the most commonly one such places of the body,

Before therefore we apply them, because they might be hungry, nether retain

Which being in this sort praepared, to apply one the body, we must first

And so far forth as she sucks not strong enough, or if so be we desire

As soon as the one Horseleeche is fall of, if we please, we may apply an

with his finger so lunge therone, How we shall restrain the blood
