small compresse, bind the same therone, if so by any means possible it may
be done.

Of Boxes, and howe we may apply them. Chap. 9.
A Box, is an instrumete, of Surgery, the magnitude where of, must
aeqvallye be proportioned, according to the greatness of that part, vvheron
we purpose, and intende to apply them, alsoe divers forms, figures: Difference
of Boxes*
for some there are which are short, thick, others which have a lunge neck,
belovve vvyde, which commonly draw better the any other. Others are of divers
substance, for there are some of Copper, others of Horne, of Tin, and of
glass, which we most commonly use, because through the glass we might
see, whether they draw much blood or not. There are alsoe some which are
made of vvoode, and some baked of earth: yea for want of Boxes, we may use
little vvoodde dishes, or little earthen pots. Touching there form, they
must have a vvyde mouth, and a reasonable broad belly, thick rovvnde edges,
because in the applying thereof they should not chance to hurt.

And if so be they are very great, they must the have a little hoale on the
one side, which with vvaxe must be occluded, before we apply the that we may
give the air, when we will take of the same, some there are, which lay a
little stick, cross over in the mouth thereof, on the which they impose a
little piece, or inch of a candle, which in the application thereof they
incende, and light.

How we shall apply the boxes.
The manner of applicatione is this. That we first of all, perfricate, and
rub the place Vvherone we intend to apply the situatinge that part of
applicatione, in the right form thereof, because the muscles, may lye in there
right situatione, and places, and not be recurved this way, or that vvaye,
because the boxes being thereon fastened, might not vvithe the reflection of
the part, or ioyncte, fall thereof, when as the foresayed recurved muscles,
should reverte again to there accustomed locationes, and places. This being
done, we must impose therone a counter or any other piece of coin, with a
little, flock of flexe, or tovve incended in the midst of that place,
vvherone we desire to apply the Boxes, least that the fire chance to touche
the skin, vvheron immediately, we must vvhelve the Boxes, turning the same
a little rovvnde, because he should the better fasten thereon: the which as
then we must cover, with a double vvarmed clout. Some there are which in
the bottom of the Boxes apply and lay a plaster, and therone a little tovve,
in place of an inch of candle, which in the applicatio, theyt set one fire,
with a candle.

Discriptione of the horne Boxes.
As touchinghe the horns, they are vvyde above, and narrovve belovve, having a
little perforatione in there midst, and internally, a little leathern tuge,
which is very thin, which stops the foresayed hoales. They are applied
without fire, sucked with the mouth. To vvitt with a little pipe, or with
a quille, which we impose in the foresayed perforation, wherewith the
foresaid tunge is thrust backevvardes, which stops the horne, when it
hath dravven and sucked sufficient, dravvinge the pipe therout, the foresayed
tunge, internally shutteth, cleaves it self so close to the hole, as we may
see in a kind of ballon, when he is blovven up.

Those which are ignorant of this secrete, stop, occlude the hole with a
little vvaxe, which in deed is not so necessary, and needfulle. Those little
Boxes which are laid in warm water, are applied, after that we have putt
the flame of the candle, therein and must then with all expeditione be thereon
imposed.

The use of small boxes.
The use of these small Boxes, is three fouled: Namely thervvith to vvithdravve,
and repell, the blood, and humours, which are concursed to any place: to
draw forth any particular thing which nature ca not expel from her: to
draw, out or cause to consume, any occluded ventosityes, in any parts of our
body: wherefore we apply them one divers, sundry places: they are very good
and commodious to be placed behid in the neck, against all rheumes vvich are
incident unto the eyes: behind in the midst of the neck are they necessary
to be applied, for those which are short of respiratione, and troubled with
the cough: one both the shoulder blades, against payne, and dolor in the
head against Hemicraniam, against ophthalmye, and payne in the teeth: we
apply the alsoe in place of phlebotomye one the right hipp, against bleedige
at the nose: in like sort alsoe close to vvoemens breasts, when as there
menstruositye, too superfluouslye flovveth from them, and when there lye
included any ventosityes in the Liver: on the left side, when as there is any
vvynde retained in the Milte: on the Navelle against the vvynde colicke: on the
Vreteres, to cause the gravel to descede, which is contained, in the kidnyes:
on the rump, against the vlcerations, and the Hemorrhodes of the fundament: on
the hippes, against all vesicall disseases, and of the vvombe, and provocation
the monethlye sickness, or menstruositye.

To conclude, we may apply them, The Boxes may be applied one all parts of
mans body.
on all parts of mans body, yea allsoe one the self same place where the
pain is, which we endevoure to cure, to retract and draw therout all
humours, which are theri secluded, as when we desire to draw any humours
outvvardes, which lye profoundlye deeply occulted, and hid, or else alsoe,
any ventositye, which in the foresayed part lies enclosed, as on any bit,
or prick, of any venomous creature, least that the venom might chance to
penetrate and pearce, into some of the vvorthyest parts: one the Venus
botches, or Bubones, one the venoumouse, Pestilentialle Parotides. But
desiring to apply them against any superfluous efluxione of blood, we must
then situate them on the contrary side considering the alliance, communitye
of the veins which is between them, through the which the blood is
retracted, dravven back. It chances also sometimes, that we nether apply
the Boxes, on the disseased, or dolorous place, nether one the contrary side
thereof, but one that part which next, and proximately thereunto is situated:
as when we desire to suscitate and provoacke, the retained and kept back
mestruosityes, we as then place the Boxes, one the bone Pubis in the flanckes,
and allsoe one the flatnes of the hippes.

The Boxes, or horns, are sotimes applied with scarificationes, and sometimes
without: If so be we apply them without scarificationes, they then only
draw certain dampes unto them: but if there be any bad humours, in those
parts, we as then scarifye it: And the dissease proceeding out of winds,
and ventosityes, we then apply them without scarifications, but when we
are minded to scarifye any part, we first set the Boxes therone, and
having again removed, and taken the same of, we the prick, or scarify
therein, ether with a lancet, or with the poyncte of a razor, whether it be
deep, or not deep, according as we suppose, and judge the blood, to be
gross, or subtle, but allvvayes we must note not to scarifye deeper then the
skin: touching the number of the foresayed scarificatios, if so be we are
not intended to draw much blood there out, we may not as then make many
scarificatios: but if we intend to draw much blood therout we must then
make many scarifications: vvhero we must again apply the boxes, so that when
we desire to draw much blood therout, we must apply the foresayed boxes
two, or three times thereon, everye time scarifye the place, especially when
there is contained in that part any venoumouse ventositye, or gross, and
corrupt blood. In delicate, and dayntye persons, which are tender of flesh,
and have an aperte or open skin, in such persons we must scarifye but once,
notvvithstandinge must apply the boxes two, or three times after other thereon:
which being finished, and having vviped, and dried the part, we must then
apply one the scarlficatione, the Cerotum Galeni, or else the Vnguentum Rosarum.

THE SEVENTH TREATISE OF THE OPEratione of Chyrurgerye, Containing six
Chapters.
Of the Caries, or of the corruption rottinge of bones. Chap. 1. Of the
fistles of the Privityes or fundamente. Chap. 2. How we ought to draw forth
Childrene out of their mothers body, which of themselves can not be borne.
Chap. 3. Wherefore the externalle parts or ioynctes of the body, must be
extirpatede, as Arms and Legs vvheraboutes the same must in the fore said
ioynct be done. Chap. 4. How we ought to effect, the extirpation, or
dissection of any ioynct, restraynge the blood after we shall have laid
down the patient. Chap. 5. How we ought to extirpate the spoylede,
superflvous fingers, how we shall separat those fingers which are combinede,
grovven together. Chap. 6.
Of the Caries, and corruption of the bones. Chap. 1.
Disctiption of the natural bones The bones are as well subjected unto all
manner of disseases, as is the flesh.
THe bones, not being spoylede corrupt, are of natur vvhyt smooth, solide. They
are according to the sayings of ancient Chyrurgianes and our daily
experience, subjected unto all evell dispositions, which the flesh is
subjected unto, yea allso unto apostematios. Celsus avouchethe, that all bones
which have any impedimet, are ether hurt, corrodede, corrupted, burst,
broken, crushed, plettered, or out of ioyncte. We may praeiudice the bones to
be altered, To know the corrupted bones through sight.
or polluted, through our sight feeling, matter, and impuritye, which
therout issues: when we perceave it to be yellovv or purple, and at the last
black, as very learned lye, discretlye the said Author, writes, first the
corrupted bone vvaxeth fattye then black, Through feeling.
or putilaginous, that is corroded: when as in touching with the privet or
searchinge iro, we feel the bone to be rugged, and not plain, and the privet,
enters therein, as if it vveare a piece of corrupted, vvorme eaten vvoode,
especially we knovvinge the same not to have binn bared of his flesh, nether
hath binn of the air illuminated: because that sometimes the altered bone, for
that it hath a lunge while lain bare, is grow to be so harde, thick, there
without great pains, we can not fasten thero with the grating iron,
through the matter, which is thin, Through the matter.
subtle, clear, faetide, stinckinge, blackishe: when as rovvnd about the
vlceration especially, is regenerated a tender viscouse flesh where through
the vlceration can not, be cicatrized, although she be cicatrized, thereafter
redubleth herforces, breacketh open again. Occasion of the corruption of the
bones
Such alteration proceeds of some cocurret humours, that descede on the bared
bones, as appears when they are through soackede with any pestiferouse
humours, or else because they are of their flesh pellicle, or mebrane Periostio,
denudatede and barede vvherthrough they corrupt vvaxe dry for want of blood
wherewith they should be nourished, or else because they are to full of
humiditye, moisture, through the purulet matter, which cotinuallye thereon
distills, corrodeth: ether because they are to much anoyncted with oil, to
much fatty salves thero applied, whereby the vlceration is corrupted,
putrified: Or because she is polluted, with the matter of the vlceratione,
which supernaturallye, from above is descended thereon distilled.

VVheas therefore for certaynty we know that there is putrefactio, rottenes in
the bone we must then do our devoyr to know how lardge, profovvnd the same
is, because thereby we may the surer know, how with most conveniece we
should remove abolish the same cause therein a separatione, because it is
necessary that the vivificet part, expelle drive from it the mortified, or
else the mortifiede allso cause mortificatione in the vivificent parts.

Magnitude of the putrefactio.
Concerning the magnitude thereof, we may discern it through the sight,
unless it be operted with any viscoufe or slimy flesh, we supposing
doubting to be more putrefactione of bones, that is patefiede evidente unto
us, through the circumiacent parts of the vlceraration, which as then are
purple coloured through the spongiouse flesh, through the elevatione of the
edges of the vlceratione through the diuturnalle continuace of the same in one
estate: Hippocrates.
For as Hippocrates affirms: In the venomouse vlcerations, which for the
space of a year have continuede, or longer, it can not otherwise be, but
necessarilye in the bone under the flesh of the vlceratione, must needs be a
corruption, putrefaction a corrosione, some pieces thereof come out, the
Cicatrises also will continually be cocavouse, hollovve. To cure the
putrefacted bones.
We therefore certaynlye knovvinge this, we must as then dilate the vlceratio
denudate the bone, dilligently marck how large the foresayede putfactio is:
which must be effectede with causticke medicamentes, by the which the
spongiouse flesh, must be cosumede, according as the foresayede vlceratio
reqvireth. Celsus councelleth us that first we make an inscisione in the
skine, to denudate the bone, if so be the putrefaction of the bone, be greater
then the vlceratione, discidinge cuttinge of all the flesh rovvnde about, unto
the sovvnde bone: touching the depth of the putrefactione of the bone, we may
know it through the privet or searchinge iron, when as we feel thereon,
the which pearcinge deep or not deep in the same, shevveth unto us that the
foresayede corruption of bone is great or small. But if w see the bone to be
black dry as it happens bereft of blood the privet the will not enter
therein, wherefore the depth of such a corruption, can so certainly be knovven,
as through the perforative Trepane, or else through the little ground dravver,
wherewith we must perforate the foresayede bone through which perforatione
of bone, we easily shall perceave, whether the bone be black, vvhyte, or
red, whether there issuethe blood out or not if it be so, it is then a sign
that the putrefaction, of the bone is not deep. And if so be we perceave, the
perforated bone to be black, it is then a token, that the putrefactione is
deep, because the corruption of the bone, pierces so deep, when we
perceave that the bone is vvhyte, red, so far is it then incorruptede.

To the which Caries, or corrupting of boes we must use the actualle
Cauterye, or else allso the potentialle or the Raspatorium: If so be the
corruption of the bone penetrates not very deep, How log we ought to
raspe.
we must then grate the foresayed bone with a peculiar Raspatorye, in
raspinge of the same, stiffly crush thero, that we may qvicklye penetrate
through the corruption, the operatione, vvih all festinatione be effectede
done. All danger being now passed, we perceavinge the bone to be vvhyte,
smooth pure, solide, it is then sufficient: For it is impossible, that the
foresayede corruption of bones may have an end, as long as we do not
liberat, free the same, by one meas or other, from all danger, corruption. When
we perceave, in raspinge the bone to give blood from it, it is then a sign
that all corruption is therout, and that the bone is well disposed, because
that no corrupt or alterede bones will give from the any blood. This being
done, we as then use the poulder of Aristolochiae of Mirrha of Ireas of Aloe
to liberate the foresayed bone fro al corruption praevente the same to be any
more putrefacted and corrupted. If we suppose, that the Raspatorium, be not
sufficient enough, to remove the foresayed corruption, as when she is deeply
corroded invvardes, The fire is the surest remedy to repel all corruptions out
of the boes.
when as there is a peculiar corruption imprinted in the bone, the same alsoe
being fat, oylye, if so be the patient vveare not timorouse of the fire, the
afsuredest remedy as then is, that we cauterize the foresayed corruption,
with an actual Cauterye, which conforteth that part, and consumes all
venoumouse humours, with all festinatione causes the corrupted bones to
separate, causes little, or no pain at all, because the foresayed bone is
insensible, and doth not communicate his vnhemence unto the other circumiacent
parts.

When as therefore, Manner how to apply the Cauterye.
we desire to use it according to the depth, greatness of the corruption, or
Caries, we must therein use a mean, of applying the foresayed Cauteriu on the
bone, to vvitt until through the porositye of the bone there comes out, a
frothy matter, no lodger: for if we held it lodger thereon, it should through
his great callidity, through the exsiccatinge virtues thereof, not only
consume, the humeditye of the putrefaction of the bone but alsoe the natural
humiditye, moisture through which occasio, the flesh engenders increases
between the sovvnde the corrupt bones: This doing, nature in a certain
time thereafter, will separate the corrupted bone, fro the sovvndebone,
engendringe betvvee them both, a tenerouse flesh, which by little little
hardens, as if it vveare Pomgranate-kernelles, which foresayed flesh, we
oftentimes may see, grow clean through the corrupted bone, that in such
sort, as the small tender grass, grovveth through the grovvnde, as the is
the suppuration, matter good laudable, vvhyte, or reddishe coloured, and
without stincke. And we must here alsoe farther note, that the Chyrurgian,
may sometimes gently move, the corrupted bone being cauterized, stir it,
because nature there through may be opitulated, unto the separatione of the
cauterised bone, and the foresayed bone lying sovvhat raised, may be lifted on
high, because as the it houldeth fast noevvhere, but we may not with any
violence take the same thereof, for in so doing, the sovvnde, bone, before
copletlye it be covered with flesh might chance of the air, to be again
infected, and corrupted.

The Cauterization must be reiterated
Nether is it sufficient, that once we apply the Cauterye thereon, but must
divers sundry times do the same, nether must it be glovvinge or red hot, but
reasonable hot, removinge of the forsayed cautery fro one place of the bone to
the other, or houldig of the same in one place, until the place be thoroughly
vvarmed.

What we ought to do in a deep Caries.
The corruption, therefore or Caries being very deep, we must them trepane or
boar it with a perforative Trepane, unto the sovvnd bone, or with the small
ground dravver, make divers hoales therein, the one close to the other, of an
aeqvall depth also, as the corruption of the bone is, in which trepanned
hoales, we must as the apply some little hot actual cauteryes because the
foresayed bone, there by may be thoroughly dried: By this operation, the
corrupted bone will separate it self fro the sovvnde bone, as heretofore we
have said. If so be that the whole substance of the bone be corrupted,
corroded, we must then vvholye remove, take the same a way. A history of
Albucasis.
As Albucasis, to this purpose, recites a memorable history of on whom at
three sundry times he took away almost the whole shin bone, dividing also
the operation, in three sundry aeqvall times, because the patient was not
able, to tolerate so great payne, because of his great imbicillitye, alsoe
because Albucasis feared least that under his hades he should have died,
because through certain evacuations, he allvvayes fell into great fayntnes,
but in the end was perfectly cured: and in place of bone, there engendered in
the place, a harde carnositye, which so miraculously fortified from day to
day, that he during some continuance of time thereafter, could again
courageously go one that leg.

When as therefore it is reqvisite, to apply the foresayed actual Cauterye one
any certain place, we must dilligently consider, how we might liberate,
defend, The circumiacet parts of the vlceration must be freed.
the circumiacet parts of the Vlceratione, least they be touched of the
cautery, which we may with most conveniece do, with some certain
plasters, because nether the fat nor any other ebullient humiditye, which
through the calliditye or heate of the cauterye might chance to run therout,
one the self same circumiacet parts, of the vlceratio, that thereby they
chanced not to be burned, seared, which would be the cause of great payne
and dolor.

Now therefore to correct cure the Caries, or corruption of bones, there are
divers Chyrurgianes of our times age, in place of the actual Cauterye use the
potentialle: Divers remedies against the Caries.
amongst the which the oil of vitrioll is in the most chiefest, in like sort
alsoe the oil of franckinsece, which of divers Chyrurgians is highly
commended, and esteemed.

Of the Fistel of the Fundament. Chap. 2.
ANd because we have not heer intended generally to write of al fistles, to
discourse of their matter, conditions, what parts be thereof polluted, of
their occasiones, signs, prognosticationes, of the remedies, of their cures,
it seemed expedient unto me, briefly to show, the means, to cure those
which reveal, demostrate themselves in the Fundament, that especially through
a convenient handling, Intention of the Author.
or operatione of Chyrurgerye when as shortly and breeflye I shall have
taught, in what sort we may best and easyest know them.

Those Fistles which are ingedred in the fundament, are of divers sundry
sorts: for some of the are occulte, others patefyede, the occulted are so
called, because we no where externally espy them, and internally in the
foresayed fundament, The fistles of the fudament, are ether-hidden or aperte.
or excrementall gut have their apertio, issue in the muscle Sphincter,
whether they be situated vpvvardes or dovvnvvardes, or any where one the side,
adioninge unto any of the two buttocks. Which allthoughe we can not
evidentlye perceave them, yet may judge, that the foresayed parts, are hurt
or hidered, through the payne and through the stinckinge matter, and
humiditye, which issues out of the foresayed fundament, through the which
commonly, the shirts of the patient are polluted, through certain
vlcerations also, which before times in that part they have had, or through
certain internal hemorrhodes, or through any inveterate vvoud, which was
badly cured. We may also sometimes see them vvithe our eyes, through the
Speculum Any.

The patefyed Fistles of the sudamet.
The patefyed Fistles, are therefore so called, because immediately without any
danger we may evidentlye see them: Of the which there are some which lye
croocked, and curved, although they have but one apertion, which visiblye we
may see, yet they have divers ways, branches, or cubicles, like
Conyeburrovves, which of their patent Orificium, in the body, run this
way and that way, which with the searchinge iron sometimes we may find,
when as we thrust it this way or that way, where we feel it of his own
accorde to enter, together also, out of the great qvantity of matter, which
daily issues therout, we may easily judge, that the foresayed Fistle, must
needs have more concavityes, then by the external fistulouse apertione we
can judge, which at sometimes is not great.

There are alsoe some Fistles, which externallye have but one issue, and do
not pass through the concavitye of the external, or arsegutt, or through the
muscle Sphincter, as easily through the privet or searchinge we may perceave
when we thrusting the same therein, we ca not any where perceave it against
our fingers, to the which intent we must thrust the same middle finger of the
left hand in the fundamet, for if it so be that we between our finger, and
the privet feel any thing it is then a sign that it doth not pearce
through but if we feel it against our finger, it the pierces through.

And now as concerning the curation of the same, A three fouled means to cure
the Fistles of the fundament.
we will not here treat of any things belonging unto the medicamentes,
but only speak of the Chyrurgerye to the curing of this dissease: the which
on a threefoulde manner is taught unto us, as well in the curatione of the
internalle, as externalle Fistles: to vvitt, ether through ligature,
inscisione, or through Cauterye.

Although Avicenna, with more others are of opinione, that with any of these
foresayed remedies we ought in no sort to meddle there with unless, they
vveare too intolerable, and molestiouse unto the patient, but that with clean
linen, with Cotton, and with decent and convenient lavamentes, we ought to
sustain them. But for all this not withstanding, I will here describe unto
you, our use and customs of the ancient Chyrugians.

Now therefore as well to cure the publiqve, and patent Fistles, as the occult
and hidden, we must cause the patient conveniently to lye, namelye that the
patient must lye one his back, with his legs on high, retaining and
houldinge the foresayed legs, close unto his belly, and this is the manner,
and customs of ayncient Chyrurgians. But we now a days, cause the patient
to stand on both his legs, with his head, lying on a bed, disioyninge
separatige the foresayed legs vvyde the one fro the other, which alsoe of
two servantes or staders by must so be held, least that he again might
chance to shut them. The patient being thus situated, towards the light, we
shall then thrust the middlemost finger of the left hand into the fundamente,
having with some certain oil anoyncted your finger, and pared close your
nails: and in our right hand we must have a privet or searchinge iro, in
form of a needle, in the which at the on end there of must be a thread, which
foresayed needle, or Privet we must thrust into the Orificium of the Fistle
until we sensibly feel the same on our finger, and then with our finger
recurvate, the end of the foresayed needle, neatlye, conveniently reduce the
same unto the fundamet, and then draw the same therout, dilligently
observinge, that internally we hurt or vvoude nothing, How we must bind a
public, or patent Fistle.
and the thread alsoe being passed through the fundament we must then cut of
the foresayed needle from the thread, tie the ends of the foresayed thread
both together, dravvinge the same reasonable fast, and close together, and
with a slidinge knott bind the same together, because daily we might knitt
the same closer, and continuing this same so lunge in this sort, until such
time as we shall have cut away all the thread which was remayninghe
between, both the Orificia, and both the apertions become to one, and the
thread conseqventlye, without any more dissectione of the same, shall be taken
thereout.

But we perceavinge the Fistle to have no issue in the concavitye the
arsegutt, and the needle doth not come out against our finger being in the
foresayed arsegutt, and that there as yet is some pellicle, or membrane
between them both, the needle having a reasonable sharpe acuitye or poyncte,
we must thrust the foresayed threded needle, or Privett, boldly, and
audaciouselye therthroughe, into the foresayed concavitye of the Arsegutt,
because we might also draw the foresayed thread therout, as already above we
have metioned.

We must alsoe here farther note, that this privet, or needle, must ether be
of goulde or silver, because she may the easier internallye in the foresayed
gut be recurved. We use heervnto a silver plate, which thereunto expressly,
we caused to be made, as this same plate, the foresayed Privett, or needle,
before amongst the figures of the instruments, are also defigured, and set
down unto us.

The thread must be of course thread, the same being tvvisted three or four
times double. And because that unto some, the dissectione of this simple
thread, falls somewhat tedious, they annoyncte the same with some Causticke
medicametes. Gvido, Opinion of Gvido.
effects this dissection, with a rescindent actual Cauterye, to praevent
the same that it pearce no deeper, then it ought, he thrusts in the fiistle
a hollow Privett, on the which concavitye, he with the foresayed Cauterye
pierces the skin, and in like sort alsoe cutteth through the fistle,
brings, dirimateth away with it the callositye, praeventeth also the
fluxione of blood.

It is surer to make an inscision in the fistle, the to tie it.
Others will not in any sort, have the fistle cauterized, nor bovvnde, tied,
but will only have that same to be inscided with a croocked lancett, which
is between both the Orisicia, to vvitt of the fistle, and the excrementall,
or arsegutt, that alsoe which internallye is grovven callouse, they will have
it to be crudifyed and made ravv, or excoriated, as we are used to do in the
haremouthes: yet daily experience teaches us, that the ligature is more
certain, the the inscisione, wherein we need not to take avvaye the
callosity, because it oftetimes chances, that we endevoringe to remove, and
take away this callositye, we discide, and cut of any of the Fibres, of the
muscle Spincter, which being wounded, or hurt, the patient there after can
in no sort restraygne his stools, or excrementes.

It may seem admiraculouse, strange unto some, that I write that, the fistle
may be cured through the ligature only, without removinge the callositye,
because it seems oppugnant unto reason, because all combinatione, healinge
together, is caused through the ioyninge or touching of weak things
together: yet I dare affirm, that all those, which I have seen tied, have bin
perfectly and sovvndlye cured without in any sort to have touched, or removed
the callositye.

Now to come to the operation of the hidden fistles, having situated the
patient as before is said, we must then apply therein, the Speculum Any,
opening the fundamet thervvith, with the least pain we ca, as vvyde as is
possible, allvvayes annoynctinge the foresayed Speculum with oil, through
the which said apertione, the Orisicium, of the vlceratione may appear be
apparet unto us, be-tvvixt which concavityes or branches, we must thrust in
a privet, which must be of a reasonable thickenes, or crasitude, like unto a
small little needle wherewith we are vvonted to make our stitches, which
must be blunt, in the one end must with a thread be threded, which
foresayed needle, or privet, we must thrust unto the bottom, or grovvnde of
the fistle, which penetrates unto the skin, as we easily may feel with
our finger, on the which, with the croocked lancet we must make an
inscision, the needle being in this sort passed through, the Speculu Any
being taken away, we must then draw out the needle, and cut her from the
thread, and tie both the ends thereof together.

Other say we ought besides the privett, internallye to make an inscisione
through the vvhol Fistle, unto the other hole, which is made on the other
side: but there is most certayntye in the ligatione, to avoid the effluxion of
blood, vvhenas there is sufficient flosh enough between.

There have bin certain ancient Chyrurgianes, which could not agree unto
the Speculum Any, but have only thrust there finger into the fundament, to
dilate the same, and besides the same a thin and pusille privett, wherewith
they have so lunge felt thervvith this way and that way up, and down, till
such time as they have fovvnde the Orificium, of the fistle, which is felt as
if it vveare a thing rent, or torn, and now having fovvnde the foresayed
Orificium, An other practice or invention.
they then close to there finger thrust in the privett, conducinge the same
along the finger supernallye, or vpvvardes, or thither where they suppose the
bottom, or ground of the fistle to desist, and end: which through feeling
of the finger of the other hand we may easily discern: and having fovvnde
the end thereof, and perceavinge the skin or the flesh not to be very
thick, in stead of ligatione, we violently thrust the privett there through,
vpvvardes, the privett being passed therthroughe, they then cut open all that
which is situated between the two Orificia of the Vlceration, or else they
thrust a thread there through, and so bind it through.

Amongst those which are of opinione, that we ought to cure the fistle
through an actual Cauterye Albucasis discribeth unto us the means, Albucasis
vvillinge us to use a Cauterye of iron thereunto, which must be very subtle,
and glovvinge, or redhott, and that it be proportioned according to the
greatness of the fistle, thrustinge the same tvvice, or thrice therein, until
such time as all the callositye is taken away there from, laudinge the fervent,
glovvinge Cauterye, above the cuttinge or rescindent Cauterye, because the
glovvinge Cautery, as he says corrects and amends the vnteperatnes of
that part there follows no effluxione of blood the callosity thereof is
burned away, the superfluouse humidity is exsiccated, which was concursed,
and assembled together unto that patte.

How we ought to extract, draw forth little infantes out of their mothers
body, which of themselves can not be borne. Chap. 3.
Admonition for the Chyrurgiane.
BEfore we offer to employ our hades, on such on operatione: it seemed expediet
unto me to admonish the Chyrurgiane what vvoeme may escape this danger,
because we should obtain great blame and discredit, unto our selves, if so
be the woman being in childebearth, should chance under our hands to dye,
she supposing, by our help, council to be released out of this danger, The
signs where through the Chyrurgian is hindered, to draw forth the child out
of his mothers body, vvhic of it self ca not be borne.
Wherefore those which we suppose to be in danger of death, we must not in
any sort lay hades on them* because the countenance and cheer of those
vvoemen which are in labour, or childebearthe and also there face,
administers sufficient knowledge unto us, what event, or success the matter
shall have: for those which are in any great danger, of their lives, have a
strange wonderful behaviour over them, to vvitt that she troubles, or
molests her self with nothing, hath a fearful sight, which is clean
contrary to her naturalle being, is debilitated, hath hollovve eyes, a sharpe
nose, a feeble Pulls, which beats obscurely, and without time: She is
vvholy covicted, as if she had the soporiferouse dissease, with out all
strength, clean laid along, although we speak unto her, yet we can not
avvake her, if we do log trouble, pluck her, she speaks very feeblelye,
with no strength, and then again lies, as it vveare in a great sleep: the
air alsoe flies out of her throat. Those in the which is any strength left,
they fall into Spasmo, or convulsion of synnues. Others after they hay
continued a long time in their labours, as are those which have bin troubled
molested thervvith the space of five, or six months, they are vvholye
decayed, grow lean, vvholy consumed, for want of food, because alsoe that
which she hath eaten, is not changed into any nurture, the vvhol boodye
being thorough soacked with humidity, especially the face, whereof her
svveat most commonly, is fattye, axungiouse, and smearye.

But those which can suffer the manual operatione, are fit, to have the
child dravven out of their body, have none of these fore rehearsed accidents,
wherefore withal festinatio alsoe they must be opitulated, helped, on this
manner as heerafter follows notvvitstanding without rashly, or temorously
to begin the same, without being certifyede, of the Midvvyfe, what the
reason or occasion might be, vvhye the woman can not be delivered, or bring
forth her child, There are three things which make Child birth difficult.
being certified hereof, as well of the parturinge vvoma, as of the
Midvvyfe, in as much as is possible, dilliggetly considering the same, having
well perpeded it, therout to know the just occasion of this heavines
sorrow, considering, whether it takes his occasion of the parturiet women,
or else out of the child, because thereby we might know how to rule, govern
our selves

If so be this danger consists in the mother, The mother.
it happens then, ether because of her mistrust or small hope, or because
she is fearfulle, and fayntharted, having the vvombe, and the neck or
entrance of the sam, small anguste, because she is yonge small of body,
tender, and delicate: Or else because the entrance of the foresayed VVombe,

against the bearth of the infant: Ether because that the mother, hath a stone
in her Bladder, which therein alsoe being oppressed, seeks by all means
possible to come therout, and being come into the mouth, or entrance of the
Bladder, crushes there the entrance of the VVombe, where through she is
angust, or else because therein is some cicatrice, where through she is narrovver,
then she ought to be, where through she ca not in any sort stretch forth her
self.

Child bearth alsoe in some vvoeme is hindered above all these foresayed
accidents, because they are to fearful, are to much affrighted, of the

do not yet know thereafter to govern themselves, as vveare condecent, and
needfulle. Others are grovvne impotent, through any praecedente sicknesses,
and having no strength at all to deliver the conceptione or fruit from them.

The child alsoe may be occasion hereof, The child.
because it is to feeble, and ca not help it self, in the deliverance of his
mothers labours, and that especially when it is dead, and svvolle: in like
sort also the same being to gross, and great, ether in any part of his

then one, to vvitt, two, three, or four, whereof the one might praesent his
arm, an other his leg, or any other part, all at one time.

Afther the infat praesenteth himself, the childebirth falls easy, or
difficult: because, that follovvinge the natural Childebirth, the child
allvvayes praesenteth first his head, having his arms stretched out alogest

we must use our remedies as herafter shall be showed.

The external occasions.
Touching the external occasiones, they are violet heate, vvherthrough the
strength forces of our bodies are convicted: contrarilye, there is great could,
vvherthrough, all the conduictes are stopped as allsoe are those persons which
we fear, or hate.

The child alsoe as lunge as it lies dry, the water as yet is not broke
out, vvherthroughe it can have no passage, because the ways passages are
dry, not slippery, not smooth, as in the water streams we may see, that
the stones, through the slipperishenesse of the water, The operatione.
are carried away. In like sort alsoe all sorrow, tribulatio, stops
occludeth the entrance of the vvombe: as cotrarilye the mean, reasonable
iucunditye, opens the same.

All these foresayed occasions, must be compared, unto there cotraryes, as if so

Signs of a dead Child in his mothers body.

that vvholy to draw it out is impossible for us or else if it be a Monster, or

cuttinge the Head thereof in pieces, then pluck out first the the one piece,

be tender, yet the splinters thereof might chance to hurt the vvombe, so that
it is allvvayes the surest way we cut of the Arms, and legs in their
ioynctes.

It chances also sotimes, that we dravvinge forth the Child by the legs,
that only the head tarrieth therein which thereafter without great difficulty
we ca not get it out, the with extreme danger, because the same rovvleth up
and down in the concavitye of the vvobe: this therefore being happened, there

the dead child as yet remaining in the vvobe
And the Chyrurgiane which sits on the right side of the woman must with

The Child being dravven therout, we must dilligently consider, that we
do not break a sunder the navel, because it may serve for a conductor,

through our right hand must follovve until we be come to the matrice

together again, and must then circumligate the belly, as is required, and
necessary.

In this forerehearsed danger, is oftentimes incident unto the woman a far
more lamentable and pitiful accident, to vvitt, a discendinge, and sinckinge do
vvnvvardes of the vvombe, which between the hippes sinks out, through the
which the woman, can not go. The manner to elevate again the sunk disceded
Matrice.
If so be therefore this chanced, we must gently, and easily by degrees
thrust in again the same, as we have said of the guts, or entralles being
sunk out, whereof we must cause the disseased and sick woman to keep her
bed some certain cotinuance of time, with her buttocks a little exalted,
and if so be the Matrice in her rising chanced again to sink out, we must
then again, thrust in the same.

And to praevent her that she do not again sink out of the body, we must
the thrust in the Matrice a Pessarium, like a rovvnde Apple, which must have a
hole in the middle therofe, as heer before amongst the instruments we may
see it defigured unto us where we shall alsoe find, the form, the figure,
the manner of using the same.

Upon what occasion, the external parts as Arms, and legs are, and must
be extirpated, or cut of and in what place it must be done. Chap. 4.
When, vvhye the external part, must be saved of.
THe externalle members of mas body, as are Arms, legs, are cut, or savved
of, when as they are sqvised, plettered, and broke, the Veins, Arteryes,
Synues, vvholy be lacerated, cut of: Or else when they concerning any
externalle occasion are hurt, or indammaged, whereby they sotimes vvholy come
to be mortified, that suddaynlye, or else sometimes alsoe by degrees, so that
sometimes there ensues such a Gangraena, or mortification, that not only the
flesh, all the other mollified parts, of the foresayed ioyncte, do mortify
corrupt, but alsoe the Bones themselves, so that there is no hope at all, of
any health, then only through the extirpatione of the same, fearing least
that the foresayed Gangraena, should farther infect pollute all the
circumiacent parts, vvherethroughe the patient might suddaynlye chance to dye.
Notvvithstandinge the Chyrurgiane may not in any forte aproch unto his
exstreame, last remedy, before he have tried all other remedies, to ease
appease this forsayed mortificatione, to save the ioyncte: because that such an
operatione, Extirpatione of any ioyncte, is effected with great danger.
can not be done, then through extreme danger, as oftentimes the patients
themselves, do dye under our hades, because of the great effluxione of blood,
or else some great fayntnes. Others immediately after the extirpatione fall into
a Phrensye, in extreme, intolerable payne, in Spasmo, in a could svveat,
vvheron praesentlye, follows death. Wherefore first of all we ought to
admonish the friends, kinsfolk of the patient, certify unto them that this
operatio is full of danger, and is as well lamentable for the Chyrurgiane as
for to patient: and that we ought not thereof the make any great matter of
worth, or estimation, saying there is no other hope, nor any other success to
be expected, that it seems more convenient, and necessary, to extirpate the
mortified ioyncte, and so to avoid fly death, which is more terrible, and
fearful, then the loss only of one ioyncte.

And before we begin this operatione, that we must extirpate, the foresayed
ioyncte, it is necessary that first of al we know, the place where it must be
done, Reason, how to make choice of the place, where we should extirpate the
ioyncte.
because of divers opinions which are had thereof: for some are of opinione,
that in the ioyncte we ought to do it, because in this place it would fall
easier for the Chyrurgiane to execute the same, is alsoe more tolerable and
easier for the patient, bebecause there it may be done with more festinatione,
with a simple inscision of a well rescindent and cuttinge knife, if only the
Chyrurgiane be agile, and expert, in finding of the ioyncte.

Now as touching those which suppose all vvoudes of the ioynctes, to be more
subjected, Reason for those which extirpate any mebre in the ioyncte.
unto deadly, and dolorous accidents, the those which hap to be three
fingers above, or under the same, yet for all that they are no less
davngerouse, because of the Tendones, and al other sunnuish parts, which as
then are alsoe cut of, which are round more thicker there then in the
ioynctes themselves: VVherfor we need not to fear, that there will follovve
more payne, or convulsione, although the extirpatione which is done, three,
or four fingers belovve, or above the same, because the Tedones, and the
synnues are as well in the on place as in the other aequallye cut of. And
which is more, if we extirpate a membre in the ioyncte, the marrovve, of the
pipe as the is not denudated, because of the Epiphysis, wherewith it is
covered, and contained: But contrarilye if we saw through the focille, or
pipe, the marrovve as the is denudated, for the praeservatione of which Nature
with great difficulty, must have a lunge time, to engedre a Callum thereunto,
and to cover it, because nature must separate that end of the savved bone: And
besides all this, there follows no superfluous effluxion of blood, as
Hippocrates in his boocke of Hemorrhoidibus, vvitnesseth unto us.

Although that all these foresayed reasons, Reason, for those which extirpate
the membre above or belovv the ioyncte.
are sufficient enough, to persuade the yonge Chyrurgian, to extirpate the
members in their ioynctes and Hippocrates, allsoe commands councelleth the

opinions together, that we ought to extirpate the membre, Reason, for those,
which extirpate the membre above or belovv the ioyncte.
three, or four fingers under, or above the ioyncte, according to the
dispositioe reqviringe of the mo^tified ioynct: For first of all the opratione
is done with such festination so certain, because of the facility thereof, for
we may right well know that the whole infectede, spoylede part, most
commonly svvelleth, the finitimate parts also thereunto lying, that without
great danger difficulty, we can not know the ioyncte or the place thereof
in the which we ought very vvarily to enscide: Farther allso the ioynctes are
most comonlye very difficult, clean to cut them of, or extirpate them,
because of the bones, which are placed the one in the orher.

Touching therefore the certayntye thereof experience teaches us, that an
inconvenience may as well come of the one, as of the other: And such vvoundes,
are no less mortalle, then the vvoundes of the ioynctcs. Secondly, there much
sooner follows a cicatrice, because of the great qvantitye of flesh,
wherewith the bone on all sides is circumcinglede, covered which in that

although the Cicatrice can not so soon be effectede, the patient
nevertheless needs not abstain from going, that without payne allso,
laying his knee in a stilte, wherewith he shall as then abide the time of his
complete sanatione: The which he in that sort should not be able to do,
before that the cicatrice we are vvholye curede, obduratede, if so be his
leg had bin extirpatede in the ioyncte, because the whole body resting
thereon, through the confricatione, or rubbinge together of the same might
chance to breacke open again. Experience of the Author.
As touching my self, I have allvvayes food that the cicatrice hath ever
bin very difficult, yea almost impossible to be curede effectede in the
ioynct, but imediatlye thereafter is openede again, how little soever the body
restede therone. Conclusione.
Wherefore I also am of the same mind, opinione, with all other common
Chyrurgianes that as well considering the festinatione, facility of the
operatione, as allso the brevitye of the generatione of the cicatrice, that we
ought to do our extirpation or savvinge of, of any membre, three, or four
fingers breadth above, or under the ioynct, to vvitt, of the Leg, if it be
under the knee: For allthoughe the Gangraena, or mortificatione of the Leg,
vveare only belovv in the foresayed Leg, the middle of the Leg as yet
illaef unhurt, sound, vvherfor it is all ways better to make the stump
short, then lunge, because vvithe his length, it is associated with a certain
deformitye, might cause great impediment unto the patient, with thrustinge
of the same, heer against this thing, there against somewhat else. Which in
the Arm is contrary because there it is better, that we suffer him ^o keep as
lunge a part thereof, as is possible to be by any means done,

It is right true, if so be the Gangraena, Exceptio.
or mortificatione of the Leg, do end in the ioynct or, close thereunto, no
higher then to the beginning of the hipp we must then allvvayes rather do

Because the accidents might allvvayes be vvorse, considering the great

vpvvardes, so much the greater we find them: Nota.
And then the cicatrized part, should be constraynede to rest on the

his life, we should be constrained to make an other extirpatione.

How we ought to do the extirpatione, or sawinge of, of a ioyncte and how we
may stop the blood, after we shall have laid down the Patient. Chap. 5.
Having noted the place, where we intende to do the extirpatione, we must
situat the patient as it is reqvirede, not only considering the nature,
disposition qvallitye of the part, which we intende to extirpate, Situation
of the patient in the extirpation of a Ioyncte
but allso one the Chyrurgiane his commodity, that the patient sit not against
the day, ligt of the Chyrurgian, not to high, nor to lovve, nor on a place
where he might slip as it oftentimes hath chanced, the patient sitting to
far in the bed. Some are of mind that we ought to set the patient on a
stool because he may sit the faster, convenienter: Others effect this
operation, the patient sitting on the edge of a bed, fearing least he should
grovv faint, or syncopize, might the with more ease be got into his bed
restig place the extirpatione beingedone.. But my use is, to cause the patient
to ^itt in a reasonable lovve chair, because all beds are not of one hight,
nether are so ready, and fit for our purposes nether so well towards the
day, whereas we may set the chair vvheare we our selves list: farther the
patient is more surer in such a place, may alsoe better, with more convenience
be held, and better towards the Chyrurgians hand, as well to cut through
the flesh, as to saw of the bone and restraygne the blood: the servantes
allsoe shall have more better oportvnity to houlde the patient fast in more
safety.

How we may well convenientlye extirparte a Ioynct.
Therefore to effect and finish this operatione, the Chyrurgyne must stand
between the legs of the patient, and must cause a servant, or stader bye as
much as is possible with both his hands to strip up the skin the muscles
which are situated above the extirpatione, after he shall have caused the
patient to bend the ioyncte, that as well for the longatione of the skin,
as lengtheninge of the Veins, and Arteryes, which after the extirpation of
the Ioyncte, the ligature being taken away, will more easier as then
demonstrate them selves, be more easier taken houlde of vvithe the Crovvesblil,
and they tyen or cauterized: Then we must immediately one the place, where we
intend to make our extirpation, lay a strong ligatvre of strong linen, the
same being reasonable closely drawn together and that because of divers
occasions: First of all because the Ioynct may be praeseted the stedyer and
faster to the Operator, and because the flesh should the better bear, and
receave the inscione of the croocked, or curved knife: Secondly, because the
sensibilitye of the foresayed Ioynct, in the scissure, might partly be
therthrough benumbed, and somewhat mitigated: Thirdly, because after the
inscision, in sovvinge of the bone, the blood might be so lunge through the
foresayed ligature restraygned, kept back.

Farther more, by this means we keep the skinn muscles on high, which
after the operatio, the ligature being dissolved, made loose, sink again
dovvnvvardes, and cover the savved bone, by which means the Cicatrice the
sooner grovveth therover, and serves the foresayed bone thereafter, in steed
of a Cushone.

There may not be any flesh one the bone when we intede to saw it of.
All which being finished, we must then with a croocked knife, which is
very sharp, cut through the flesh close unto the ligature roude abovvte, unto
the bare bone, without leavig any thing betvvee the bones vncutt, if there be
two, scrapinge of with the back of the knife the membrane Periostium,
wherewith the bone is covered, because we do not chance to tear the same,
with the teeth of the saw, which would cause an intolerable payne unto
the patient, also in serratinge, or savvinge might be some hinderance: In like
sort also we must remove the flesh as much out of the way as is possible,
then with a well cuttinge saw, saw of the whole leg as close to the
flesh as may be, dilligently noting, that with the teeth of the saw we
do not in any sort touch the foresayed flesh.

The leg now being extirpated, The Ioynct being extirpated, we must the
make loose the ligature.
we must the dissolve make loose the ligamets, which vveare tied above the
wound, strip dovvnevvardes the skin, and the muscles, because the bone of
al sides may be well covred: which being done, if so be there be but little
blood issued therout, we must not then (especially where there is a
Gangraena,) so soon restraygne the same, but as yet let it a little more
bleed, that as lunge, as you suppose it may without any davnger to the
Patient be done, because that part there through might the better ease her
self, and be disburdened, and so much the less be subject unto any
inflamationes.

The blood having indifferentlye and reasonably issued therout according to
the strength of the Patient, we must as then stop, Instruction how we shall
koovv whether it must be stenched, or let bleed.
and restraygne it, which we must do through the imposition of the fingers
one the mouths, or apertios of the Veins, which we must ether bind or else
cauterise the one or the other according as the same shall be needful, and
requisite, it be ether through binding, or cauterizinge, as the ancient
Chyrurgians of former times have done, we have committed it to the memory, of
those of our times.

For as in example, Virtves of the actual Cauteryes.
if there be any member corrupted, and mortified, which must be extirpated,
after which extirpatione, the fluxion of blood, must be far more stopped,
through the applicatione of glovvinge cauteryes, on the appertio, or mouths
of the veins, the through the ligation, or binding of the same, because the
glovvinge cauteryes, make one the foresayed Orificia of the veins an Escara,
vvherthrough the foresayed Orificia of the veins are occluded and the blood
stenched, because it ca no more issue out, by which means it dravveth unto
it self, also consumes al the venoumouse evaporations, dampes, and brings
the to nought, which as yet through the foresayed veins, have bine retained
in the supersituated parts, although, it seems not that the foresayed part
is in any sort corrupted, or putrified, then only soacked through with some
certain venoumousenes, vvhertroughe sometimes that part hath bin prepared
as it vveare to some mortificatione, or Gangraenatione: that we supposing to
take houlde one the veins with the Crovvesbill, and so to bind them, do
most commonly chance to break between the Crovvesbill, or else how
gentlelye soever we knit the thread together, one that sort to tie them, are
through the tyinge of the thread notvvithastndinge cut a sunder:
vvherthroughe we are in the end constrained to use the actual Cauterye.

Galenus.
And not without occasion, Galenus testifies unto us, to be a thing very
expediente, necessary, to stench, and restraygne the blood through actualle
Cauteryes, which through any corruption, hath corroded through the veins,
because they, nether are able to suffer the Crovvesbill, thereby to be drawn
out, nether the tyinge, bindinge. It is also the most surest way, to cauterize
all that, which through the Gangraena is corrupted, or else, that we apply
thereon any Causticke medicamentes, as we are used to do one the originalle of
al corruptions.

Wherefore if so so be the Chyrurgian have any suspicione, of any
venoumousenes, which after the extirpation, might be dravven invvardes
towards the finitimate parts theraboute, it is as then the surest remedy,
that to the restraygninge, and stenchinge of the blood, he have in
praeparatione, and readiness, three, or fovr fervent, glovvinge cauteryes,
vyhich he must apply on the Orificia of the descided veins, How through
cauterisatione we should restraygne, steche blood.
without houlding, or depressing the same to harde thereon, because thus doing
there may be left behind a good Escara: And if it chanced that the blood
through the applicatione of one cautery vveare stenched, it is then
sufficiently cauterized, nether must we any more cauterize the same Vain,
prosecutinge of the same, on the other veins.

Instructio how to restraygne blood through ligature.
Contrarilye, we being constrained to extirpate any membre, being very much
plettered, and broken, which through no Gangraena,, or corruption is
putrified, it is as then most conveniet, to restraygne the blood, through
taking hold of the Veins, or Arteryes, with the Crovvesbill,
compraehendinge allsoe some part of flesh thervvith, which with a good
strong thread we must bind together, as allsoe the flesh, which with the
thread is tied, is an occasion, that the ligature must be the more certayner,
and surer.

And as Galen finds it good, to stench the blood, through actual
cauteryes, whereby is any corruption, or putrefactione, in like sort alsoe
he commends the ligature in the effluxione of blood, whereby is no
corruption, Accorde to agree, the right vvorshipfull Mr. Gourmelen, Mr. Pare.
or any venoumousnes. Which right well may be an agreemet betvvixt to great
personages of our time, where of the on is a Physicione, and the other a
Chyrurgiane, because of a certain dispute, which they had, concerning this
matter, of the means, which we ought to use, in the restraygninge of blood,
as they have agitated most inmicisiously this disputatione the one against the
other without the one, understanding the other.

The blood therefore through the foresayed means being stenched,
restraygned, we must as then strovve some restringet poulder one that part,
and apply therone divers dry plumaciolles, or flatt tents, and therone an
ordinary restringent, or defensive plaster, laying rovvnde about the stump,
a plaster of Refrigerans Galeni, because that the foresayed defensive, should
not chance to cleave too fast therone, and so bind the part, as it
requires, and then curing the same as a simple vvounde, allvvayes
dilligently considering that we do not take away the Escara, which
through the foresayed Cauteryes is made, nether the threedes allsoe where
with the Veins have bin tied, if so be at least there be any.

It happens allsoe sometimes, Of the dressinge after the blood is stopped.
that the Veins after that they have bin cut of do draw themselves
invvardes, so that with the Crovvesbill in no sort we can take houlde
therone: It might allsoe chance, that the tied Vain, might chance to untie,
vvherthrough the patient is charged with a new effluxion, of blood.

If so be any of these accidents chanced, and come unto your hands, and it
seemed best unto you, to Cauterize the Vain, then to bind her, or else rather
to bind, then to Cauterize her, and such a mischance, or accident happened
into your hands unexpected, and not being therone provided, without having
any Cauteryes in are adines, the right vvorshipfull Mr. Pare councelleth us
very fitlye to stench the blood. The which if in two or three or four Veins
it chanced, or Arteryes, at one time then must the servant, of one of the
circumstantes, lay the ends of his fingers, one each vain one, gentlelye
crushinge the same, one the Orificia of the Veins, because as Galen says the
Chyrurgiane may have time, to restraygne the blood: And then take a needle of
a fingers length, or longer, and of a reasonable crassitude, which is very
sharpe, and rescindent, as here before is defigured unto us, being threded
with a strong thread, wherewith the vain after this sort follovvinge must
be tied.

Having first of al considered, where the bleeding Vain is situated, How
we should do this ligature to stench the blood
you must the thrust your needle therthroughe, beginning on the skin a good
fingers breadth higher then the vvounde, one the side of the Vain, making the
same to come contradictorilye out of the vvoude, to vvitt one the side, alsoe
somewhat lower then the Orificium of the Vain, because the thread may be
thervnder, there to circumcingle the same, suffering the end of the thread to
hang one the skin, without extractinge, or vvholye dravvinge out of the
same: Then you must again, thrust the same needle inter nallye in the vvounde,
one the other side of the vain, because in so doing, the thread, which
with both his ends is come forth externallye on the skin may take houlde on
the foresayed vain, with some certain portione of flesh, so through both
the stitches of the needle, passages of the thread, may stifflye be bovvnde
together, through both the ends of the foresayede thread, laying between
the thredes a little compresse of leather, tvvice, or thrice dubbled, as thick
as a little finger, because there through the payne might be praeventede, which
through the foresayed stiff bindinge, might be caused, because the knott,
through the continuance of time should not cut through the skin.

A certain ligature.
When as this ligature is convenietlye done, she as then is very certain,
which allso may be done in all parts of the body, where there is any
fluxione of blood, as in great vvoundes of the Arms, of the Hippes, or of
the Throat. Here before amongst my Instruments of Chyrurgerie, you shall
find them defigurede unto you.

How we should extirpate, the superfluous, corruptede fingers, separate those
which are grown, conioynede together Chap. 6.
The hand is an instrument, of instruments.
THe hand, which is an instrument, of instruments, is dividede in five
fingers: And it sometimes allso chances, that besides the thumb, or the
little finger, there grovveth a sixte finger, which is all carnal, or fleshye,
or else composede of some small bones: Yet is nether the one nor the other
complete, whether ye consider ether their figure, or their magnitud, greatness,
as being against the course of nature, vvherthroughe they are an impediment to
the actiones of the hand. It may allso well chance, the one finger of the
hand to be pletterede, without being able to keep the same from
Gangraenatione. Besides this there commethe in the ends of the fingers,
sometimes a certain vlceratione, Curation of the Panaris, or Paronichia.
which is callede Panaris, or Paronichia: The which cause such vehement
payne, through the venoumouse matter, that therthroughe the bone corrupts,
rots, yea the inflammatione allso begins most commonly in the bone: The
which to remedy, before that the bone be corruptede, we must make an
inscision in the end of the finger according to the length thereof, beginning at
the extreameste end of the ioyncte, unto the bare bone, because the venoumouse
matter, which lies inclosede betvvene the bone, and the pellicle, which
covers the same, might have some or other issve. The inscisione beig done,
we must suffer it to bleed as lunge, till it of it self restraygneth
stops, the thrust the finger in good Aqvavitae, wherein we must before
dissolve a little Treackle: And if so be notvvithstandige all these remedies
the finger as yet corrupted farther, and must be extirpated, we may the very
aptly effect the same with our rescindent, or cutting pinsers, wherewith,
without great payne, we may with, How we may remove the superfluous
fingers.
one nipe clip it of. There are other, which lay the finger on a little
block of vvoode, as the with a razor hevv it of. On this manner also we may
extirpate the superfluouse finger wherein there is any bone.

And so far forth as if the fingers, vveare from the nativitye, and birth,
or through combustione, or else through any vlceratione, healed and joined
together, we must then with a razor, separate them the one from the other,
and according to the length there of cut them asunder, and then with an
exsiccating plaster, being separated cure the, and so skin them, by which
means every finger will separatlye heal. If so be that there came any
vlceration in the finger, after the which there follows a deformed
Cicatrice, vvherthroughe the finger, grovveth croocked: We must then try
thereon some mollifying remedies, to cause the finger thervvith to rectifye,
grow straight:

And if so be by no means it vvilbe so, as it sometimes chances, when
the foresayed Cicatrice is great, and inveterated, ether the sinnues, tendones
or skin have bin hurt, we must not therefore proceed with the same as a
thing which is incurable, for if so be we cut, ether the one or the other,
the finger thereafter should nether be able to bend, nor stretch out, yet is it
necessary that the bending, proceed before the stretchinge out, and the
stretching out, or rectifying of the same, before the recurvatione, so that he
should allvvayes be right, and very inconvenient, as being better that he be
reasonable croocked, then right, because of the inconvenience, which there
through we receave: For we supposing to shut, close the hand, and to
fasten on any thing, this finger as then should stand right out: But when it is
only the skin which makes the Cicatrice, through the which the finger is
recurved, we must then clean cut of the same for as it being harde,
callouse, can not be through the finger erected, and stretched forth. Curation
of a croocked finger.
Having therefore now rectified this finger, by this means, we as the make a
nve Cicatrice therone, must note that in the generation of the foresayed
Cicatrice, the finger do not chance to be again recurved, or shrincke up
again: A finger-case of lattinne, or of silver.
to the praevetig of the which, I know no better means, after he be cured,
that there one we vveare a finger case, of lattinne, or of silver, one the
plaster, one the combustion. This fingercase must be covered with Taffatye, or
with any other decent substance, conveniently, with a band, fastened above
on the hand, which fingercase shall do more commodity, help, the all the
splintes which we might apply thereon.

The thube or the finger being vvholely lamed.
There happens alsoe a dissease, clean contrary, unto all other disseases of
the fingers, especially in the thumb, having received therone a blovve on the
Tendones, whereby the same is erected, again the foresayed Tendones being
cut of, can in no sort be again elevated, nether stretched forth,
vvherthroughe he lies in the hand immovable, and can not be stirred. The
same chances alsoe in the hand, having received any vvounde in the hand
one the Tendones, or alsoe above the hand, where through the hand hangethe
falls dovvnevvardes, as is she vveare paraliticke, and of her self is not
able to be lifted up. As touchig therefore the thumb, the fingers: we must
ether have a thumbcase, or a finger case vvherthroughe they must be heaved up:
And for the hand, a glove, vvherthroughe the hand may be held up. Which in
the boocke of Mr. Pare, are described.

THE EIGHT TREATISE OF THE OPERATIone of Chyrurgerye, wherein is discoursede and
handelede of the Cauteryes, and of the Setonne Containing five Chapiters.
What a Cauterye is, the Species, and differences thereof. Chap. 1. Of the
invention use of the Cauteryes, in what disseases, and one what places we
may apply them. Chap. 2. Of the Potentialle Cauteryes, and how we should
make them. Chap. 3. On what manner we ought to apply the Potentialle
Cauteryes. Chap. 4. Of the Seton, how we ought to apply it. Chap. 5.
What a Cauterye is, of the Kinds, differences thereof. Chap. 1.
Having with my self resolved purposed, to write sovvhat, of the Cauteryes,
it is first expedient and necessary that we know, what a Cauterye is,
howe many kinds, and Species therbe thereof: what their use is, on what
bodies, on what disseases we ought to apply them, and the manner howe we
shall apply the same. The name of a Cauterye may be under stood in two
sorts.
Therefore to understand their nature, we must first know, what we by the
name Cauterium understand: For it may be understood one two sorts or
ways, Simplely, and not Simplelye: Symplely, considering the instrument, the
Causticke matter, which adureth in any part, as Galen, in his sixth of the
simples in his 27 Chap. describes: Not because of the sign which remains
in the Cauterized part, that is because of the little Vlceratione, which
remains, therein, when as the Escara is fallen therout: So that this vvorde
Cauterium, in properlye being understood, can no otherwise be understood,
then a little Vlceratione, in any external part of the body, which
through the art of the Chyrurgiane, is made therein, through some comburent,
adustive medicamentes, to give issve, passage to some certain matter, of some
dissease.

VVherfor the differeces, of these Cauteryes, The differences of the Cauteryes
not properlye taken.
or Vlcerationes, are as it vveare drawn, from their place, being, vvheron
they are applied, also from their efficient cause. The essence, being of
these Cauteryes, consists in their form, figure, vvherfor some there are
which be rovvnde, some contradictorye, others right, great, small, deep, or
not deed: All the which have but one only apertio, or sometimes also two, is
the called a transforatione, or Seton: And also of all other places, vvheron
they may be possiblelye applied, as on the Head, in the Neck, Arms, Legs,
finally in all parts of the body, vvherone they might be applied, when only
the agilitye, or action of the part can not there through be hindered, or
hurt: Of their efficiet cause, which is take out of the diversitye of the
matter, which is applied one any part of the body, or engendered in the
same. The matter which continually is applied on the body, actuallye
combureth, Actual Cauterye
or potentiallye, wherefore they are called actual, or potentialle Cauteryes:
That which in our body is engendered, can come through any sharpe, corrodent,
or biting humours, vvherthroughe the skin is corroded, bitten through,
exulcerated, of which vlceratione, may be effected made a Cautery, or
fontanelle, which may be called actual Cauterye.

Differences of the cauteryes simply taken.
Here of we may conjecture and suppose, that the Cauteryes, fontanelles,
vveare invented, following nature therein, there through to give passage, to
that which is contrary, opposite unto her, whereof she is perturbated,
molested, it be ether in quality, or in qvantitye, as heerafter we will
farther speak of. And as touchig the Cauteryes, which we call instruments,
their principalle, especial difference, is taken, of their substance, form,
figure, of their tarditye slovvnes, or festinatione, of adustion, of their
depth, shallovvnes, of the manner of their applicatione: touching their
matter, or substance, because they actually, in deed do combure, burn, or
potenciallye, they are therefore called actual, or potencialle Cauteryes.

The substance of the actual cauteryes
The form of those which actuallye burn is almost innumerable, which are
made hot, glovvinge. The antique ancient Chyrurgians, have commonly made
their Cauteryes of mettles, as of gould, of siver, of Iron, of copper.
Archigenes.
Archigenes hath cauterised the lachrimall fistles with liquefacted lead,
which through a little pipe he dropped therein. They vveare of opinion, that
the cauteryes of gould caused less payne, Substance of the cauteryes which the
ancient Chyrurgians used.
vveare far more easier to be suffered, that alsoe the Cauterized place,
should not avoid so much matter, and the adustio is not so dangerous,
because gould amongst all other mettles is the most temperate, wherefore it
burns not so violently, as Iron, because it is not so codensated of
substance: wherefore in like sort alsoe the Cauteryes which are made of
Copper, do not so closely burn, or cauterize, as those which are made of
Iron, because the copper, is not so solide of matter, wherefore we desiring
strogelye, violently to cauterize we must take such cauteryes, which are
made of the condesest, A kind of spongiouse matter full of dust grovvinge on
the moutaynes wherewith we use to stench blood.
most firmest matter, substance. Sometimes have also the aunciete professors as
Hippocrates,, recited, to have cauterized, with tents, which they dipped in
ebullient, seethige oil, or with dry incended puffs as we call them or
with the root Aristolochiae, being madefyed in seething oil, or with the
roots of Strutium, as Caelius Aurelianus, Dioscorides, and Attilus, which
have cauterized with incended Goats dung. Aetius hath cauterised the
corrupted gummes, Aurelianus, Dioscorides. Attilus. Aetius. Albucasis Guido.
with ebullient oil, which he applied thereon with vvolle being therein
madefyed. Albucasis in like sort, hath cauterized the hollovve, and concavouse
teeth with seethinge butter. Guido hath cauterised the bones, with
liquefacted Brimstone, to vvitt of the Caries. So that out of all these we
may sufficiently understand, that we may make as many sorts of cauteryes,
as there are substances, which may be incended.

The foresayed cauteryes, Differences of the cauteryes cocerninge their
figures.
especially those, which are made, composed of any mettle, are also
differinge in their figures the one from the other: For everye cauterye must be
proportioned, according to the dissease, and that vvheron we will apply the
same: so that some are like heads of great nails, triangled, others rovvnde,
like unto a butto, others cutting, some not dislike unto a half moon, and
others circlevvyse: As we may behoulde, and see, the circles of Albucasis,
other in form of a rapersblade, as Celsus discribeth, there with to cauterize
the fissures in the lips.

They are alsoe differinge the one from the other in their depth, or
shallovvnes: for we need sometimes to cauterize the skinn only, as Hippocrates
vvilleth us to do, in the pains of the shoulders: We must alsoe sometimes
cauterize the flesh, as in the Sciatica: Cauteryes to cauterize deeply.
Some times also to the bone, and crushe the same reasonable close thereunto as
we must do, when as we must cauterise above on the head, we must
sometimes cauterize, into the concavity of the body, as when we must
cauterise into the breast, to let there out any matter, sometimes we do but only
scorche or singe above one the place.

And because that amongst the cauteryes, Differences of the cauteryes taken
out of their action.
(considering the matter whereof they are composed) there be some which are
quicly heated) and some which continue longer hot, then others even so there
are some, which considering their poderousnes, or levitye, tarditye, or
festinatio, ether in violence, or pain of their operation, are differinge the
one from the other.

The Cauteryes are alsoe differing, Difference in number.
in their number, for at sometimes we apply but one, sometimes two, three,
four, yea alsoe fifteen at one time, as Aetius comandeth us, to the curing of
the vlcerations of the Breast.

They differ alsoe in their manner of applicatione, for some are applied
alone, Difference taken out of the manner of the application
others in little pipes, which at one end are aperte, and open, or have any
appertios, or vvindovves in the sides thereof.

Of the Invention, and of the use of the Cauteryes, and to what disseases, and
one what places, they must be applied. Chap. 2.
IT may rightvvell be said that nature hath showed unto us, The invetio of
the cauteryes is dimonstrated unto us through nature.
the invention, of the Cauteryes, fotanelles: For as sotimes the parts being
overlade with to many humours ether their tartnes, or venoumousnes are
troubled, they in the end, discharge, unburden themselves, in some part of
the body, of that which molests the, as in one of the Arms, or legs,
causinge there to there solace, and ease, some excoriatione, or apertione. So
that the Physitiones, Chyrurgianes, to help heerin the nature, have there
through fovvnde out the use of the Cauteryes, Fontanelles, effectinge by their
art, that which nature oftentimes, through her own commotione thereunto
endevoreth to do, wherefore we alsoe use the Cauteryes, as oftentimes as we
desire to make any derivatione, revulsione, interceptione, or evacuatione, of
any matter, the which ether considering her qvantitye, or qvallitye, or
with both of them together, might in any sort be occasion of any harm. We
alsoe use them, not only when as we desire, to transpire, and cause to
evaporate any venoumouse vapours, but also, when we would cause any
concurrent matter to exsiccate, consume, endevoringe to draw the same from
vvithine externally, as well through the extraction, which it heer makes,
as through the vlceratio, which thereafter remains, as through certain
little open fistles, or small runinge issues wherewith we draw the matter
from invvardes, outvvardes, there to evacuate the same, and cause to digress
from the one place to the other.

Cauteryes are profitable says Galen, Hippocrates, in all corrosive,
ambulative vlcerationes, against fluxions of the eyes, in the tumor Aegylops,
Vtilitye of the cauteryes
when as the fore arm, is sunk and descend under the Armepittes: In the
Sciatica, payne of the hippes, for they retracte draw back the humours,
which trouble in that place hinder those parts: In the Gangraena: in the
extirpatione of the mebres, to stench, stop the blood, so forth in all
fluxions of blood, in all other internal disseases what soever, as in the
Consumptione, in vlcerations of the Breast, Albucasis
and in the Dropsye. Abucasis avoucheth the Cauteryes generally, to be
profitable, in all disseases, and sicknesses, which take their original, of
any matter, or without matter. They are also profitable, and commodiouse, for
all bitinges, stinginges of any venoumouse creature, in the Carbuncles and
Venus botches, because through their heat, they consume the venoumousnes of the
humours, annihilate them and out of the depth draw the vpvvardes. They are
alsoe very profitable, in al critic Apostemations, which are could,
vvaterye, and which are very slow in maturatione because that through
their caliditye, they opitulate, and help to ripen, the could, tough humours,
which are in that part. They are alsoe good, against all corruption of
bones, because they exsiccate the venoumousnes of the same, and consume it, and
cause the corrupted bones to separate from the sovvnde.

We may apply the on all parts of our body, and especially, being
needfulle, to make any apertione, to give issue and passage, to any matter, or
vapoure, which therine is assembled.

There are some which have applied the cauteryes one the upper part of the

about the sagittal suture, where she joins her self with the Coronalle

Mr. Pare.
against the Hemicraniam, or pain of the one side of the head, and against

We Cauterize alsoe the Eyeliddes, which are relaxated, in like sort also

Some there are which set five cauteryes one the head of those which we

Whereof we must set the first cautery, one the Coniunctio, of the sagitalle
