Of the Potentialle Cauteryes, how we ought to make them. Chap. 3.
The potetiall Cauterye, and the matter thereof
THe potetiall Cauteryes are those, which through their intolerable, extreme
heate, being with their forces set to work together with the benefits of
our natural caliditye, burn as it vveare a fire in that part, vvherone they
are applied, leavinge behind them an Escara, or crust: Of the which the
matter of the Corrosive is in great number, and hot, unto the fourth degree:
Amongst the which there are some, which are extreme hot, a little gentler,
according as their operatione is tardive. Amongst them are numbered, the
Cantharides, the Tartre, the common Vitrioll, or the calcined, the vnslissed
limbs, the Auripigment, the Arsenicvm, the sublimate, the Aqvafortis, the Oil
of Vitriolle with more others) the which we novvadayes, do seldom use, in
such sort as they are, with out praeparinge, or mixinge of them, to make any
Fontanelles, because that experience, hath taught us some, which are far
more convenient, less dangerous.

The use of the actual Cauterye.
The actual Cauterye, is much convenieter* then the Potential, whether it be
we consider on the nature substance, on the healthfullnes festinatione, and
certayntye, in operatione: for the fire, is a simple element, having no other
forces then by the heate thereof, exsiccation, without havige associated unto
it any venoumousnes, wherefore the operatione thereof is festivous, certain,
healthful, piercing deeper therine when we please, without causing any
accidents in the circumiacent parts, although, that thervvith we chance to
touch them, that because of the subtilenes thereof, and considering the virtues
of his substance. It is an enemy unto all corruption, vvherfor, it freethe
alsoe from all covruptione, putrefactione, yea it consumes all venoumouse
matter, qvallityes, which in that part might lye occulted, and hidden,
consumes also all superfluouse humidityes, and corrects alsoe all
intemperate could, and moisture.

Where on the contrary, the matter, The use of the potential Cauterye the
matter, virtues thereof, and vvherone we ought to consider.
whereof the Potential cauteryes are made, are oftentimes venoumouse: And how
soever they be praepared, yet there operatione, action, is uncertain,
retardate, and slow, and sometimes alsoe daugerouse, without simply
knovvinge the vittues therofe, howe to limit his operatione: Because sometimes
it spreads it self broader, then our meaning is it should, and causes
more detriment, and harm in corrosione, then vvillinglye we would, it
should, because it doth not only combure burn the place vvhereone it is
applied: But being united, with our naturalle caliditye, it extends, and
pierces alsoe deeper in the flesh then is necessary it shovlde,
vvherthroughe that part being by little, and little calefyed, and vvarmed, it
imprints not only one that place his venoumouse nature, but extends it
self farther, through the circumiacent veins, Arteryes, Synnues, in the
vvorthye parts, spoiling, oftentimes burning, the good, well disposed
complexion of that part, out of the which commonly follovve very bad
accidents, and vlcerations, which thereafter very difficultlye vvilbe cured,
yea also some times a Gangraena.

Notvvithstandinge although the Chyrurgians novveadayes, are bold enough in
the application of actual Cauteryes, novvithstandinge most commolye they use
the potentialle considering the fear, affrightednes, which the Patients
conceave therofe, so that the actual Cauteryes are almost therthroughe
relapsede into oblivione: It is right true, Velvet Cauteryes.
that the Potential Cauteryes which now adays we use are indeed of
velvet, very excellent, where of I never as yet knew any bad accident to
follovve, through the lunge use whereof, we have learned the certayntye howe
to make them.

We must note, that in the Potential Cauteryes, or Ruptoryes, we add
nothing, which hath any venoumouse nature or qvallitye: Because they,
considering their virtues, are through our natural calliditye compelled to do
their operation, which by little, little suscitateth, the redormitinge,
soporiferouse forces thereof, so that it is not possible, but that of
necessity, this venoumouse nature, must imprint some parcel of her
venoumousnes in that part, if so be at the least there be any venoumousnes
mixed thervvith: wherefore most commonly one this sort we praepare it, that
immediately it might show forth his operatione.

We make now adays Cauteryes in form of Trociskes, of divers kides of
Ashes, Saultes, The matter of the potential Cauteries which now adays are
in use.
limes. The ashes are made of Oacke wood, of Figgetreevvoode, of VineBranches,

Mr. Pare.
Amongst all Cauteryes which are made that is one of the best, which Mr. Pare,
calls the Cauterye of Velvet, when it is only sodden, made, as it reqvireth
to be done: notvvithstandinge it seemed convenient unto me, here to describe

Receipt, of the Velvet Cauterye
Take fault of glass, Potashes, vnflist lime, of each a povvnde, ashes which
are burned of the inveterate sides of a vvynepipe, two povvnde: Put all these
together in a great earthen pot, infuse thereon 18, or 20, povvnd of clear
water, and let it so stand soackinge 8. or 10 days, or as lunge, till one
your tunge you feel, the lye to be very sharpe, and tart, or until that
there may drive an egg thereon, with a stick daily stirring of the same, and
then we must let it to clarify, sink, whereof you must defuse the
uppermost thereof, and ether straygne it through a thick close clout, or else
through a Felt, without stirring of the bottom or foeces thereof, of which
aeqvall part, you must make Trociskes, as heer after we will demonstrate.

Sal alcali pulverizated very small, four ovvnces, commone ashes of vvoode

Allthoughe that we have all these foresayed things in a readiness, An
observatione in seethinge of the saulte to the Cauterye.
thereof to make a lye or lixivye, unless that you ebulliate your fault, very
short, dry, it will as then have no great forces, will qvicklye be
liqvefacted, again dissolved. Therefore to sees a right your lye you shall
infuse it in a Basin, let her there seethe, and ebulliat, or evaporate, till
such time as she be very thick, like unto honey, mix, stir it well

present divers colours, as blew, by little little, will grovv thicker, yet
not vvithstandinge, we must yet make greater fire thervnder, Liqvefactinge
fire
with violent flames, as if it wear a liqvefactige fire, because the

water, of the which you must take a little on your Spatula, lay it on a
stone, if so be it immediately vvex harde, it is the a sign that it is
sufficiently sodden: For if so be as then you keep it longer one the fire it
will as then burn, again be changed into ashes, as being needful that
there be some humiditye thereby to keep it in his vvonted thickness: which
being done, you must then remove your Basin from the fire, with the Spatula,
take out the fault, or your Cauterye therout, whilst as yet it be hot, least
that it cleave therone to harde, whereof yon must then make great or little
Trociskes, which immediately you must put, into divers little glasses violles,
least that through the air they chance to be altered, and changed, and
stop the same very close, How we must praeserve the cauteryes
use the vvhensoever it shall please you.

And to praevent, that the Escara, of the cauteryes, or ruptoryes, be not to
harde, a certain friend of mine for certayntye imparted unto me, that in the
end of the ebullitione of your lixivye, when she is about the thickness of
honey, we infuse thereunto a little Aquavitae, or spanish vvyne, or else any
other strong vvyne, as Malmasye. And to make them so, that they should not
cause any great payne, we must as the add unto it a little Opium, which is
liqvefacted in Aquavitae, but I never tried it.

The manner howe we ought to apply these Cauteryes. Chap. 4.
Now therefore condecentlye to apply all cauteryes as well actual, VVheron
we ought to consider in the application of the Cauteryes.
as Potential, without expectinge therein any reproach, we must first of all
consider, one those, which are able to tolerate them: In what places they may
be best set, and applied: one what manner we may apply them: for commonly,
the vvithered, lean personnes, can not tolerate them, unless it vveare one the
head, because their bodies, are nothing else then Membranes, Veins, Synnues,
vvherthrough sotimes great pain might be caused, or any vayn, or Artery might
chance to be through corroded, one al which things we must note, when as
we must apply any Cauteryes: we must also consider, one the nature of the
Patient: Divers considerations.
for a strong gross body as a Labourers, or country clown, may far more
easier tolerate, suffer a Cauterye, then a tender delicate body, or of a
woman: nether can any obdurate part, as is the skin of the head, so
easily be corroded through, as any other soft or tender part, wherefore we
must houlde the Cauterye longer one the one place, then one the other. A little
actual Cautery, or a little parcel of a potetiall Cautery, will work as
forciblye, one a tender, soft body, as a great cauterye would do, one a
strong gross, and obdurate body.

If so be, it be a replete, corpulet body, or an vnhealthsame body, we must
then first of al Phlebotomize, or purge the same, because in the cauterized
parts there chance not to come, any concursione of humours. When we desire to
apply any Cauteryes, or make any Fontanelles, we must consider that we
apply them not one any synnuishe part, nether on the ends, or beginninges, of
any muscles, nether any great Vain, Arterye, or Synnue.

Places vvheron we apply the Cauteryes.
We apply them most commonly on the Head, one the Arms, and one the legs,
betvvixte all corners of the head, for the curing of divers disseases, which
here before we have recited, the ancient Chyrurgias have made their choice
of that place, where the Sagittalle suture, joins and unites her self
with the Coronalle, which place is like unto a threfould way of the head:
the which place because we should not miss of the same, is showed unto us,
of Albucasis: To vvitt, that we cause the Patient, to put forth the one of his
hands, How we shall find in the head the place vvheron we must apply the
Cauterye.
and lay the end thereof, (which is the vvriste, or bedinge of the hand) one
the original, beginning of his Nose, between both his Eyes, and the stretch
forth his middlemost, or longest finger, towards the crovvne, or superior
part of his head, one that place where as the foresayed finger shall have
his endig, there we must apply the Cautery, because that there is the place
where the sagitall suture unites her self with the Coronall suture: if so
be through the applicatio of this cauterye, we desire to do any vtilitye,
profit, we must the suffer the same to penetrate unto the bone: for by this
means the bone being denudated, it will exfoliate, peel, through the which,
the apertione continues the longer open.

Wherefore, divers, after they have applied the poteciall cautery, the next day
follovvinge have cut of the Escara, do apply thereon an actual cautery,
without any danger, as oftentimes I have seen to be done of Mr. Pare.

We may apply alsoe cauteryes, of corrosives, in the posterior parts of the
Head, in the end of the suture lambdoide, behind under the Ear, therthroughe
to avoid the muscle Crotaphite: To the surer finding of the which place
therefore, we must cause the patient to open, shut his mouth, and so to feel
the motion of the said muscle. Monsr. Martel chosen, ordinary Chyrurgian to
the king, which was an expert man, he applied cauteryes behind the Ears,
in the cocavity which is situated behind the little flap of the Ear,
called Fibra, Cauterye in the Fibra.
and that against all inveterate disseases of the Eyes, whereof he certified
me, to have allvvayes had ominouse and happy success, we allsoe sometimes
apply the Cauteryes in the Neck, in the hollovvnes, or concavousnes thereof,
but we must consider that they do not penetrate to deep least that we
chance to adust, or corrode, the eight small musckles, of the Head, which
are in this place situated, which might cause dangerous accidents, as I
oftentimes have known to chance.

The most commonest now in use, Means how fittlye to apply the Cauterye on
the Arm.
is to apply the Cauteryes, one the arms, where most curiously, with great
care we must make electione of the place: the most fittest place is in the
midst of the arm, towards the inner side thereof, right betvvee the
brachialle musckle, the musckle Biceps close to the side of the head vain.
And convenientlye to make choice of this place, we must lay the first finger
one that place, stiffelye crushinge the same, with the other hand, fouled
shut the elbovve, again strech him out, allso lift up the Arm, because
through this motione of the Arm we might perceave, whether there be no
synnue or Tendone situated thervnder, to vvitt under your finger, whether you
find in that place no concavity, or interstitiu of muscles, which is very
fit, for a Peas or little pellet to be therine, to keep open, apert the
foresayed Cauterye: having now with your finger fovvnde out the most fittest
place we must there apply the Cauterye. Means how to apply the cauteryes
convenientlye on the leg.
And touching the same which we desire to apply one the legs, the most
covenietest place there unto is two, or three fingers breadth belovve the knees,
where we are used to tie our garters, whether it be internallye, or
externallye one the leg: But those that are much used to ride on horseback,
also to cause better abscessione of humours, vvherthrough the Sciatica is
caused, for the it is better to apply them in the external parts of the
legs, in like sort alsoe for vvoemen.

Admonitione in the application of the actual Cauteryes.
Having therefore well, dilligently considered on the fittest places, and
those which are also the surest, you must the first race away the hair, if
at the least there be any. Which being finished, we must then situate the
Patient on a convenient place, being held of some other person, if so be it
be needful, the you shall take your Cautery, which must be proportioned,
fashoned according alsoe unto that part which you intende to cauterise, and
must be glovvinge, red hot, or at the least as hot as is required, because
your operatione may at one time be finished, if it by any means be possible,
which according as shall be required, you must imprint the same deep, or
shallovve in that part, considering, taking heed, that in no sort you
chance to hurt the circumiacent, or finitimate part thereunto, to the
praeventing whereof, there are invented, found out divers defensions for the
foresayed Cautery, as certain pipes, little cases being composed, and made of
Iron, least that we should chance to combure, the finitimate, approximate
parts, whether it vveare through the fault of the patient, or else through
temerousnes, timorousnes of the Chyrurgian, as in other places we more at
large have spoke of, alsoe thereby have placed the discriptione of the foresayed
Cauteryes.

For the reiterated Cauteryes.
Being therefore necessary, oftentimes to reiterate the Cauterye, as in any
great Gangraena, or in any Carry of legs, we must then cover, the
approximate places thereunto where we intend to Cauterize, with Compresses,
being madefyed in Plantine-vvater, or any other humiditye, yea also vestite
the edges of the vlcerationes, with plasters of Infrigidans Galeni, or else
with some linen clouts, being madefyed, and dipped in any distilled
Aquosityes, or vvaters.

Now being sufficiently Cauterized, we must then by all means indevoure to
qualify ease the payne, and dolor thereof, to cause the Escara to separate:
To ease the pain, and Separate the Escara.
which must be effected through any warm, and moist remedies, which is
aequallye proportioned with our humiditye, calidity, because through the
humiditye, or moyster thereof it may soacke through the Escara, and also the
finitimate parts thereof, which are obdurated, dry, because through the
caliditye thereof, it might suscitate, and draw unto him, the naturalle
caliditye, out of the internal parts of the body into the external parts of
the same, by this means expel that fro him which is mortified combured.
Amongst all remedies there is none better, the that which is made of Oil of
Eggs, of insulsed, and fresh Butter, of Venetiane Terebebentine, addig in the
end thereunto a little poulder of Ireas, Aristolochiae, and a little Beanemeale,
vvherthroughe alsoe all the purulente matter, which hangs therone as lime,
and an Escara is purifyed. Some use a Digestive made of yolckes of Eggs, of
oil of Roses, of Terebitine: But that is not so necessary, as the formest,
because it doth not so califye, coagulating as the yolcke of an Egg is
vvonted to do, and alsoe immediately vvaxeth dry, and exsiccated.

The potential Cauterye, How we must apply the potential Cauterye
which makes an Escara, or scabbe, being as it vveare a Vicarius, of the
actvall, is in this manner follovvinge applied. We must first of all make a
plaster, of Diacalcitheos, or some such like, as great as a large piece of
tvvelve pence, in the midst where of we must cut a little hole, as great as
the nail of ones little finger, or a little greater, according to the bigness
as we desire to have the apertione: and if you desire to have the apertion
somewhat lunge you must then alsoe make the hole in the plaster somewhat lunge,
then lay the same on that place which we desire to open: And in the hole of
the foresayed plaster, vvherthroughe we perceave the skinn denudated, lay your
potenciall Cauterye, or Corrosive, whether it be great, or small according
as you desire to make the apertion great, or little deep, or shallovv: Which
being done, we must lay thero, a small dubbled clovte, which must be sovvhat
greater the the Cautery, The cauterye makes a far greater Escara then the
hole of the Plaster.
thervvith to cover the same, and thereon as yet an other plaster, which must
be a little broader then the first, and one this plaster an other compress,
which must be of two fingers breadthe and again thereon an other which must
be yet greather, and then tyinge the same with a ligature, acordig as the
part shall the be requiring.

The Cauterye therefore having done his operatio, which most commonly in the
space of two hovvres is finished, if so be the said cauterye be strong,
violent enough, we must as then take it of: and presently to cause the Escara
to separate, we must make a crossevvise in scisione therein: Other suffer her,
of her self to separate, without inscisioue: Some inscide her rovvnde, and
then lift her up, so cut her loose from under. But we desiring through the
application of a Cauterye, to make an apertio, for the evacuating of any
matter, we must not only the inscide the foresayed Escara, but also pearce
somewhat deeper therein, until such time as we have sovvnded the matter, to
give her passage.

Divers means to keep, open a Fontanel, which through a potential cauterye
is made.
If so be the Cauterye be applied to make a fotanelle, we must then cause the
Escara through the said remedves to separate, and keep open the hole, or the
foresayed vlceratio, as great as the rotunditye of a bullet: to the
effectuatinge of which, some use a great Peas, which through the great
humiditye wherewith she is soacked svvelleth as thick as big again, as she
was before, by the which means we may keep open the apertione, as lunge as
pleases us: others make little pellets of elder pith, of Rhabarber, of
Agarico, of the black neesinge root, of a Gallenut, of Getian, some of
goulde, or of sylver being hollovve, concavoyse, but the most convenient,
fittest which I have experimeted, are made of vvhyte vvaxe, amongst the which
is mixed, verdegriece, Cantharides, hermodactilles, a little auripigment,
adding thereunto a little pirosin. This kind of little Balls, vveare invented
of Monsr. Hubert, Chyrurgiane to the king, one of the most expert Chyrurgians
of our times.

What cotinuance we may keep open the vlceration.
Touching the time, of keeping open these Fotanelles: Celsus teaches us, in
his fourth boocke and 22. Chap. that it is oftentimes necessary for the
Chyrurgiane, to exvlcerate the parts of the body, with a glovvinge Cautery
being, as it vveare, an aeternall prohibitione, that we should not suffer
such vlceratios so quickly to close again, as of themselves they would, but
that we must sustain and keep them apert open, till such time as the dissease,
which through this apertione we intend to cure, be vvholye finished, and
cured.

Of the transforation, or seton, and of the manner how we ought to apply it.
Chap. 5.
Seto Properlye taken.
THe transforation, or the Seton, may in two sorts be understood, properlye,
and improperly, we simplely understand it, considering the thread which we
draw clean through the skin, with the needle, which thread in aunciet
time, was made of, course hair, as of horsehayre, or any other such like,
which of the latinistes is called Seta: But we now a days make our thread
of silcke, of Cotton, Seto improperlee understood.
or of Course yarn. Improperly, we understand by the Seto, a logestretchinge
vlceration, in any external part of the body, which is clean thrust
through the double skin, and that through agilitye, and dexteritye with a
glovvinge Cauterye.

We apply especially the transforatio, In what parts we ought to apply the
Seton.
or the Seton, in three parts of the body: to vvitt behind clean through the
Neck, although some to follow, the direct concurrence of the fibers, apply
the same in the length therofe: In the Navle, and in the Scrotum, when as it
is full of matter, ventosity, as it oftentimes is vvonte to happen in the
Dropsye.

This transforatione, is used, ether for any regressione of humours, or for an
evacuation, The use of the Seton.
or expulsione of the same: For we applying the foresayed transforatione
behind in the neck, it then revelleth, and draw the back again those
humours which concur towards the Eyes with those allsoe which run
toward the Mouth, and the Breast, and retains allsoe those humours, which
descend, and sink, behind in the Backebone, and in the Hippes: He being
dravven through the Navle, or Scrotum, he then dravveth there through all the
Aquositye, and ventositye which is therein contained.

It is now adays also applied on two manner of ways, namelye with
Pinsers, Two sorts of application of the transforation.
and with an actual Cauterye, or through the Needle only. The transforation
which only is laid in the Neck, must be applied betvvixt the second the
third Vertebram. Which conveniently to effect, we must cause the patient to
sit on a little stool, and least that the transforatione should copraehend
more one the one side then one the other we must denotate a line with Inck in
the middle of his neck, or else on the same part where we will apply the
tranforatio, How we ought to apply the transforation behind through the
neck.
causing the patient to hold his head on high, and leene backevvardes over,
because thereby, the skin might be the looser, and stretch the farther
commanding then one of the servantes, or standers by, that with one of his
hands he take hold one the skinn, close by the hair, as deep as he ca, ether
according the length, or thvvarte over the skinn, following the line which
theri is made, liftig the same in the middle on high, which the the
Chyrurgian, with his left hand shall take hold one in the midst of the
same, and elevate it, because that he with his right hand, with a round,
well cuttinge threded needle which must be threded with a thick thread, may
thrust clean through the same, the foresayed thread being in this sort
thrust therthroughe we must cut him of close to the Needle. The transforation
with an actual Cautery.
But if rather you desired, to effect the foresayed transforatione with a
hot Iron, the skin being lift up on both sides, through some servant, or
through the Chyrurgiane with his Pinsers, which he must houlde fast in his
left hand, dilligently considering least he take houlde thervvith of any
Muscles of the Neck, which are situated under the foresayed skin, as to
that purpose, intent he must cause the Patient to bend his neck, hold his
head up, or on high, right by this means to know, whether any of the
foresayed, Muscles are compraehended of the Pinsers or not. Which being done
the Chyrurgyane must nipe, shut close together the foresayed pinsers thereon
applied, thereby to benumme dissipate the senses feelig which through the hot
iron might be caused, as the with a triagled glovving cautery thrust through
the hole of the pinsers so through the elevated skin alsoe: The which
foresayed skin being in this sort perforated, immediately again being
dravven therout, without suffering the skinn to fall, we must thrust through
the perforatione, a needle with a thick double thread which thread must be
soacked in the remedies above rehearsed for the Cauteryes, or else at the least
being made fat in some Digestive, then the said thread being dissected, and
cut of, we must suffer him to remain therein, as is above rehearsed, apply
therone a Plaster of Refrigerans Galeni, for the first or second day, and
then a Plaster of Betonica.

This foresayed thread, or Seton, we must suffer it to continue therein, as
lunge as it shall be needful, as we have said of the Fontanelles.

As concerning the transforation, A transforation applied in the Scrotum.
which in the navle, or in the Scrotu may be applied it will be sufficient
when we take the Scrotum, the extremest end of the navle, so perforate the
skinn being doubled, with a reasonable great or gross needle, which must be
threded with a woollen, cotton, or silcke thread, and applying therone, the
self same remedies, of the which we have in the transforatione of the neck
rehearsed.

THE NINTH TREATISE OF THE OPERATIone of Chyrurgerye, wherein is discoursede
and handelede of the Ligatures, or vvindinges, circumvolutions, of the
convenience or the situation of the parts, Containing six Chapiters.
What the Ligamentes, or Circumvolutiones are, their matter, quality, form,
and quantitye. Chapit. 1. The Kinds, and differences of the Ligamentes. Chap.
2. Rules, and general instructions, vvherone we must consider, in all
circumvolutiones, and Ligamentes. Chap. 3. Howe we ought to tie the Ligament,
and again make loose the same. Chap. 4. Howe we must ligate, and tie, the
broken Arms, Legs, and Hippes. Chap. 5. Of the placinge, and situatione of
those parts, one the which the operatione, is set one work. and employed.
Chap. 6.
What the Ligamentes or Circumvolutiones are their matter, quallitye, form,
and quantitye. Chap. 1.
Having therefore spoken, treaated of the combinationes, and sutures,
vvherthrough al vvoudes, divisione, or separatio of the whole is inserted
combined together again, and the lips or edges of the foresayed vvoundes are
kept close together shut, we will now treacte, and handle of the
Ligamentes, or bindinges, of the wounded parts wherein we ought to consider,
what their matter, their quality, form, quantitye, differences are, What
Ligamentes are.
howe we ought to wind, and again vnvvinde the same.

The vvindinge therefore, or dressinge of a vvounde is nothing else, then a
vvindinge about, or circumvolution, of the Ligatures, which is as much to
say, with a broad and lunge ligamet, wherewith not only, the wounded
part, butt allsoe the fin itimate, and circumiacent parts, are involved, and
circumvestede by this means to restore them to their Former estate, and
natural being.

Matter of the Ligamentes.
Those Ligametes according to the affirmations of aunciet Chyrurgias, are of
divers matter, as of Linen, vvoolle, or Leather: the linen Ligamentes are the
most commonest of all the other, which at that time they used, when as they
would stifflye compresse, bring close together any part: they used
woollen rovvlers, in the fractures with vvoundes, gently in that sort to
retain together the broken bones, to cause no payne, or inflammatione at all.
Hippocrates used the Ligamentes of leather, Hippocrates.
in the fractures of the Nose, the nethermost chavv bone: But of what matter
soever we make the they must not be too much vvorne out, because that they may
be strong enough, to be dravvn close together, stretch stifflye out: Farther
more they must be even and smooth, nether must they have any silvages, nether
must it have any harde seames, must be cut aright the thread, according the
length of the linen, not cotradictorily: because they may tie smooth and
evenlye, without the one side being hardre tied then the other, as oftentimes
it chances, when the rovvler is cut contradictorryelye: It is right trevve,
that the ligature vvhervvithe after Phlebotomye we tie the arm, yields it
self better being so cut, but it chances heerin, because in that place it
yields it self better for the bending of the arm.

Touching their qvallity, they must be clean vvhyte, least that through
their impuritye they be a hinderance, or interruption unto the part, because
by that means they may the better receave the humidityes, as Oxicrate, vvyne,
or any such like, wherein we chance to madifye, vvett them. They must allso
be fine light because through their ponderousnes, they do not overburden the
part, suscitate, provoke payne, cause inflammation. Thirdly they must be soft,
flexible because through the obduratnes thereof, they might chance to hurt
that part. Fourthlye, of linen, which must be very vvhyte, not to closely
vveaved because so they are more better to be placede, the matter, the
evaporationes might the easier therthroughe transpire, which being therein
occluded, cause inflamatione, itchinge.

Form figure of the Ligamentes.
Concerning their form, figure, we must know, that these foresaede
Ligamentes, or rovvlers, are ether rovvled up, or involved, siplelye, or
dubblelye, unto the midst of the foresayed ligature of an aeqvalle latitude
in all places, as are those which we use in the fractures of the Arms,
Hippes, Legs: In like sort allso in the vvoundes, vlcerations of the same
parts. The double ligament or rovvler is ether of on piece of linen, on the
ends being diverselye cut, or on an other place, in other ends, as are the
ligamets of the Head, which are cut into four, or six ends: And in four
ends for the Scrotum: Or in the ligament, which is sovved together, made of
divers pieces, as are those which are used for the flancks, for the Breasts,
for the Testicles, for the Fundament, that especially we desirige to spare,
be chairye of the linen. And as much as concerns their longitude, latitud
that consists on the consideratione, judgment of the Chyrurgiane, which
before hand must see the figure, know it, must thereby allso be able to
know, the conformatione, sitvatione of the part, the diversitye of the
dissease: for when as we are vrgede to make a great circumvolutione, we
must then consider on the length, make it as long as is reqvired: cosideringe
the breadth, we must make the according to the longitude, latitude of the
dissease, The Ligamet must be broader then the dissease.
so that the foresaid ligament must be greater the the dissease, because with
on circuvolutione do not only involve the vvounde but allso both the ends
thereof as well on the one side as the other: for if so be the ligamente vveare
small, he must the crushe the dissease, vvherthrough great payne, and
inflammatione might becausede.

And to speak of the qvantitye of the foresayed ligamentes, Quantitye of the
Ligametes.
that is nothing else then the mean, and measure which we must use and
observe ether in stiffly or loosely bynding, as in respect, of the person. For
as the one man is grosser more corpulent then the other, so there are also
disseases, whereof the one is more painful then the other, wherefore we must
consider, that the ligament be nether too stiffly nor loosely tied, for all
ligatures which are too stiffly bovvnde, cause payne, concursione of humours,
with inflammations, yea sometimes allso a Gangraena: And those ligamentes which
are too loose, they are profitable for nothing, cause that the restauratede
parts, which are sitvatede, move therout, as the broken Legs the
dislocatede members, yea allso the lips of the vvoundes which are insertede,
and brought together through the loose ligature do again separate the one
from the other. And finallye in one vvorde to conclude, the mediocritye of
ligatione, is a great solace, and comfort to the Patient, as he will then
with his own mouth testify.

We must note that all ligatures must not in a vvoud so fast be ligatede,
Wherone in Ligatione we ought to consider.
as in a Fracture less in a Fracture which is without a vvounde, then in a
simple vvounde: We must allso harder, more violenter bind ligate on the
vulneratede part, one the fracture, the on any of the approximate, next
adioynig parts, thereby to repell, drive back again the humours, which do
thereon sink and allso crushe out, those which are sunk into the finitimate
parts, which is least shut: for in so doing, we keep those parts
occludede, liberate, free from all inflammations. Farther more in the end of
the ligatione we espy a tether tumefactione, out of which we may judge that
the part is reasonably shut: if so be the tumor be harde, obduratede,
blackishe, it is then a sign that the part is too stiffly tied. And whereas
at all we perceave any tumefaction, it is a sign that the ligament is too
looselye tyede.

Of the kinds, differences of the ligamentes. Chap. 2.
Two sorts of ligaments in general.
HIppocrates gennerally hath made two sorts of Ligamets: where of the first
is, that which of it self, by his virtues did prosper, First kind of
ligature.
opitulate the curing of the disseases, and without the which they can not
be cured because that with out the same, the part can not be contaynede in his
statione, or form, wherein she must be contained, to be cured, to be
praevented that there happen no fluxione unto the part, nether the matter
being therein congregatede, without the said ligature could not be driven,
expellede therout. As we may note on the ligamentes, which in the vvoundes of
the Head we use, which allso in all concavouse vlcerations we use, in
Fractures, in dislocatios, recurvatione of Ioynctes, in the separatinge of
those parts, which against nature lye the one above the other, in the
reductione of the separatede parts, in the apertione of the parts, which are
to closely occludede, shut against the mind of the Chyrurgiane, will
combine themselves together.

The second sort of Ligament.
The second ligament, is that which is ordaynede, not only because of it
self it is commodiouse, profitable, but Per accidens, bye chance, it is but
only used, to contain, keep the remedies, compresses, on the disseasede
part, as on a great, dolorous inflammatione, on a great Apostematione, or on
any other dolorous part.

Redivisio of the first kind of Ligature.
And touching the ligature which of it self is profitable, commodiouse, there
of there are two kinds, which of the use, where unto they are ordaynede, are
taken: which is ether to cotayne the parts as close in their natural being
because therthroughe they may be combinede vnitede, wherefore this ligatione is
called, the agglutinative, or incarnative ligatione: Or else to praevente, the
fluxione which might chance to come to that part, expel the same therout,
which is molestiouse, troublesome unto him, wherefore he is called the
expulsive, or repellinge ligature.

The incarnative or conglutinating ligation, Icarnative Ligament.
is commoly used in this form on the wounds. To vvitt, that we roll up the
rovvler, or ligature on both his ends, unto the midst thereof, whereof in
each hade we must take the one end being rovvled up: laying that part of the
ligamet which is not rovvled up, on the other side of the vvounde, reducinge
both the ends of the ligatione, which we have in our hands above on the
vvounde, that there through, we might adioyne, bring together, the separated
parts, lips of the vvounde, crossinge the foresayed ligature, in form of a
Burgundiane cross, or in form as in the margin is set down unto you: Then
we must reduce both the ends to the part, bringing one end, towards the
superior part of the membre, because thereby the fluxione may be praevented,
the other end, on the inferior part, that the blood therthrough, which is
sunk, and descend into that part, might be depressed, and crushed out: and
the fore said Ligature must be of such a breadthe, that when as he is rovvled
up, applied on the wounded part, may not only compraehende the vvounde but
alsoe both the ends, of the same.

If so be the vvoude vveare ample, great, and the ligature, considering his
latitude, Nota.
could not be accommodated, we must as then compraehende but the one half of
the vvounde, the other half thereafter.

Such a Ligament therefore must be reasonable closely vvounde, but allvvayes a
little stiffer one the wounded part, Hippocrates.
as Hippocrates vvilleth us to do, yea also, in the vvoundes with Fracture,
because there through might be expressed, and crushed out, the blood, which
in that part is sunk, least that there should happen any inflammatione
thereunto, then an Apostematione.

The expulsive, or repellinge ligamente, Expulsive Ligamet.
is very much used in Fistles, in concavouse vlcerations, thervvith to expel
the matter, which is descending to the grovvnde or bottom of the same, which
through the lunge retentione, internallye corrodeth that part. Admonitione.
This ligature is allso very commoeiouse in the Varices or burst veins, in
tumefacted Legs, but we may not use it in Fistles, nor in any concavouse
vlcerationes, unless first of all they be putrifyede, their callositye taken
therout, nether in inflammationes:

This ligamente is imposed, How we may make the expulsive ligament.
with the one end being revolvede, rovvled up, beginning with the sovvnde
part, which is sitvated close to the bottom of the Sinus, where he must be
somewhat more dravven together, shut, then is reduced again toward the
wounded part, toward the mouth, or apertion of the concavitye, with out
bindinge the same to stiff, consideratione, of the inferior part.

As if the Sinus be in the leg, the bottom of the same under the Knees, is
alsoe higher, hath his issu in the crassitude of the calfe, we must them
begin to bind, one the Knees, finish in the inferiore parts thereof.
Contrarilye if so be the inferior part of the Leg, the issue thereof be by
the Knees, we must the beginn the ligatio by the foot, end the same close to
the Knees. But if we desire to use this Ligature in great Arms, and great
Legs, which are Varicouse, we must then begin from belovv the leg, or
Arm, vpvvardes, which is the most farthest, from the original of the Veins
and supernallye end the same, to vvitt, about the Heart, the Liver, which are
the originals of all Veins, Arteryes, heerby to repell the humours which are
in that part, to praevent that there concur no more unto that place.

As far forth therefore, as if that part be rugged, and vnsmoothe, as is that
Leg, which is far more thicker in the Calfe thereof, then in the lovvermost
parts thereof, we must the fro palm, to Palmebreadthe, a little more the half
through cut the same, and fovvlde both sides cotradictorilye the one to the
other, so looslye sow the same, by which means the said Ligament, is made
to be recurvated, and croockedlye vvounde, like a Bovve. Having therefore
stifflye rovvled op this foresayed ligature, or rovvler, An attractive
Ligature.
we may thervvith as smoothlye wind involve a Leg, without making, therein
any inaeqvalitye, or furrovves causing that side which was cut of, the again
sovved, aeqvallye to agree with the thinnest part: the vvhol part which is
the longest, to agree with the greatest part. Above these two foresayed
Kinds, differeces of Ligamentes, we may as yet add thereunto a third Kind:
The which we call an attractive Ligamet, which we may use when we desire
to retracte, any blood, nourishment, or any other vital Spirits into that
part, the which, the part of it self can not draw, attracte unto it,
because her attractive forces are debilitated, very much feebled.

The second kind of Ligament above rehearsed.
We will somewhat alsoe speak of that ligament, which of it self is for no
use profitable but accidentallye cures, through his continuace of the
remedies, which are applied unto the dissease, wherefore we may call it the
retentive Ligament: wherein we must consider three especial things. First in
what disseases it is commodious: Secondly on howe many fashones, it may be
composed, made: Thirdly, Ligamet for the dissease.
lastelye, howe we ought to use the same.

Touching the first, we ether use the same, considering the part, or the
Dissease, which can tolerate no other: Considering the Dissease, as any
vvounde, or vlceratione, accompanied associated, with any great payne, or
inflammatione, or when we disire an Apostematione to be matured: Considering
the part, as when a vvounde is in the Head, in the Neck, in the Belly, or
in the Testicles, which parts ca not indur to be stiffly tied, or bovvnd,
therefore need no ligatione, but only to retain the remedies therone, because
that the other stiff ligatures, bring with them great discommodity.

Concerning the second poyncte: Ligamet for the part.
The retentive Ligamet, must have two, three, or four ends, according to the
form, situatione of the part which we would dress. Touching the third
poyncte, that must be begun in the dissease, ended on the other side of the
same.

Of the Rules, general Instructions, whereon we must consider, in all Rowles,
Ligatures Chap. 3.
Therefore convenientlye, Two things to be considered, when as decentlye we
desire to wind any part.
decentlye to wind, or involve any part, or mebre, we must note, consider
on two especial things, whereof the first is, the wounded part, which we
will wind: the second is the aegritude, or dissease. Touching therefore the
disseased part, we take this for a general Rule, that she must be tied,
that in such a form, figure, as we will have her to continue, lye: For if so
be we wind a folded, curvated part, which thereafter must be situated
right, and extented, there will as then conseqventlye ensue such or the like
Accidents: to vvitt, that the Ligature will dissolve, loosen, that there
vvilbe caused great pain, dolor in the part, because the Muscles, Veins,
Arteryes Synnues, the very bones alsoe have an other situatione, being
erected, extended, then when as they are recurved, and croocked.

As in example: If so be a broken Leg, Example.
being recurved, so on that manner be circumligated, vvounde which ought
othervvyse to be tied, being stretched extended right forth, without doubt
all the Ligature will go loose, when as we desire to extend the same, out
of the which will follovve great pain, because the broke Bones, Veins,
Arteryes, Synnues, Muscles, do not as the keep the same place, as they have
done, the part being bovvnde, tied.

Contrarilye, we desiring to dress a fracture in an Arm, he must then be
shut, folded together, for if so be we dress him being extended, when as
thereafter he shall be recurved bended, then the Bones in therre lying, with the
other parts, be turned into some other fashone, vvherthroughe the Ligature,
will in one place be loosened, in another spaned, which also suscitateth payne
in that part: For it must necessarilye follovve, that when as a tied part be
changed, and then ether shut bended, or extended, the there are some of the
Muscles extended, crushed, as in the rotunditye, in the abbreviatione, others
vveackened, united: And when they are exrected, they must needs then be
crushed through the Ligature, out of the which must necessarily follovve
intolerable payne, because of the Compresse, which in ferreth with it in
that part great fluxiones, other accidents, mischances.

The dissease.
Now for as much as cocerneth the dissease, we must circumligate, involve an
Arm or a broken Leg with a vvounde, on an other manner, and fashion, then a
Fracture with out vvoude: on an other fashion a Leg, which hath but a simple
Vlceratione, then a Leg, which hath a concavouse, or Fistulouse vlceratione:
And a Ioyncte which is full of payne, must one an other manner be tied, then
that wherein is no payne.

Decetlye to wind.
Therefore covenientlye, decentlye to ligate or wind, the Ligature must be
right, closely rovvled up, because we may houlde him the faster, stedyer in
the hade, without stirring, or glidinge this way or that vvaye, as he might
chance to do the same not being stiffly rovvled up, because that through his
hardenes he may the better be handled, conduced, so that all ligationes, far
covenienter, more decet to more contentment of the Patient, and the
circumstantes, or standers by may be effected, then other vvyse, when as the
Ligature is laid double, croocked, curved, uneven.

Howe we ought to tie the Ligament again make loose the same. Chap. 4.
Wherone we ought to, consider in the fasteninge of the ligature.
THe Chyrurgiane must consider, note, that there be no seames knots, or any
vnevennes, in the Ligament, which might chance to crushe the wound: and the
Ligatione alsoe being finished, considering that the end of the Ligature, be
not fastened, one the vvounde, nether one any place where there is any payne,
for unless with a pin, we fasten it, or else with a Needle sow it, it
allvvayes infers payne with it: wherefore we must fast the said
Ligature, ether higher, or lower, or one the side of the same, one the end of
the rovvler, one such a place of the Ligature, vvherone the Patient doth not
lye, as behind one the Head, in the Temples of the Head, one the Back, on
the Buttocks, nether one the Flanckes, or Armepittes,

And concerning the dissolutione, or vntyinge, therine we must consider two
things: Two things vvherone we must consider in the dressinge of a vvounde
Namelye one the time, when the Patient must be dressed, one the convenience of
doing the same: and heerin disagreethe the practise of the ancient
Chyrurgianes, from us: For touching the dressinge of vvoundes ( Celsus
vvilleth) that we ought not to dress the vvoude, but in three days once,
suffering it two days* Celsus.
to continue without dressing: And then vvilleth he us, that we dress it but
once in five days. And as touching the simple fractures, Hippocrates.
Hippocrates councelleth us, that in three days once we dress the But now
adays, we do not observe this manes, for we dress the Patient after the
first dressinge 24, hovvres, unless we feared any great fluxione of blood,
wherefore we suffered the Patient to lye lodger with out dressing, to vvitt,
two, three, four, or five days lunge: It is right true, that we sometimes
make loose the ligamet in two, or three days once, without touching of the
plaster, only to give air to the part, to note, whether there be nether
Inflamatione, nor any other Accident come there unto: But when as the vvounde
is come to very great suppuratio, that there is much matter, payne, or
Inflammatione at hand, we as then dress the vvounde two, or three times in
the space of 24 hours, to vvitt, all eight hovveres once, if it be possible.

And touching the simple Fractures, For simple fractures.
we keep the sometimes, six, or seven days undressed, unless that there
vveare any Accidents, at had, but allvvayes we tarry as lunge as is
possible: for how little soever we touch a broke leg, the ends of the
bones, of the fracture are nevertheless, stirred, dislocated, the on rubbed
against the other, vvherthroughe is caused payne, and the combinatione, or
together healinge is hindered, because that all conglutinatione, can not be
with out continual coniunctione of the one part with the other.

The redressinge, or dressinge, How we may getlelye take of the Ligament.
must be done one this manner: to vvitt, that we easily make loose the band,
or rovvler, now with the one hand and then with the other looseninge of the
same, always houldinge the whole Ligament in the hand. But because most
commolye the first dressinge of the vvounde, cleaves so fast, as if with
glevve it vveare thereon fastened, because of the blood matter, which is
therone dried, and baked, we must first of all therefore, madefye, moysten the
same with a little vvarmed vvyne, and thervvith soacke the same because the
ligament may be taken, revolved thereof without payne, yea or else also in the
vvindinge of, of the same coming on the vvounde, we might there everye time
cut it of, because in so doing, we might by peecemeale take away the same,
without in any sort hurtinge of the Patient.

How we shall dress the broken Arms, hippes, and legs. Chap. 5.
Our manner of dressinge of al broke legs may be assimilated, and compated,
with the manner of the ancient Chyrurgianes.
AL though that our common practise, of the dressinge of all broken Legs,
seems to be alienate, and to differ, from the manner of doing, of the
antique, ancient professors, Chyrurgias, it is notwithstanding in such sort,
that we easily can compare them together. All the ancient physicians,
Chyrurgianes, have bin of opinio that in the Fractures we ought to use a
double kind of Ligament: Namelye inferiore ligamentes, which they called
Hypodesmidas, superior ligatures, which they called Hypodesmous: they have
these names because of their situatione, because some of them are tied, and
bovvnd under some again above. And as touching the inferior ligametes,
Hippocrates makes mention of two sorts.

Two sorts of inferior, or vnderligametes.
Whereof the first, the shortest, begins one the fracture, laying always
the one end contradictorilye therone because it should not vvholy lye, on the
place of the pain, which ligament must be tied round about the fracture, then
be reduced vpvvardes, where he as the ends: This ligament must closely be
vvounde together, because so the fluxio, which might chance to sink into the
disseased part, might the better be kept therout. The second ligament, which
almost, must be as lunge again as the first, is allso begun one the same
manner, to vvitt, on the fracture, laying only therone, a turn, or two,
winding dovvnevvardes, to crushe therout the blood which might chance to
sink into the fracture, with circumvolutios, which must be laid a little
more a part the one fro the other, then in the first ligament they vveare: for
we must take heed of making to great expressiones of blood, in the ends
of the Arms, or legs, that without imflammatio they can not receave much
thereof, and the ligament being come down, must wind the same vpvvardes
again, to come again to that place where we did beginn, because both these
ligamentes might hold fast, and the muscles be brought into their natural
situation, which through the two foremost ligationes might be brought therout.

Subdivisio of the second inferior ligature.
Other ancient Chyrurgianes make of this nethermost ligature, two ligationes,
whereof the one is the surest the second for the fractures, which begins
one the broke part: having thereafter, made one, or two circumvolutiones, then
is the foresaved ligature reduced dovvnevvardes. The other which may be the
third for the fractures, must be begun, on the beginning of the part,
ending supernally where the first ligatio is ended passing over the Fracture:
So that they impose therone three ligationes all which three we may call
inferior ligametes. The first which ascends from the fracture one high, as
from the middle of the leg toward the knee. The second which descedeth
from the fracture dovvnevvardes, as from the middle of the leg towards the
foot. The third, which from the extreameste part of the Ioyncte, ascends
superiorlye as fro the the soul of the foot towards the knees.

But novveadayes folovving our common practise, An astringent plaster on the
fracture.
which we use before these three ligamentes, we apply first of all, one the
place of the fracture an astringent Plaster, which is made of Bolus, of
flower, or volatill meal, of vvhytes of Eggs, of Oil of Roses of a little
Terebentine, (in stead of Cerotu Galeni, which the aunciet Chyrurgians used:)
After the first dressing we use the Plaster of Diacalcitheos, or of Diapalma,
liquefacted in oil of Roses, with vinegar. The ligature now being decetly
vvoud, because the legs, the hippes the Arms, are thinner belovve then above
they are, we must therefore one such slenderness apply some certain copresses
because the foresayed ioyncte may be in all places of an aequall crassitude,
thickenes, the splinter, which must be made of stiff paper, of lattinn, or of
any other substance, be very evenlye, smoothlye applied therone, tied under
the inferior ligatures, or rovvlers.

These splinters must be three in number, which must be excavated like a
gutter, whereof the first must be a little broader, then the other to by
applied under the fracture, to compraehende all that, which is belovve the
fracture, be as it vveare a fundament, or foundatio thereunto. The other two
one both the sides of the fracture, being somewhat separated the one fro the
other, least that in the ligatio they chased to glide the one over the other.
They must also be of such a longitude, as the broken part requires.

These three foresayed splintes being thus imposed, one the inferior rovvlers,
Applycatione of the inferior Ligatures.
we must then tie the fracture that it may lye fast and steady the part be
contained in a good quallitye disposione. With the first of these two
ligatures, we must beginn, at the extremest part of the membre, fro under
vpvvardes. The second must beginn fro above end belovv: we must allsoe note
that the one of those rovvlers, must take his beginning internally in the
membre, must proceed from the left to the right side, because they may cross
the one over the other, in such a form as in the margin you may see, or in
form of a St. Andrevves Cross, because it may be involved very closely
thereon.

It seems that Hippocrates hath used no other ligature, in the Fractures
with vvounde, but that he rovvled the same somewhat loose.

Ligametes for shot vvoudes, with crushing of bones.
But in those fractures which vveare shot, or with any other such like
instrument, or engine, be done, wherein is great crushinge of bones, because
we should little need to handle this part, which is thus broken, and
crushed, least the acute, and sharpe ossicles, or bones, frustles, the which
in the pertractatione of the same, might chance to prick the Flo^he, Tedones,
Synnues the Peritoneum, we must there fore use heervnto great Copresses, being
folded three, or four on the other, and so sovved together in the middle,
being in two places cut through, the compresse being of the breadth of a
palm, of ones hade, or theraboute, which we revolve the one towards the
other part, as if we desired to circumligate the part. These, or such like
Compresses, we must by little little shove under the crushed part, or mebre:
the same being immudifyed, impure, and we desire to lay an other thervnder,
we as then sow a clean white Compresse, one the impured Compresse, as then
we draw away the impure compresse from under the Fracture then subseqvetly
follows the mundifyed, clean compresse thervnder, in the stead of the
immundifyed. I have constituted the defiguratione, of these Compresses, here
before amongst the figures of the Instruments, in the table of the
Glossocomium, which of Hippocratis is called Ambi, on which place, in the
forsayed leaf, is defigured unto you, a broken leg, with the Ligamentes of
the same.

Of the situation, and of the constitutione or collocatione of those parts,
one the which the forsayed Operatione must be effected. Chap. 5.
No man is ignorant, how necessary, requisite these operationes are, to the
resanatio, of the disseases, wherewith mas body is daily, continually
oppugned opressed: and indeed, this operation should in vayn be used, if so be
that not only the whole body, but also the forsayed disseased parts, vvhero
we effect our Operation, vveare not thereafter, constituted situated, and
with most convenience collocated.

Therefore decentlye, with most couvenience to collocate the same, Vitilitye in
constitution of the part.
the Patient must first of all lye on his bed, (if so be at the least the
magnitude, and largeness of the dissease require the same,) that in such a form,
that the vvouded, or vulnerated part; may be with most ease, coveniece
collocated, as it is reqvisite, for they are not al together situated in one
fasho, manner: It is right true, that there are some certain rules, as are in
the ligatures, which must only be used one disseases.

First of al therefore, generally, Three things to be considered in the
collocatio of a part, or membre.
to collocate rightly situate al wounded parts, we must consider three
especial things: to vvitt, that the situation, be Soft, Smooth High: Soft,
because a hard situatio, sometimes might chance to disturb, and irrequiate,
not only the vulnerated part, but also the approximate parts there unto, by the
which means great pain, inflammation, consequently also an attractione, a
fluxion must necessarily follovve in that part: which is more, the Patient is
constrained not being able to tolerate the obdurate collocatio, to ease and
yield solace to that part, by oftentimes turning, and revertinge of
himself, which is very unprofitable for him, because that part, requires
nothing else then quietude, without being much disturbed, and molested.

Even, and smooth, becavse that the vneve, Plain, smooth situation.
implanitude collocation, infers pain recurvatio in the Ioyncte, to vvitt,
the one part resting on somewhat, the other part, dependinge without
resting one any thing.

On High, because that therbye, High situatione.
al concursione of humours is praevented, which otherwise through the low
situatio of the Iovncte, might be therthervvardes drawn.

Considering this occasion, have the Chyrurgians always caused the broken or
wounded Arm to be dependently vvorne one the breast the leg to be collocated
on high, to wit sovvhat higher then the body, the Patient lying on bed,
because he may nether be erected, nor sit.

Nether is it only necessary that the membre, or part be collocated softly,
smoothly, The aperon of a wound must all ways be recurvated dovvnevvardes if
it be possible.
highly, but it is also requisite, (if so be there be any vvounde, or
vlceration, which especially is fistulouse) that the apertio thereof, if so be
it is possible, recurvate it self dovvnevvardes, because that the matter,
might have the liberater a passage, to enter forth at, without making any
greater cocavityes, do not chance to incece, or inflame the approximate
parts, nether to corrode them, which would bring with it divers, sundry
accidents: praeposterate the resanatione of the same.

Secondarilye, The situatione of the Ioyncte must be natural, accustomed.
the Ioyncte must be situated in such a form, and fashone, which is agreinge
with the naturalle disposione of the Ioyncte, and alienated from all payne:
which two observationes, are well agreinge the one with the other: For all
fashons which cause no payne, that is the naturalle, and accustomed figure,
and collocatione thereof: Even as the painful, and vnnaccustomed figure is
against nature, so that the natural, and accustomed figure, or fasho, is
vvholy without pain: which happeninge, the Patient must a lunge continuance
keep his Ioyncte in that being, because so he shall feel no, or at the
least very small payne at all which is very comodiouse, to the resanation of
all vvoundes, vlceratios, Fractures also for all dislocatios, as it is in like
sort profitable for all other disseases vvhatsoever,

The ancient Chyrurgians, have called, this form of situatione, the right
and true collocatione, because all the Veins, Arteryes, Synnues, muscles, as
then are right, nether are they extended, nether lye they recurved.

The natural situatio which of the aucient professors, is called the right
situation.
And although that the Arm have binn anglevvyse collocated, notvvithstading
we esteem it as then to lye right, because that all the foresayed parts, are
accounted to lye right, not extended, nether recurved, that this collocation of
the Arm is naturalle, as here after we will more largelye speak of.

An example of the situatio of any disseased part.
And by degrees to specifye, the situatione of each, sundry part, we will
here describe some of them for an example: when as there is any vvounde,
Apostemation, or dislocation in any Ioyncte, we must then with all diligence
note the situation there of: for through a bad situatione, not only
immediately are caused many accidents, but after the sanatione thereof, the
Ioynct sometimes tarries right out extended, which ought to have bin
croocked, or else abides croocked where it ought to be exteded or else
combines it self, joins with some other thing, which ought to have binn
separated, seioyncte. An excellent observation
Wherefore if the vvounde be in the uppermost part of the shoulder, we must
then impose under the Armepites of the Patient, a great linne balle, hang the
arm one the Breast, somewhat bear, out his elbovve, because that the head of
the arm, may be somewhat elevated, the separated parts, may again be
inserted the one with the other and so much the better be cured together
again: The contrary whereof must be observed, if so be that in the inferior
part be any vvounde, as under the Armepittes: for the wound being cured,
we do not lift up sometimes, the Arm let him fall down again, stir him
othervvayes, without causinge any payne, and that the Arm is not held from
the Breast, the Patient thereafter, because of the Cicatrice shall not be able
to lift up his Arm because through the same the Arm is grovven stiff. As
thereof we have an example, exemplifyed unto us of the throat, in the Neck
also of many and divers, which being wounded, or burned, the cicatrice hath
remained so obdurated, that they there through have ether held their Heads, to
much on high or to much stoupinge, or haging backvvarde, or to much dependinge
ether on the right, or one the Leftsyde.

If so be that there be an Apostemation, The anglevvyse figure, is the natural
form of the Arm.
in the Elbovve, or else that the same be dislocated, or wounded, we must the
situat the Elbovve angle, or cornervvyse: for such aforme is very necessary
for the same, natural, accustomed unto hi: although, the cornervvyse figure is
that which makes an acute corner, notvvithstading we mean thereby through
the anguled figure* with the ancient Chyrurgians, that which hath his corner
right but yet not so clean, and vvholy erected, but that which attaygneth
somewhat toward the rectitude, so that it comes somewhat bluntly, and such
a figure is not as yet truly the mean, between the inflexione, and the
extensione, but is the same which yields her self, toward the extensione:
If so be the Arm be inserted and combined, with being able to be recurved,
or exteded we may yet as then better, and with more facility help him
self, then if he vveare vvholye recurved, or vvholye extended.

We may alsoe observe the same in the hand, The hand must be shut.
for we must keep the fingers shut, half bended, and if so be the dissease
vveare in one finger only, it vveare then better that the same continued ether
inflected, or incurved, the that he vveare straight, for the property of the
hand is to gripe and take houlde, the which she can not effect, and bring
to pass, then through the recurvatione of the same.

If so be above in the Hipp there chance to come, an Apostemation, or
vvovnde, The hipp must be stirred.
we must then in the consolidatinge of the same, observe that which in the
shoulder we have seen, as being necessary to stir the foresayed part, on
divers fashons, because the head of the bone, do not insert it self, and
grow fast in the pan.

We observe in the Ioyncture of the knees the contrary, for if so be, The
leg must be kept right.
that there be a vvounde, or an Apostemation, or else in the finitimate part,
the Patient must the hold his leg right, without having the heals dravven
in towards his buttocks, as it in divers persons happens, and that to the
hinderance of the Patients, when they are cured.

And we must dilligently note, that the leg be collocated as right, as is
possible, and if so be by chance it lay recurved, we must then as gentlelye,
as is possible stretch and extend the same again, othervvyse the patient
being cured, might chance to be mutilate, and without great indecoru, or
deformity should not be able to go, as we have knovvene the same to hap
unto two great personages, of our times.

Now as concerning the foot, the Toes, they must allsoe be extended, and not
recurved, or inflected, as we have spoken of the had, fingers: Discommodity
of having the Leg croocked
For if so be they continued so croocked, the body as then should not be
able to rest, or stayede thereon: So that the situation of the Arm, is
cotrarye to the Hand, to the Leg, and to the Foot: because the one must be
recurvede, or croocked, and the other extended right out.

The end of the French Chyrurgerye, containing the manual Operations.
Methodicalle discourse, or rehearsal, of the Originalles, occasiones, Signs,
Tokens, Accidents, chances, Prognosticationes, and Remedies, against the
Bloodyeflixe, or Dysenterye.
BY all other miserable and pitifulle disseases which do commonly vex, and
trouble the Entralles, and run through the same, as is the Cholicke, the
restrictione, or constipatione, Ileus, Lyenteria, Diarrhea, Tenasmus, the
vvormes, many other such like yet me thincketh the Dysenteria, or the Bloody
flixe, of all these to be most intolerable, Bloodyeflixe.
and most mortal: And also amongst the Commonaltye, amongst our French
Martiallistes or vvorriours, amongst the Svvitsers, Countryemen, Englishemen,
farther amongst all sorts, and Kinds of vvarriours, it is so common, that it
seems this dissease, as soon as they are gone out of their houses, that there
nothing else follows them, the the shaddovv of their body, vvherthrough in
all leagers through this dessease at sometimes there are more poeple deprived
of their lives, then through all the inqvietude, through all povertye,
through the Plague, yea more alsoe then of the enemy are Killede: And
touching my opinione herein, I take this sickness to be a pestilentialle
spirit, and a certain essence of the Plague: So that necessarilye there under
must be occulted, hidden, a secret, ineffable venom, or poison, which
through defilinge, and infectione, spreads it self from the one body into
the other, Because the intestines or entralles, of a sovvnde, and vvelfaring
man, through some inclinatione, or Sympathy, are made partakers of such
venoumouse vapours, of him whose guts are polluted with this dissease, and
by that means also are imparted with the bloodyeflixe: As in the cosumptione
we may see, which proceeds out of some vlceratione of the Lunges, and also
in the Ophthalmye of the Eyes, the same to be as infectiouse, that the on body,
may obtain the same of an other: Which comes to pass, Reason of Aristotle.
as Aristotle says, because that the Lunges, the Eyes, are continually
contained in a perpetualle motion, vvherthrough the venoumousnes is the easier
imparted to an other body: Health being, as it vveare a continual rest, and
an assured estate.

And as the entralles, have also their perpetualle motione, the which in
Greek we call Peristaltica, in like sort alsoe may they impart their
dissease unto other entralles, as the eyes unto the eyes and the Lunges do
unto the lunges.

Of the Causes, Signs, Accidents, prognosticationes, and remedies, I will
somewhat discourse, considering more the necessity thereof, thereby to
instruct the yonge Chyrvrgian, the because of any pleasure, which I take
therein, or any ostentatione.

The Dysenteria, or bloodyeflixe, Description of the Bloodyeflixe.
is a sangvinolent excrement of the Belly, with great dolor, and as it
vveare scissure which the Latinistes, call Tormina: And this dissease may be
taken properlye, or improperlye.

The improper Dysenteria, or Bloodyeflixe, Dysenteria Improperlye taken.
is a fluxione of blood, with out any great pains, or travayle, whereof
there are two sorts, or Kinds, ether when we avoid pure, clean blood,
which we call Sangvinolenta, Cruenta or else when we avoid unclean blood,
and impure.

The pure clear blood issveth forth of the Mesenterium, vvheare a lunge time
it hath lain occulted, cogregated, as it oftentimes chanced, in those which
are gross of Belly. And sometimes alsoe it proceeds out of the Liver, of the
Milte, of the Hemorrhodes, and sometimes also out of some great veins, and out
of other parts, yea and also out of the whole habitude of the body: All
which differeces, must through their own signs be known, because the
resanatione of the same may be the surer.

As for example it happens commonly, Those which are deprived of any
Ioyncte, are very subject unto the Bloodyflixe.
through any great superfluitye of bleeding, in those which have lost an
Arm, or a leg, that on certain times they have the bloodyeflixe* In which
kind of Mysenteria, we need not much to fear, nether need we to use those
remedies heervnder described: But must restraygne such Patients, from great
comestiones, and superfluouse drinckinge of VVyne, and cause him to be well
phlebotomised: whereof we in this place endevoure to advertise, and instruct
the yonge Chyrurgiane, because that without making any great delay, he might
be adiuvable helpefull to the patient with these remedies which consequently
follovve: the which in passing by, it seemed convenient unto me to rehearse:
Because it is not my intent, in this place, only to speak, of the improper
Dysenteria, curatione thereof, but of the true, proper bloodyeflixe, or
Dysenteria, which in all Leagers is common, familiar, and infectiouse.

The impure Bloodyeflixe, is the truest, assured, and is assimilated unto
water, wherein nue slaughtered flesh, hath binn washed: the vvaterye
Bloodyeflixe, Fluxus Hepaticus.
is called Fluxus Hepaticus, the Liver laske, which is caused, through the
imbicillitye, of the retentive forces of the liver, through the which the

bereft of all his forces therthroughe also the stomach is feebled, that it can
not any more compraehend, any meat, or drink, to feed one, nether digest
the same, so that by this means the whole body consumes and vvithereth.

The black, heavye, thick bloodyeflixe, proceeds especially out of the Milte,
which is, as it vveare a Poole, or receptacte, of al melancholic, thick,
and blackebloode.

Dysenteria properlye taken.
But that which properly, or uprightly is called Dysenteria, or Bloodyeflixe,
that is a common vlceration of the entralles, out of which first of all the
viscositye of the same is driven, expelled out, then the Axvngiousenes therofe,
with a little red blood intermingled: Thirdly, the internalle membrane of

more, more grovving vvorse, as the consequently follows the carnositye, the
very substance, of the corroded, corrupted guts: all which kides, are

or mordicante pain, with continual punctios, with continualle concupiscence
of going to stool, with intollerabilitye, with a continual comotio. Which
foresayed Dysenteria, or Bloodyeflixe, ether happens to the small entralles
or to the great intestines, the which we first espy out of the situatione of
the payne, out of the diversitye, and mixture of the matter,

When as therefore the upright Bloodyeflixe is in the small guts, Signs
when the small guts ate hurt.
as then we see blood, certain membranouse pellicles, intermixed with the

the body have the one with the other, is wonderful, to be admired, because
as soon as the action of one part is hindered, or suffers any thing, al
the other parts of the body do thervvithe conspire, and sympathise.

If so be the said vlceratio be in the great guts, or entralles, Signs of
the great* intestines
there as the drives one the deiectiones, or excrementes certain drops of

The occasion of the bloodyeflixe is the venoumouse puissance and force,
Occasion cause of the Dysenteria.
Dynamis of the acute, saulte, and mordicant humours, which being as it
vveare on the journey, to descend right to the guts, but they come

Also through any vicious, acute gross cibaryes and those which are apt

It is right true, that this dissease, Indicati^ how we may easily, or
difficultlye attain to the Bloodyeflixe.
oftentimes reagneth in the Prime, or vernal time of the year, and especially

with any facility, difficulty, or impossibility may be repelled, and cured.

We esteem the cure of the Dysenterya to be of more facility, the same
being in the great guts, or intestines Caecum, Colon, Rectum, the it being
in the small entralles, Duodenum, Ieiunum and Ileum.

We take also the same to be less dangerous, in yonge persons, and in the
men, then in yonge children, and vvoemen.

In a longevalle, or lunge continuing Dysenterye, it is a bad sign when the
appetite is departed, yet a far more vvorse sign, when as there are
associated unto the same Agues, or Imflammationes.

They which are of most experience may judge of this poyncte.
As is that which is caused, out of any Apostemation, being burst out of the
Liver, or of the Milte, which very rarelye happens, and yet more rare out
of the pulmonicalle Apostemationes, whereof the matter disgorges it self in
the left ventricle of the Heart, and so into the Artery called Aorta, the
trunk, or body of all other Arteryes, fro thence into the Veins of the
Mesentery, which are exteded to the entralles, the which passage or way we
can not then through imagination compraehend, and which is very obscure. And
if so be there followed any peculiar thing therout, besides the causticke,
and venoumouse matter it might the infer fearful, dangerous accidents,
to the hart of the Patient, (which is the vvelspring of life) the only
original, of vital spirits, which are diffused over the whole body, are
occasion of the action, motione, also all other agilityes of the body.

The evomitio of the choleric humours, in the beginning of the dissease, do
bear vvitnes alsoe of the danger follovvinge.

The bloody flixe, which is caused out of any melacholicke humours, is esteemed
to be without any hope.

The convulsio of synnues, the Hickough, the parbraking, are forerunners, as it
vveare, embassadoures of death.

In like sort allsoe when we espy, a black spott behind the left ear, as
big as a Vetche, vvherbye is great alteratione, that allso is a sign of
Death, as Hippocrates vvithnesseth unto us. If so be this dissease, through
negligece, or through malice, or aulteration, or by any other means, came to
be inveterated, the Patient as then will vvholye consume, vvexe so feeble,
that without great difficulty he shall not be victor thereof.

This therefore is the cause, or occasio, the species, kinds of this dissease,
the afflicted, opressed part being aperte, and known, we must now
proceed to the resanatione of the same.

The final end of the curatione, is ether generalle, or specialle.

The general end is to be noted, ether on the dissease, or on the Physitione,
as on both the especiallist, most principal personages, which act and set

the Dysenteria.
the patient must keep himself reposed, and quiet, because all vlcerationes,

vvithdravve themselves into other external parts of the body.

possibly may or can, without constraygninge himself thereunto,

The Chyrurgian must first of al consider on the use of the astringent
medicamentes, What astringent medicametes the Patient must use.
which before repast, or comestion are used: for he cotayninge the viands, or
cibaryes, they help also to the digestio of the same: but by the astringent
things I understand, mean, which are reasonablelye fortifyinge, and
confortative, and those which are helpefulle to the concoctione: For it vveare
the greatest, absurdest error of the world, if so be in the first,
especially in an vnhealthfulle body, to use violent stopping astringent
medicametes, for it vveare nothing else, the to shut keep our enemy or
thief within doors.

Wherefore his viands, or meat drink, What his meates, drinckes must be.
must only be Diureticke, astringent causinge to void urine, because through
the urine especially, all aquositye of the blood is evacuated: but if so be you
perceave, that the great toughenes of the acute sharp adusted Pituita, or

permit some considering the imbicillitye of their stomachs, to use drink a

Medicamentes which we must eschevve.
Farthermore we must beware, and take heed, of all sharpe, mordicant

Thirdly we must consider on the Liver, especially on the stomach, because

The specialle end must be the fluxione, or the dissease it self.

The Scopus of the fluxione is, to stop, to diverte the concurrent humours,

consideration, as before we have said, least that in reseratinge of the same
you increase the ague, the which with reason, and experience may chance.

Remedies which may be taken out of the reason.

But amongst the external remedies, Phlebotomye is very commodiouse for the

The Patient is praeserved by his strength, through good nurture,

Finallye, we must in an extreme Dysenteria, for the last remedy, endevoure

Mandragora, the could seeds, the Philounium. Reqvies Nicolai, many oaths such
like compositiones, which are unto this dissease used, which may not be
administered the with great judgment and advice had therone.

The end of this Treatise of the Dysenteria, or Bloodyeflixe.
An Apologye, for the Surgeons, where through is apparantlye, evidentlye

There is novvadayes in all France, but one expert Chyrurgiane, whom everye

Comparasion.
So that novvadayes, the Chyrurgianes also do not any more ask, nether do

Out of which ignorace, persuasion, as not to know, what vvoundes are
