such motios turbatio of the mind, are of great forces, in the body of man,
they cause therein ether great aegritudes disseases, or else death: for if to be
the motions, and perturbations of the mind, have pover to increace the
natural caloure, or to diminis he the same, of causing the same to draw in
vvardes, or to expel the same outvvardes to spread it self by the whole
body: such motios of the natural calour, which do reduce with the the
vital spirits, the blood, is an occasio of all disseases, of health: there
must then certaynlye subseqvute, that the said motions, and perturbationes of
the soul, and mind, have all power in their hands of our health.

So that it is even as Aristotle, Lib. de motu animalis Chap. 5. says, that
the motions, and turbations of the mind, do cause such a remotio and
alteration of the natural caloure: as Hippocrates also testifies the same
unto us sect. 5. of the 6. Epidemiorum, Galenus Cap. 5. secundae de Symptomatum
causis and Chap. 5. of the second of Methodes; Through pertubations of the
mind, a man may dye.
where he demostrateth, that there are many which through motione, and
perturbatione of the minds have died: Wherefore Galen also, on the 14. rule,
of the 4. section, of the sixth Epidemiorum, those which internallye, have a
sharpe corrodent caloure, (wherefore by by so much the more we must prohibit
the wounded) to abstayn from ire, and choler, and from all turbations of the
mind, vvherbye the humours might be moved. The said Galen in his book de
sanitate tuenda demonstrates, that all turbations of the soul all motions of
the mind, do ingendre great quantitye of Cholera whereby are caused great
inflamatios fluxions in the wounded part and per conseqvence a Gangraena, and
sometimes in the end a sudden death.

Amongst all other turbations of the spirits, Joy is that which ought to
cause the least accidents unto our bodies, notwithstanding was the same so
immoderate, and violent in Chilonio lacedemonico, Sudden death of Joy.
in Diagero Rhodiano, both the which so joyed themselves, perceavinge their
children victoriouslye to return over their enemies, that of joy they both
immediately, and suddaynlye died: for through such immoderate joy, the blood
and the vital spirits of the Heart, are with such vehemecy reverted from the
Heart, and diffused and disperced, through the universal body that the
Heart, is vvholye destitute of his natural caloure, the person must
necessarilye fall into syncopizatione, or fayntnes, out of which commonly
follows death: Virtues of moderate joy.
But if the joy be moderate, it fortifyeth, and strengthens the animale, the
naturalle virtues, stirs up the vital spirits, suggerateth the digestione,
and is consequently commodious for all the constitutione of mans body,
vvherthrough the Ioynctes, are throughely soacked, moystened, because of the
humiditye which is disperced through the whole massa of the blood, so that
by this means, the vvoundes are better modified, and incarnated and those
parts do increace, and pingvifye.

Effects of Cholera.
In like sort alsoe doth Cholera, or anger, and yet also more, then the
immoderate joy: because heerby, besides all this that in the spirits humours of
the body is caused great corrvptione and they through their great heat,
caloure chance to inflame, and by this means consequently, the whole
habitvde of the body is replete with putrid agues, how little sick soever
the person be, which foresayed agues, if they chance to afflict the patient
unto death as it oftentimes happens, we most commolye then attribute the
same unto his wound, Accidents of sorrow.
as if of the Chyrurgiane he had not bin well handled, and not to the ague.
Is it not evidente enough unto us, what accidents are caused through
sorrow, and grief, how healthful, sovnd soever the personne be which is
thervvith entrapped? for she so reserateth, and as it vveare strictly
occludeth the Heart, that by no means there can engendre any vital
spirits, and how few soever there are, yet they may not be dispercede with
the blood through the whole body, because the same is gross, and tenebrous,
by the which the vital virtues, and al their accoplices, are debilitated: so
that in the end a man is in his mind vexed, hebede, The melacholicke do hate
themselves.
the Heart omitteth all joy, pleasure, he odiously hates himself, falling
into desperatione, and raging, having lost his livelye colour in his face,
consuming the body, whereby oftentimes must follow death.

It will not also be alienate unto our purpose, to the a poroving of my
sayings, that I here recite that which the father of eloquence hath writ
ad Atticum, the words whereof are these: It vveare an excellent matter my
good friend Atticus, that man could live, without meat, or drink: but yet a
far more excellelleter matter if so be without any envy, and hate we
could Live: because those viands which we eat, do corrupt nothig else the
our humours, but the traitorous envy, and sorrow, do consume us eve unto the
bones: Envye, malice consume the bones, corrode the entralls, as we playnlye
may behoulde: for man sickeneth, through some certain envye, malice, of the
which he by continuance of time dies. Doest thou not know by experience,
that two torturors, which will deprive a ma of life, that the torturer of
grief, and sorrow is the most cruellest, yea then of the Gluttonye? Effects
of stupefactione.


Nether may we here omit to recense, and speak of the obstupescece, fear,
wherewith the covvardes, faint hearted are oftentimes touched, and taken: This
stupor causes in us, the same accidetes which the sorrow causes but
somewhat greater for the time: for this fore said stupefactione, and fear,
expulseth from him, retracteth toward the heart (but with more festination,
more raptnes, then the sorrow) the blood the vital spirits, vvherfor we
may perceave, that the face in the time of stupefactione, fear, vvaxeth pale,
and the external parts could, with tremblinge of the whole body: the Belly
relaxateth, the speech fails, with a great reverberation of the heart,
because that through the great quantity of blood, of the spirits, which
suddaynly do retire thethervvardes, being almost suffocated can very
difficultlye move it self, but greatly desires to be refreshed, and
discharged of such a sarcinatione, so that oftentimes there follows death,
because the blood being drawn towards the Heart, suffocates it self
there, by that means the natural calor and the vital spirits beig
extingvished, Error of the vulgar, common poeple.
without the which the life of man can not be preserved.

If so be that any body being wounded, through perturbatios of the mind do
chance to dye, the vulgar common poeple, will not attribute the occasio of
death unto chose praecedent, or praenominated occasions, but far more to the
negligence, and ignorance, of the Chyrurgian, which hath not intreacted him,
as it vveare convenient he had done, Fear and nicenes do impeach health.
although that those which have a more sovvnder judgment of such occasions,
will judge clane contrary thereunto, and others. The like also may be said of
those, which will not allovv of the will, and intent of the Chyrurgian,
nether of any other remedies which vveare commodious, and proffitable for his
health, being ether to timorous, or delicate, to suffer any apertion, which
for his disease had bin necessary, to administre issue, passage to some
corrodent matter, or parcels of bones, that lye there praepared ready to be
taken out, which by their remansione in that place, do also corrupt the
finitimate parts, do alter permutate the remanent part of bone, and corrupt
the Marrovve, whereby the health, can not suddaynlye follovve, as the
Chyrurgiane is exoptatinge, vvishinge for the same, yea also by this means
oftentimes remain incurable, by which occasion the Chyrurgiane, The History
of Duke d'Aumalle aftervvardes Duke of Guise.
with his Patient must hope for that which his Patient will not suffer, and
yet notvvithstandinge is required of his disease. And to this end Du Bellay,
recites in his memories, that the Duke d'Aumall, son to the Duke a Gvyse,
being mortally vvouded, with a spliter of a lauce, which pierced his Eye,
said to the Chyrurgians, entreat, or handle not my son as a Prince, or
mighty Lord, but as a Pioner, or servant: which was alsoe an occasion of
his resanation, because he suffered, Contemplation of instruments.
that the tronchone of the Lance, which stuck clean through his head, to be
with force, and violence drawn therout.

And if therefore we desire to enter into the conteplatio of the diversityes of
instrumemetes wherewith the vvoudes are made, we shall then find sufficient
occasion, to establish the unexpected death considering only the matter
whereof the vvoundes are received, as well of the small, as great vvoundes.
Those which have written of Agriculture, Whervvith the vvounde is made must
be considered
or tillage, Gardening, as Cato Plinius, Columella say, that theris great
difference, whether a tree be with an Iron knife engraffed, or with a knife
of Bone. And we make little or no difference, whether our bodies are wounded
with Lead, Iron or Brass, seeing that brass being mixed vvithe the matter of
our vvoundes resolves it self into viriditye, which corrodeth the flesh,
and causes sometimes such an inflammatio, that death thereafter follows.
Nether is it sufficient, that in short time, we have accommodated this mettle
unto our ruin, but now by little and little we make it four cornerde, which
is far more dangerous, the if it vveare round, because through the

vvhero it glances, without any small hope of resanatione: as also sometimes
the bullets are fastened together with coppervvyer by which means they cut
a sundre all flesh, veins, arteryes and Synnues, which are in that part.

And because that those men, which comonlye we must give account of our cures,

Discommodityes of wars
to trample one which not only one or two days have binn in great trouble
but the whole before winter have suffered in tolerable could, and marched

Many there are which for want of succour do dye.
Others dye because in time they are not dressed and succoured, which as yet
not long a go is happened, unto our great sorrow, lamentatione, to Captain
Roux, who was shot in his cheek, rising, to avoid a clistery, which was
administered unto him, had such a fluxione of blood out of his vvounde, during
the space of eight hovvres, without finding any Chyrurgiane which could
restraygne the blood, so that in the end he was constrained to yield his
spirit, my partakers, or compagnions, Ponget, le Gendre, and Hubert, and I,
being charged to tarry with the King that night in the trenches: therefore the
Chyrurgians ought no more to be blamed, then when the disease is so deeplye
rooted, that there is no more remedy or cure for the same, for as we say by
the common proverb, or old adagye.

To serotine doth Doctor then begin,
When the disease to deep is rooted in.
Many things are oftentimes vvatinge unto the vvouded.
There are othervvyse many things wanting by the vvouded, wherewith we might
succour the, solace the as it is requisite we should. We see oftentimes

being destitute of old linen to dress them with, have very small store of
victuals, and drink, I here omit the poor soldiers, who being wounded
must lodge on the earth.

Obiectio.
Thereto may now be answered: I confess that sometimes of the year are
intemperate, vnordinate, that the air also is infected, corrupted that the
wounded also have want, are destitute of many things, briefly to conclude,
that many of the above rehearsed things do fail: notvvithstandinge there are
many cured without any accidents chauncinge to their vvoundes, and for
whose sake take little pains, have no great expenses, although they are
very sorelye wounded contrarily, other of little shallovv vvoudes do dye.
Ansvver.
To this object I answer, that all vvoundes which seem to be large ample,
are not therefore mortal or perilous, as are the great carnal or fleshye
dissolutions or vvoundes, wherein none of the vvorthiest parts are touched
which are necessary to the life of man, wherein are nether hurt great
Veins, Arteryes or synnues, but which are only great, cut in the flesh,
as in the Buttock, in the calfe of the Legs or in an Arm and yet
notvvithstandinge be called a great vvounde, not being so dangerous, as the
least punctio of a synnue is, or the hurt of a great Vain or Arterye, or else
a little fissure of the Cranium, or any small thrust in the Breast, or the
inferior part of the Belly, percinge into the concavitye of the same.
Dangerous vvoundes
And if so be there vveare any which of such vvoundes vveare cured, being
great vvoundes, not considering the apertione, but because of the vvouded
part they ought to be well disposed, to buste strong, to resist such an
evell, impeach the accidents which happen thereunto, that they must also be
rightly disposed tempered without any of the vvorthye parts, or those
which are necessary unto life being thervvithe polluted or corrupted, and such
may easily be cured: Necessary things a good curatione.
So that those which are not vvholye sovvnde of body having received such a
vvounde, are in great danger of death, as others considering the difference of
the time and of the difference of the body, and body, and of parts thereof:
there are some, which are of so good a temperature, that they with great
facility are cured of such vvoudes, the which in others bodies wear mortal:
Contrarily there are some persons, which have received a wound in some abiecte
and not necessary part or Ioyncte, without profoundly penetrating the same,
whereof notvvithstading they died, ether through the constitutio of the time or
through the disposition of the body, the humours where of have their
deflueces towards the wounded part or else through any other occasions, here
before alleged: but the vulgar poeple, can not compraehend all these
circumstances, and considirations, as the Chyrurgians do in such persons.

For to speak truth we look on the time, The vulgar, and common poeple, ca
not so deeplye judge consider any thing as the Chyrurgiane.
one the year, day, number of poeple, all which have bin the one as
sorelye hurt, or vvoundede as the other, yet for all that, those which we
esteem most dangerously vvoudede, we judge to be out of all hope, are
sotimes first of all cured: Where of we do not so much admire, after we
have after death anotomizede the dead corpse: for besides there vvounde we
find, an other apparente occasion of death.

As it is as yet in fresh memory happened to the Lord of Bellay Baron of
Tovarce, History of my lord of of Bellay.
King at d'Ivetot, who having received a shot before Rove, or Roane in his
left Arm, where of the focille of the Elbovve was broken the vvounde being
in good suppu^atio, yielding good matter, and the flesh very rubicund,
grayned as we would desire, the Arm remaining in his natural
dispositione, as well above, as beneathe, without any payne, or
inflammatione, very few, or no agues at all adioyninge thereunto,
notwithstanding al which being brought to his house, tvventy days after his
wound received, is he fallen into a hot, venomous, or furious ague, which

Physicione to the King, have therein taken, whose experience is sufficiently

An other History of my Lord of Gyuri.
The contrary hereof chanced, to the great contentation of all the Nobility,
to my Lord of Gyuri who having received, a terrible shot, in his sinistre
shoulder, the entrace whereof was right in the ioyncture, the bullet having
touched a great part of the apophysis of the bone of the Arm, pearcinge a
lunge the Omoplate, remaining, in the inferiore angle of the foresayed
Omoplate, which bullet was happily extracted drawn out by Mr. Lavenot,
svvoren Chyrurgiane to the King, which very excellently hath dressed him the
first time: notvvithstandinge, he is of this great wound (I say great
vvounde considering that part of the body) vvholy cured, without perceaving
therein any bad accidents.

Nature cures all diseases.
The which we must attribute, partly to the good Temperature compositione,
and nature, (which must cure all diseases,) when as she is evel entreated of
expert, and learnede Physicians, and Chyrurgians, and thereof aided, as very
excellently hath done amongst others, the right vvorshipfull Mr. Portaile,
Counceller, chief Chyrurgian to the King, which as now is chief amongst us:
of Mr. Gillis des Rus, his Chyrurgiane, which dressed him: and we are heer
farther to note, and observe, that there may be an other which being vvouded in
the same place of his body, shall be treacted of the self same Chyrurgians, and
shall yet for all that dye.

And for the confirmatione, of al that which is before rehearced, I will
conclude this my Apology, through my Lords heerevnder nominated, beginning
with my Lord de Hallot de Montmorency, who being on Horseback by the City
of Rovan, received a shot of a canon, of the which his Horse died, he being
prostrated on the earth, his Leg being in dives pieces broke right under the
Ioyncte of the Knee, notvvithstandinge all which, with his good disposition
nature hath cobated against all the malice of time, magnitude of the vvounde,
being very successivelye cured of the right vvorshipfull Mr. Martel,
Chyrurgiane to the King, A^o the History.
who is very expert in all the operations of Chyrurgerye. Contrarilye, my
Lord the Baron of Salignac, being wounded only with a simple shot of a
handgunn, without any fracture of bones, in the end dyed thereof, al though
notvvithstandinge he was assisted by the right vvorshipfull Mr. Pouget, alsoe
one of the Kings Chyrurgians and chieffe in Montpelliers having seen the
hour, in the which the Gangraena chanced to his vvounde, many fluxions
oppressed him, by which means he very difficultlye could be cured: Which
we must only attribute uto his teperature, which was not so good as in
deed it vveare to be exoptated it had.

The most happy cure which Mr. des Hayes hath effected, A another History.
one also of the Kings Chyrurgians, which is a very vvyse man in the art of

The same allsoe happened, in my Lord the Duke of esperno, An other History.
who being wounded with a greateshott, which dilaniated a part of his

but alsoe the good constitutione of the time: For a little before (which was
about the besieging of Chartres) the died most of all the vvouded, The
constitutione of the time is comodious to resanatione
how sound notwithstanding soever they vveare, how little soever they vveare
wounded: which partly we must attribute to the bad constitution of the
time, as in deed at that time it was a disordered weather: because in a

others my Lord the Earl of Flex, An other History.
vvhoe in the end of the besieginge, received a short of a Canon in the

On the same time, my Lord of Favolle was cured, of a shot, which brake

And for an inventio, or practice, which we might say to be vvholy against

whereof in the second dressinge, clean through the dissolutione of the

At the same time trepanede M Gabriell du Tertre, An other History.
a very expert Chyrurgiane in all his operationes, a certain vvomane which

of two months therbefore all those which vveare wounded in the Head died,
notvvithstandinge all the secourse we could do them yet for all that they
dyed suddaynlye.

All which being noted, we must attribute the deaths of so many valiant
Capitaynes, Conclusione of the Apologye.
worthy Lords, and brave soldiers, on such occasions, and disorders, and

As Hesiodus said, sometimes is the day our mother, and sometims our
stepmother.

End of the Apologye for the Chyrurgians.
